\begin{document}

\author{Bernardo Ardini [2146900]}
\date{Padova, 14 marzo 2026}
\title{\textbf{Investigations on some gradient elastoplasticity models: existence results, qualitative analysis and numerics}}

\frontmatter

\include{frontespizio}

\newpage

\vspace*{\fill}

\begin{center}
\large\textsc{Abstract}
\end{center}
\vspace{1em}
Gradient elastoplasticity theories include the dependence on the gradient of the field describing the plastic distortion within a solid body. From the modeling viewpoint, this is motivated by the need to incorporate an internal characteristic length scale and describe size effects. The addition of the dependence on the plastic distortion gradient entails also mathematical benefits for the proof of existence theorems. Indeed, it ensures a sufficiently strong compactness to perform the various passages to the limit when nonlinearities -- which come from complex constitutive models or the geometry of large deformation -- are involved.

In this thesis, we develop and analyze some models of gradient plasticity, mainly addressing the modeling aspects and the existence theory. To develop the mechanical models, we systematically adopt a general framework based on the principle of virtual powers and inspired by the approach to gradient plasticity of Gurtin and Anand, where a microscopic balance of microforces -- those describing the plastic response -- is added alongside the usual macroscopic balance of forces. The first model we study is a simplified one-dimensional model of a metallic cylindrical bar. The assumption is that the configurations are described in terms of solely three fields defined along its length: the axial displacement of the cross section, their transversal dilation, and the axial plastic distortion. We consider two versions of that model: a linearized model valid in the regime of small deformations, and a geometrically nonlinear model that allows for large deformations. In both cases, we develop an existence theory, by resorting to the theory of evolutionary variational inequalities of Han and Reddy for the small deformation model, and to the theory of energetic solutions of rate-independent systems by Mielke and Theil for the large deformation model. Then, we perform an analysis of the particular boundary value problem that describes the tensile test -- the problem in which an assigned tensile force or elongation is prescribed at the ends -- with the aim of understanding whether the model is able to describe the necking phenomenon. We show that the linearized model is not able to predict necking, and we perform a formal stability analysis of the model at large deformations, gaining qualitative insights about the onset of necking.

The second gradient plasticity model we focus on regards plastic materials whose yield strength depends on hydrostatic pressure. Instead of adopting the theory of non-associated plasticity -- commonly employed in the geotechnical modeling of soils and rocks -- we propose an alternative approach. This approach is able to formally reproduce the classical one, even thought its mathematical and modeling foundations differ, avoiding the need of considering non associated flow rules (that are known to be mathematically more difficult to handle) and providing directly a clearer variational structure to the problem. The key idea is to include a kinematic constraint on the volumetric part of the plastic distortion (linking it to a damage field describing the amount of microcraks opened within the material), impose it through a Lagrange multiplier representing an internal pressure, and make the constitutive relation of the deviatoric plastic microforce dependent on it. To prove that the model admits solutions, we develop a general existence theory for constrained mechanical systems and in presence of a dissipation potential possibly depending on the Lagrange multiplier. The proof is based on a time discretization approach: the problem is first discretized in time and the existence of a time-discrete approximate solution is established; then, the actual continuous-in-time solution is recovered in the limit of vanishing time step. The incremental problem that emerges from the time discretization has a nonlinear saddle-point structure and is solved as follows: first, the constrain is exploited to explicitate an unknown in terms of the remaining unknowns; these are found for an arbitrary value of the Lagrange multiplier by solving a static nonlinear problem (the existence of solution is established using a non-smooth version of the Minty--Browder theorem). Finally, the Lagrange multiplier is recovered as the solution of a fixed-point problem, the existence of which is obtained by an application of the Schauder fixed-point theorem.

\vspace*{\fill}

To make the analysis more tractable it would be better if the localization is captured also by a small strain model. In the field of metal plasticity the necking is an example of strain localization. Since Von Mises plasticity with hardening at small strain does not exhibit bifurcation (uniqueness and stability are is guaranteed) then to detect the necking instability it is necessary to consider the geometric nonlinearity. Instead in materials whose yield strength depends on the pressure (for example granular materials), localization is evident also in the small strain regime (the formation of shear bands). For that reason I decided to focus my attention to that model.

%Instead of adopting the usual formulation of the nonassociative Drucker--Prager model I reformulated it in an alternative way for the following two reasons
%\begin{itemize}
%\item[-] \emph{Mechanical reason}. The Drucker--Prager model is formulated by formally adopting the same framework used in metal plasticity to materials whose yield strength depend on pressure. The difference from metal plasticity is that volumetric plastic strains are allowed and the yield region is chosen in the space $\text{Sym}$ of all symmetric stress tensors instead of $\text{DevSym}$. This means that the deviatoric and volumetric plastic response are described as a unique plasticity mechanism. This is actually nonphysical since while the deviatoric plastic response is a friction mechanism (that increases with the pressure, a sort of internal Coulomb friction), the change in the volumetric plastic strain (the dilatancy) is due to a kinematical constrain (the grains must distance themselves from each other to be able to slide over each other). Since the Drucker--Prager model associative overestimates dilatancy the nonassociative behavior is introduced. This ad hoc modification is a clear sign that the deviatoric plastic response should be treated independently from the changes in the volumetric plastic strain instead.
%\item[-] \emph{Mathematical reason}. Non associative plasticity is difficult to be handled mathematically since it has a not evident variational structure~\cite{morafrancfort}.
%\end{itemize}

To formulate the model I started from the framework of plastic strain gradient viscoplasticity of Gurtin and Anand (developed for metals) based on the principle of virtual powers and on macroforce and microforce balances.
%
%I have proved the existence of a global in time solution using the Rothe method (up to errors in the proof). First we need to solve the discrete in time problem. At each time step there is a nonlinear mixed elliptic (saddle point) problem to solve (it is mixed due to the presence of the constrain). It is solved by analogy with the linear mixed elliptic problem: first the constrained is solved, then knowing the constrained space we solve the leading elliptic problem, then the Lagrange multiplier is determined. The leading elliptic problem is solved using the Minty--Browder theorem (the minimization works as well but it fails when the material is dilatant because the potential structure is lost, for that reason I opted for the Minty--Browder theorem). Due to the pressure dependent yield strength the leading elliptic problem gives a pressure dependent solution. The existence of the pressure is obtained with the Schauder fixed point theorem. The need of compactness, necessary to apply the Schauder fixed point theorem, obliged us to add a further regularization term depending on the gradient of the macroscopic strain (in this way we have constructed a second gradient mechanical model~\cite{gurtin-fried}). Maybe without the second gradient regularization the solution could be found in $BD(\Omega)$ has happen in~\cite{daldessol09} but I decided to not investigate this possibility. To get the time continuous solution we need to pass to the vanishing time step limit. This is done by first obtaining a priori estimates on the kinematical variables and their rates and then weakly convergent subsequences are extracted. To handle the nonlinear dependence on the pressure in the limit passage, we establish the strong convergence of the pressure using the Aubin--Lions lemma. To get the a priori estimates on the rates I added both a macroscopic and a microscopic viscosity.
%

This is done locally recasting the problem as an ODE using the implicit function theorem and then proving the local existence, uniqueness and regularity in time using the Cauchy--Lipschitz--Picard theorem. This ensure that the global solution is unique and regular in time.

\chapter{Introductory notions regarding mechanical modeling and plasticity theory}
\label{chap:modeling}

%\section{Review of kinematics of deformable bodies}

\section{A general framework for developing mechanical models}
\label{sec:ppv}

In this section we describe a general framework that allows to systematically develop mechanical models. We will use it repeatedly through this thesis. The framework we are going to present, is based on the principle of virtual powers, which is a powerful and elegant tool to describe the equilibrium of the ``forces'' acting on a mechanical system. The ``forces'' must be intended in a generalized sense here. In fact, the configurations of the mechanical system are described as elements of a space (possibly infinite dimensional) of configurations, and therefore the generalized forces must be understood as actions that ``push'' the configuration of the system within this space. Moreover the configuration of the mechanical system can include also the description of internal or microscopic conditions of the system, so that a generalized forces does not necessarily correspond to a mechanical macroscopic distribution of force in the usual sense.

Some historical information about the birth of the principle of virtual powers can be found in~\cite{ppv-encyclopedia} and in~\cite{ppv-maugin}. Modern works that use the principle of virtual powers to develop systematically mechanical model are~\cite{germain,gurtin-fried,gurtin-fried-turbulence}, where second gradient theories are considered, and~\cite{gurtin-anand-small,gurtin-anand-large,non-smooth-thermomechanics} that treat materials with internal variables.

\subsection{Kinematics}

Kinematics is concerned with the description of the possible \emph{configurations} of a mechanical system. We assume that they can be described as the elements of a separable and reflexive Banach space $Z$, the \emph{space of configurations}.
\begin{ex}
\label{ex:modeling:particles}
Consider $N$ material points in $\mathbb{R}^d$. Their configuration can be described by the matrices $x=(x_1,\dots x_N)\in\mathbb{R}^{d\times N}$, where $x_{\alpha}\in\mathbb{R}^d$ with $\alpha\in\{1,\dots,N\}$ is the position of the $\alpha$th material point.
\end{ex}
\begin{ex}
\label{ex:modeling:large-strain}
The configurations of a deformable body at large strain in $\mathbb{R}^d$, can be identified with the deformations $\chi\in W^{1,p}(\Omega)^d$ with $p\in(1,\infty)$, where $\Omega\subset\mathbb{R}^d$, open and bounded subset of $\mathbb{R}^d$ is some reference configuration of the body. Every $x\in\Omega$ is the reference position of a material point and $\chi(x)$ is the position occupied by that material point in the deformed configuration. The condition of incompenetrability, $\det\text{grad}\chi>0$ a.e.\ in $\Omega$, was purposely excluded from the definition of the configuration space in order to preserve the linear structure. Even thought we do not consider the incompenetrability constrain at the level of kinematical description, it can be imposed in a later moment, as we will show.
\end{ex}
\begin{ex}
\label{ex:modeling:micro}
Consider a body that can be subject to damage. This can be modeled considering an internal damage variable $\alpha\in W^{1,p}(\Omega)$ with $p\in(1,\infty)$. Where $\alpha=0$ the material is sound, while where $\alpha=1$ the material is completely damaged. The material is partially damaged where $\alpha\in(0,1)$. One should add the constrain $\alpha\in[0,1]$, but this is not imposes at the level of definition of the space of configurations, again in order to preserve the linear structure.
\end{ex}
Examples~\ref{ex:modeling:large-strain} and~\ref{ex:modeling:micro} suggest that the assumption that space of configurations has a linear structure is not restrictive in most mechanical models, even when the truly attainable configurations actually should lie in a space $M$ with the structure of a manifold. Indeed in most cases, $M$ can be seen as a subset of a larger linear space of configurations $Z$. Then we can formulate the model on $Z$ and impose that the evolution of the configuration of the system is restricted to $M$ by including suitable reactions, as we will see. Thus the geometric complexity of $M$ can be avoided and reduced to the analytical complexity of managing the reactions within $Z$ required to enforce the constraint

\begin{rmk}
As Example~\ref{ex:modeling:micro} show, the configuration of the mechanical system can include also the description of an internal state of the system that does not necessarily correspond with the macroscopic observable placement of the system in the space. This is particularly true also in the case of plasticity theory, that will be discussed in the next section.
\end{rmk}

\subsection{Statics} With statics we mean the treatment of equilibrium of forces, in a generalized sense, acting on a mechanical system. Any \emph{generalized force} acting on the mechanical system, can be described as a linear functional $\mathcal{F}\in Z^*$. Given any $\tilde w\in Z$, the duality pairing $\dual{\mathcal{F},\tilde w}$ can be interpreted as the projection of $\mathcal{F}$ along the direction $\tilde w$. If we interpret $\tilde w$ as a virtual rate, that is $\tilde w=\dot{\tilde z}(0)$ for an arbitrary evolution $\tilde{z}\in C^1([0,1],Z)$ of the configuration of the system, then $\dual{\mathcal{F},\tilde w}$ is called the virtual power of $\mathcal{F}$ along the virtual rate $\tilde{w}$.
\begin{ex}(Example~\ref{ex:modeling:particles} continued)
\label{ex:modeling:particles-continued}
A generalized force can be seen as an element $F=(F_1,\dots,F_N)\in(\mathbb{R}^{d\times N})^*=\mathbb{R}^{d\times N}$. The virtual power acts on $\tilde v=(\tilde v_1,\dots,\tilde v_N)$ as
\[
\dual{F,\tilde v}=\sum_{\alpha=1}^NF_\alpha\cdot\tilde v_\alpha.
\]
Here $F_\alpha$ is the force acting on the $\alpha$th material point.
\end{ex}
\begin{ex}(Example~\ref{ex:modeling:large-strain} continued)
Consider the functional $\mathcal{F}\in W^{-1,p^*}(\Omega)^d$ of the form
\[
\dual{\mathcal{F},\tilde v}=\int_\Omega P:\Grad\tilde vdX+\int_\Omega f\cdot\tilde vdX+\int_{\partial\Omega}h\cdot\tilde vdX,
\]
where $P\in L^{P^*}(\Omega,\mathbb{R}^{d\times d})$, $f\in L^{p^*}(\Omega)^d$ and $h\in L^{p^*}(\partial\Omega)^d$. The first integral represents a generalized force that acts in the direction of the local bending of the body, the second integral represents a volume force distributed over $\Omega$, the last integral a surface force distributed over $\partial\Omega$.
\end{ex}
Consider now a time interval $[0,T]$ and an actual evolution of the configuration of the system, $z\colon[0,T]\to Z$. For simplicity, in this discussion we assume that $z\in\text{AC}([0,T],Z)$ even thought less regular evolutions, for example exhibiting jumps, can be considered as we will see subsequently in this thesis. Along its evolution, the mechanical system is subject to various generalized forces and we call $\mathcal{F}\colon[0,T]\to Z^*$ their sum. We require that equilibrium holds, that is
\[
\mathcal{F}(t)=0\quad\text{for a.e.\ $t\in(0,T)$}.
\]
This can be written also as
\begin{equation}
\label{eqn:modeling:ppv}
\dual{\mathcal{F}(t),\tilde{w}}=0\quad\text{for all $\tilde w\in Z$ and a.e.\ $t\in(0,T)$}.
\end{equation}
Equation~\eqref{eqn:modeling:ppv} is the statement of the principle of virtual powers, that is, the virtual power of the generalized force acting on the system along all virtual rates must vanish.

\subsection{Constitutive relations}

To get an evolution equation, able to predict the evolution of the mechanical system, it is necessary to link $\mathcal{F}$ to the evolution $z$ through constitutive relations. We focus on constitutive relations of the form
\[
\mathcal{F}(t)\in\hat{\mathcal{F}}(z(t),\dot{z}(t),t)\quad\text{for a.e.\ $t\in(0,T)$},
\]
where $\hat{\mathcal{F}}\colon Z\times Z\times[0,T]\rightsquigarrow Z^*$. We postulate that
\begin{equation}
\label{eqn:modeling:constitutive-relations}
\hat{\mathcal{F}}(z,w,t)=-\partial_z\mathcal{E}(z,t)-\partial_w\mathcal{R}(z,w,t),
\end{equation}
where $\mathcal{E}\colon Z\times[0,T]\to(-\infty,\infty]$ and $\mathcal{R}\colon Z\times Z\times[0,T]\to[0,\infty]$ are called respectively the \emph{energy} and the \emph{dissipation potential}. The $\partial$ symbol can have different definitions according to the regularity of $\mathcal{E}$ and $\mathcal{R}$. If FrĂ©chet differentiability is assumed then $\partial$ denotes the FrĂ©chet differential, while if only convexity is assumed it denotes the subdifferential. Less regularity requires to use extended notions of subdifferentials such as the FrĂ©chet subdifferential. In view of~\eqref{eqn:modeling:constitutive-relations}, we can split $\mathcal{F}=\mathcal{F}_c+\mathcal{F}_d$, where $\mathcal{F}_c(t)\in-\partial_z\mathcal{E}(z(t),t)$ is the conservative generalized force and $\mathcal{F}_d(t)\in-\partial_w\mathcal{R}(z(t),\dot{z}(t),t)$ is the dissipative generalized force. The requirement that $\mathcal{F}_d$ has a dissipative behavior can be formulated as
\[
\dual{\mathcal{F}_d(t),\dot{z}(t)}\le0\quad\text{for a.e.\ $t\in(0,T)$}.z
\]
This means that the projection of $\mathcal{F}_d$ along the direction of the rate is negative, or equivalently that the power of $\mathcal{F}_d$ is nonpositive. This is automatically satisfied if
\[
\text{$\dual{\xi,w}\ge0$ for all $(z,w,t)\in Z\times Z\times[0,T]$ and $\xi\in\partial_w\mathcal{R}(z,w,t)$}.
\]
This last condition is called \emph{dissipation inequality}.

In most situations one can split $\mathcal{F}=\mathcal{X}-\mathcal{I}$, where $\mathcal{X}$ and $\mathcal{I}$ from $[0,T]$ to $Z^*$ are the external generalized force and the internal generalized force respectively. The internal generalized force is due to the internal response of the mechanical system, while the external generalized force represents the external actions imposed on it. In this case the constitutive relations are usually specialized to
\begin{itemize}
\item[(i)] $\mathcal{E}\colon Z\times[0,T]\to(-\infty,\infty]$ of the form $\mathcal{E}(z,t)=\Psi(z)-\dual{\mathcal{X}(t),z}$, where $\Psi\colon Z\to(-\infty,\infty]$ is the free energy, describing the conservative internal response, and $\mathcal{X}\colon[0,T]\to Z^*$ is the external generalized force,
\item[(ii)] $\mathcal{R}(z,w,t)=\Phi(z,w)$, where $\Phi\colon Z\times Z\to[0,\infty]$ is the dissipation potential describing the dissipative internal response.
\end{itemize}
Then the constitutive relations can be written as $\mathcal{I}(t)\in\hat{\mathcal{I}}(z(t),\dot{z}(t))$ for a.e.\ $t\in(0,T)$, where $\hat{\mathcal{I}}\colon Z\times Z\rightsquigarrow Z^*$ is given by
\begin{equation}
\label{eqn:modeling:constitutive}
\hat{\mathcal{I}}(z,w)=\partial\Psi(z)+\partial_w\Phi(z,w).
\end{equation}
In this case the equilibrium condition reads
\begin{equation}
\label{eqn:modeling:doubly:nonlinear-neat}
\partial\Psi(z(t))+\partial_w\Phi(z(t),\dot z(t))\ni\mathcal{I}(t)=\mathcal{X}(t)\quad\text{for a.e.\ $t\in(0,T)$}
\end{equation}
or
\begin{equation}
\label{eqn:modeling:doubly-nonlinear}
\partial_z\mathcal{E}(z(t),t)+\partial_w\Phi(z(t),\dot z(t))\ni0\quad\text{for a.e.\ $t\in(0,T)$}.
\end{equation}
This is known as the \emph{doubly nonlinear equation}. Now the evolution problem can be formulates as follows.
\begin{prb}
\label{prb:modeling:doubly-nonlinear}
Let $z_0\in\dom\mathcal{E}(\cdot,0)$ and $\mathcal{X}\colon[0,T]\to Z^*$. Find $z\in\text{AC}([0,T],Z)$ such that~\eqref{eqn:modeling:doubly-nonlinear} holds.
\end{prb}
\begin{rmk}
Before imposing particular constitutive relations, $\mathcal{I}$ and $\mathcal{X}$ are completely undefined. However we notice that, before making specific constitutive assumptions, when one develop a new mechanical model it is useful to understand which is the particular structure that $\mathcal{I}$ and $\mathcal{X}$ must assume in order to describe the forces involved in the mechanical system of interest. This is the approach that will be followed in this thesis.
% For example, if the mechanical system is the one described in Examples~\ref{ex:modeling:particles} and~\ref{ex:modeling:particles-continued} with $N=2$ and we want to build a mechanical model in which the only internal actions are a force $f(t)\in\mathbb{R}^d$, then we consider the case $\dual{\mathcal{I}(t),\tilde v}=f(t)\cdot(\tilde v_1-\tilde v_2)$. The force $f(t)$ is still undefined and can be fixed through suitable constitutive assumptions.
\end{rmk}
\begin{rmk}
In terms of $\Phi$, the dissipative inequality reads
\begin{equation}
\label{eqn:modeling:dissipative-inequality}
\text{$\dual{\xi,w}\ge0$ for all $(z,w)\in Z\times Z$ and $\xi\in\partial_w\Phi(z,w)$}.
\end{equation}
A possible condition ensuring~\eqref{eqn:modeling:dissipative-inequality} is that $\Phi(z,\cdot)$ is convex and $\Phi(z,0)=0$. Indeed if $(z,w)\in Z\times Z$ and $\xi\in\partial_w\Phi(z,w)$, then by definition of subdifferential, $0=\Phi(z,0)\ge\Phi(z,w)+\dual{\xi,0-w}$, so that $\dual{\xi,w}\ge\Phi(z,w)\ge0$.
\end{rmk}
\begin{rmk}[Rate-independent versus rate-dependent models]
The a mechanical model described by~\eqref{eqn:modeling:doubly:nonlinear-neat} is said to be rate independent if when $z$ is a solution with loads $\mathcal{X}$, also $\tilde z(s)=z(\zeta(s))$ is a solution with loads $\mathcal{X}(s)=\mathcal{X}(\zeta(t))$, where $\zeta\colon[0,S]\to[0,T]$ is a smooth and monotone increasing reparametrization of time. Thus rate independence is equivalent to the requirement that $\partial\Phi(z,w)=\partial\Phi(z,\lambda w)$ for all $\lambda>0$. This is true is $\Phi(z,\lambda w)=\lambda\Phi(z,w)$ for all $\lambda>0$ and in this case $\Phi$ is said to be positively 1-homogeneous.
\end{rmk}

\subsection{Adding constraints with the method of Lagrange multipliers}
\label{subsec:lagrange-multipliers}

To include a kinematical constrains on the process, say $z(t)\in K$ for all $t\in[0,T]$, one can set $\mathcal{E}(z,t)=\infty$ if $z\notin K$. This can be done by including $\iota_K$ in $\mathcal{E}$. In this way $\mathcal{F}_c(t)$ will include the reaction $\mathcal{P}(t)\in-N_K(z(t))\subset Z^*$ required to enforce the constrain. Here $N_K=\partial\iota_K$.
\begin{ex}(Example~\ref{ex:modeling:large-strain} continued)
Let $\Phi\colon W^{1,p^*}(\Omega)^d\to(-\infty,\infty]$. To describe an elastic response, we choose $\Psi$ of the form
\[
\Psi(\chi)=\int_\Omega W(X,\Grad\chi(X))dX,
\]
where $W\colon\Omega\times\mathbb{R}^{d\times d}\to(-\infty,\infty]$ is a continuous free energy density. Incompenetrability impose that $W(X,F)=\infty$ if $\det F\le0$.
\end{ex}
\begin{ex}(Example~\ref{ex:modeling:micro} continued)
To ensure that $\alpha(x)\in[0,1]$ for a.e.\ $x\in\Omega$, one choose $\Phi\colon W^{1,p}(\Omega)\to(-\infty,\infty]$ such that $\Phi(z)=\infty$ if $\alpha\notin[0,1]$ on a set of positive measure.
\end{ex}
However there are situations in which it would be desirable to treat the constraint reaction $\mathcal{P}$ as an independent variable instead. In this sense $\mathcal{P}$ can be considered as an additional descriptor of the configuration of the system. This is particularly useful if one wants to consider constitutive relations depending on the reaction $\mathcal{P}$ itself, as it happens for the model that is discussed in Part~\ref{part:my-model}. Then we split the generalized force as $\mathcal{F}=-\mathcal{I}+\mathcal{X}+\mathcal{P}$. The constitutive relations are imposed on $\mathcal{I}$ only as
\[
\mathcal{I}(t)\in-\partial_z\Psi(\mathcal{P}(t),z(t))-\partial_w\Phi(\mathcal{P}(t),z(t),\dot z(t))\quad\text{for a.e.\ $t\in(0,T)$},
\]
where now $\Psi\colon Z^*\times Z\to(-\infty,\infty]$ and $\Phi\colon Z^*\times Z\times Z\to[0,\infty]$. Instead
$\mathcal{X}$ is assigned while $\mathcal{P}$ is included among the unknowns of the evolution together with $u$. Then the evolution equation can be written as
\begin{equation}
\label{eqn:modeling:doubly-nonlinear-equation-constrain}
\begin{split}
z(t)&\in K\\
\mathcal{P}(t)&\in-N_K(z(t))
\end{split}
\quad\text{and}\quad\partial_z\mathcal{E}(\mathcal{P}(t),z(t),t)+\partial_w\Phi(\mathcal{P}(t),z(t),\dot z(t))\ni\mathcal{P}(t)\quad\text{for a.e.\ $t\in(0,T)$},
\end{equation}
where now $\mathcal{E}\colon Z^*\times Z\times[0,T]\to(-\infty,\infty]$ is defined by $\mathcal{E}(\mathcal{P},z,t)=\Psi(\mathcal{P},z)-\dual{\mathcal{X}(t),z}$. Then the evolution problem becomes as follows.
\begin{prb}
\label{prb:constrained}
Let $z_0\in\dom\mathcal{E}(\cdot,0)$ and $\mathcal{X}\colon[0,T]\to Z^*$. Find $z\in\text{AC}([0,T],Z)$ and $\mathcal{P}\colon[0,T]\to Z^*$ such that~\eqref{eqn:modeling:doubly-nonlinear-equation-constrain} holds.
\end{prb}

\section{A review of plasticity theory}
\label{sec:plasticity-review}

%Le origini della teoria della plasticitĂ  si fanno risalire al lavoro sperimentale di Tresca~\cite{Tresca1872} alla fine del diciannovesimo secolo sulla deformazione dei metalli. Egli riconobbe che il comportamento di un metallo Ă¨ elastico fintanto che gli stress non superano una certa soglia: oltre la soglia, detta di soglia di snervamento, essi ``fluiscono'' in modo plastico accumulando deformazione permanente. Tresca, basandosi probabilmente sul lavoro di Coulomb sulla stabilitĂ  dei terreni~\cite{Coulomb1776}, propose anche un primo criterio matematico per descrivere la soglia di snervamento, basato sulla massima tensione di taglio. Poco dopo De Saint Venant~\cite{SaintVenant1871} e LĂ©vy~\cite{Levy1871} produssero la prima teoria per spiegare l'evoluzione temporale di un solido rigido-plastico soggetto a carichi esterni. Agli inizi del ventesimo secolo la teoria fu estesa da Prandl~\cite{Prandtl1924}, Von Mises~\cite{vonMises1928} e Reuss~\cite{Reuss1939} al caso elastoplastico. Fino agli anni settanta la teoria della plasticitĂ  veniva formulata in termini incrementali, legando cioĂ¨ l'incremento temporale dello stress al rate dello strain. Una formulazione variazionale, matematicamente piĂą elegante e solida, fu introdotta da Moreau~\cite{Moreau1974} negli anni sessanta. Con lo scopo di dare una fomulazione coerente dei problemi della plasticitĂ  ( e dell'attrito), Moreau inventĂ˛ anche importanti nozioni di analisi convessa (come i subdifferenziali) necessarie per trattare i problemi non lisci della meccanica.

The origins of the theory of plasticity can be traced back to the experimental work of Tresca~\cite{Tresca1872} in the late nineteenth century on the deformation of metals. He recognized that the behavior of a metal is elastic as long as the stresses do not exceed a certain threshold: beyond the threshold, called the yield point, they ``flow'' in a plastic way, accumulating permanent deformation. Tresca, probably inspired on Coulomb's work on the stability of soils~\cite{Coulomb1776}, also proposed a first criterion to describe the yield threshold, based on the maximum shear stress. Shortly afterwards De Saint Venant~\cite{SaintVenant1871} and LĂ©vy~\cite{Levy1871} produced the first theory to explain the temporal evolution of a rigid-plastic solid subjected to external loads. At the beginning of the twentieth century the theory was extended by Prandl~\cite{Prandtl1924}, Von Mises~\cite{vonMises1928} and Reuss~\cite{Reuss1939} to the elastoplastic case. Until the seventies, the theory of plasticity was formulated in incremental terms, i.e. linking the temporal increase in stress to the rate of strain. A variational formulation, mathematically more elegant and robust, was introduced by Moreau~\cite{Moreau1974} in the sixties. With the aim of giving a coherent formulation of the problems of plasticity (and friction), Moreau also invented important notions of convex analysis (such as subdifferentials) necessary to deal with the non-smooth problems of mechanics.

To describe the experimentally observed increase of the yield strength of metals at small scales, since the work of Aifantis~\cite{Aifantis1984} in 1984, has been developed plasticity models (see~\cite{FleckHutchinson1993} for the early developments) that include a dependence on the gradient of the kinematical field describing the plastic distortion. This allows to include in the model an internal characteristic length scale that is not considered by classical plasticity. One refer to this kind of models as the gradient plasticity theory. Modern formulations are given in~\cite{gurtin-anand-small},~\cite{gurtin-anand-large} and \cite{BertramForest2020}. The importance of the gradient plasticity theory in this thesis is mainly due to a mathematical issue rather then a modeling concern. Indeed we will see that the inclusion of the dependence the plastic distortion gradient will be necessary to ensure compactness properties needed to handle successfully the nonconvex character of plasticity at large strain in Part~\ref{part:his-model} and the complex nonlinear couplings of the model discussed in Part~\ref{part:my-model}.

%L'estensione della teoria al caso di grandi deformazioni Ă¨ stata affrontata a partire dagli anni sessanta, e non Ă¨ mai stato raggiunto un consenso unanime tra le diverse scuole~\cite{Naghdi1990,DavoliFrancfort2021}, soprarttutto per quel che concerne la scelta e l'interpretazione della variabile descrivente la deformazione plastica permanenente~\cite{Naghdi1990}. In questa tesi adotteremo l'approccio di Gurtin e Anand~\cite{gurtin-anand-fried,gurtin-anand-large} e di Mielke et al.~\cite{mielke}, l'unico per il quale Ă¨ stata succefully obtained an  existence theory.

The extension of the theory to the case of large deformations has been addressed since the sixties, and a unanimous consensus has never been reached between the different schools~\cite{Naghdi1990,DavoliFrancfort2021}, especially with regard to the choice and interpretation of the variable describing the permanent plastic deformation. In this thesis we will adopt the approach of Gurtin and Anand~\cite{gurtin-anand-fried,gurtin-anand-large} and of Mielke et al.~\cite{mielke}, the only one for which a theory of existence was successfully obtained.

In this section we give a concise description of (gradient) plasticity theory, both at regime of small and large deformations, inserting it in the general framework proposed in Section~\ref{sec:ppv}. We will confine ourself to the plasticity of metals; in Part\ref{part:my-model} we will discuss how to theory can be modified to describe other types of materials. The focus of this discussion will be on the formulation of the mechanical models, in the following chapter we will address the mathematical questions regarding the existence of solutions.
%Our presentation is based mainly on~\cite{gurtin-anand-fried,gurtin-anand-small,gurtin-anand-large}.

\subsection{Plasticity theory at small deformations}

Let $\Omega\subset\mathbb{R}^d$, a bounded, open and Lipschitz domain, be the reference configuration of a body. Each $x\in\Omega$ is the reference position of a material point belonging to the body. The macroscopic configuration of the body is described by the displacement field $u\colon\Omega\to\mathbb{R}^d$ that assign to each material point $x\in\Omega$ the displacement $u(x)$ from its reference position. The displacement gradient is defined as $H=\grad u$ and is additively decomposed as $H=W+E$, where $W=\ske H$ represents a rigid spin of the material while $E=\sym H$ is the strain. The distortion of the microscopic structure of the body is described by the field $H_p\colon\Omega\to\text{Lin}$, called plastic distortion. We decompose additively $H_p=W_p+E_p$, where $W_p=\ske H_p$ is the plastic spin and $E_p=\sym H_p$ is the plastic strain. In what follows we assume\footnote{Nonzero plastic spin are considered by the micropolar continuum theories, or Cosserat theories ,introduced by the Cosserat brothers~\cite{cosserat1909} at the beginning of the twentieth century. They are suitable for describing, for example, granular materials when is relevant the change of the orientations of the grains.} for simplicity that that $W_p=0$. Moreover we also add the hypotheses of plastic incompressibility $\tr E_p=0$ so that $E_p\colon\Omega\to\text{DevSym}$: this hypotheses is suitable for the description of metals but it is not good for other kind of materials such as geomaterials, as we will discuss in Part~\ref{part:my-model}. Now the configuration of the system is described by the couples $z=(u,E_p)\in Z$ where the space $Z$ is chosen case by case depending on the particular constitutive model.

The plastic strain $E_p$ can be considered as the elastically relaxed strain of the material, so we define the elastic strain as $E_e=E-E_p$.
\begin{rmk}
\label{rmk:plasticity-simplified}
To clearly understand the significance of the definition of elastic strain $E_e$, it is helpful to have this picture in mind. We view the strain $E$ as ``resting upon'' the plastic strain $E_p$, with the elastic strain $E_e$ acting as the coupling mechanism that transfers force between them. This concept can be illustrated with the following simple mechanical model. Consider a damper with one end fixed to a wall and the other at horizontal position $x_p\in\mathbb{R}$, and a spring in series with the damper whose end is at horizontal position $x\in\mathbb{R}$. The configuration of the system is described by the couples $z=(x,x_p)\in\mathbb{R}^2$, and we view $x$ as the ``macroscopic'' configuration and $x_p$ as the internal or ``microscopic'' configuration. We define $x_e=x-x_p\in\mathbb{R}$ the length of the spring.
\begin{center}
\begin{tikzpicture}[thick, scale=1]
% Connessioni verticali
\draw (0,-1) -- (0.5,-1);
\draw (4,-1) -- (3.5,-1);
\draw[pattern=bricks] (4,-2) rectangle (6,-0);

% Ramo Inferiore: Molla Te (disegnata segmento per segmento)
\draw (0.5,-1) -- (0.571,-0.75) -- (0.714,-1.25) -- (0.857,-0.75) -- (1,-1.25) -- (1.143,-0.75) -- (1.286,-1.25) -- (1.429,-0.75) -- (1.5,-1);
\node[anchor=north] at (1,-1.25) {$T_e$};
\node[anchor=south] at (1,-0.75) {$x_e$};
\draw (1.5,-1) -- (3,-1);

% Ramo Inferiore: Smorzatore Tp
\draw (3,-1.125) -- (3,-0.875); % Pistone
\draw (2.5,-0.75) -- (3.5,-0.75) -- (3.5,-1.25) -- (2.5,-1.25); % Cilindro
\node[anchor=north] at (3,-1.25) {$T_p$};

\draw[fill=black] (0,-1) circle (0.03);
\node[anchor=south] at (0,-1) {$x$};

\draw[fill=black] (2,-1) circle (0.03);
\node[anchor=south] at (2,-1) {$x_p$};

\draw[latex-] (-1.1,-1) -- (-0.1,-1);
\node[anchor=north] at (-0.55,-1) {$F$};
\end{tikzpicture}
\end{center}
On $x$ acts the external force $F\in\mathbb{R}$, the wall exert a force $T_p\in\mathbb{R}$ on the damper and the ``macroscopic'' and ``microscopic'' points $x$ and $x_p$ exchange a force $T_e\in\mathbb{R}$. If we call $\tilde w=(\tilde v,\tilde v_p)\in\mathbb{R}^2$ a generic virtual rate of the system, and we define accordingly $\tilde v_e=\tilde v-\tilde v_e\in\mathbb{R}$ the elastic virtual rate, this configuration of forces is described by the internal and external virtual powers
\[
\dual{\mathcal{I},\tilde w}=T_e\tilde v_e+T_p=T_e(\tilde v-\tilde v_p)+T_p\tilde v_p\quad\text{and}\quad\dual{\mathcal{X},\tilde w}=F\tilde v
\]
respectively. Thus the equilibrium condition $\mathcal{I}=\mathcal{X}$ can be written as the ``macroscopic'' balance of forces $T_e=F$ together with the ``microscopic'' balance of forces $T_p=T_e$.
\end{rmk}
Motivated by the consideration in Remark~\ref{rmk:plasticity-simplified}, we consider the virtual rates $\tilde w=(\tilde v,\tilde V_p)\in Z$ of $z$, we define the elastic virtual rate as $\tilde V_e=E\tilde v-V_p$ and we consider and internal and an external virtual powers functionals of the form
\begin{equation}
\label{eqn:powers-plasticity}
\dual{\mathcal{I},\tilde w}=\int_\Omega T_e:\tilde V_edx+\int_\Omega\left(T_p:\tilde V_p+K_p\threedots\grad\tilde V_p\right)dx
\quad\text{and}\quad
\dual{\mathcal{X},\tilde w}=\int_\Omega f\cdot\tilde vdx+\int_{\partial\Omega}h\cdot\tilde v
\end{equation}
respectively. In the expression of the internal virtual power, $T_e\colon\Omega\to\text{Sym}$ is the elastic stress, $T_p\colon\Omega\to\text{DevSym}$ is the plastic microforce and $K_p\colon\Omega\to\text{DevSym}\otimes\mathbb{R}$ is the plastic microstress. The fact that $K_p$ appears qualifies the plasticity theory we are developing as a gradient plasticity theory. In contrast to classical plasticity, where $K_p=0$, gradient plasticity allows a ``contact'' interaction at the level of the microscopic structure. The external virtual power includes a volumetric force $f\colon\Omega\to\mathbb{R}^d$ and a surface force $h\colon\partial\Omega\to\mathbb{R}^d$. Also external microforces acting on the microscopic configuration, i.e. terms conjugate to the plastic virtual rate $\tilde V_p$, could be added, but in order to keep the discussion simpler we neglect them.

By a simple application of the Green theorem and the fundamental lemma of the calculus of variations, one easily get the strong form of the equilibrium condition $\mathcal{I}=\mathcal{X}$,
\begin{subequations}
\label{eqn:equilibrium-plasticity}
\begin{gather}
\diver T_e+f=0\quad\text{in $\Omega$} \\
T_en=h\quad\text{in $\partial\Omega$} \\
\dev T_e-T_p+\diver K_p=0\quad\text{in $\Omega$} \\
K_pn=0\quad\text{in $\partial\Omega$}.
\end{gather}
\end{subequations}
The first two conditions express the balance of macroscopic forces, while the last two represents the balance of microscopic forces.

The choice of constitutive relations is driven by the idea that the elastic response is conservative while the plastic response is dissipative. Thus one choose a free energy $\Psi\colon Z\to\mathbb{R}$ and a dissipation potential $\Phi\colon Z\to[0,\infty)$ of the form
\[
\Psi(z)=\int_\Omega W_e(E_e)dx+\int_\Omega W_p(E_p,\grad E_p)dx\quad\text{and}\quad\Phi(\dot z)=\int_\Omega R(\dot E_p)dx,
\]
where
\begin{itemize}
\item[(i)] the elastic energy density $W_e\colon\text{Sym}\to\mathbb{R}$ and the plastic energy density $W_p\colon\text{DevPla}\to\mathbb{R}$ are differentiable and
\item[(ii)] the plastic dissipation potential density $R\colon\text{DevSym}\to[0,\infty)$ is convex and satisfies $R(0)=0$.
\end{itemize}
The condition $\mathcal{I}\in\partial\Psi(z)+\partial\Phi(\dot z)$ imposes that
\[
T_e=\dind{W_e}{E_e}(E_e),\qquad T_p\in\dind{W_p}{E_p}(E_p,\grad E_p)+\partial R(\dot E_p)\quad\text{and}\quad K_p=\dind{W_p}{\grad E_p}(E_p,\grad E_p).
\]
Usually one assumes a linear elastic response, corresponding to
\[
W_e(E_e)=\frac{1}{2}\mathfrak{C}E_e:E_e=\frac{1}{2}\kappa(\tr E_e)^2+\mu|\dev E_e|^2,
\]
where $\kappa>0$ is the bulk modulus and $\mu$ is the shear modulus. A possible choice of the plastic free energy is
\[
W_p(E_p,\grad E_p)=w_p(E_p)+\frac{1}{2}l^2\mathfrak{b}_2|\grad E_p|^2
\]
where $\mathcal{b}_2>0$ is a constant with the dimensions of a stress and $l>0$ is a constant with the dimensions of length. The term containing the gradient of $E_p$ act as a regularization of the plastic strain field, since it penalize sharp variations of $E_p$. Moreover it includes an internal length scale on the model, that is otherwise absent in the classical non-gradient plasticity theory. The quantity $B_p=w_p'(E_p)$ is called back-stress and represents the conservative part of the plastic microforce. The classical Von Mises rate rate-independent constitutive relation for the dissipative part of the plastic microforce is obtained choosing $R(\dot E_p)=Y|\dot E_p|$, where $Y>0$ is the yield strength. In this case
\[
\partial R(\dot E_p)=
\begin{cases}
\bar B(0,Y) & \text{if $\dot E_p=0$} \\
\left\{Y\dfrac{\dot E_p}{|\dot E_p|}\right\} & \text{if $\dot E_p\neq0$}.
\end{cases}
\]
\begin{rmk}[Rate-dependent versus rate-dependent plasticity]
A peculiar characteristic of the Von Mises constitutive relation is that $R$ is 1-homogeneous so that the plastic microforce $\partial R(\dot E_p)$ depends on the direction only of the plastic strain rate, but not depends on its modulus. As observed in previous subsection, this implies that the material response is rate-independent. From the modeling viewpoint, the behavior of metals at room temperature is only slightly rate dependent, and the rate-dependence increase with increasing temperatures.
\end{rmk}
Now, the microforce balance can be written as
\[
\dev T_e-B_p+\diver K_p\in\partial R(\dot E_p).
\]
The mechanical meaning of Von Mises costitutive relation is that, when $|\dev T_e-B_p+\diver K_p|<Y$, then $\dot E_p=0$ and no plastic strain accumulates. If one choose $w_p(E_p)=\frac{1}{2}\mathfrak{B}_1|E_p|^2$, then $B_p=\mathfrak{B}_1E_p$ so that the back-stress model an increasing resistance of the material to plastic flow. This behavior is common in metals and is called hardening. Refined hardening models can be obtained by choosing $w_p$ appropriately.
\begin{rmk}
\label{rmk:flow-rule}
More general expression of $R$ can be obtained as follows. One choose a bounded, closed and convex set $C\subset\text{DevSym}$ and impose that
\[
\dev T_e-B_p+\diver K_p\in C\quad\text{and}\quad\dot E_p\in N_C(\dev T_e-B_p+\diver K_p)
\]
where $N_C$ is the outer normal cone to $C$. The first condition is the requirement that the dissipative plastic force must belong to $C$, the second is a flow rule of the plastic strain. Using a well known result of convex analysis~\cite[Corollary 5.2]{ekeland-temam}, this is equivalent to
\[
\dev T_e-B_p+\diver K_p\in\partial\sigma_C(\dot E_p)
\]
where $\sigma_C\colon\text{DevSym}\to\mathbb{R}$ is the support function of $C$, defined by
\[
\sigma_C(A)=\sup_{S\in C}S\cdot A.
\]
This behavior is obtained by choosing the dissipation potential density $R=\sigma_C$.
\end{rmk}

\subsection{Plasticity theory at large deformations}

Let $\Omega\subset\mathbb{R}^d$, open, bounded and Lipschitz, be the reference configuration of the body. The macroscopic configuration of the body is described by the placement map $\chi\colon\Omega\to\mathbb{R}^d$ that assign to each material point $x\in\Omega$ its position $\chi(x)$. The local state of deformation is described by the deformation gradient $F=\grad\chi\colon\Omega\to\text{Lin}$. The Cauchy deformation tensor is defined as $C=F^TF$ and have the important property of being invariant under superimposed rigid placement: if $\chi^*(X)=Q\chi(X)+a$, with $Q\in\text{Orth}^+$ and $a\in\mathbb{R}^d$, then $F^*=QF$ and $C^*=C$. The distortion of the microscopic structure is described by the plastic distortion $F_p\colon\Omega\to\text{Lin}$. Then the configuration of the system is described by the couple $z=(\chi,F_p)\in Z$ where the particular definition of the function space $Z$ depends on the choice of constitutive relations and it is not important now. The virtual rates are denoted as $\tilde w=(\tilde v,\tilde V_p)\in Z$.
\begin{rmk}
In accordance with the discussion made in Section~\ref{sec:ppv}, we do not include the constrain $\det F>0$ (the impenetrability condition) and $\det F_p=1$ (the plastic incompressibility condition) in the definition of $Z$ in order to preserve the linear structure of the space of configurations.
\end{rmk}

The plastic distortion can be considered as the locally elastically relaxed state of the material, so we define the elastic distortion as $F_e=FF_p^{-1}$ for all $z=(\chi,F_p)$ with $\det F_p>0$. A measure of the elastic distortion that is not affected by superimposed rigid placements\footnote{Given $Q\in\text{Orth}^+$ and $a\in\mathbb{R}^d$, if one superimpose the rigid placement $x\mapsto x^*=Qx+a$, then the placement transforms as $\chi^*=Q\chi+a$ while $F_p^*=F_p$ does not change since it is referred to the reference configuration.} is the Cauchy elastic distortion tensor $C_e=F_e^TF_e=F_p^{-T}CF_p^{-1}$. Given a time evolution $z=(\chi,F_p)\colon\Omega\times[0,T]\to\mathbb{R}^d\times\text{Lin}$ with $\det F_p>0$, we have that
\[
\dot F_e=\dot FF_p^{-1}-FF_p^{-1}\dot F_pF_p^{-1}=\dot FF_p^{-1}-F_e\dot F_pF_p^{-1}.
\]
Consistently with that, for all $z,\tilde w\in Z$, we define $\tilde V_e=(\grad\tilde v)F_p^{-1}-F_e\tilde V_pF_p^{-1}$.

Motivated by Remark~\ref{rmk:plasticity-simplified}, we decide that for $z=(\chi,F_p)\in Z$ with $\det F_p>0$, the internal and and external virtual power functionals are of the form
\begin{equation}
\label{eqn:ppv-large-plasticity}
\dual{\mathcal{I},\tilde w}=\int_\Omega P_e:\tilde V_edx+\int_\Omega\left(T_p:\tilde V_p+K_p\threedots\grad\tilde V_p\right)dx
\quad\text{and}\quad
\dual{\mathcal{X},\tilde w}=\int_\Omega f\cdot\tilde vdx+\int_{\partial\Omega}h\cdot\tilde v
\end{equation}
Here $P_e\colon\Omega\to\text{Lin}$ is the elastic first Piola stress, $T_p\colon\Omega\to\text{Lin}$ is the plastic microforce, $K_p\colon\Omega\to\text{Lin}\otimes\mathbb{R}$ is the plastic microstress, $f\colon\Omega\to\mathbb{R}^d$ is the external volumetric force and $h\colon\partial\Omega\to\mathbb{R}^d$ is the external surface force. The strong form of the equilibrium equation $\mathcal{I}=\mathcal{X}$ can be easily obtained using the Green lemma and the fundamental lemma of the calculus of variations; we find
\begin{gather*}
\diver(P_eF_p^{-T})+f=0\quad\text{in $\Omega$} \\
P_eF_p^{-T}n=h\quad\text{in $\partial\Omega$} \\
F_e^TP_eF_p^{-T}-T_p+\diver K_p=0\quad\text{in $\Omega$} \\
K_pn=0\quad\text{in $\partial\Omega$}.
\end{gather*}
This corresponds to the balance of macroscopic and microscopic forces.

The choice of the constitutive relations follows the same criteria used with the small deformation model. We consider $\Psi\colon Z\to\mathbb{R}\cup\{\infty\}$ and $\Phi\colon Z\times Z\to[0,\infty]$ of the form
\[
\Psi(z)=\int_\Omega W_e(F_e)dx+\int_\Omega W_p(F_p,\grad F_p)dx\quad\text{and}\quad\Phi(z,\dot z)=\int_\Omega R(F_p,\dot F_p)dx.
\]
This gives the constitutive relations
\[
P_e\in\dind{W_e}{F_e}(F_e),\quad T_p\in\dind{W_p}{F_p}(F_p,\grad F_p)+\dind{R}{\dot F_p}(F_p,\dot F_p)\quad\text{and}\quad K_p\in\dind{W_p}{\grad F_p}(F_p,\grad F_p).
\]
The elastic free energy density $W_e\colon\text{Lin}\to\mathbb{R}\cup\{\infty\}$ is assumed to satisfy $W(F_e)=\infty$ if and only if $\det F_e\le0$ (in order to enforce the impenetrability constrain) and to be differentiable on its domain. The requirement that the energy is indifferent with respect to superimposed rigid motions impose $W_e$ to be of the form $W_e(F_e)=\hat{W}_e(F_e^TF_e)$ for some $W_e\colon\text{Sym}\to\mathbb{R}\cup\{\infty\}$. Similarly the plastic free energy density $W_p\colon\text{Lin}\times\left(\text{Lin}\otimes\mathbb{R}\right)\to\mathbb{R}\cup\{\infty\}$ is supposed to satisfy $W_p(F_p,\grad F_p)=\infty$ if $\det F_p\neq1$ so that the plastic incompressibility is implied. The analogue of the Von Mises constitutive relation (known as $J_2$ flow theory) is obtained choosing
\[
R(F_p,\dot F_p)=\begin{cases}
Y|\dot F_pF_p^{-1}| & \text{if $\det F_p=1$} \\
\infty & \text{otherwise}.
\end{cases}
\]
Notice that in this definition appears the deviatoric plastic rate $L_p=\dot F_pF_p^{-1}$ (see also Remark~\ref{rmk:plasticity-lie-group}).

\begin{rmk}
\label{rmk:plasticity-lie-group}
A more geometric approach to plasticity theory at large strain (that we do not adopt in the spirit of the discussion we made in Section~\ref{sec:ppv}) is the following. Let $M$ be the manifold of the couples $z=(\chi,F_p)\in Z$ such that $\det F>0$ and $\det F_p=1$. It is a Lie group (at least formally) with respect to the group operation
\[
\begin{split}
\bullet\colon M\times M&\to M, \\
(z^1,z^2)&\mapsto z^1\bullet z^2=(\chi^1\circ\chi^2,(F_p^1\circ \chi^2)F_p^2).
\end{split}
\]
Given a time evolution $z=(\chi,F_p)\colon[0,T]\to M$, we define the velocity gradient as $L=\dot FF^{-1}$ and the plastic rate as $L_p=\dot F_pF_p^{-1}\colon\Omega\times[0,T]\to\text{Lin}$. Notice that the plastic strain rate is deviatoric, i.e., $L_p(x,t)\in\text{Dev}$ for all $(x,t)\in\Omega\times[0,T]$. Indeed since $\det F_p=1$, then
\[
0=\frac{d}{dt}\det F_p=\cof F_p:\dot F_p=\det F_p F_p^{-T}:\dot F_p=I:\dot F_pF_p^{-1}=\tr L_p.
\]
If we define the elastic rate as $L_e=\dot F_eF_e^{-1}$ we easily see that $L=L_e+L_p$. This means that the Lie algebra is the linear space $\mathfrak{m}$ of the virtual rates $\tilde w=(\tilde v,\tilde L_p)$ with $\tilde v\colon\Omega\to\mathbb{R}^d$ and $\tilde L_p=\tilde V_pF_p^{-1}\colon\Omega\to\text{DevSym}$. Consistently we let $\tilde L=(\grad\tilde v)F^{-1}$ and $\tilde L_e=\tilde L-\tilde L_p$. Then we can consider the internal and external virtual powers functional $\mathcal{I}$ and $\mathcal{X}$ as elements of $\mathfrak{m}^*$ of the form
\[
\dual{\mathcal{I},\tilde w}=\int_\Omega T_e:\tilde L_edx+\int_\Omega\left(T_p:\tilde L_p+K_p\threedots\grad\tilde L_p\right)dx
\quad\text{and}\quad
\dual{\mathcal{X},\tilde w}=\int_\Omega f\cdot\tilde vdx+\int_{\partial\Omega}h\cdot\tilde v,
\]
\end{rmk}
where $T_p$ and $K_p$ does not coincides with the ones appearing in~\eqref{eqn:ppv-large-plasticity}.

\chapter{Solution concepts and general existence results}
\label{chap:solution-concepts}

In this chapter are discussed some existence results for the abstract evolutionary equations discussed in Section~\ref{sec:ppv}. In Section~\ref{sec:roubickeck} we present the existence result~\cite[Proposition 11.1]{roubicek-pde} for doubly nonlinear equations when the dissipation potential has a quadratic growth. We will see that the quadratic growth of the dissipation potential allows to seek solution which are absolutely continuous in time, since in this case the dissipative generalized force is sufficiently strong to avoid jumps. In Section~\ref{sec:existence-theorem-abstract-constrained} we prove a new existence result that extend that of previous section to the case in which an unilateral kinematical constrain is added: the constrain is treated including a Lagrange multiplier and the dissipation potential is made dependent on it. This is the result that we will use to develop the existence theory for the model discussed in Part~\ref{part:my-model}. In Section~\ref{sec:evi} is reported the existence result~\cite[Theorem 7.3]{han-reddy} for evolution equations with a quadratic free energy and a 1-homogeneous dissipation potential. In this case, even thought the dissipation potential has not a quadratic growth, it is possible to find solutions that are absolutely continuous in time: this is due to the particular combination of the fact that the energy is quadratic and the dissipation potential is 1-homogeneous. This result will be applied in Chapter~\ref{chap:small-neck} to develop the existence theory for the one-dimensional model of a metallic cylindrical bar at small deformations. In Section~\ref{sec:energetic-solution} we will present the notion of energetic solutions as presented in~\cite{francfort,rate-independent-systems}. This is a very general solution concept for rate independent systems introduced by Mielke and Theil~\cite{mielke1999mathematical} in 1999 to treat rate-independent systems with nonlinear and possible non-convex free energies. The main feature of this formulation is that it does not require any type of differentiability of the free energy or the dissipation potential and of the solution with respect to time: indeed their definition is based on a global stability conditions that does not requires to consider local variations. This is particularly useful when the nonlinearities involved in the model would be difficult to handle in terms of variations and when the evolution can exhibit jumps. Energetic solutions are suitable to prove the existence of solutions for rate-independent plasticity at large deformations: we will indeed use it to develop the existence theory for the model of a metallic cylindrical bar at large deformations in Chapter~\ref{chap:large-neck}.

The results of this chapter, except for that in Section~\ref{sec:existence-theorem-abstract-constrained}, are known results, so that in these cases we will report only a sketch of the proofs, referring to the original sources for the complete discussion. Instead the proof of our new result in Section~\ref{sec:existence-theorem-abstract-constrained} will be reported integrally.

\section{An existence result for doubly nonlinear equations}
\label{sec:roubickeck}

In this section we discuss the existence results~\cite[Proposition 11.1]{roubicek-pde} for doubly nonlinear evolutionary equations in the form~\eqref{eqn:modeling:doubly:nonlinear-neat}. This result cover the case in which the free energy density $\Psi$ is smooth (actually the requirement is that the part of the generalized internal force that depends on the configuration only is single-valued, even thought it may have a non-potential structure) and $\Phi$ does not depends on the configuration but on the rate only. The crucial assumption is that the dissipation potential has a quadratic growth: this will implies that the dissipative generalized force is strong enough, preventing the system to exhibiting jumps. This allows to seek the solution in the space of absolutely continuous evolutions.

\begin{rmk}
The result presented in this section will not be directly used in the sequel of the thesis. We report it since it was the basis from which we developed the general existence theorem of Section~\ref{sec:existence-theorem-abstract-constrained}. The result presented in that section is indeed a generalization of the theorem of the present one, when a constrain is added and a dissipation potential depending on the Lagrange multiplier is considered.
\end{rmk}

\subsection{Assumptions and formulation of the problem}

Let $Z$ and $H$ be a reflexive and separable Banach spaces with $Z$ compactly and densely embedded in $H$. Notice that $Z\subset H\subset H^*\subset Z^*$. We assume that there exists a seminorm $|\cdot|_Z$ in $Z$ and $0<c_P<C_P$ such that
\[
c_P(\norm{z}_H+|z|_Z)\le\norm{z}_Z\le C_P(\norm{z}_H+|z|_Z)
\]
for all $z\in Z$. We are interested in the doubly nonlinear equation of the form
\[
\mathcal{F}(z(t))+\partial\Phi(\dot z(t))\ni f(t)
\]
for a.e.\ $t\in(0,T)$. The map $\mathcal{F}\colon Z\to Z^*$ is supposed to be of the form
\[
\mathcal{F}(z)=\mathcal{B}(z)+\frac{1}{2}\dual{\mathcal{A}z,z}.
\]
Here $\mathcal{A}\in\mathscr{L}(Z,Z^*)$ is coercive with respect to $|\cdot|_Z$ in the sense that there exists a constant $C_A>0$ such that $\dual{\mathcal{A}z,z}\ge C_A|z|_Z^2$. The map $\mathcal{B}$ is Lipschitz as a map from $H$ to $H^*$ with Lipschitz constant $L$. The dissipation potential $\Phi\colon H\to[0,\infty]$ is supposed to be of the form
\[
\Phi(w)=\Phi_0(w)+\frac{1}{2}\dual{\mathcal{D}w,w},
\]
where $\Phi_0\colon H\to[0,\infty]$ is convex, lower semicontinuous, satisfying $\Phi_0(0)=0$ and $\mathcal{D}\in\mathscr{L}(H,H^*)$ is coercive in $H$ in the sense that there exists a constant $C_D>0$ such that $\dual{\mathcal{D}w,w}\ge C_D\norm{w}_H^2$. Next we assume that $f\in H^1((0,T),H^*)$, that $z_0\in Z$ and that the compatibility condition $\mathcal{F}(z_0)=f(0)$ holds.

Now the problem that we consider is the following.
\begin{prb}
\label{prb:roubickeck}
Find $z\in AC([0,T],Z)$ such that $z(0)=z_0$ and $\mathcal{F}(z(t))+\partial\Phi(\dot z(t))\ni f(t)$ for a.e.\ $t\in(0,T)$.
\end{prb}

\subsection{Existence theorem}

The existence of solution to the above problem is established by the following theorem. We will report only some ideas of the proof since they will utilized also to prove the existence result of Section~\ref{sec:existence-theorem-abstract-constrained}. A complete proof of the following theorem is integrally reported in~\cite[Proposition 11.1]{roubicek-pde}.
\begin{thm}
Under the assumption of previous subsection, there exists a solution $z\in W^{1,\infty}((0,T),Z)$ to Problem~\ref{prb:roubickeck} that also satisfies $\dot z\in C^0([0,T],Z)$.
\end{thm}
\begin{proof}[Sketch of the proof]
The proof relies on a time discretization approach, so it is first established the existence of solutions to a discrete-in-time version of the problem: the continuous in time solution will be recovered as the limit of the discrete-in-time ones as the discretization step size vanishes. Specifically fix $N\in\mathbb{N}$, let $\tau_N=T/N$ and $t_k=k\tau_N$ for $k\in\{0,\dots,N\}$, and consider the problem to find $\{z_N^k\}_{k=1}^N\subset Z$ such that $z_N^0=z_0$ and
\begin{equation}
\label{eqn:discrete-roubickeck}
\mathcal{F}(z_N^k)+\partial\Phi\left(\frac{z_N^k-z_N^{k-1}}{\tau_N}\right)\ni f_N^k\quad\text{for $k\in\{1,\dots,N\}$},
\end{equation}
where
\[
f_N^k:=\frac{1}{\tau_N}\int_{t_N^{k-1}}^{t_N^k}f(s)ds.
\]
The existence of a discrete-in-time solution is proven inductively by solving iteratively~\eqref{eqn:discrete-roubickeck}: to this aim the Minty--Browder theorem in the form of Corollary~\ref{cor:corollary-5-19} is used. Here the quadratic growth of $\Phi$ is crucial to prove the required coercivity.

Then one defines $z_N$ as the piecewise affine interpolation of $\{z_N^k\}_{k=0}^N$. The derivative $\dot z_N^k$ is piecewise constant and
\[
\dot z_N(t)=\dot z_N^k:=\frac{z_N^k-z_N^{k-1}}{\tau_N}\quad\text{for $t\in(t_N^{k-1},t_N^k]$ and $k\in\{1,\dots,N\}$}.
\]
We also set $\dot z_N^0:=0$. Let also $\hat z_N$ be the piecewise affine interpolation of $\{z_N^k\}_{k=0}^N$. Now notice that~\eqref{eqn:discrete-roubickeck} can be written more explicitly as
\[
\tau_N\Phi_0(w)\ge\tau_N\Phi_0(\dot z_N^k)+\dual{f_N^k-\mathcal{F}(z_N^k)-\mathcal{D}\dot z_N^k,w-\dot z_N^k}\quad\text{for all $z$ and $k\in\{1,\dots,N\}$}.
\]
Notice that due to the assumption that $\mathcal{F}(z_0)=f(0)$ this is true also for $k=0$. Next consider this inequality for $w=\dot z_N^k$ and for $k-1$ in place of $k$ and $w=\dot z_N^k$. Summing the resultant inequalities together, performing some estimates and summing the result for $k\in\{1,\dots,N\}$ one arrives at
\begin{equation}
\label{eqn:estimates-roubickeck}
\frac{C_A}{2}|\dot z_N^n|_Z^2+\frac{C_D}{2}\tau_N\sum_{k=1}^n\left\lVert\frac{\dot z_N^k-\dot z_N^{k-1}}{\tau_N}\right\rVert_H^2\le\frac{1}{C_D}\tau_N\sum_{k=1}^n\left(\left\lVert\frac{f_N^k-f_N^{k-1}}{\tau_N}\right\rVert_{H^*}^2+L^2\norm{\dot z_N^k}_H^2\right).
\end{equation}
Letting
\[
S_N^n=\tau_N\sum_{k=1}^n\left\lVert\frac{\dot u_N^k-\dot u_N^{k-1}}{\tau_N}\right\rVert_H^2
\]
and noticing that
\[
\norm{\dot z_N^k}_H^2\le TS_N^k,
\]
it follows that
\[
\frac{C_D}{2}S_N^n\le\frac{1}{C_D}\tau_N\sum_{k=1}^n\left(\left\lVert\frac{f_N^k-f_N^{k-1}}{\tau_N}\right\rVert_{H^*}^2+L^2TS_N^k\right).
\]
The fact that $f\in H^1((0,T),H^*)$ implies that $\tau_N\sum_{k=1}^n\left\lVert\frac{f_N^k-f_N^{k-1}}{\tau_N}\right\rVert_{H^*}^2$ is bounded uniformly in $N$ and $n$. Then the discrete Gronwall inequality (Proposition~\ref{prop:gronwall}) implies that $S_N^n$ is bounded uniformly in $N$ and $n$. This gives a uniform bound on $\norm{\dot{\hat z}_N}_{L^2((0,T),H)}$. Returning back to~\eqref{eqn:estimates-roubickeck}, it follows that also $\norm{z_N}_{W^{1,\infty}((0,T),Z)}$ is uniformly bounded. Thus, up to extracting a subsequence, it holds that $z_N\overset{*}{\rightharpoonup}z$ in $W^{1,\infty}((0,T),Z)$. Moreover Aubin--Lions--Simons lemma (Theorem~\ref{thm:aubin-lions}) implies that $z_N\to z$ in $C^0([0,T],H)$ and $\hat{z}_N\to\dot z$ in $C^0([0,T],H)$. A simple estimate also implies that $\dot z_N\to\dot z$ in $L^2((0,T),H)$. This allows to pass in the limit $N\to\infty$ in~\eqref{eqn:discrete-roubickeck} thus proving that $z$ is a solution to the continuous-in-time problem.
\end{proof}

\section{An existence theorem for constrained mechanical systems}
\label{sec:existence-theorem-abstract-constrained}

In this subsection we develop an existence theory for kinematically constrained mechanical systems. We will consider a specialized class of the constrained mechanical models treated with the method of Lagrange multipliers that we described in Subsection~\ref{subsec:lagrange-multipliers}.

Our existence result is an extension of that in~\cite[Proposition 11.1]{roubicek-pde} that was discussed in the previous section. Our extension consists in the addiction of a kinematical constrain and of a possible dependence of the dissipation potential on the Lagrange multiplier. The motivation that lead us to develop that existence theory is the application presented in Chapter~\ref{chap:pressure-sensitive-small} of Part~\ref{part:my-model}.

\subsection{Notation, assumptions and formulation of the problem}

Let $U,\Lambda$ be separable and reflexive Banach spaces and let $Z=U\times\Lambda$ be the space of configurations. The generic configuration is $z=(u,\lambda)\in Z$ with $u\in U$ and $\lambda\in\Lambda$ while the virtual velocities are denoted with $w=(v,\mu)\in Z$ with $v\in U$ and $\mu\in\Lambda$. Assume that $H\supset U$ is a separable and reflexive Banach space and that the embedding of $U$ in $H$ dense and compact. Let also $|\cdot|_U$ be a seminorm in $U$ satisfying the abstract PoincarĂ© inequality, i.e., there exists $0<c_P<C_P$ such that
\begin{equation}
\label{eqn:abstract-poincare}
c_P\left(\norm{u}_H+|u|_U\right)\le\norm{u}_U\le C_P\left(\norm{u}_H+|u|_U\right)\quad\text{for all $u\in U$.}
\end{equation}
Let $\mathcal{L}\in C^{0,1}(H,\Lambda)\cap C^{1,1}(H,\Lambda)$ and consider the kinematical constraint $\lambda=\mathcal{L}(u)$. We assume that there exists a constant $C_L>0$ such that $\norm{\mathcal{L}(u)}_\Lambda\le C_L$ and $\norm{\mathcal{L}'(u)}_{\mathscr{L}(U,H)}\le C_L$ for all $u\in U$. Up to redefining $C_L$ we can assume that $\text{Lip}(\mathcal{L})\le C_L$ and $\text{Lip}(\mathcal{L}')\le C_L$. The kinematical constrain can be written as $z\in K$ where
\[
K=\{z=(u,\lambda)\in Z\;|\;\lambda=\mathcal{L}(u)\}.
\]
We treat it with the method of Lagrange multipliers so we consider the reaction $\mathcal{P}\in N_K(z)\subset Z^*$, meaning that $\dual{\mathcal{P},w}=0$ for all $w=(v,\mu)\in Z$ satisfying $\mu=\mathcal{L}'(u)v$. We parametrize $\mathcal{P}$ in terms of a $p\in\Lambda^*$ as
\[
\dual{\mathcal{P},w}=\dual{p,\mu-\mathcal{L}'(u)v}\quad\text{for all $w\in Z$}
\]
so that the normality condition is automatically satisfied. Let $\Psi\colon Z\to\mathbb{R}$ of the form $\Psi(z)=\Psi_0(z)+\frac{1}{2}\dual{\mathcal{A}u,u}$ with $\Psi_0$ and $\mathcal{A}$ satisfying the following properties:
\begin{itemize}
\item[($\Psi$1)] $\Psi_0\in C^{1,1}_\text{loc}(H\times\Lambda)$ and there exist $C_\Psi^2,C^1_\Psi,C^0_\Psi>0$ with $\dual{D\Psi_0(z),z}\ge-C_\Psi^2\norm{z}_{H\times\Lambda}^2-C_\Psi^1\norm{z}_{H\times\Lambda}-C_\Psi^0$ for all $z\in Z$;
\item[($\Psi$2)] there exists a constant $C_\Psi^3>0$ such that $\norm{D_\lambda\Psi_0(z)}_{\Lambda^*}\le C_\Psi^3\norm{z}_{H\times\Lambda}$;
\item[(A)] $\mathcal{A}\in\mathscr{L}(U,U^*)$ is such that $\mathcal{A}^*=\mathcal{A}$ and there exists $C_A>0$ such that $\dual{\mathcal{A}u,u}\ge C_A|u|_U^2$ for all $u\in U$.
\end{itemize}
Let $\Phi\colon\Lambda^*\times U\to[0,\infty]$ of the form $\Phi(p,v)=\Phi_0(p,v)+\frac{1}{2}\dual{\mathcal{D}v,v}$ with $\Phi_0$ and $\mathcal{D}$ such that:
\begin{itemize}
\item[($\Phi$1)] for all $p\in\Lambda^*$ the map $\Phi_0(p,\cdot)\colon H\to[0,\infty]$ is convex, lower semicontinuous, proper and satisfies $\Phi_0(p,0)=0$;
\item[($\Phi$2)] $\dom\Phi_0=\Lambda^*\times D_\Phi$ where $D_\Phi\subset H$ is closed and convex;
\item[($\Phi$3)] there exists $C_\Phi>0$ such that
\[
|\Phi(p_1,v)-\Phi(p_2,v)|\le C_\Phi\norm{p_1-p_2}_{\Lambda^*}\norm{v}_H,\quad|\Phi(p,v_1)-\Phi(p,v_2)|\le C_\Phi(\norm{p}_{\Lambda^*}+1)\norm{v_1-v_2}_H
\]
and
\[
|\Phi(p_1,v_1)-\Phi(p_2,v_1)+\Phi(p_2,v_2)-\Phi(p_1,v_2)|\le C_\Phi\norm{p_1-p_2}_{\Lambda^*}\norm{v_1-v_2}_H
\]
for all $p,p_1,p_2\in\Lambda^*$ and $v,v_1,v_2\in D_\Phi$;
\item[(D)] $\mathcal{D}\in\mathscr{L}(H,H^*)$ with $\mathcal{D}^*=\mathcal{D}$ and there exists $C_D>0$ such that $\dual{\mathcal{D}v,v}\ge C_D\norm{v}_H^2$.
\end{itemize}
Let also $f\in H^1([0,T],H^*)$ and $z_0=(u_0,\lambda_0)\in Z$ such that
\begin{equation}
\label{eqn:initial-stability}
\lambda_0=\mathcal{L}(u_0)\quad\text{and}\quad f(0)=D_u\Psi_0(z_0)+\mathcal{A}u_0+\mathcal{L}'(u_0)^*p_0,
\end{equation}
where $p_0:=D_\lambda\Psi_0(z_0)$. Then the abstract problem that we consider is the following: it is a specialization of Problem~\ref{prb:constrained} when the structure of the constrained set is as we just described.
\begin{prb}
\label{prb:existence-abstract}
Find the triple $(u,\lambda,p)$, with $u\in\text{AC}([0,T],U)$, $\lambda\in C^0([0,T],\Lambda)$ and $p\in C^0([0,T],\Lambda^*)$, such that $z(0)=z_0$ and
\begin{align}
\label{eqn:problem-abstract-first}
D_u\Psi_0(u(t),\lambda(t))+\mathcal{A}u(t)+\partial\Phi_0(p(t),\dot u(t))+\mathcal{D}\dot u(t)+\mathcal{L}'(u(t))^*p(t)&\ni f(t) \\
\label{eqn:problem-abstract-second}
D_\lambda\Psi_0(u(t),\lambda(t))-p(t)&=0 \\
\label{eqn:problem-abstract-third}
\lambda(t)&=\mathcal{L}(u(t))
\end{align}
for a.e.\ $t\in(0,T)$.
\end{prb}

\subsection{The existence theorem and its proof}

We are now going to prove the following existence theorem.
\begin{thm}
\label{thm:abstract-existence}
Under the assumptions previously stated, there exists a solution $(u,\lambda,p)$ to Problem~\ref{prb:existence-abstract}. Moreover $u\in W^{1,\infty}((0,T),U)$ and $\dot u\in C^0([0,T],H)$.
\end{thm}
\begin{rmk}(Ideas behind the proof)
In this remark we describe which where the ideas behind the method we used to prove the existence of solution. As we said before, the starting point was the proof of~\cite[Proposition~11.1]{roubicek-pde}, whose sketch we reported in Section~\ref{sec:roubickeck}. The idea to use the time discretization approach and the way in which the priori estimates are performed, is taken directly from there. The true difficulty was to extend the proof of the existence of solutions to the incremental problem that arises from the time discretization. The procedure that we have developed is a nonlinear generalization of the one used to prove the existence of solutions to the generalized linear saddle point problem in Theorem~\ref{thm:mixed-elliptic}.
\end{rmk}

The proof relies on a time-discretization approach: the time internal is subdivided with an arbitrary but fixed step size and the existence of solutions to it is established for sufficiently small step size; then the continuous-in-time solutions is obtained as the limit (in term of a subsequence) of the discrete-in-time solutions as the step size vanishes.

\subsubsection*{Solution of the discrete-in-time problem}

So we first prove the existence of a solution to a discrete-in-time version of Problem~\ref{prb:existence-abstract} that we now introduce. Let $N\in\mathbb{N}$, $\tau_N=T/N$, $t_N^k=k\tau_N$ for $k\in\{0,\dots, N\}$. The discrete problem requires to find $\{z_N^k\}_{k=0}^N\subset Z$ and $\{p_N^k\}_{k=1}^N\subset\Lambda^*$ such that $z_N^0=z_0$ and
\begin{align}
\label{eqn:first-equation-discrete}
D_u\Psi_0(u_N^k,\lambda_N^k)+\mathcal{A}u_N^k+\partial\Phi_0\left(p_N^k,\frac{u_N^k-u_N^{k-1}}{\tau_N}\right)+\mathcal{D}\frac{u_N^k-u_N^{k-1}}{\tau_N}+\mathcal{L}'(u_N^k)^*p_N^k&\ni f_N^k \\
\label{eqn:second-equation-discrete}
D_\lambda\Psi_0(u_N^k,\lambda_N^k)-p_N^k&=0 \\
\label{eqn:third-equation-discrete}
\lambda_N^k&=\mathcal{L}(u_N^k)
\end{align}
for all $k\in\{1,\dots,N\}$. There we have defined
\[
f_N^k=\int_{t_N^{k-1}}^{t_N^k}f(s)ds.
\]
We also formally set $f(s)=f(0)$ for $s<0$ and accordingly we let $f_N^0=f(0)$. To prove the existence of solutions the idea is to eliminate $\lambda_N^k$ through the constrain condition~\eqref{eqn:third-equation-discrete}, then to solve~\eqref{eqn:first-equation-discrete} in $u_N^k$ for each fixed $p_N^k$, then eventually reconstruct $p_N^k$ by mean of~\eqref{eqn:second-equation-discrete}. So we start the proof of the existence of a solution to the discrete-in-time problem substituting $\lambda_N^k$ from the last equation into the first equation obtaining
\[
D_u\Psi_0(u_N^k,\mathcal{L}(u_N^k))+\mathcal{A}u_N^k+\partial\Phi_0\left(p_N^k,\frac{u_N^k-u_N^{k-1}}{\tau_N}\right)+\mathcal{D}\frac{u_N^k-u_N^{k-1}}{\tau_N}+\mathcal{L}'(u_N^k)^*p_N^k\ni f_N^k.
\]
For each $N\in\mathbb{N}$ and $p_N^k\in\Lambda^*$ fixed, this is a nonlinear equation for $u_N^k\in U$ that can be written as
\begin{equation}
\label{eqn:equation-unk}
F(u_N^k)+\partial\Xi(u_N^k)\ni f_N^k,
\end{equation}
where $F\colon U\to U^*$ is defined by
\[
F(u)=D_u\Psi_0(u,\mathcal{L}(u))+\mathcal{L}'(u)^*p_N^k
\]
and $\Xi\colon U\to[0,\infty]$ by
\[
\Xi(u)=\frac{1}{2}\dual{\mathcal{A}u,u}+\tau_N\Phi_0\left(p_N^k,\frac{u-u_N^{k-1}}{\tau_N}\right)+\frac{1}{2\tau_N}\dual{\mathcal{D}(u-u_N^{k-1}),u-u_N^{k-1}}.
\]
Here is understood that $F$ and $\Xi$ depends parametrically on $p_N^k\in\Lambda^*$, $N$ and $u_N^k\in U$, but in order to keep the notation simpler we do not explicitly indicate them. We prove that~\eqref{eqn:equation-unk} admits a solution $u_N^k$ for each $p_N^k$ fixed. To this aim we check the hypotheses needed to apply Corollary~\ref{cor:corollary-5-19} with $\Xi$ in place of $\Phi$ and $F$ in place of $A$. It is trivial that $\Xi$ is convex, lower semicontinuous and proper. The fact that $F$ is pseudomonotone is proved in the following lemma.
\begin{lemma}
Let $U,H$ be separable and reflexive Banach spaces with $U$ embedded compactly and densely in $H$. Let $F\in C^0(H,H^*)$. Then $F$ is pseudomonotone as a map from $U$ to $U^*$.
\end{lemma}
\begin{proof}
Let $u_n\rightharpoonup u$ in $U$. Then for each subsequence $u_{n_k}$ there exists a further (not relabeled) subsequence such that $u_{n_k}\to u$ in $H$. By continuity of $F$ it holds that $F(u_{n_k})\to F(u)$ in $H^*$. Then
\[
\lim_{k\to\infty}\dual{F(u_{n_k}),u_{n_k}-v}=\dual{F(u),u-v}\quad\text{for all $v\in U$}.
\]
Since the subsequence $u_{n_k}$ was arbitrary, actually
\[
\lim_{n\to\infty}\dual{F(u_{n}),u_{n}-v}=\dual{F(u),u-v}\quad\text{for all $v\in U$}.
\]
This implies that $F$ is presudomonotone as a map form $U$ to $U^*$.
\end{proof}
Next we need to verify the required coercivity property. This is the content of the following lemma.
\begin{lemma}
If $N$ is sufficiently large, there exists a function $\zeta\colon[0,\infty)\to[0,\infty)$ with the property that
\[
\lim_{r\to\infty}\zeta(r)=\infty
\]
such that for all $1>\epsilon>0$ it holds
\[
\frac{\Xi_\epsilon(u)+\dual{F(u),u}}{\norm{u}_U}\ge\zeta(\norm{u}_U)\quad\text{for all $u\in U$,}
\]
where $\Xi_\epsilon$ is the Yosida approximation of $\Xi$.
\end{lemma}
\begin{proof}
We have that
\[
\begin{split}
\dual{F(u),u}&=\dual{D_u\Psi_0(u,\mathcal{L}(u)),u}+\dual{\mathcal{L}'(u)^*p_N^k,u}=\\
&=\dual{D\Psi_0(u,\mathcal{L}(u)),(u,\mathcal{L}(u))}-\dual{D_\lambda\Psi_0(u,\mathcal{L}(u)),\mathcal{L}(u)}+\dual{\mathcal{L}'(u)^*p_N^k,u}\ge\\
&\ge-C_\Psi^2\norm{(u,\mathcal{L}(u))}_{H\times\Lambda}^2-C_\Psi^1\norm{(u,\mathcal{L}(u))}_{H\times\Lambda}-C_\Psi^0-C_\Psi^3C_L(1+C_L)\norm{u}_H-C_L\norm{p_N^k}_{\Lambda^*}\ge\\
&\ge-C_\Psi^2(1+C_L)^2\norm{u}_H^2-C_\Psi^1(1+C_L)\norm{u}_H-C_\Psi^0-C_\Psi^3C_L(1+C_L)\norm{u}_H-C_L\norm{p_N^k}_{\Lambda^*}.
\end{split}
\]
Next notice that for all $1>\epsilon>0$ it holds that
\[
\begin{split}
\Xi_\epsilon(u)&=\inf_{v\in U}\left[\frac{\norm{u-v}_U^2}{2\epsilon}+\Xi(v)\right]\ge\inf_{v\in U}\left[\frac{\norm{u-v}_U^2}{2}+\frac{1}{2}C_A|v|_U^2+\frac{1}{2\tau_N}C_D\norm{v-u_N^{k-1}}_H^2\right]\ge\\
&\ge\inf_{v\in U}\left[\frac{c_P^2\left(\norm{u-v}_H^2+|u-v|_U^2\right)}{2}+\frac{1}{2}C_A|v|_U^2+\frac{1}{4\tau_N}C_D\norm{v}^2_H-\frac{1}{2\tau_N}C_D\norm{u_N^{k-1}}_H^2\right],
\end{split}
\]
where we used that $\epsilon<1$, the inequality~\eqref{eqn:abstract-poincare} and the fact that
\[
\begin{split}
\norm{v-u_N^{k-1}}_H^2&\ge(\norm{v}_H-\norm{u_N^{k-1}}_H)^2=\norm{v}_H^2+\norm{u_N^{k-1}}_H^2-2\norm{v}_H\norm{u_N^{k-1}}_H\ge\\
&\ge\norm{v}_H^2+\norm{u_N^{k-1}}_H^2-2\left(\frac{1}{4}\norm{v}_H^2+\norm{u_N^{k-1}}_H^2\right)=\frac{1}{2}\norm{v}_H^2-\norm{u_N^{k-1}}_H^2.
\end{split}
\]
Then
\[
\begin{split}
\Xi_\epsilon(u)&\ge\inf_{v\in U}\left[\frac{c_P^2|u-v|_U^2}{2}+\frac{1}{2}C_A|v|_U^2\right]+\inf_{v\in U}\left[\frac{c_P^2\norm{u-v}_H^2}{2}+\frac{1}{4\tau_N}C_D\norm{v}_H^2\right]-\frac{1}{2\tau_N}C_D\norm{u_N^{k-1}}_H^2\ge\\
&\ge c_p^2\left(\inf_{v\in U}\left[\frac{(|u|_U-|v|_U)^2}{2}+\frac{C_A}{2c_P^2}|v|_U^2\right]+\inf_{v\in U}\left[\frac{(\norm{u}_H-\norm{v}_H)^2}{2}+\frac{C_D}{4\tau_Nc_P^2}\norm{v}_H^2\right]\right)-\frac{1}{2\tau_N}C_D\norm{u_N^{k-1}}_H^2=\\
&=c_p^2\left[I\left(\sqrt{\frac{C_A}{2c_P^2}}|u|_U\right)+I\left(\sqrt{\frac{C_D}{4\tau_Nc_P^2}}\norm{u}_H\right)\right]-\frac{1}{2\tau_N}C_D\norm{u_N^{k-1}}_H^2,
\end{split}
\]
where we have defined $I\colon\mathbb{R}\to\mathbb{R}$ by
\[
I(r)=\inf_{s\in\mathbb{R}}\left[\frac{(r-s)^2}{2}+s^2\right]=\frac{r^2}{3}.
\]
Thus
\[
\Xi_\epsilon(u)\ge\frac{C_A}{6}|u|_U^2+\frac{C_D}{12\tau_N}\norm{u}_H^2-\frac{1}{2\tau_N}C_D\norm{u_N^{k-1}}_H^2.
\]
Choosing $N$ sufficiently large such that
\[
C_1:=\frac{C_D}{12\tau_N}-C_\Psi^2(1+C_L)^2>0
\]
we find that
\[
\begin{multlined}
\frac{\Xi_\epsilon(u)+\dual{F(u),u}}{\norm{u}_U}\ge\\
\ge\frac{\frac{C_A}{6}|u|_U^2+C_1\norm{u}_H^2-C_\Psi^1(1+C_L)\norm{u}_H-C_\Psi^0-C_\Psi^3C_L(1+C_L)\norm{u}_H-C_L\norm{p_N^k}_{\Lambda^*}-\frac{1}{2\tau_N}C_D\norm{u_N^{k-1}}_H^2}{\norm{u}_U}.
\end{multlined}
\]
Now the quantity on the right hand side tends to infinity as $\norm{u}_U\to\infty$, so the thesis follows.
%Now $\norm{u}_H\le\norm{(u,\mathcal{L}(u))}_{H\times\Lambda}\le\norm{u}_H+C_L$ so that the thesis follows immediately.
\end{proof}
Hence we know that for all $p_N^k\in\Lambda^*$ there exists $u_N^k=\mathcal{U}(p_N^k)$ such that~\eqref{eqn:equation-unk} holds. The map $\mathcal{U}\colon\Lambda^*\to U$ is well defined (in the sense that the solution to~\eqref{eqn:equation-unk} is unique) and satisfies some regularity properties, as showed in the following lemma.
\begin{lemma}
\label{lemma:properties-U}
If $N$ is sufficiently large, the map $\mathcal{U}\colon\Lambda^*\to U$ is well defined, is demicontinuous from $\Lambda^*$ to $U$, belongs to $C^0(\Lambda^*,H)$ and is bounded, in the sense that it maps bounded subsets of $\Lambda^*$ into bounded subsets of $U$. Moreover
\[
\norm{\mathcal{U}(p)}_H\le C_U^0+\frac{C_U^1}{N}\norm{p}_{\Lambda^*}\quad\text{for all $p\in\Lambda^*$}
\]
where $C_U^0,C_U^1>0$ are constants independent on $p$ and $N$.
\end{lemma}
\begin{proof}
Since we are now interested in the behavior of the solution set for $p_N^k$ variable, we explicitly replace it with a more generic symbol $p\in\Lambda^*$.

First we prove that there exists a constant $B>0$ (depending on $p$) such that each solution $u\in U$ to~\eqref{eqn:equation-unk} satisfies $\norm{u}_U\le B$. Notice that $u\in U$ is a solution to~\eqref{eqn:equation-unk} if and only if
\begin{equation}
\label{eqn:equation-unk-explicit}
\Xi(v)+\dual{F(u),v-u}\ge\Xi(u)+\dual{f_N^k,v-u}\quad\text{for all $v\in U$}.
\end{equation}
Choosing $v=u/2$ we get
\[
\Xi(u)-\Xi\left(\frac{u}{2}\right)+\frac{1}{2}\dual{F(u),u}-\frac{1}{2}\dual{f_N^k,u}\le0.
\]
This is equivalent to
\begin{equation}
\begin{multlined}
\frac{1}{2}\langle \mathcal{A}u,u\rangle+\tau_N\Phi_0\left(p,\frac{u-u_N^{k-1}}{\tau_N}\right)+\frac{1}{2}\tau_N\langle\mathcal{D}(u-u_N^{k-1}),u-u_N^{k-1}\rangle-\\
-\frac{1}{8}\langle \mathcal{A}u,u\rangle-\tau_N\Phi_0\left(p,\frac{\frac{u}{2}-u_N^{k-1}}{\tau_N}\right)-\frac{1}{2}\tau_N\left\langle \mathcal{D}\left(\frac{u}{2}-u_N^{k-1}\right),\frac{u}{2}-u_N^{k-1}\right\rangle+\\
+\frac{1}{2}\langle D_u\Psi(u,\mathcal{L}(u)),u\rangle+\frac{1}{2}\langle\mathcal{L}'(u)^*p,u\rangle-\frac{1}{2}\langle f_N^k,u\rangle\le0.
\end{multlined} 
\end{equation}
After some simple estimates
\begin{equation}
\label{eqn:boundedness}
\begin{multlined}
\frac{3C_A}{8}|u|_U^2-C_{\Phi}(\|p\|+1)\left(\frac{3}{2}\|u\|_H+2\|u_N^{k-1}\|_H\right)+\frac{3C_D}{8\tau_N}\|u\|_H^2-\\
-\frac{3}{2\tau_N}\|\mathcal{D}\|_{\mathscr{L}(H,H^*)}\|u_N^{k-1}\|_H\|u\|_H-\frac{1}{\tau_N}\|\mathcal{D}\|_{\mathscr{L}(H,H^*)}\|u_N^{k-1}\|_H^2-\\
-\frac{1}{2}C_{\Psi}^2(1+C_L)^2\|u\|_H^2-\frac{1}{2}C_{\Psi}^1(1+C_L)\|u\|_H-\frac{1}{2}C_{\Psi}^0-\\
-\frac{1}{2}C_{\Psi}^3(1+C_L)C_L\|u\|_H-\frac{1}{2}C_L\|p\|_{\Lambda^*}\|u\|_H-\frac{1}{2}\|f_N^k\|_{H^*}\|u\|_H\le0
\end{multlined}
\end{equation}
Choosing $N$ sufficiently large we can make the coefficient
\[
\frac{3C_D}{8\tau_N}-\frac{1}{2}C_{\Psi}^2(1+C_L)^2
\]
of $\norm{u}_H^2$ is positive. Then inequality~\eqref{eqn:boundedness} implies\footnote{The reasoning is as follows. First of all, taking into account Lemma~\ref{lemma:a-priori-0} below, we know that $\norm{u_N^{k-1}}_U\le C_0$ uniformly in $N$ and $k$. So we can replace $\norm{u_N^{k-1}}$ with $C_0$. Next call $x=|u|_U$, $y=\norm{u}_H$ and $z=\norm{p}_{\Lambda^*}$. Then the inequality can be rewritten as $Ax^2+NB y^2-Cy^2-Dzy-Ey-Fz-G\le0$ where all the coefficient are positive. From $NB y^2-Cy^2-Dzy-Ey-Fz-G\le0$ it follows that
\[
y\le\frac{2}{NB-C}(Dz+E)+2\sqrt{\frac{Fz+G}{NB-C}}.
\]
It is trivial that this implies the existence of $a,b>0$ independent on $p$ and $N$ such that $y\le a+bz$.} that $\norm{u}_U$ is indeed bounded by a constant that remains bounded as $p$ remains bounded and there exists $C_U^0,C_U^1>0$ independent on $N$ and $p$ such that $\norm{u}_H\le C_U^0+\frac{C_U^1}{N}\norm{p}_{\Lambda^*}$.

Next we prove that the solution to~\eqref{eqn:equation-unk} is unique, so that $\mathcal{U}$ is well defined. To this aim let $u_1,u_2\in U$ two solutions. We consider~\eqref{eqn:equation-unk-explicit} and we substitute $v=\frac{u_1+u_2}{2}$ and alternatively $u=u_1$ or $u=u_2$. Summing the two resultant inequalities together, we get
\begin{equation}
\label{eqn:estimates-unicity}
\Xi(u_1)+\Xi(u_2)-2\Xi\left(\frac{u_1+u_2}{2}\right)+\frac{1}{2}\langle F(u_1)-F(u_2),u_1-u_2\rangle\le0
\end{equation}
that explicitly reads
\[
\begin{multlined}
\frac{1}{2}\langle \mathcal{A}u_1,u_1\rangle+\frac{1}{2}\langle \mathcal{A}u_2,u_2\rangle-\left\langle \mathcal{A}\frac{u_1+u_2}{2},\frac{u_1+u_2}{2}\right\rangle+\\
+\tau_N\Phi_0\left(p,\frac{u_1-u_N^{k-1}}{\tau_N}\right)+\tau_N\Phi_0\left(p,\frac{u_2-u_N^{k-1}}{\tau_N}\right)-2\tau_N\Phi_0\left(p,\frac{\frac{u_1+u_2}{2}-u_N^{k-1}}{\tau_N}\right)+\\
+\frac{1}{2\tau_N}\langle \mathcal{D}(u_1-u_N^{k-1}),u_1-u_N^{k-1}\rangle+\frac{1}{2\tau_N}\langle \mathcal{D}(u_2-u_N^{k-1}),u_2-u_N^{k-1}\rangle-\\
-\frac{1}{\tau_N}\left\langle \mathcal{D}\left(\frac{u_1+u_2}{2}-u_N^{k-1}\right),\frac{u_1+u_2}{2}-u_N^{k-1}\right\rangle+\\
+\frac{1}{2}\langle D_u\Psi_0(u_1,\mathcal{L}(u_1))-D_u\Psi_0(u_2,\mathcal{L}(u_2)),u_1-u_2\rangle+\frac{1}{2}\langle\mathcal{L}'(u_1)^*p-\mathcal{L}'(u_2)^*p,u_1-u_2\rangle\le0
\end{multlined}
\]
Firstly we notice that
\[
\frac{1}{2}\dual{\mathcal{A}u_1,u_1}+\frac{1}{2}\dual{\mathcal{A}u_2,u_2}-\left\langle\mathcal{A}\frac{u_1+u_2}{2},\frac{u_1+u_2}{2}\right\rangle=\frac{1}{4}\dual{\mathcal{A}(u_1-u_2),u_1-u_2}\ge\frac{C_A}{4}|u_1-u_2|_U^2,
\]
that
\[
