\tau_N\Phi_0\left(p,\frac{u_1-u_N^{k-1}}{\tau_N}\right)+\tau_N\Phi_0\left(p,\frac{u_2-u_N^{k-1}}{\tau_N}\right)-2\tau_N\Phi_0\left(p,\frac{\frac{u_1+u_2}{2}-u_N^{k-1}}{\tau_N}\right)\ge0
\]
since $\Phi_0(p,\cdot)$ is convex, and that
\[
\begin{multlined}
\frac{1}{2\tau_N}\dual{\mathcal{D}(u_1-u_N^{k-1}),(u_1-u_N^{k-1})}+\frac{1}{2\tau_N}\dual{\mathcal{D}(u_2-u_N^{k-1}),(u_2-u_N^{k-1})}-\\
-\frac{1}{\tau_N}\left\langle\mathcal{D}\left(\frac{u_1+u_2}{2}-u_N^{k-1}\right),\frac{u_1+u_2}{2}-u_N^{k-1}\right\rangle=\\
=\frac{1}{4\tau_N}\dual{\mathcal{D}(u_1-u_2),u_1-u_2}\ge\frac{C_D}{4\tau_N}\norm{u_1-u_2}_H^2.
\end{multlined}
\]
Secondly, since $u_1$ and $u_2$ are confined on a ball of radius $B$, there exists a local Lipschitz constant $C_\Psi^4>0$ such that
\[
\frac{1}{2}\langle D_u\Psi_0(u_1,\mathcal{L}(u_1))-D_u\Psi_0(u_2,\mathcal{L}(u_2)),u_1-u_2\rangle\ge-\frac{1}{2}C_\Psi^4(1+C_L)\norm{u_1-u_2}_H^2.
\]
Eventually we have
\[
\frac{1}{2}\langle\mathcal{L}'(u_1)^*p-\mathcal{L}'(u_2)^*p,u_1-u_2\rangle\ge-\frac{1}{2}C_L\norm{p}_{\Lambda^*}\norm{u_1-u_2}_H^2.
\]
Thus, choosing $N$ sufficiently large such that
\[
\frac{C_D}{4\tau_N}-\frac{1}{2}C_\Psi^4(1+C_L)-\frac{1}{2}C_L\norm{p}_{\Lambda^*}>0,
\]
the left hand side in~\eqref{eqn:estimates-unicity} bounds $\norm{u_1-u_2}_H^2$, so that $u_1=u_2$.

Up to now we have proved that $\mathcal{U}$ is well defined and we have verified the boundedness properties. It remains to be proved that $\mathcal{U}$ satisfies the continuity properties. To this aim consider a sequence $\{p_n\}\subset\Lambda^*$ such that $p_n\to p$ in $\Lambda^*$ for some $p\in\Lambda^*$. Let $u_n=\mathcal{U}(p_n)$ and notice that, by the first part of the proof, $u_n$ are bounded in $U$. Thus for each subsequence $\{u_{n_j}\}$ there exists a further (not relabeled) subsequence such that $u_{n_j}\rightharpoonup u$ in $U$ and due to the compact embedding of $U$ into $H$, up to extracting a further subsequence, also $u_{n_j}\to u$ in $H$. In principle $u\in U$ depends on the particular subsequence, but, if we prove that $u=\mathcal{U}(p)$, it is actually unique and we conclude. To prove this we pass to the limit $j\to\infty$ on the equations satisfied by $u_{n_j}$. These can be written explicitly as
\[
\begin{multlined}
\frac{1}{2}\langle Av,v\rangle+\tau_N\Phi_0\left(p_{n_j},\frac{v-u_N^{k-1}}{\tau_N}\right)+\frac{1}{2}\tau_N\langle D(v-u_N^{k-1}),v-u_N^{k-1}\rangle+\\
+\langle D_u\Phi_0(u_{n_j},\mathcal{L}(u_{n_j}))+\mathcal{L}'(u_{n_j})^*p_{n_j},v-u_{n_j}\rangle\ge\\
\ge\frac{1}{2}\langle Au_{n_j},u_{n_j}\rangle+\tau_N\Phi_0\left(p_{n_j},\frac{u_{n_j}-u_N^{k-1}}{\tau_N}\right)+\frac{1}{2}\tau_N\langle D(u_{n_j}-u_N^{k-1}),u_{n_j}-u_N^{k-1}\rangle+\langle f,u_{n_j}-v\rangle.
\end{multlined}
\]
Since $u\mapsto\frac{1}{2}\dual{\mathcal{A}u,u}$ form $U$ to $\mathbb{R}$ is convex and strongly continuous so it is also weakly lower semicontinuous. Then
\[
\liminf_{j\to\infty}\frac{1}{2}\dual{\mathcal{A}u_{n_j},u_{n_j}}\ge\frac{1}{2}\dual{\mathcal{A}u,u}.
\]
That
\[
\tau_N\Phi_0\left(p_{n_j},\frac{v-u_N^{k-1}}{\tau_N}\right)\to\tau_N\Phi_0\left(p,\frac{v-u_N^{k-1}}{\tau_N}\right)
\]
and
\[
\frac{1}{2}\tau_N\langle D(u_{n_j}-u_N^{k-1}),u_{n_j}-u_N^{k-1}\rangle+\langle f,u_{n_j}-v\rangle\to\frac{1}{2}\tau_N\langle D(u-u_N^{k-1}),u_{n_j}-u_N^{k-1}\rangle+\langle f,u-v\rangle
\]
is trivial due to the strong convergence of $p_{n_j}$ in $\Lambda^*$ and $u_{n_j}$ in $H$. Then
\[
\begin{multlined}
\left|\langle\mathcal{L}'(u_n)^*p_n,v-u_n\rangle-\langle\mathcal{L}'(u)^*p,v-u\rangle\right|\le\\
\begin{split}
&\le\left|\langle\mathcal{L}'(u_n)^*(p_n-p),v-u_n\rangle+\langle\mathcal{L}'(u_n)^*p,v-u_n\rangle-\langle\mathcal{L}'(u)^*p,v-u\rangle\right|\le\\
&\le\|C_L\|\|p_n-p\|_{\Lambda^*}\|v-u_n\|_H+\left|\langle(\mathcal{L}'(u_n)^*-\mathcal{L}'(u)^*)p,v-u\rangle\right|+\left|\langle\mathcal{L}'(u_n)^*p,u-u_n\rangle\right|\le\\
&\le\|C_L\|\|p_n-p\|_{\Lambda^*}\|v-u_n\|_H+C_L\|u_n-u\|_H\|p\|_{\Lambda^*}\|v-u\|_H+C_L\|p\|_{\Lambda^*}\|u-u_n\|_H\to0
\end{split}
\end{multlined}
\]
The remaining terms are treated analogously.
\end{proof}
To complete the existence of the discrete-in-time solution it remains to show that there exists $p_N^k\in\Lambda^*$ such that also~\eqref{eqn:second-equation-discrete} is satisfied. This can be written as the following fixed point problem:
\begin{equation}
\label{eqn:fixed-point}
p_N^k=D_\lambda\Psi_0(\mathcal{U}(p_N^k),\mathcal{L}(\mathcal{U}(p_N^k)))=:\mathcal{T}(p_N^k)
\end{equation}
where $\mathcal{T}\colon\Lambda^*\to\Lambda^*$. The existence of a solution to~\eqref{eqn:fixed-point} follows from the Schauder fixed-point theorem in the form of Corollary~\ref{cor:schauder-improved} thanks to the following lemma.
\begin{lemma}
If $N$ is sufficiently large, the map $\mathcal{T}$ belongs to $C^0(\Lambda^*,\Lambda^*)$ and maps each bounded subset of $\Lambda^*$ into a precompact set in $\Lambda^*$. Moreover there exists constants $C_T^0\in[0,\infty)$ and $C_T^1\in[0,1)$ such that $\norm{\mathcal{T}(p)}_{\Lambda^*}\le C_T^0+C_T^1\norm{p}_{\Lambda^*}$.
\end{lemma}
\begin{proof}
The continuity of $\mathcal{T}$ follows immediately from the continuity of $D_\lambda\Psi_0$, $\mathcal{L}$ and $\mathcal{U}$. Next we prove that $\mathcal{T}(B(0,r))$ is precompact in $\Lambda^*$ for all $r>0$. Let $\{\mathcal{T}(p_n)\}\subset\mathcal{T}(\Lambda^*)$ with $\{p_n\}\subset B(0,r)$. We need to prove that, up to extracting a subsequence, there exists $p\in\Lambda^*$ such that $\mathcal{T}(p_n)\to p$ in $\Lambda^*$. To this aim notice that $\mathcal{U}(p_n)$ are bounded in $U$ by Lemma~\ref{lemma:properties-U}, so that by the compact embedding of $U$ in $H$, up to extracting a further subsequence, it holds that $\mathcal{U}(u_n)\to u$ in $H$, for some $u\in U$. Then by continuity it follows that $\mathcal{T}(p_n)\to D_\lambda(u,L(u))\in\Lambda^*$. This means exactly that $\mathcal{T}(\Lambda^*)$ is precompact in $\Lambda^*$.

Next we have
\[
\norm{\mathcal{T}(p)}_{\Lambda^*}\le C_\Psi^3(1+C_L)\norm{\mathcal{U}(p)}_H\le C_\Psi^3(1+C_L)\left(C_U^0+\frac{C_U^1}{N}\norm{p}_{\Lambda^*}\right).
\]
Choosing $N$ sufficiently large the thesis follows.
\end{proof}

\subsubsection*{A priori estimates and construction of the limit solution}

Summarizing, up to now we have proved the existence of $(u_N^k,\lambda_N^k)=(\mathcal{U}(p_N^k),\mathcal{L}(\mathcal{U}(p_N^k)))\in Z$ for $k\in\{0,\dots,N\}$ and $p_N^k\in\Lambda^*$ for $k\in\{1,\dots,N\}$, solutions to the discrete-in-time problem, when $N$ is sufficiently large. Now we need to pass to the limit $N\to\infty$ in order to prove the existence of the continuous-in-time solution.
\begin{rmk}[On notation]
We denote with $\bar u_N\in W^{1,\infty}((0,T),U)$ and $u_N\in W^{1,\infty}((0,T),U)$ respectively the piecewise constant and affine interpolation of $\{u_N^k\}_{k=0}^N$. In particular
\[
\bar u_N(0)=u_N^0=u_0\quad\text{and}\quad\bar u_N(t)=u_N^k\quad\text{for all $t\in(t_N^{k-1},t_N^k]$ and $k\in\{1,\dots,N\}$}.
\] The derivative $\dot u_N$ is piecewise constant in the sense that a representative is given by
\[
\dot u_N(t)=\dot u_N^k:=\frac{u_N^k-u_N^{k-1}}{\tau_N}\quad\text{for all $t\in(t_N^{k-1},t_N^k]$ and $k\in\{1,\dots,N\}$}.
\]
We also formally set $u_N^{-1}=u_0$ so that $\dot u_N^0:=0$. We notice that $\dot u_N^k\in D_\Phi$ for all $k\in\{0,\dots,N\}$. We denote with $\hat u_N\in W^{1,\infty}((0,T),U)$ the affine interpolation of $\{\dot u_N^k\}_{k=0}^N$. We set $\lambda_N(t)=\mathcal{L}(u_N(t))$, $\bar\lambda_N(t)=\mathcal{L}(\bar u_N(t))$, $p_N(t)=D_\lambda\Psi_0(u_N(t),\lambda_N(t))$ and $\bar p_N(t)=D_\lambda\Psi_0(\bar u_N(t),\bar\lambda_N(t))$ for all $t\in[0,T]$. Notice that $\lambda_N^k=\lambda_N(t_N^k)=\bar\lambda_N(t_N^k)$ and $p_N^k=p_N(t_N^k)=\bar p_N(t_N^k)$ for $k\in\{0,\dots,N\}$, where we have defined $p_N^0=p_0=D_\lambda\Psi_0(z_0)$. Let also $\bar f_N$ be the piecewise constant interpolation of $\{f_N^k\}_{k=0}^N$.
\end{rmk}
\begin{rmk}[The case $k=0$]
Having set $u_N^{-1}=0$ and $p_N^0=p_0$, recalling that $f(0)=D_u\Psi(z_0)+\mathcal{A}u_0+\mathcal{L}'(u_0)^*p_0$ and noticing that $0\in\partial\Phi(p_0,0)$, it follows that~\eqref{eqn:first-equation-discrete} holds also for $k=0$.
\end{rmk}
We now obtain some a priori estimates. The first estimate is obtained by testing the equation at each step $k$ separately and allows to get a uniform bound on $\norm{u_N}_{L^\infty((0,T),U)}$.
\begin{lemma}
\label{lemma:a-priori-0}
There exists a constant $C_0>0$ such that $\norm{u_N}_{L^\infty((0,T),U)}\le C_0$ for all $N$. Moreover also $\norm{\dot u_N}_{L^2((0,T),H)}$ is uniformly bounded in $N$.
\end{lemma}
\begin{proof}
We notice that~\eqref{eqn:first-equation-discrete} can be written as
\begin{equation}
\label{eqn:inclusion-explicit}
\tau_N\Phi_0(p_N^k,v)\ge\tau_N\Phi_0(p_N^k,\dot u_N^k)+\dual{f_N^k-D_u\Psi_0(u_N^k,\lambda_N^k)-\mathcal{A}u_N^k-\mathcal{L}'(u_N^k)^*p_N^k-\mathcal{D}\dot u_N^k,v-\dot u_N^k}
\end{equation}
for all $v\in U$. Setting $v=0$, using the positivity of $\Phi_0$ and rearranging the terms, we get
\[
\dual{\mathcal{A}u_N^k,\dot u_N^k}+\dual{\mathcal{D}\dot u_N^k,\dot u_N^k}\ge-\dual{f_N^k-D_u\Psi_0(u_N^k,\lambda_N^k)-\mathcal{L}'(u_N^k)^*p_N^k,\dot u_N^k}.
\]
Since
\[
\dual{\mathcal{A}u_N^k,\dot u_N^k}=\frac{1}{\tau_N}\dual{\mathcal{A}u_N^k,u_N^k-u_N^{k-1}}\ge\frac{1}{2\tau_N}\dual{\mathcal{A}u_N^k,u_N^k}-\frac{1}{2\tau_N}\dual{\mathcal{A}u_N^{k-1},u_N^{k-1}}
\]
and performing some trivial estimates, we find that
\[
\begin{multlined}
\frac{1}{2\tau_N}\langle \mathcal Au_N^k,u_N^k\rangle-\frac{1}{2\tau_N}\langle \mathcal Au_N^{k-1},u_N^{k-1}\rangle+\langle \mathcal D\dot{u}_N^k,\dot{u}_N^k\rangle\le\\
\le\|f_N^k\|_{H^*}\|\dot{u}_N^k\|_H+C_\Psi^2(1+C_L)^2\norm{u_N^k}_H^2+C_\Psi^1(1+C_L)\norm{u_N^k}_H+C_\Psi^0+\\
+C_\Psi^3(1+C_L)\norm{u_N^k}_H+C_LC_\Psi^3(1+C_L)\|u_N^k\|_H\|\dot{u}_N^k\|_H.
\end{multlined}
\]
Applying Young inequality
\[
\begin{multlined}
\frac{1}{2\tau_N}\langle \mathcal{A}u_N^k,u_N^k\rangle-\frac{1}{2\tau_N}\langle \mathcal{A}u_N^{k-1},u_N^{k-1}\rangle+C_D\|\dot{u}_N^k\|_H^2\le\\
\le\frac{2}{C_D}\|f_N^k\|_{H^*}^2+\frac{C_D}{8}\|\dot{u}_N^k\|_H^2+C_\Psi^2(1+C_L)^2\|u_N^k\|_H^2+C_\Psi^1(1+C_L)\|u_N^k\|_H+\\
+C_\Psi^0+C_\Psi^3(1+C_L)\|u_N^k\|_H+\frac{2}{C_D}C_L^2(C_\Psi^3)^2(1+C_L)^2\|u_N^k\|_H^2+\frac{C_D}{8}\|\dot{u}_N^k\|_H^2
\end{multlined}
\]
Multiplying by $\tau_N$ we arrive at
\[
\begin{multlined}
\frac{1}{2}\langle \mathcal Au_N^k,u_N^k\rangle-\frac{1}{2}\langle\mathcal Au_N^{k-1},u_N^{k-1}\rangle+\frac{3}{4}C_D\tau_N\|\dot{u}_N^k\|_H^2\le\\
\le\frac{2}{C_D}\tau_N\|f_N^k\|_{H^*}^2+\tau_N\left[C_\Psi^2(1+C_L)^2+\frac{2}{C_D}C_L^2(C_\Psi^3)^2(1+C_L)^2\right]\|u_N^k\|_H^2+\\
+\tau_N(C_\Psi^1+C_\Psi^3)(1+C_L)\|u_N^k\|_H+\tau_NC_\Psi^0.
\end{multlined}
\]
Summing for $k$ from 1 to $n$, we obtain
\begin{equation}
\label{eqn:inequality-first-order}
\begin{multlined}
\frac{C_A}{2}|u_N^k|_U^2+\frac{3}{4}C_D\tau_N\sum_{k=1}^n\norm{\dot u_N^k}_H^2\le\frac{2}{C_D}\tau_N\sum_{k=1}^N\|f_N^k\|_{H^*}^2+\\
+\left[C_\Psi^2(1+C_L)^2+\frac{2}{C_D}C_L^2(C_\Psi^3)^2(1+C_L)^2\right]\tau_N\sum_{k=1}^n\|u_N^k\|_H^2+\\
+(C_\Psi^1+C_\Psi^3)(1+C_L)\tau_N\sum_{k=1}^n\|u_N^k\|_H+TC_\Psi^0+\frac{1}{2}\dual{\mathcal{A}u_0,u_0}.
\end{multlined}
\end{equation}
Now let
\[
S_N^n=\tau_N\sum_{k=1}^n\norm{\dot u_N^k}_H^2.
\]
Notice that by Jensen inequality
\[
\begin{split}
\|u_N^k-u_0\|_H^2&=\left\|\sum_{j=1}^k({u}_N^j-{u}_N^{j-1})\right\|_H^2\le k^2\left(\frac{1}{k}\sum_{j=1}^k\|{u}_N^j-{u}_N^{j-1}\|_H\right)^2\le k^2\frac{1}{k}\sum_{j=1}^k\|{u}_N^j-{u}_N^{j-1}\|_H^2=\\
&=k\tau_N\;\tau_N\sum_{j=1}^k\left\|\frac{{u}_N^j-{u}_N^{j-1}}{\tau_N}\right\|_H^2\le TS_N^k,
\end{split}
\]
thus $\norm{u_N^k}_H^2\le2\norm{u_0}_H^2+2TS_N^k$. Moreover
\[
\tau_N\sum_{k=1}^N\|f_N^k\|_{H^*}^2=\tau_N\sum_{k=1}^N\left\|\frac{1}{\tau_N}\int_{t_N^{k-1}}^{t_N^k}f(s)\,ds\right\|_{H^*}^2\le\tau_N\sum_{k=1}^N\frac{1}{\tau_N}\int_{t_N^{k-1}}^{t_N^k}\|f(s)\|_{H^*}^2\,ds=\|f\|_{L^2((0,T),H^*)}^2
\]
By H\"older and Young inequalities
\[
\tau_N\sum_{k=1}^n\|u_N^k\|_H\le T^{1/2}\left(\tau_N\sum_{k=1}^n\|u_N^k\|_H^2\right)^{1/2}\le\frac{1}{2}\tau_N\sum_{k=1}^n\|u_N^k\|_H^2+\frac{T}{2}.
\]
Then~\eqref{eqn:inequality-first-order} implies that
\[
\begin{multlined}
\frac{3}{4}C_DS_N^n\le\frac{2}{C_D}\|f\|_{L^2((0,T),H^*)}^2+\\
+2T\left[C_\Psi^2(1+C_L)^2+\frac{2}{C_D}C_L^2(C_\Psi^3)^2(1+C_L)^2+\frac{(C_\Psi^1+C_\Psi^3)(1+C_L)}{2}\right]\left[\tau_N\sum_{k=1}^nS_N^k+\norm{u_0}_H^2\right]+\\
+(C_\Psi^1+C_\Psi^3)(1+C_L)\frac{T}{2}+TC_\Psi^0+\frac{1}{2}\dual{\mathcal{A}u_0,u_0}.
\end{multlined}
\]
By application of the discrete Gronwall inequality (Proposition~\ref{prop:discrete-gronwall}) it follows that $S_N^n$ is bounded uniformly in $n$ and $N$. This means that $\norm{\dot u_N}_{L^2((0,T),H)}$ is bounded uniformly in $N$. Now for all $t\in(0,T)$ it holds that
\[
\norm{u_N(t)}_H=\left\|\int_0^t\dot u_N(s)ds\right\|_H\le\int_0^T\norm{\dot u_N(s)}_Hds\le T^{1/2}\norm{\dot u_N}_{L^2((0,T),H)}.
\]
Moreover returning to~\eqref{eqn:inequality-first-order} we obtain that $|u_N^k|_U$ is uniformly bounded in $k$ and $N$. Then it follows that $\norm{u_N}_{L^\infty((0,T),U)}$ is uniformly bounded in $N$.
\end{proof}
The second a priori estimate is obtained by testing the equations at two adjacent time steps $k$ and $k-1$ and allows to get a uniform bound on $\norm{\dot u_N}_{L^\infty((0,T),U)}$.
\begin{lemma}
The quantities
$\|\dot{\hat{u}}_N\|_{L^2((0,T),H)}$, $\norm{\dot u_N}_{L^\infty((0,T),U)}$ and $\norm{\hat u_N}_{L^\infty((0,T),U)}$ are bounded uniformly in $N$.
\end{lemma}
\begin{proof}
Consider~\eqref{eqn:inclusion-explicit} for $k\in\{1,\dots,N\}$ and with $v=\dot u_N^{k-1}$:
\begin{equation}
\label{eqn:inclusion-k}
\tau_N\Phi_0(p_N^k,\dot u_N^{k-1})\ge\tau_N\Phi_0(p_N^k,\dot u_N^k)+\dual{f_N^k-D_u\Psi_0(u_N^k,\lambda_N^k)-\mathcal{A}u_N^k-\mathcal{L}'(u_N^k)^*p_N^k-\mathcal{D}\dot u_N^k,\dot u_N^{k-1}-\dot u_N^k}.
\end{equation}
Consider also~\eqref{eqn:inclusion-explicit} with $k-1$ in place of $k$ for $k\in\{1,\dots,N\}$ and with $v=\dot u_N^{k-1}$:
\begin{equation}
\label{eqn:inclusion-k-1}
\begin{multlined}
\tau_N\Phi_0(p_N^{k-1},\dot u_N^k)\ge\tau_N\Phi_0(p_N^{k-1},\dot u_N^{k-1})+\\
+\dual{f_N^{k-1}-D_u\Psi_0(u_N^{k-1},\lambda_N^{k-1})-\mathcal{A}u_N^{k-1}-\mathcal{L}'(u_N^{k-1})^*p_N^{k-1}-\mathcal{D}\dot u_N^{k-1},\dot u_N^k-\dot u_N^{k-1}}.
\end{multlined}
\end{equation}
Summing together~\eqref{eqn:inclusion-k} and~\eqref{eqn:inclusion-k-1} and rearranging the terms, we get
\[
\begin{multlined}
\tau_N\left[\Phi_0(p_N^k,\dot u_N^k)-\Phi_0(p_N^k,\dot u_N^{k-1})+\Phi_0(p_N^{k-1},\dot u_N^k)-\Phi_0(p_N^{k-1},\dot u_N^{k-1})\right]+\\
+\dual{f_N^k-f_N^{k-1},\dot u_N^k-\dot u_N^{k-1}}+\dual{D_u\Psi_0(u_N^k,\lambda_N^k)-D_u\Psi_0(u_N^{k-1},\lambda_N^{k-1}),\dot u_N^k-\dot u_N^{k-1}}-\\
-\dual{\mathcal{L}'(u_N^k)^*p_N^k-\mathcal{L}'(u_N^{k-1})^*p_N^{k-1},\dot u_N^k-\dot u_N^{k-1}}\ge\\
\ge\tau_N\dual{\mathcal{A}\dot u_N^k,\dot u_N^k-\dot u_N^{k-1}}+\dual{\mathcal{D}(\dot u_N^k-\dot u_N^{k-1}),\dot u_N^k-\dot u_N^{k-1}}
\end{multlined}
\]
for $k\in\{1,\dots,N\}$. Using ($\Phi$3) and performing some trivial estimates, it follows that
\[
\begin{multlined}
\frac{\tau_N}{2}\dual{\mathcal{A}\dot u_N^k,\dot u_N^k}-\frac{\tau_N}{2}\dual{\mathcal{A}\dot u_N^{k-1},\dot u_N^{k-1}}+C_D\norm{\dot u_N^k-\dot u_N^{k-1}}_H^2\le\tau_NC_\Phi\norm{p_N^k-p_N^{k-1}}_{\Lambda^*}\norm{\dot u_N^k-\dot u_N^{k-1}}_H+\\
+\norm{f_N^k-f_N^{k-1}}_{H^*}\norm{\dot u_N^k-\dot u_N^{k-1}}_H+\norm{D_u\Psi_0(u_N^k,\lambda_N^k)-D_u\Psi_0(u_N^{k-1},\lambda_N^{k-1})}_{H^*}\norm{\dot u_N^k-\dot u_N^{k-1}}_H+\\
+\norm{p_N^k}_{\Lambda^*}\norm{\mathcal{L}'(u_N^k)-\mathcal{L}'(u_N^{k-1})}_{\mathscr{L}(H,\Lambda)}\norm{\dot u_N^k-\dot u_N^{k-1}}_H+\norm{\mathcal{L}'(u_N^{k-1})}_{\mathscr{L}(H,\Lambda)}\norm{p_N^k-p_N^{k-1}}_{\Lambda^*}\norm{\dot u_N^k-\dot u_N^{k-1}}_H.
\end{multlined}
\]
By Lemma~\ref{lemma:a-priori-0} we know that $u_N^k$ are bounded in $U$, and in particular in $H$. Since also $\norm{\lambda_N^k}_\Lambda=\norm{\mathcal{L}(u_N^k)}_\Lambda\le C_L$, then, using that $D\Psi_0$ is locally Lipschitz and $\mathcal{L}$ is Lipschitz, it follows that there exists a constant $C_\Psi^4>0$ such that
\[
\norm{D_u\Psi_0(u_N^k,\lambda_N^k)-D_u\Psi_0(u_N^{k-1},\lambda_N^{k-1})}_{H^*}\le C_\Psi^4\norm{(u_N^k,\lambda_N^k)-(u_N^{k-1},\lambda_N^{k-1})}_{H\times\Lambda}\le C_\Psi^4(1+C_L)\tau_N\norm{\dot u_N^k}_H.
\]
Moreover
\[
\begin{split}
\norm{p_N^k}_{\Lambda^*}&=\norm{D_\lambda\Psi_0(u_N^k,\mathcal{L}(u_N^k))}_{\Lambda^*}\le\norm{D_\lambda\Psi_0(0,0)}_{\Lambda^*}+C_\Psi^4(1+C_L)\norm{u_N^k}_H=\\
&=\norm{D_\lambda\Psi_0(0,0)}_{\Lambda^*}+C_\Psi^4(1+C_L)C_0=:C_p<\infty
\end{split}
\]
and similarly
\[
\norm{p_N^k-p_N^{k-1}}_{\Lambda^*}\le C_\Psi^4(1+C_L)\tau_N\norm{\dot u_N^k}_H.
\]
Then
\[
\begin{multlined}
\frac{\tau_N}{2}\dual{\mathcal{A}\dot u_N^k,\dot u_N^k}-\frac{\tau_N}{2}\dual{\mathcal{A}\dot u_N^{k-1},\dot u_N^{k-1}}+C_D\norm{\dot u_N^k-\dot u_N^{k-1}}_H^2\le\tau_N^2C_\Phi C_\Psi^4(1+C_L)\norm{\dot u_N^k}_H\norm{\dot u_N^k-\dot u_N^{k-1}}_H+\\
+\norm{f_N^k-f_N^{k-1}}_{H^*}\norm{\dot u_N^k-\dot u_N^{k-1}}_H+C_\Psi^4(1+C_L)\tau_N\norm{\dot u_N^k}_H\norm{\dot u_N^k-\dot u_N^{k-1}}_H+\\
+C_pC_L\tau_N\norm{\dot u_N^k}_H\norm{\dot u_N^k-\dot u_N^{k-1}}_H+C_LC_\Psi^4(1+C_L)\tau_N\norm{\dot u_N^k}_H\norm{\dot u_N^k-\dot u_N^{k-1}}_H.
\end{multlined}
\]
Applying Young inequality we get
\[
\begin{multlined}
\frac{\tau_N}{2}\dual{\mathcal{A}\dot u_N^k,\dot u_N^k}-\frac{\tau_N}{2}\dual{\mathcal{A}\dot u_N^{k-1},\dot u_N^{k-1}}+\frac{C_D}{6}\norm{\dot u_N^k-\dot u_N^{k-1}}_H^2\le\frac{3}{2C_D}\norm{f_N^k-f_N^{k-1}}_{H^*}^2+\\+
\frac{3}{2C_D}\tau_N^2\left[\tau_N^2C_\Phi^4(C_\Psi^4)^2(1+C_L)^2+(C_\Psi^4)^2(1+C_L)^2+C_p^2C_L^2+C_L^2(C_\Psi^4)^2(1+C_L)^2\right]\norm{\dot u_N^k}_H^2.
\end{multlined}
\]
Letting
\[
C_1=T^2C_\Phi^4(C_\Psi^4)^2(1+C_L)^2+(C_\Psi^4)^2(1+C_L)^2+C_p^2C_L^2+C_L^2(C_\Psi^4)^2(1+C_L)^2
\]
and dividing by $\tau_N$, the above inequality can be written as
\[
\frac{1}{2}\dual{\mathcal{A}\dot u_N^k,\dot u_N^k}-\frac{1}{2}\dual{\mathcal{A}\dot u_N^{k-1},\dot u_N^{k-1}}+\frac{C_D}{6}\tau_N\left\lVert\frac{\dot u_N^k-\dot u_N^{k-1}}{\tau_N}\right\rVert_H^2\le\frac{3}{2C_D}\tau_N\left(\left\lVert\frac{f_N^k-f_N^{k-1}}{\tau_N}\right\rVert_{H^*}^2+C_1\norm{\dot u_N^k}_H^2\right).
\]
Summing for $k$ from 1 to $j$, using the coercivity property of $\mathcal{A}$ and the fact that $\dot u_N^0=0$, we get
\begin{equation}
\label{eqn:a-priori-estimate}
\frac{C_A}{2}|\dot u_N^n|_U^2+\frac{C_D}{6}\tau_N\sum_{k=1}^n\left\lVert\frac{\dot u_N^k-\dot u_N^{k-1}}{\tau_N}\right\rVert_H^2\le\frac{3}{2C_D}\tau_N\sum_{k=1}^n\left(\left\lVert\frac{f_N^k-f_N^{k-1}}{\tau_N}\right\rVert_{H^*}^2+C_1\norm{\dot u_N^k}_H^2\right).
\end{equation}
Let
\[
S_N^n=\tau_N\sum_{k=1}^n\left\lVert\frac{\dot u_N^k-\dot u_N^{k-1}}{\tau_N}\right\rVert_H^2.
\]
Then by Jensen inequality
\[
\begin{split}
\|\dot{u}_N^k\|_H^2&=\left\|\sum_{j=1}^k(\dot{u}_N^j-\dot{u}_N^{j-1})\right\|_H^2\le k^2\left(\frac{1}{k}\sum_{j=1}^k\|\dot{u}_N^j-\dot{u}_N^{j-1}\|_H\right)^2\le k^2\frac{1}{k}\sum_{j=1}^k\|\dot{u}_N^j-\dot{u}_N^{j-1}\|_H^2=\\
&=k\tau_N\;\tau_N\sum_{j=1}^k\left\|\frac{\dot{u}_N^j-\dot{u}_N^{j-1}}{\tau_N}\right\|_H^2\le TS_N^k
\end{split}
\]
From~\eqref{eqn:a-priori-estimate} it follows that
\[
S_N^n\le\tau_N\sum_{k=1}^n\left(\frac{9}{C_D^2}\left\|\frac{f_N^k-f_N^{k-1}}{\tau_N}\right\|_{H^*}^2+\frac{9T}{C_D^2}S_N^k\right)
\]
Applying the discrete Gronwall inequality, i.e.\ Proposition~\ref{prop:discrete-gronwall}, we get
\[
S_N^n\le\left(\tau_N\sum_{k=1}^n\frac{9T}{C_D^2}\right)\exp\left(\frac{9}{C_D^2}\tau_N\sum_{k=1}^n\left\|\frac{f_N^k-f_N^{k-1}}{\tau_N}\right\|_{H^*}^2\right)\le T\frac{9T}{C_D^2}\exp\left(\frac{9}{C_D^2}\tau_N\sum_{k=1}^N\left\|\frac{f_N^k-f_N^{k-1}}{\tau_N}\right\|_{H^*}^2\right).
\]
But since $f\in H^1((0,T),H^*)$, by repeatedly applying H\"older inequality, using Fubini--Tonelli theorem and the fact that $\dot f(s)=0$ for $s<0$, it follows that
\[
\begin{split}
\tau_N\sum_{k=1}^N\left\|\frac{f_N^k-f_N^{k-1}}{\tau_N}\right\|_{H^*}^2&=\frac{1}{\tau_N}\sum_{k=1}^N\left\|\frac{1}{\tau_N}\int_{t_N^{k-1}}^{t_N^k}\int_{t-\tau_N}^s\dot{f}(s)dsdt\right\|_{H^*}^2\le\frac{1}{\tau_N^3}\sum_{k=1}^N\left(\int_{t_N^{k-1}}^{t_N^k}\int_{t-\tau_N}^t\|\dot{f}(s)\|_{H^*}dsdt\right)^2\le\\
&\le\frac{1}{\tau_N^3}\sum_{k=1}^N\left(\int_{t_N^{k-1}}^{t_N^k}\tau_N^{1/2}\left(\int_{t-\tau_N}^t\|\dot{f}(s)\|_{H^*}^2ds\right)^{1/2}dt\right)^2\le\\
&\le\frac{1}{\tau_N}\sum_{k=1}^N\int_{t_N^{k-1}}^{t_N^k}\int_{t-\tau_N}^t\|\dot{f}(s)\|_{H^*}^2dsdt=\frac{1}{\tau_N}\int_0^T\int_{t-\tau_N}^t\|\dot{f}(s)\|_{H^*}^2dsdt\le\\
&\le\int_0^T\frac{1}{\tau_N}\int_s^{s+\tau_N}\|\dot{f}(s)\|_{H^*}^2dtds=\|\dot{f}\|_{L^2((0,T),H^*)}^2.
\end{split}
\]
This shows that $S_N^N=\|\dot{\hat{u}}_N\|_{L^2((0,T),H)}$ is bounded uniformly in $N$. Returning back to~\eqref{eqn:a-priori-estimate} we get that also $\norm{\dot u_N}_{L^\infty((0,T),U)}$ and $\norm{\hat u_N}_{L^\infty((0,T),U)}$ are bounded uniformly in $N$. This concludes the proof.
\end{proof}
Summarizing, the a priori estimates that we have obtained imply that $\norm{u_N}_{W^{1,\infty}((0,T),U)}$, $\norm{\hat u_N}_{L^\infty((0,T),U)}$ and $\norm{\dot{\hat{u}}_N}_{L^2((0,T),H)}$ are bounded uniformly in $N$. Thus there exists $u\in W^{1,\infty}((0,T),U)$ such that, up to extracting a subsequence, $u_N\overset{*}{\rightharpoonup}u$ in $W^{1,\infty}((0,T),U)$. By Aubin--Lions--Simons lemma (Theorem~\ref{thm:aubin-lions}) it holds also that, up to extracting a further subsequence, $u_N\to u$ and $\hat{u}_N\to v$ in $C^0([0,T],H)$, where $v\in C^0([0,T],H)$. Also the convergences stated in the following lemmas hold.
\begin{lemma}
It holds that $\norm{u_N-\bar u_N}_{L^2((0,T),U)}\to0$ and in particular $\bar u_N\to u$ in $L^2((0,T),H)$. Moreover $u_N(T)\rightharpoonup u(T)$ in $U$.
\end{lemma}
\begin{proof}
We have that
\[
\begin{split}
\|\bar{u}_N-u_N\|_{L^2((0,T),U)}^2&=\sum_{k=1}^N\int_{t_N^{k-1}}^{t_N^k}\|u_N^k-u_N^{k-1}-\dot{u}_N^k(t-t_N^{k-1})\|_U^2dt=\\
&=\sum_{k=1}^N\int_{t_N^{k-1}}^{t_N^k}\|\dot{u}_N^k\|_U^2\left(\tau_N-t+t_N^{k-1}\right)^2dt=\\
&=\left[\int_0^{\tau_N}(\tau_N-t)^2dt\right]\frac{1}{\tau_N}\|\dot{u}_N\|_{L^2((0,T),U)}^2=\frac{\tau_N^2}{3}\|\dot{u}_N\|_{L^2((0,T),U)}^2.
\end{split}
\]
Since $\norm{\dot u_N}_{L^2((0,T),U)}$ is bounded uniformly in $N$, it follows that $\norm{u_N-\bar u_N}_{L^2((0,T),U)}\to0$. The fact that $u_N\to u$ in $C^0([0,T],H)$ implies that $\bar u_N\to u$ in $L^2((0,T),H)$.

To prove that $u_N(T)\rightharpoonup u(T)$ in $U$, observe that for any $\xi\in U^*$ it holds that
\[
\langle\xi,u_N(T)\rangle=\langle\xi,u_0+\int_0^T\dot{u}_N(t)dt\rangle\to\langle\xi,u_0+\int_0^T\dot{u}(t)dt\rangle=\dual{\xi,u(T)},
\]
where we use that $\dot u_N\overset{*}{\rightharpoonup}\dot u$ in $L^\infty((0,T),U)$.
\end{proof}
\begin{lemma}
It holds that $\dot u_N\to v$ in $L^2((0,T),H)$ and in particular $v=\dot u$.
\end{lemma}
\begin{proof}
We now that
\[
\begin{split}
\|\dot{u}_N-\hat{u}_N\|_{L^2((0,T),H)}^2&=\sum_{k=1}^N\int_{t_N^{k-1}}^{t_N^k}\left\|\dot{u}_N^k-\dot{u}_N^{k-1}-\frac{t-t_N^{k-1}}{\tau_N}(\dot{u}_N^k-\dot{u}_N^{k-1})\right\|_H^2dt\le\\
&\le\sum_{k=1}^N\int_{t_N^{k-1}}^{t_N^k}\|\dot{u}_N^k-\dot{u}_N^{k-1}\|_H^2\left(1-\frac{t-t_N^{k-1}}{\tau_N}\right)^2dt=\\
&=\left[\int_0^{\tau_N}\left(1-\frac{s}{\tau_N}\right)^2ds\right]\tau_N\norm{\dot{\hat{u}}_N}_{L^2((0,T),H)}^2=\frac{\tau_N^2}{3}\norm{\dot{\hat{u}}_N}_{L^2((0,T),H)}^2.
\end{split}
\]
Since $\norm{\dot{\hat{u}}_N}_{L^2((0,T),H)}$ is bounded uniformly in $N$, it follows that $\|\dot{u}_N-\hat{u}_N\|_{L^2((0,T),H)}\to0$.
\end{proof}
\begin{rmk}
Since $D_\Phi$ is convex $\hat u_N(t)\in D_\Phi$ for all $t\in[0,T]$, and since $\hat u_N\to\dot u$ in $C^0([0,T],H)$ and $D_\Phi$ is closed in $H$, then $\dot u(t)\in D_\Phi$ for all $t\in[0,T]$.
\end{rmk}
Next we build the time-continuous $\lambda$ and $p$. We define $\lambda=\mathcal{L}(u)\in C^0([0,T],\Lambda)$ and $p=D_\lambda\Psi_0(u,\lambda)\in C^0([0,T],\Lambda^*)$. The convergences of $\lambda_N,\bar\lambda_N$ and $p_N,\bar p_N$ to $\lambda$ and $p$ respectively are ensured by the following lemma.
\begin{lemma}
It holds that
\begin{align}
\lambda_N\to\lambda\;\text{in $C^0([0,T],\Lambda)$},\qquad & \bar\lambda_N\to\lambda\;\text{in $L^2([0,T],\Lambda)$} \\
p_N\to p\;\text{in $C^0([0,T],\Lambda^*)$},\qquad & \bar p_N\to p\;\text{in $L^2([0,T],\Lambda^*)$}.
\end{align}
\end{lemma}
\begin{proof}
Using that $\mathcal{L}$ is Lipschitz, we have
\[
\norm{\lambda_N(t)-\lambda(t)}_\Lambda=\norm{\mathcal{L}(u_N(t))-\mathcal{L}(u(t))}_\Lambda\le C_L\norm{u_N(t)-u(t)}_H
\]
for all $t\in[0,T]$, so that
\[
\norm{\lambda_N-\lambda}_{C^0([0,T],\Lambda)}=\norm{\mathcal{L}(u_N)-\mathcal{L}(u)}_{C^0([0,T],\Lambda)}\le C_L\norm{u_N-u}_{C^0([0,T],H)}\to0.
\]
Moreover
\[
\begin{split}
\|\bar{\lambda}_N-\lambda\|_{L^2((0,T),\Lambda)}^2=\int_0^T\|\mathcal{L}(\bar{u}_N(t))-\mathcal{L}(u(t))\|_\Lambda^2dt\le\\
\le\int_0^TC_L^2\|\bar{u}_N(t)-u(t)\|_H^2dt=C_L^2\|\bar{u}_N-u\|_{L^2((0,T),H)}^2\to0.
\end{split}
\]
The convergence of $p_N$ and $\bar p_N$ follows similarly form the fact that $\mathcal{L}$ is Lipschitz and $D_\lambda\Psi_0$ is locally Lipschitz.
\end{proof}

\subsubsection*{Passage to the limit of vanishing step size}

Now we have to prove that $(u,\lambda,p)$ is a solution to Problem~\ref{prb:existence-abstract}. Notice that~\eqref{eqn:problem-abstract-second},~\eqref{eqn:problem-abstract-third} are satisfied by construction and $z(0)=z_0$. Then we only have to show that~\eqref{eqn:problem-abstract-first} is satisfied for a.e.\ $t\in(0,T)$. To this aim, notice that~\eqref{eqn:first-equation-discrete} can be written as
\begin{equation}
\label{eqn:inclusion-bochner-discrete}
\begin{multlined}
\int_0^L\big[\Phi_0(\bar{p}_N(t),v(t))-\Phi_0(\bar{p}_N(t),\dot u_N(t))+\\
+\dual{D_u\Psi_0(\bar u_N(t),\bar\lambda_N(t))+\mathcal{A}\bar u_N(t)+\mathcal{D}\dot u_N(t)+\mathcal{L}'(\bar u_N(t))^*\bar p_N(t)-\bar f_N(t),v(t)-\dot u_N(t)}\big]dt\ge0
\end{multlined}
\end{equation}
for all $v\in L^\infty((0,T),U)$. We want to pass in the limit $N\to\infty$ of that inequality. To this aim we look separately the limit of each term. First we notice that
\[
\begin{split}
\left|\int_0^T\Phi_0(\bar{p}_N(t),v(t))dt-\int_0^T\Phi_0(p(t),v(t))dt\right|
&\le C_\Phi\int_0^T\|\bar{p}_N(t)-p(t)\|_{\Lambda^*}\|v(t)\|_Hdt\le\\
&\le C_\Phi\|\bar{p}_N-p\|_{L^2((0,T),\Lambda^*)}\|v\|_{L^2((0,T),H)}\to0
\end{split}
\]
and
\[
\begin{multlined}
\left|\int_0^T\Phi_0(\bar{p}_N(t),\dot{u}_N(t))dt-\int_0^T\Phi_0(p(t),\dot{u}(t))dt\right|\le\\
\le\int_0^T\left|\Phi_0(\bar{p}_N(t),\dot{u}_N(t))-\Phi_0(p(t),\dot{u}_N(t))\right|dt+\int_0^T\left|\Phi_0(p(t),\dot{u}_N(t))-\Phi_0(p(t),\dot{u}(t))\right|dt\le\\
\le C_\Phi\|\bar{p}_N-p\|_{L^2((0,T),\Lambda^*)}\|\dot{u}_N\|_{L^2((0,T),H)}+C_\Phi(\|p\|_{L^2((0,T),\Lambda^*)}+1)\|\dot{u}_N-\dot{u}\|_{L^2((0,T),H)}\to0.
\end{multlined}
\]
Next
\[
\begin{multlined}
\left|\int_0^T\langle D_u\Psi_0(\bar{u}_N(t),\bar{\lambda}_N(t)),v(t)-\dot{u}_N(t)\rangle dt-\int_0^T\langle D_u\Psi_0(u(t),\lambda(t)),v(t)-\dot{u}(t)\rangle dt\right|\le\\
\le C_\Psi^4\left(\|\bar{u}_N-u\|_{L^2((0,T),H)}+\|\bar{\lambda}_N-\lambda\|_{L^2((0,T),H)}\right)\|\dot{u}_N-\dot{u}\|_{L^2((0,T),H)}\to0
\end{multlined}
\]
and
\[
\begin{multlined}
\left|\int_0^T\langle \mathcal{D}\dot{u}_N(t),v(t)-\dot{u}_N(t)\rangle dt-\int_0^T\langle\mathcal{D}\dot{u}(t),v(t)-\dot{u}(t)\rangle dt\right|\le\\
\le\int_0^T\left|\langle\mathcal{D}\dot{u}_N(t),\dot{u}_N(t)-\dot{u}(t)\rangle\right|dt+\int_0^T\left|\langle\mathcal{D}\dot{u}_N(t)-\mathcal{D}\dot{u}(t),v-\dot{u}(t)\rangle\right|dt\le\\
\le\|\mathcal{D}\|_{\mathscr{L}(H,H^*)}\left(\|\dot{u}_N\|_{L^2((0,T),H)}+\|v-\dot{u}\|_{L^2((0,T),H)}\right)\|\dot{u}_N-\dot{u}\|_{L^2((0,T),H)}\to0.
\end{multlined}
\]
Then
\[
\begin{multlined}
\left|\int_0^T\langle\mathcal{L}'(\bar{u}_N(t))^*\bar{p}_N(t),v(t)-\dot{u}_N(t)\rangle dt-\int_0^T\langle\mathcal{L}'(u(t))^*p(t),v(t)-\dot{u}(t)\rangle dt\right|\le\\
\le\int_0^T\left|\langle\mathcal{L}'(\bar{u}_N(t))^*\bar{p}_N(t),v(t)-\dot{u}_N(t)\rangle-\langle\mathcal{L}'(\bar{u}_N(t))^*p(t),v(t)-\dot{u}_N(t)\rangle\right|dt+\\
+\int_0^T\left|\langle\mathcal{L}'(\bar{u}_N(t))^*p(t),v(t)-\dot{u}_N(t)\rangle-\langle\mathcal{L}'(u(t))^*p(t),v(t)-\dot{u}_N(t)\rangle\right|dt+\\
+\int_0^T\left|\langle\mathcal{L}'(u(t))^*p(t),v(t)-\dot{u}_N(t)\rangle-\langle\mathcal{L}'(u(t))^*p(t),v(t)-\dot{u}(t)\rangle\right|dt\le\\
\le
\begin{multlined}[t]
C_L\|\bar{p}_N-p\|_{L^2((0,T),\Lambda^*)}\|v-\dot{u}_N\|_{L^2((0,T),H)}+\\
+C_L\|\bar{u}_N-u\|_{L^2((0,T),H)}\|p\|_{C^0([0,T],\Lambda^*)}\|v-\dot{u}_N\|_{L^2((0,T),H)}+\\
+C_L\|p\|_{L^2((0,T),\Lambda^*)}\|\dot{u}_N-\dot{u}\|_{L^2((0,T),H)}\to0
\end{multlined}
\end{multlined}
\]
and
\[
\begin{multlined}
\left|\int_0^T\langle\bar{f}_N(t),v(t)-\dot{u}_N(t)\rangle dt-\int_0^T\langle f(t),v(t)-\dot{u}(t)\rangle dt\right|\le\\
\le\|\bar{f}_N-f\|_{L^2((0,T),H^*)}\|v-\dot{u}_N\|_{L^2((0,T),H)}+\|f\|_{L^2((0,T),H^*)}\|\dot{u}_N-\dot{u}\|_{L^2((0,T),H)}\to0,
\end{multlined}
\]
where we used that $\bar f_N\to f$ in $L^2((0,T),H^*)$ since, by applying Jensen inequality
\[
\begin{split}
\|f-\bar{f}_N\|_{L^2((0,T),H^*)}^2&=\sum_{k=1}^N\int_{t_N^{k-1}}^{t_N^k}\|f(t)-\bar{f}_N(t)\|_{H^*}^2dt=\sum_{k=1}^N\int_{t_N^{k-1}}^{t_N^k}\left\|f(t)-\frac{1}{\tau_N}\int_{t_N^{k-1}}^{t_N^k}f(s)ds\right\|_{H^*}^2dt=\\
&=\sum_{k=1}^N\int_{t_N^{k-1}}^{t_N^k}\frac{1}{\tau_N}\int_{t_N^{k-1}}^{t_N^k}\|f(t)-f(s)\|_{H^*}^2dsdt=\sum_{k=1}^N\int_{t_N^{k-1}}^{t_N^k}\frac{1}{\tau_N}\int_{t_N^{k-1}}^{t_N^k}\left\|\int_s^t\dot{f}(r)dr\right\|_{H^*}^2dsdt\le\\
&\le\sum_{k=1}^N\int_{t_N^{k-1}}^{t_N^k}\int_{t_N^{k-1}}^{t_N^k}\int_s^t\|\dot{f}(r)\|_{H^*}^2drdsdt\le\sum_{k=1}^N\tau_N^2\int_{t_N^{k-1}}^{t_N^k}\|\dot{f}(r)\|_{H^*}^2dr=\tau_N^2\|\dot{f}\|_{L^2((0,T),H^*)}^2.
\end{split}
\]
Moreover
\[
\begin{multlined}
\limsup_{N\to\infty}\int_0^T\langle A\bar{u}_N(t),v(t)-\dot{u}_N(t)\rangle dt=\\
\begin{split}
&\begin{multlined}[t]
=\lim_{N\to\infty}\int_0^T\langle A(\bar{u}_N(t)-u_N(t)),v(t)-\dot{u}_N(t)\rangle dt-\\
-\liminf_{N\to\infty}\int_0^T\langle Au_N(t),\dot{u}_N(t)\rangle dt\\+\lim_{N\to\infty}\int_0^T\langle Au_N(t),v(t)\rangle dt=
\end{multlined}\\
&=-\liminf_{N\to\infty}\int_0^T\frac{d}{dt}\left[\frac{1}{2}\langle Au_N(t),u_N(t)\rangle\right]dt+\int_0^T\langle Au(t),v(t)\rangle dt=\\
&=-\liminf_{N\to\infty}\left[\frac{1}{2}\langle Au_N(T),u_N(T)\rangle-\frac{1}{2}\langle Au_0,u_0\rangle\right]+\int_0^T\langle Au(t),v(t)\rangle dt\le\\
&\le-\frac{1}{2}\langle Au(T),u(T)\rangle+\frac{1}{2}\langle Au_0,u_0\rangle+\int_0^T\langle Au(t),v(t)\rangle dt=\int_0^T\langle Au(t),v(t)-\dot{u}(t)\rangle dt,
\end{split}
\end{multlined}
\]
where we used that $\norm{\bar u_N-u_N}_{L^2((0,T),U)}\to0$, $u_N\overset{*}{\rightharpoonup}u$ in $L^\infty((0,T),U)$, $u_N(T)\rightharpoonup u(T)$ in $U$ and $u\mapsto\dual{\mathcal{A}u,u}$ is weakly lower semicontinuous as a map from $U$ to $\mathbb{R}$ (since it is strongly continuous and convex). We also used that, as a simple application of Lions--Magenes lemma, it holds that
\[
\frac{d}{dt}\left[\frac{1}{2}\dual{\mathcal{A}u_N(t),u_N(t)}\right]=\dual{\mathcal{A}u_N(t),\dot u_N(t)}\quad\text{and}\quad\frac{d}{dt}\left[\frac{1}{2}\dual{\mathcal{A}u(t),u(t)}\right]=\dual{\mathcal{A}u(t),\dot u(t)}
\]
for almost every $t\in(0,T)$. Now we can take the supremum limit for $N\to\infty$ of~\eqref{eqn:inclusion-bochner-discrete} and get
\begin{equation}
\label{eqn:inclusion-bochner}
\begin{multlined}
\int_0^L\big[\Phi_0(p(t),v(t))-\Psi_0(p(t),\dot u(t))+\\
+\dual{D_u\Psi_0(u(t),\lambda(t))+\mathcal{A}\bar u(t)+\mathcal{D}\dot u(t)+\mathcal{L}'(u(t))^*p(t)-f(t),v(t)-\dot u(t)}\big]dt\ge0
\end{multlined}
\end{equation}
for all $v\in L^\infty((0,T),U)$. Since $v$ is arbitrary this is equivalent to~\eqref{eqn:problem-abstract-first}.

Thus we have proved Theorem~\ref{thm:abstract-existence}.

\section{An existence result for rate-independent evolutionary equations with quadratic energy}
\label{sec:evi}

In this section we describe an existence result for evolutionary equations of the form~\eqref{eqn:modeling:doubly:nonlinear-neat} when the free energy $\Psi$ is quadratic and the dissipation potential does not depend on the configuration and is positively 1-homogeneous in its rate. It is treated from~\cite{han-reddy}. The fact that the dissipation potential does not have a quadratic growth prevents us from applying the existence theorem that we presented in Section~\ref{sec:roubickeck}.However, the fact that the free energy is quadratic and the dissipation potential is convex and 1-homogeneous still allows us to obtain sufficiently strong a priori estimates to demonstrate the existence of absolutely continuous solutions in time.

The plasticity theory at small deformations described in Section~\ref{sec:plasticity-review} when a quadratic hardening function $w_p$ is assumed satisfies the assumptions required in this section. Moreover we will use the content of this section to prove the existence of solutions to the small deformation version of the model of a cylindrical bar in Chapter~\ref{chap:small-neck}.

\subsection{Assumptions and formulation of the problem}

Let $Z$ be a separable and reflexive Banach space. Assume that 
\begin{itemize}
\item[($\Psi$)] the free energy $\Psi\colon Z\to\mathbb{R}$ is quadratic in the sense that $\Psi(u)=\frac{1}{2}\dual{\mathcal{A}u,u}$ for some $\mathcal{A}\in\mathscr{L}(Z,Z^*)$ satisfying $\dual{\mathcal{A}z,z}\ge C_A\norm{z}_Z^2$ for some $C_A>0$;
\item[($\Phi$)] the dissipation potential $\Phi\colon Z\to[0,\infty]$ is convex, proper, lower semicontinuous, positively 1-homogeneous and Lipschitz on its domain.
\end{itemize}
Assumption ($\Psi$) immediately implies that $\Psi$ is FrĂ©chet differentiable with FrĂ©chet differential $D\Psi(u)=\mathcal{A}$ for all $u\in V$. In this case Problem~\ref{prb:modeling:doubly-nonlinear} reads as follows.
\begin{prb}
\label{prb:evi}
Given $f\in H^1((0,T),Z^*)$ with $f(0)=0$, find $z\in\text{AC}([0,T],Z)$ such that $z(0)=0$ and $\mathcal{A}z(t)+\partial\Phi(\dot z(t))\ni f(t)$
for a.e.\ $t\in(0,T)$.
\end{prb}

\subsection{Existence theorem}
%\begin{equation}
%\label{eqn:evi}
%\dual{\mathcal{A}z(t),w-\dot u(t)}+\Phi(v)-\Phi(\dot u(t))\ge\dual{\mathcal{X}(t),v-\dot u(t)}\quad\text{for all $v\in V$}
%\end{equation}
The following theorem ensure the existence and uniqueness of solutions to Problem~\ref{prb:evi}. We report only a very brief sketch of the proof. The complete proof is reported in~\cite[Theorem 7.3, Theorem 7.4]{han-reddy}.
\begin{thm}
\label{thm:han-reddy}
Under the previously stated assumptions, there exists a unique solution $z\in\text{AC}([0,T],Z)$ to Problem~\ref{prb:evi}. It holds in particular that $\dot z\in L^2((0,T),Z)$. Moreover there exists a stability constant $C_S>0$ such that, for all loads $f_1,f_2\in H^1((0,T),Z^*)$ with $f_1(0)=f_2(0)=0$, the corresponding solutions $z_1,z_2\in\text{AC}((0,T),Z)$ satisfy
\[
\norm{z_1-z_2}_{L^\infty((0,T),Z)}\le C_S\norm{f_1-f_2}_{L^2((0,T),Z^*)}.
\]
\end{thm}
\begin{proof}[Sketch of the proof]
Also the proof of this theorem relies on a time-discretization approach. Thus fix $N\in\mathbb{N}$ and let $\tau_N=T/N$ and $t_N^k=k\tau_N$ for $k\in\{0,\dots,N\}$. We look for a discrete-in-time solution $\{z_N^k\}_{k=0}^N$ satisfying $z_N^0=z_0$ and
\[
\mathcal{A}z_N^k+\partial\Phi\left(\frac{z_N^k-z_N^{k-1}}{\tau_N}\right)\ni f_N^k\quad\text{for $k\in\{1,\dots,N\}$, where $f_N^k:=f(t_N^k)$.}
\]
Letting
\[
\Delta z_N^k:=z_N^k-z_N^{k-1},
\]
this can be written explicitly as
\[
\left\langle\mathcal{A}z_N^k,w-\frac{\Delta z_N^k}{\tau_N}\right\rangle+\Phi(w)-\Phi\left(\frac{\Delta z_N^k}{\tau_N}\right)\ge\left\langle f_N^k,w-\frac{\Delta z_N^k}{\tau_N}\right\rangle\quad\text{for all $w\in Z$}.
\]
Since $\Phi$ is positively 1-homogeneous, this is equivalent to
\begin{equation}
\label{eqn:differential-inequality}
\langle\mathcal{A}\Delta z_N^k,w-\Delta z_N^k\rangle+\Phi(w)-\Phi(\Delta z_N^k)\ge\langle f_N^k-\mathcal{A}z_N^{k-1},w-\Delta z_N^k\rangle\quad\text{for all $w\in Z$}.
\end{equation}
If we define $\mathcal{J}\colon Z\to\mathbb{R}\cup\{\infty\}$ by
\[
\mathcal{J}_N^k(w)=\frac{1}{2}\dual{\mathcal{A}w,w}+\Phi(w)-\dual{f_N^k-\mathcal{A}z_N^{k-1},w},
\]
it is possible to prove that $\Delta z_N^k$ is a solution to~\eqref{eqn:differential-inequality} if and only if it is a minimizer of $\mathcal{J}_N^k$. Now the existence of a minimizer can be proved through the direct method of the calculus of variations using the fact that $\mathcal{J}_N^k$ is strongly lower semicontinuous, coercive in the norm of $Z$ and convex, so that it is weakly lower semicontinuous and has weakly compact sublevels. Thus we have proved the existence of the discrete-in-time solution.

To obtain the continuous-in-time solution, first some a priori estimates are obtained. These are performed in a similar way to what was seen in the proof of the existence theorem in Section~\ref{sec:roubickeck}. That is, the variational inequality is considered at step $k$ and $k-1$ with $w$ respectively $\Delta z_N^{k-1}$ and $\Delta z_N^k$, and the resulting inequalities are added together. Then the result is summed for $k$ form 1 to $N$. This provide a uniform bound on $\norm{z_N}_{L^\infty((0,T),Z)}$ and $\norm{\dot z_N}_{L^2((0,T),Z)}$, where $z_N$ is the piecewise affine interpolation of $\{z_N^k\}_{k=0}^N$. Compactness allows to extract a subsequence such that $z_N\overset{*}{\rightharpoonup}z$ in $L^\infty((0,T),Z)$ and $\dot z_N\rightharpoonup\dot z$ in $L^2((0,T),Z)$. This in turns allows to pass to the limit in the variational inequality~\eqref{eqn:variational-inequality} thus proving that $z$ is a solution.
\end{proof}

\subsection{Application to plasticity theory at small strain}

Theorem~\ref{thm:han-reddy} can be used to prove the existence of solution to the plasticity model at small strain discussed in Section~\ref{sec:plasticity-review} when the hardening free energy $w_p$ is quadratic in $E_p$, the Von Mises constitutive relation for the plastic microforce is assumed. It can be treated in this way both the case in which the dependence on the gradient of the plastic strain is assumed or discarded: we now see the existence theorem in the latter case, the former case is completely analogous.
\begin{thm}
Let $\Omega\subset\mathbb{R}^d$ open, bounded and Lipschitz. Let $Z=\{(u,\pi)\in H^1_0(\Omega,\mathbb{R}^d)\times L^2(\Omega,\text{DevSym})\}$ be the space of configurations of a plastic material in the regime of small deformations, with $u$ the displacement field and $\pi=E_p$ the plastic strain field. Let $f\in H^1((0,T),H^{-1}(\Omega),\mathbb{R}^d)$ with $f(0)=0$ be the volumetric load acting on the body. Let $\Psi\colon Z\to\mathbb{R}$ the free energy of the form
\[
\Psi(z)=\frac{1}{2}\dual{\mathcal{A}z,z}=\int_\Omega\left(\frac{1}{2}\mathfrak{C}(Eu-\pi):(Eu-\pi)+\frac{\mathfrak{b}}{2}|\pi|^2\right)dx,
\]
for some $\mathfrak{C}\in\text{Sym}\otimes\text{Sym}$ symmetric and positive definite and $\mathfrak{b}>0$. Let also the dissipation potential $\Phi\colon Z\to[0,\infty)$ be defined for all $w=(v,\rho)\in Z$ as
\[
\Phi(w)=\int_\Omega Y|\rho|dx.
\]
Then there exists a unique $z\in\text{AC}([0,T],Z)$ such that $z(0)=0$ and $\mathcal{A}z(t)+\partial\Phi(\dot z(t))\ni\mathcal{X}(t)$ for a.e.\ $t\in(0,T)$.
\end{thm}
\begin{proof}
It is enough to check the hypotheses ($\Psi$)  and ($\Phi$) required to apply Theorem~\ref{thm:han-reddy}. We start with the check that $\mathcal{A}$ is coercive. To this aim, notice that
\[
\dual{\mathcal{A}z,z}\ge C_\mathfrak{C}\norm{Eu-\pi}_2^2+\mathfrak{b}\norm{\pi}_2^2\ge C_\mathfrak{C}\norm{Eu}_2^2+C_\mathfrak{C}\norm{\pi}_2^2-2C_\mathfrak{C}\norm{Eu}_2\norm{\pi}_2+\mathfrak{b}\norm{\pi}_2^2,
\]
where $C_\mathfrak{C}>0$ is the smallest eigenvalue of $\mathfrak{C}$. By Young inequality it follows that
\[
\dual{\mathcal{A}z,z}\ge C_\mathfrak{C}\left(1-\frac{1}{c}\right)\norm{Eu}_2^2+\left(b+(1-c)C_\mathfrak{C}\right)\norm{\pi}_2^2\quad\text{for all $c>0$}.
\]
Choosing $1<c<1+\frac{\mathfrak{b}}{C_\mathfrak{C}}$ it follows that $\dual{\mathcal{A}z,z}$ bounds $\norm{Eu}_2^2+\norm{\pi}_2^2$. By Korn inequality there exists $C_K>0$ such that $\norm{Eu}_2\ge C_K\norm{u}_{H^1(\Omega,\mathbb{R}^2)}$ so that the coercivity of $\mathcal{A}$ is established. That $\Phi$ is convex and Lipschitz is trivial, moreover $\Phi(0)=0$. The fact that $\Phi$ is lower semicontinuous follows by Fatou lemma.
\end{proof}

\section{Energetic solutions}
\label{sec:energetic-solution}

In this section we present the notion of energetic solutions following~\cite{francfort,rate-independent-systems}. This is a very flexible notion of solution for rate-independent systems since it does not requires differentiability assumptions of the energy and the dissipation potential and allows to treat also situations in which the evolution may exhibit jumps.

Before setting up the problem and clarifying the assumptions starting from Subsection~\ref{subsec:assumptions-energetic}, in the following subsection we give an introduction that will serve as a motivation of Definition~\ref{def:energetic-solution}.

\subsection{Introduction and motivation}

Let $Z$ be a separable and reflexive Banach space. Let the total energy $\mathcal{E}\colon Z\times[0,T]\to\mathbb{R}$ be FrĂ©chet differentiable and such that $\mathcal{E}(\cdot,t)$ is convex for all $t\in[0,T]$. Let the dissipation potential $\Phi\colon Z\to[0,\infty]$ be convex, lower semicontinuous and positively 1-homogeneous. Consider the doubly nonlinear equation in the form~\eqref{eqn:modeling:doubly-nonlinear}, that is also reported below:
\begin{equation}
\label{eqn:energetic:doubly-nonlinear}
D_z\mathcal{E}(z(t),t)+\partial\Phi(\dot z(t))\ni 0.
\end{equation}
We say that $z\in\text{AC}([0,T],Z)$ is a differentiable solution if~\eqref{eqn:energetic:doubly-nonlinear} holds for a.e.\ $t\in(0,T)$. From Proposition~\ref{prop:prop-pos-1-hom-convx} this is equivalent to
\begin{equation}
\label{eqn:energetic:equivalent-doubly-nonlinear}
-D_z\mathcal{E}(z(t),t)\in\partial\Phi(0)\quad\text{and}\quad\Phi(\dot z(t))=-\dual{D_z\mathcal{E}(z(t),t),\dot z(t)}=-\frac{d}{dt}\mathcal{E}(z(t),t)+\partial_t\mathcal{E}(z(t),t)
\end{equation}
for a.e.\ $t\in(0,T)$. The first condition is the requirement that the conservative forces belong to the admissibility set $\partial\Phi(0)$. From the definition of subdifferential, this is equivalent to
\[
\Phi(\hat z-z(t))\ge\Phi(0)-\dual{D_z\mathcal{E}(z(t),t),\hat z-z(t)}\quad\text{for all $\hat{z}\in Z$}.
\]
Exploiting the convexity of $\mathcal{E}(\cdot,t)$ it follows that
\[
\mathcal{E}(\hat{z},t)-\mathcal{E}(z(t),t)\ge\lim_{\epsilon\to 0}\frac{\mathcal{E}(z(t)+\epsilon(\hat{z}-z(t)),t)-\mathcal{E}(z(t),t)}{\epsilon}=\dual{D_z\mathcal{E}(z(t),t),\hat{z}-z(t)}.
\]
Then the first condition in~\eqref{eqn:energetic:equivalent-doubly-nonlinear}, implies
\begin{equation}
\label{eqn:energetic:stability-motivation}
\mathcal{E}(z(t),t)\le\mathcal{E}(\hat{z},t)+\Phi(\hat{z}-z(t))\quad\text{for all $\hat{z}\in Z$},
\end{equation}
where we used that $\Phi(0)=0$ by Proposition~\ref{prop:prop-pos-1-hom-convx}. Since $\Phi$ is 1-homogeneous, also the inverse implication holds, indeed
\[
\dual{D_z\mathcal{E}(z(t),t),\hat{z}-z(t)}=\lim_{\epsilon\to 0}\frac{\mathcal{E}(z(t)+\epsilon(\hat{z}-z(t)),t)-\mathcal{E}(z(t),t)}{\epsilon}\ge\frac{\Phi(\epsilon(\hat{z}-z(t))}{\epsilon}=\Phi(\hat{z}-z(t)).
\]
The mechanical meaning of condition~\eqref{eqn:energetic:stability-motivation} is the following: the energy $\mathcal{E}(z(t),t)-\mathcal{E}(\hat{z},t)$ released by the system while passing from $z(t)$ to $\hat{z}$ cannot exceed the dissipation $\Phi(\hat{z}-z(t))$. This is a stability condition. The second condition in~\eqref{eqn:energetic:equivalent-doubly-nonlinear} is the power balance. Integrating it on the interval $(0,t)$ for $t\in(0,T]$ we get the energy balance
\[
\mathcal{E}(z(t),t)=\mathcal{E}(z(0),0)+\int_0^t\partial_t\mathcal{E}(z(s),s)ds+\int_0^t\Phi(\dot z(s))ds.
\]
The first integral is the work of external forces, while the second integral is the dissipation. The following lemma gives an alternative way to express the dissipation.
\begin{lemma}
For all $0\le s<t\le T$ let
\[
\text{Diss}_\Phi(z,[s,t])=\inf\left\{\sum_{k=1}^N\Phi(z(t_k)-z(t_{k-1}))\;\middle|\;s=t_0<\dots<t_N=t\right\}.
\]
Then
\[
\text{Diss}_\Phi(z,[s,t])=\int_s^t\Phi(\dot z(r))dr.
\]
\end{lemma}
\begin{proof}
Consider any subdivision $\{t_k\}_{k=0}^N$ of $[s,t]$. By Jensen inequality and in view of the 1-homogeneity of $\Phi$, it holds that
\[
\Phi(z(t_k)-z(t_{k-1}))=\Phi\left((t_k-t_{k-1})\frac{1}{t_k-t_{k-1}}\int_{t_{k-1}}^{t^k}\dot z(r)dr\right)\le\int_{t_{k-1}}^{t_k}\Phi(\dot z(r))dr\quad\text{for all $k\in\{0,\dots,N\}$}.
\]
Then the inequality
\[
\text{Diss}_\Phi(z,[s,t])\le\int_s^t\Phi(\dot z(r))dr
\]
holds. To prove the other inequality we split the interval $[s,t]$ into intervals
\[
I_k^N=\left[s+\frac{k-1}{N}(t-s),s+\frac{k}{N}(t-s)\right]
\]
for $k\in\{1,\dots,N\}$ with $N\in\mathbb{N}$. Then, by lower semicontinuity of $\Phi$ and by the Fatou's lemma,
\[
\begin{split}
\int_s^{t}\Phi(\dot z(r))dr&\le\int_s^t\liminf_{N\to\infty}\frac{N}{t-s}\Phi\left(z\left(r+\frac{t-s}{N}\right)-z(r)\right)dr\le\\
&\le\liminf_{N\to\infty}\frac{N}{t-s}\int_s^{t-\frac{t-s}{N}}\Phi\left(z\left(r+\frac{t-s}{N}\right)-z(r)\right)dr.
\end{split}
\]
Now
\[
\begin{split}
\int_s^{t-\frac{t-s}{N}}\Phi\left(z\left(r+\frac{t-s}{N}\right)-z(r)\right)dr&=\sum_{k=1}^{N-1}\int_0^{\frac{t-s}{N}}\Phi\left(z\left(\frac{k}{N}(t-s)+r\right)-z\left(\frac{k-1}{N}(t-s)+r\right)\right)dr\le\\
&\le\int_0^{\frac{t-s}{N}}\sum_{k=1}^{N-1}\Phi\left(z\left(\frac{k}{N}(t-s)+r\right)-z\left(\frac{k-1}{N}(t-s)+r\right)\right)dr\le\\
&\le\frac{t-s}{N}\text{Diss}_\Phi(z,[s,t]).
\end{split}
\]
We have proved that
\[
\int_s^{t}\Phi(\dot z(r))dr\le\text{Diss}_\Phi(z,[s,t])
\]
as desired.
\end{proof}
Summarizing, we have seen that~\eqref{eqn:energetic:doubly-nonlinear} is equivalent to
\begin{subequations}
\label{eqn:energetic:motivation-definition}
\begin{gather}
\mathcal{E}(z(t),t)\le\mathcal{E}(\hat{z},t)+\Phi(\hat{z}-z(t))\quad\text{for all $\hat{z}\in Z$ and all $t\in[0,T]$},\\
\mathcal{E}(z(t),t)=\mathcal{E}(z(0),0)+\int_0^t\partial_t\mathcal{E}(z(s),s)ds+\text{Diss}_\Phi(z,[0,t])\quad\text{for all $t\in[0,T]$}.
\end{gather}
\end{subequations}
This conditions makes sense also if $\mathcal{E}$ is not FrĂ©chet differentiable and $z$ is not absolutely continuous.

This results suggest to define energetic solutions directly as evolutions $z\colon[0,T]\to Z$ satisfying~\eqref{eqn:energetic:motivation-definition} also when less regularity is assumed. We now explore further this possibility. Let $Z$ be a separable and reflexive Banach space and $\mathcal{E}\colon Z\times[0,T]\to[0,\infty]$. Rather than using a dissipation potential, we describe the system's dissipation in terms of a dissipation distance. This is defined as a map $\mathcal D\colon Z\times Z\to[0,\infty]$, where $\mathcal D(z_0,z_1)$ denotes the minimum energy dissipated by the system when it jumps from $z_0$ to $z_1$. The dissipation along an evolution $z\colon[0,T]\to Z$ in the time interval $[s,t]$ is defined as
\[
\text{Diss}_\mathcal{D}(z,[s,t])=\sup\left\{\sum_{k=1}^N\mathcal{D}(z(t_{k-1}),z(t_k))\;\middle|\;s=t_0<\dots<t_N=t\right\}.
\]
Then we say that $z$ is an energetic solution if
\begin{subequations}
\begin{gather}
\mathcal{E}(z(t),t)\le\mathcal{E}(\hat{z},t)+\mathcal{D}(z(t),\hat{z})\quad\text{for all $\hat{z}\in Z$ and all $t\in[0,T]$},\\
\mathcal{E}(z(t),t)=\mathcal{E}(z(0),0)+\int_0^t\partial_t\mathcal{E}(z(s),s)ds+\text{Diss}_\mathcal{D}(z,[0,t])\quad\text{for all $t\in[0,T]$}.
\end{gather}
\end{subequations}
Definition~\ref{def:energetic-solution} will make this more precise.

\begin{rmk}(Choice of the dissipation distance)
\label{rmk:minimum-dissipation}
Given a dissipation potential $\Phi\colon Z\times Z\to[0,\infty],\;(z,\dot z)\mapsto\Phi(z,\dot z)$, such that $\Phi(z,\cdot)$ is positively 1-homogeneous for all $z\in Z$, we can define the dissipation distance as
\begin{equation}
\label{eqn:energetic:dissipation-distance-definition}
\mathcal{D}(z_0,z_1)=\inf\left\{\int_0^1\Phi(z(t),\dot{z}(t))dt\;\middle|\;\text{$z\in C^1([0,T],Z)$, $z(0)=z_0$ and $z(1)=z_1$}\right\}.
\end{equation}
Notice that the range of the integral can be choosen arbitrary due to the positive 1-homogeneity of $\Phi$. The particular choice~\eqref{eqn:energetic:dissipation-distance-definition} of $\mathcal{D}$ is motivated by the following \emph{formal} consideration. Let $z\colon[0,T]\to Z$ be an energetic solution. Fix $t\in[0,T]$, $w\in Z$, $\epsilon>0$ and let $\tilde z(r)=z(t)+r\epsilon w$. Then
\[
\mathcal{E}(z(t),t)\le\mathcal{E}(z(t)+\epsilon w,t)+\mathcal{D}(z(t),z(t)+\epsilon w)\le\mathcal{E}(z(t)+\epsilon w,t)+\int_0^1\Phi(\tilde z(r),\dot{\tilde z}(r))dr
\]
so that
\[
-\frac{\mathcal{E}(z(t)+\epsilon w,t)-\mathcal{E}(z(t),t)}{\epsilon}\le\frac{1}{\epsilon}\int_0^1\Phi(z(t)+r\epsilon w,\epsilon w)dr=\int_0^1\Phi(z(t)+r\epsilon w,w)dr.
\]
Assuming enough regularity, we let $\epsilon\to 0$ and we get
\[
-\dual{D_z\mathcal{E}(z(t),t),w}\le\Phi(z(t),w).
\]
This means that $-D_z\mathcal{E}(z(t),t)\in\partial_w\Phi(z(t),\cdot)(0)$. Differentiating the energy balance
\[
-D_z\mathcal{E}(z(t),t)=\Phi(z(t),\dot{z}(t)).
\]
This means that $-D_z\mathcal{E}(z(t),t)\in\partial\Phi(z(t),\cdot)(z(t))$. Thus~\eqref{eqn:energetic:dissipation-distance-definition} formally implies that smooth energetic solutions are also solutions to the original doubly nonlinear equation~\ref{eqn:modeling:doubly-nonlinear}.
\end{rmk}

\subsection{Assumptions, preliminary results and definition of energetic solutions}
\label{subsec:assumptions-energetic}

We now introduce the assumption that are required to well formulate the notion of energetic solution. We assume that the space of configuration is of the form $Z=Y\times Q$ with
\begin{itemize}[itemsep=0.5em]
\item[(Z1)] $Y$ and $Q$ are separable and reflexive Banach spaces,
\item[(Z2)] $\mathscr{Y}$ and $\mathscr{Q}$ weakly closed subsets of $Y$ and $Q$ respectively.
\end{itemize}
We also set $\mathscr{Z}=\mathscr{Y}\times\mathscr{Q}$. The assumptions on the energy functional are that
\begin{itemize}[itemsep=0.5em]
\item[($\mathcal{E}$1)] $\mathcal{E}\colon\mathscr{Z}\times[0,T]\to(-\infty,\infty]$,
\item[($\mathcal{E}$2)] for all $t\in[0,T]$ and $E\in\mathbb{R}$ the set $\mathscr{D}_E:=\{z\in\mathscr{Z}\;|\;\mathcal{E}(z,t)\le E\}$ is bounded and weakly closed
\item[($\mathcal{E}$3)] there exists $\mathscr{D}\subset\mathscr{Z}$ such that $\dom\mathcal{E}(\cdot,t)=\mathscr{D}$ for all $t\in[0,T]$,
\item[($\mathcal{E}$4)] for all $z\in\mathscr{D}$, the function $\mathcal{E}(z,\cdot)$ is $C^1([0,T])$ and
\begin{itemize}
\item[(i)] there exist $C_1^E,C_0^E\in\mathbb{R}$ such that $|\partial_t\mathcal{E}(z,t)|\le C_1^E(C_0^E+\mathcal{E}(z,t))$ for all $z\in\mathscr{Z}$ and $t\in[0,T]$,
\item[(ii)] for each $E\in\mathbb{R}$ there exists a modulus of continuity $\omega^E$ such that for all $z\in\mathscr{D}_E$ and $t,s\in[0,T]$
\[
|\partial_t\mathcal{E}(z,t)-\partial_t\mathcal{E}(z,s)|\le\omega^E(|t-s|).
\]
\end{itemize}
\end{itemize}
Notice that~($\mathcal{E}$2) is equivalent to say that $\mathcal{E}$ is weakly lower semicontinuous and has bounded sublevels. Moreover~($\mathcal{E}$4, i) implies
\begin{equation}
\label{eqn:ES-gronwall}
\parbox[c]{0.8\textwidth}{$\mathcal{E}(z,t_0)+C_0^E\le e^{C_1^E|t_1-t_0|}\left(\mathcal{E}(z,t_1)+C_0^E\right)$ for all $z\in\mathscr{D}$ and $t_0,t_1\in[0,T]$.}
\end{equation}
This follows from the following lemma.
\begin{lemma}
Let $f\in C^1([0,T])$ and there exists $a\in\mathbb{R}$ and $b>0$ such that
$|\dot{f}(t)|\le b(f(t)+b)$ for all $t\in[0,T]$. Then for all $t_0,t_1\in[0,T]$ it holds $f(t_0)+a\le e^{b|t_1-t_0|}(f(t_1)+a)$.
\end{lemma}
\begin{proof}
Let $z(t)=f(t)+a$. Then $|\dot z|\le bz$. It follows that $\dot z\le bz$ and
\[
z(t)\le z(t_0)+\int_{t_0}^{t}b z(s)ds\quad\text{for all $0\le t_0\le t_1\le T$}.
\]
By Proposition~\ref{prop:gronwall} it follows that $z(t_1)\le e^{b(t_1-t_0)}z(t_0)$ all for all $0\le t_0\le t_1\le T$. But it holds also $\dot z\le -bz$. If we set $\zeta(t)=z(T-t)$ then $\dot\zeta\le b\zeta$. Then reasoning as before we get $z(t_1)\le\zeta(T-t_1)\le e^{b(t_0-t_1)}\zeta(T-t_0)\le e^{b(t_0-t_1)}z(t_0)$ for all $0\le t_1\le t_0\le T$. This concludes the proof.
\end{proof}
The assumptions on the dissipation distance are
\begin{itemize}[itemsep=0.5em]
\item[($\mathcal{D}$1)] $\mathcal{D}\colon\mathscr{Q}\times\mathscr{Q}\to[0,\infty]$ is a quasidistance, that is $\mathcal{D}(q_0,q_1)=0$ if and only if $q_0=q_1$ and $\mathcal{D}(q_0,q_2)\le\mathcal{D}(q_0,q_1)+\mathcal{D}(q_1,q_2)$ for all $q_0,q_1,q_2\in\mathscr{Q}$,
\item[($\mathcal{D}$2)] $\mathcal{D}$ is weakly lower semicontinuous, meaning that $q_n\rightharpoonup q$ and $\hat{q}_n\rightharpoonup\hat{q}$ implies $\mathcal{D}(q,\hat{q})\le\liminf_{n\to\infty}\mathcal{D}(q_n,\hat{q}_n)$.
\end{itemize}
Given $z_1=(y_1,q_1)$ and $z_2=(y_2,q_2)$ in $\mathscr{Z}$, sometimes we write $\mathcal{D}(z_1,z_2)$ to mean $\mathcal{D}(q_1,q_2)$.
\begin{defi}
A sequence $(z_n,t_n)\in\mathscr{Z}\times[0,T]$ is said to be stable if
\[
\sup_n\mathcal{E}(z_n,t_n)\le\infty\quad\text{and}\quad z_n\in\mathscr{S}(t_n)
\]
where the stable set is defined by
\[
\mathscr{S}(t)=\{z\in\mathscr{Z}\;|\;\text{$\mathcal{E}(z,t)<\infty$ and $\mathcal{E}(z,t)\le\mathcal{E}(\hat{z},t)+\mathcal{D}(z,\hat{z})$ for all $\hat{z}\in\mathscr{Z}$}\}
\]
\end{defi}
We add the following assumptions regarding the stable set
\begin{itemize}[itemsep=0.5em]
\item[(C1)] for all stable sequences $(z_n,t_n)\in\mathscr{Z}\times[0,T]$ such that $z_n\rightharpoonup z$ and $t_n\to t$, it holds that $\partial_t\mathcal{E}(z_n,t)\to\partial_t\mathcal{E}(z,t)$,
\item[(C2)] the stable sets are closed in the sense that, if $(z_n,t_n)\in\mathscr{Z}\times[0,T]$ is a stable sequence such that $z_n\rightharpoonup z$ and $t_n\to t$, then $z\in\mathscr{S}(t)$.
\end{itemize}
We are now ready to give the definition of energetic solutions.
\begin{defi}
\label{def:energetic-solution}
A map $z\colon[0,T]\to\mathscr{Z}$ is called energetic solution if it is stable at every instant, that is
\begin{equation}
\text{$z(t)\in\mathscr{S}(t)$ for all $t\in[0,T]$},
\end{equation}
if $t\mapsto\partial_t\mathcal{E}(z(t),t)$ belongs to $L^1((0,T))$ and the energy identity
\[
\mathcal{E}(z(t),t)+\text{Diss}_{\mathcal{D}}(q,[s,t])=\mathcal{E}(z(0),0)+\int_0^t\partial_t\mathcal{E}(z(s),s)ds
\]
holds for all $t\in[0,T]$. Here the dissipation is defined as
\[
\text{Diss}_\mathcal{D}(q,[s,t])=\sup\left\{\sum_{k=1}^N\mathcal{D}(q(t_{k-1}),q(t_k))\;\middle|\;s=t_0<\dots<t_N=t\right\}
\]
for all $0\le s<t\le T$.
\end{defi}

\subsection{Existence theorem}

The existence of energetic solutions is established by the following theorem. The complete proof can be found in~\cite{francfort}.
\begin{thm}(existence of energetic solution)
\label{thm:existence-energetic-solutions}
If the hypotheses (Z), ($\mathcal{E}$), ($\mathcal{D}$) and (C) holds, then for all $z_0\in\mathscr{S}(0)$ there exists a bounded energetic solutions $z$ such that $z(0)=z_0$.
\end{thm}
%\begin{proof}[Sketch of the proof]
%The proof is based on a time-discretization approach. So first a discrete-in-time solution is built, then some a priori estimates are performed, then in the limit of vanishing time step a convergent subsequence is extracted. Eventually the limit evolutions is proved to be an energetic solution.
%
%\textit{Construction of the discrete-in-time solution.} Fix $N\in\mathbb{N}$ and let $\tau_N=T/N$ and $t_N^k=k\tau_N$ for $k\in\{0,\dots,N\}$. We consider the problem to find $\{z_N^k\}_{k=0}^N\subset\mathscr{L}(Z)$ such that $z_N^0=z_0$ and
%\[
%z_N^k\in\argmin_{z\in\mathscr{Z}}(\mathcal{E}(z,t_N^k)+\mathcal{D}(z_N^{k-1},z))\quad\text{for $k\in\{0,\dots,N\}$.}
%\]
%The existence to this problem follows immediately from application of the direct method of the calculus of variations: let $\{z_n\}_{n\in\mathbb{N}}\subset\mathscr{Z}$ be a minimizing sequence: it belongs to a sublevel of $\mathcal{E}(\cdot,t_N^k)+\mathcal{D}(z_N^{k-1},\cdot)$ so it is bounded; by reflexivity of $Z$, up to a extracting a subsequence it converges weakly to some $z\in\mathscr{Z}$; then using the fact that both $\mathcal{D}(\cdot,t_N^k)$ and $\mathcal{D}(z_N^{k-1},\cdot)$ are weakly lower semicontinuous it follows indeed that $z$ is a minimizer. In this argument we use that fact that $z_0$ is table to prove inductively that $\mathcal{E}(\cdot,t_N^k)+\mathcal{D}(z_N^{k-1},\cdot)$ is not everywhere infinite. It is easy to prove that the discrete solutions are stable, in the sense that $z_N^k\in\mathscr{S}(t_N^k)$ for all $k\in\{0,\dots,N\}$. Indeed by construction we know that
%\[
%\mathcal{E}(z_N^k,t_N^k)+\mathcal{D}(z_N^{k-1},z_N^k)\le\mathcal{E}(\hat z,t_N^k)+\mathcal{D}(z_N^{k-1},\hat z)\quad\text{for all $\hat z\in\mathscr{Z}$}.
%\]
%By the triangle inequality it follows that
%\[
%\mathcal{D}(z_N^{k-1},\hat z)\le\mathcal{D}(z_N^{k-1},z_N^k)+\mathcal{D}(z_N^k,\hat z).
%\]
%Then
%\[
%\mathcal{E}(z_N^k,t_N^k)\le\mathcal{E}(\hat z,t_N^k)+\mathcal{D}(z_N^k,\hat z)\quad\text{for all $\hat z\in\mathscr{Z}$}.
%\]
%This means exactly that $z_N^k\in\mathscr{S}(t_N^k)$.
%
%\textit{A priori estimates on the discrete solutions.} 
%\end{proof}

\subsection{Tool to verify the compatibility conditions}

The proposition below is useful to verify the compatibility conditions (C) and will turn to be useful in the proof of the existence theorem of Chapter~\ref{chap:large-neck}. 
\begin{prop}
\label{prop:tool-verify-compatibility}
Assume that the following property holds
\begin{itemize}
\item[(PC1)] for all stable sequence $\{(z_k,t_k)\}_{k\in\mathbb{N}}\subset\mathscr{Z}$ such that $(z_k,t_k)\rightharpoonup(z,t)$ and for all $\hat{z}\in\mathscr{Z}$, there exist a sequence $\{\hat{z}_k\}_{k\in\mathbb{N}}\subset\mathscr{Z}$ such that
\[
\limsup_{k\to\infty}\left(\mathcal{E}(\hat{z}_k,t_k)+\mathcal{D}(q_k,\hat{q}_k)\right)\le\mathcal{E}(\hat{z},t)+\mathcal{D}(q,\hat{q}).
\]
\end{itemize}
Then for all stable sequence $\{(z_k,t_k)\}_{k\in\mathbb{Z}}\subset\mathscr{Z}$ such that $(z_k,t_k)\rightharpoonup(z,t)$ it holds that $\lim_{k\to\infty}\mathcal{E}(z_k,t_k)=\mathcal{E}(z,t)$. Moreover (C2) holds.

Assume that, in addiction to (PC1), also the following property holds
\begin{itemize}
\item[(PC2)] for all $\epsilon>0$ and $E\in\mathbb{R}$, there exists $\delta>0$ such that for all $t,s\in[0,T]$ and $z\in\mathscr{Z}$ the implication
\begin{equation}
\text{$\mathcal{E}(z,t)\le E$ and $|t-s|<\delta$ implies $|\partial_t\mathcal{E}(z,t)-\partial_t\mathcal{E}(z,s)|<\epsilon$}
\end{equation}
holds.
\end{itemize}
Then (C1) holds.
\end{prop}
\begin{proof}
See~\cite{francfort}.
\end{proof}

\input{balanced-viscosity.tex}

\part{One dimensional model of a plastic cylindrical bar}
\label{part:his-model}

\chapter{Introduction}
\label{chap:intro-neck}

In a MĂ©moire of 1885~\cite{Considere1885}, the French engineer M. ConsidĂ¨re collected his studies on the experimental tests he carried out on the mechanical behavior of structural metals, whose use as building materials had become widespread. Part of his study is dedicated to the tensile test of cylindrical samples: a cylindrical bar is placed under tension by controlling its elongation and the axial force to which it is subjected is measured. The experimental data reported by ConsidĂ¨re are shown in Figure~\ref{fig:considere}.
\begin{figure}[h]
\begin{tikzpicture}
\begin{axis}[xlabel={$\eta=l/L$},ylabel={$F$ [kg]},xmin=1,xmax=1.33,ymin=400]
\addplot[color=black,thick,mark=*] 
table[x=eta,y=F] {figures/necking-experimental.dat};
\end{axis}
\end{tikzpicture}
\caption{Trend of the tensile force $F$ as the elongation $\eta=l/L$ varies (where $L$ is the initial length and $l$ the length in the deformed configuration) taken from the experimental data in~\cite{Considere1885}. The sample is made of soft iron and has a length $L=201$ mm and a diameter of 16 mm in the initial configuration.}
\label{fig:considere}
\end{figure}
In the first phase of the test, the behavior of the specimen is elastic (the steep part of the graph in the figure). Subsequently the yield point is reached and at first the axial force increases as the deformation increases. This behavior is known as \emph{hardening} and is due the increase in the internal resistance of the material to yield as the plastic deformation accumulates. The hardening effect competes with the shrinkage of the specimen section which tends to weaken the material, since the axial force is distributed over an increasingly smaller section. This leads to a decrease in the slope in the curve in the figure, until the force begins to decrease when the geometric effect of the decrease in section prevails over hardening. ConsidĂ¨re observed that reaching the maximum tensile force corresponds approximately to the moment in which the bar, which had initially deformed uniformly along its entire length, begins to shrink more at a localized point, which experimentally is a priori undetermined, depending on the imperfections of the specimen. This phenomenon, which was already known before ConsidĂ¨re studies although it had not yet been characterized quantitatively, is known as \emph{necking}. The criterion that links the start of the necking to the achievement of the maximum traction force is now known as \emph{ConsidĂ¨re criterion}. ConsidĂ¨re experimentally observed that the length of the region affected by the neck is independent of the length of the bar but increases with the diameter.

%I primi contributi verso la comprensione teorica del fenomeno del necking nell'ambito della teoria della plasticitĂ  risalgono alla seconda metĂ  del Novecento, quando Ă¨ il fenomeno Ă¨ stato studiato come manifestazione della perdita di stabilitĂ  della \emph{soluzione triaviale}, cioĂ¨ quella in cui la barra rimane in uno stato di deformazione uniforme. In un lavoro~\cite{Hill1958} del 1958, R. Hill ha sviluppato una teoria generale per trattare i fenomeni di biforcazione e di perdita di stabilitĂ  nell'ambito della teoria della plasticitĂ  incrementale. La teoria della plasticitĂ  incrementale era il framework utilizzato prima che venisse introdotta, negli anni Settanta, la moderna teoria della plasticitĂ  basata su solide base variazionali, quella che abbiamo discusso nei capitoli introduttivi~\ref{}. Nella teoria della plasticitĂ  incrementale la relazione costitutiva lega il rate dello stress al rate dello strain, cosicchĂ© il \emph{problema incrementale} di determinare ad ogni istante il campo velocitĂ  del solido elastoplastico emerge naturalmente. La teoria della biforcazione di Hill riguarda l'unicitĂ  della soluzione di tale problema incrementale. Utilizzando la teoria della biforcazione di Hill, negli anni Settanta in~\cite{HillHutchinson1975,HutchinsonMiles1974}, viene dimostrato che la perdita di stabilita della soluzione uniforme avviene poco dopo che la forza di trazione raggiunge il suo massimo, in accordo con il criterio di ConsidĂ¨re. Nello stesso periodo viene effettuata anche la prima simulazione numerica mediante gli elementi finiti dell'occorenza del fenomeno del necking~\cite{Needleman1972} permettendo per la prima volta di predire accuratemante la forma del neck.
%
%In questa parte della tesi ci proponiamo di studiare il fenomeno del necking nellâ€™ambito della moderna teoria matematica della plasticitĂ . Lâ€™obiettivo finale sarebbe quello di ottenere risultati analoghi a quelli ottenuti nell'ambito della plasticitĂ  incrementale, ma inseriti nel quadro concettuale piĂą coerente della teoria plasticitĂ  che abbiamo descritto nel Capitolo~\ref{}. In questo contesto vorremo studiare il necking, non come una perdita di unicitĂ  del problema incrementale, ma piuttosto come una instabilitĂ  energetica della soluzione uniforme, che cessa di essere globalmente (o localmente) minimizzante e viene sostituita da una configurazione localizzata energeticamente favorita.

The first contributions towards the theoretical understanding of the necking phenomenon in the context of the theory of plasticity date back to the second half of the twentieth century, when the phenomenon was studied as a manifestation of the loss of stability of the \emph{triavial solution}, i.e. the one in which the bar remains in a state of uniform deformation. In a 1958 work~\cite{Hill1958}, R. Hill developed a general theory to deal with the phenomena of bifurcation and loss of stability within the theory of incremental plasticity. The theory of incremental plasticity was the framework used before the modern theory of plasticity based on solid variational foundations, the one we discussed in Section~\ref{sec:plasticity-review} of Chapter~\ref{chap:modeling}, was introduced in the 1960s. In the theory of incremental plasticity the constitutive relation links the stress rate to the strain rate, so that the \emph{incremental problem} of determining the velocity field of the elastoplastic solid at any instant emerges naturally. Hill's bifurcation theory concerns the uniqueness of the solution to such an incremental problem. Using Hill's bifurcation theory, in the 1970s~\cite{HillHutchinson1975,HutchinsonMiles1974} it was demonstrated that the loss of stability of the uniform solution occurs shortly after the tensile force reaches its maximum, in agreement with the ConsidĂ¨re criterion. In the same period, the first numerical simulation using finite elements of the occurrence of the necking phenomenon was also carried out, allowing for the first time to accurately predict the shape of the neck~\cite{Needleman1972}.

In this part of the thesis we aim to study the necking phenomenon in the context of the modern mathematical theory of plasticity. The final objective would be to obtain results similar to those obtained in the context of incremental plasticity, but inserted into the more elegant and mathematically robust conceptual framework of modern plasticity theory. In this context we would like to study necking, not as a loss of uniqueness of the incremental problem, but rather as an energetic instability of the uniform solution that leads to energetically favored localized configuration.

%Con la speranza di poter ottenere risultati quanto piĂą espliciti e analiticamente trattabili, abbiamo sviluppato un modello semplificato per descrivere la barra metallica, ottenuto mediante una riduzione dimensionale della teoria della plasticitĂ  tridimensionale completa. Lâ€™idea Ă¨ di conservare gli ingredienti essenziali della formulazione variazionale moderna, eliminando al tempo stesso la complessitĂ  geometrica del problema completo. L'approccio utilizzato Ă¨ ispirato alla teoria delle rod di Antman~\cite{antman} e all'approccio unidimensionale al necking discusso in~\cite{AudolyHutchinson2020}, che tuttavia si limitano alla sola teoria dell'elasticitĂ . Assumiamo pertanto che ogni configurazione della barra possa essere descritta attraverso tre soli campi scalari definiti lungo la lunghezza della barra: la posizione assiale media delle sezioni, che rappresenta la cinematica longitudinale; un parametro di dilatazione che quantifica lâ€™allargamento o il restringimento della sezione trasversale; e infine un terzo parametro scalare che rappresenta la distorsione plastica assiale media.

With the hope of being able to obtain the most explicit and analytically tractable results, we have developed a simplified model to describe the metal bar, obtained through a dimensional reduction of the complete three-dimensional plasticity theory. The idea is to retain the essential ingredients of the modern variational formulation, while eliminating the geometric complexity of the complete problem. The approach used is inspired by Antman's rod theory~\cite{antman} and the one-dimensional approach to necking discussed in~\cite{AudolyHutchinson2020}, which however are limited to elasticity theory alone. We therefore assume that each configuration of the bar can be described through only three scalar fields defined along the length of the bar: the average axial position of the sections, which represents the longitudinal kinematics; a dilation parameter that quantifies the widening or narrowing of the cross section; and finally a third scalar parameter representing the average axial plastic distortion.

%Nel Capitolo~\ref{chap:small-neck} formuliamo una versione del modello valida nel regime di piccole deformazioni. La buon positura del problema ai valori iniziali e al contorno prodotta da questo modello Ă¨ stabilita facendo ricorso alla teoria generale di esistenza per le disuguaglianza variazionali evolutive presentata nella Sezione~\ref{sec:evi} del Capitolo~\ref{chap:solution-concepts}. Quindi passiamo allo studio del problema ai valori iniziali e al contorno associato alla prova di trazione. Come Ă¨ facile aspettarsi questo modello non Ă¨ in grado di catturare l'insorgere del necking, che come abbiamo discusso Ă¨ un fenomeno di instabilitĂ  intrinsecamente dovuto alla nonlinearitĂ  indotta dalle grandi deformazioni, e lo mostriamo determinando esplicitamente la soluzione. Nel Capitolo~\ref{chap:large-neck} raffiniamo il modello affinchĂ© possa descrivere il comportamento della barra anche a grandi deformazioni. L'esistenza di soluzioni al problema evolutivo che ne deriva Ă¨ studiata mediante la nozione di soluzioni energetiche descritte nella Sezione~\ref{sec:energetic-solution} del Capitolo~\ref{chap:solution-concepts}. Quindi affrontiamo lo studio della stabilitĂ  della soluzione triviale utilizzando la nozione di stabilitĂ  globale fornita dalla definizione stessa di soluzione energetica. Se tralasciamo l'effetto della plasticitĂ  riducendosi al caso puramente elastico riusciamo ad ottenere un risultato esplicito che indica che la soluzione in cui la barra si deforma uniformemente perde di stabilitĂ  dopo che la la forza di tensione raggiunge il suo massimo. Nel caso plastico non riusciamo ad ottenere un risultato altrettanto esplicito. Proporremo alcune considerazioni euristiche che suggeriscono che l'instabilitĂ  della soluzione triviale avviene anche in questo caso dopo che forza raggiunge il suo massimo, in accordo con il criterio di ConsidĂ©re.
%
%La nostra analisi non puĂ˛ essere considerata conclusiva dal momento che non Ă¨ ancora in grado di mostrare in modo completo e rigoroso l'occorrenza del necking, nĂ¨ tantomeno Ă¨ capace di descrivere qualitativamente le proprietĂ  salienti del comportamento della barra a seguito dell'innesco dell'instabilitĂ .

In Chapter~\ref{chap:small-neck} we formulate a version of the model valid in the regime of small deformations. The existence and uniqueness of solutions to the initial boundary value problem produced by this model is established by resorting to the general theory of existence for evolutionary variational inequalities presented in Section~\ref{sec:evi} of Chapter~\ref{chap:solution-concepts}. We then move on to study the initial boundary value problem associated with the tensile test. As one might expect, this model is not able to capture the onset of necking, which as we have discussed is an instability phenomenon intrinsically due to the nonlinearity induced by large deformations, and we show this by explicitly determining the solution. In Chapter~\ref{chap:large-neck} we refine the model so that it can describe the behavior of the bar at large deformations. The existence of solutions to the resulting evolutionary problem is studied through the notion of energy solutions described in Section~\ref{sec:energetic-solution} of Chapter~\ref{chap:solution-concepts}. We then approach the study of the stability of the trivial solution using the notion of global stability provided by the definition of energy solution itself. If we ignore the effect of plasticity by reducing ourselves to the purely elastic case we are able to obtain an explicit result which indicates that the solution in which the bar deforms uniformly loses stability after the tension force reaches its maximum. In the plastic case we are unable to obtain an equally explicit result. We will propose some heuristic considerations which suggest that the instability of the trivial solution also occurs in this case after the force reaches its maximum, in accordance with the ConsidĂ©re criterion.

Our analysis cannot be considered conclusive since it is not yet able to show the occurrence of necking in a complete and rigorous way, nor is it capable of qualitatively describing the salient properties of the behavior of the bar following the triggering of the instability. We hope that a deeper analysis of this simplified oneâ€‘dimensional model will provide clearer insight into the mechanisms that govern the onset and evolution of necking.

%Nel MĂ©moire del 1885~\cite{Considere1885}, l'ingegnere fracese M. ConsidĂ¨re raccolse i suoi studi sulle prove sperimentali da lui effettuate sul comportamento meccanico dei metalli strutturali, il cui utilizzo come materiale da costruzione si era ormai ampiamente diffuso. Una parte del suo studio Ă¨ dedicata alla prova di trazione di campioni cilindrici: una barra cilindrica viene posta in trazione controllandone l'allungamento e viene misurata la forza assiale a cui essa Ă¨ soggetta. I dati sperimentali riportati da ConsidĂ©re sono mostrati in Figura~\ref{fig:considere}.
%\begin{figure}[h]
%\begin{tikzpicture}
%\begin{axis}[xlabel={$\eta=l/L$},ylabel={$F$ [kg]},xmin=1,xmax=1.33,ymin=400]
%\addplot[color=black,thick,mark=*] 
%table[x=eta,y=F] {figures/necking-experimental.dat};
%\end{axis}
%\end{tikzpicture}
%\caption{Andamento della forza di trazione $F$ al variare dell'allungamento $\eta=l/L$ (dove $L$ Ă¨ la lunghezza iniziale e $l$ la lunghezza nella configurazione deformata) tratta dai dati sperimentali in~\cite{Considere1885}. Il campione ha lunghezza $L=201$ mm e un diametro di 16 mm nella configurazione iniziale, il materiale Ă¨ ferro dolce.}
%\label{fig:considere}
%\end{figure}
%Nella prima fase della prova il comportamento del provino Ă¨ elastico (il tratto che appare ripido in figura). Successivamente viene raggiunto il limite di snervamento e in un primo momento la forza assiale cresce all'aumentare della deformazione. Questo comportamento Ă¨ noto come \emph{incrudimento} ovvero l'aumento della resistenza interna del materiale allo snervamento man mano che la deformazione plastica si accumula. L'effetto incrudimento Ă¨ in competizione con il restringimento della sezione del provino che tende invece ad indebolire il materiale, dal momento che la forza assiale viene distribuita su una sezione sempre piĂą piccola. Questo porta alla diminuzione della pendenza nella curva in figura, finchĂ© la forza non incomincia a diminuire quando l'effetto geometrico della diminuzione della sezione prevale sull'incrudimento. ConsidĂ¨re osservĂ˛ che il raggiungimento del massimo della forza di trazione corrisponde approssimativamente al momento in cui la barra, che inizialmente si era deformata in modo uniforme su tutta la sua lunghezza, inizia a restringersi maggiormente in un punto localizzato. Questo fenomeno, che era giĂ  conosciuto in passato sebbene non fosse stato ancora caratterizzato in modo quantitativo, Ă¨ noto come \emph{strizione}. Il criterio che lega l'inzio della strizione al raggiungimento del massimo della forza di trazione Ă¨ ora conosciuto appunto come \emph{criterio di ConsidĂ¨re}. Sperimentalmente si osserva che la lunghezza della zona interessata dalla strizione Ă¨ indipendente dalla lunghezza della barra ma cresce con l'aumentare del diametro.

\chapter{Model at small deformations}
\label{chap:small-neck}

In this chapter we develop and analyze a version of the one dimensional model aiming to describe the behavior of the metallic bar under tension under the assumption of small deformations. We split our discussion into three sections. The first section is devoted to the development of the model, the second to an existence and uniqueness results. The last section treats the explicit solution of two boundary value problems describing the tensile testing of the bar. In both the cases we show that the small deformation model is not able to predict the necking phenomenon. 

\section{Formulation}
\label{sec:small-model-1d}

Before starting to develop the true one dimensional model, we look at the full multidimensional model of the bar but considering a constrained kinematics that does not allows for bending and shear. This will provide the basic intuition upon which we will be able to build the reduced dimensional model.

\subsection{Motivation from the multidimensional kinematics}

Let $\Omega=(0,L)\times\Sigma$ with $L>0$ and $\Sigma\subset\mathbb{R}^{d-1}$ be the reference configuration of the cylindrical bar. We denote the generic point in $\Omega$ as $(x,y)$ with $x\in(0,L)$ and $y\in\Sigma$. We assume a macroscopic displacement of the form
\begin{equation}
\label{eqn:displacement}
\begin{pmatrix}
x \\
y
\end{pmatrix}
\mapsto
\begin{pmatrix}
u_1(x) \\
u_2(x)y
\end{pmatrix}
\end{equation}
where $u_1$ and $u_2$ are functions from $(0,L)$ to ${R}$. This assumption means that the sections of the cylindrical bar remain flat and can translate and expand uniformly. The corresponding displacement gradient is
\[
H=\begin{pmatrix}
u_1' & 0 \\
u_2'y & u_2I
\end{pmatrix}.
\]
The plastic distortion is assumed to be of the form
\begin{equation}
\label{eqn:plastic}
H_p=
\begin{pmatrix}
p & 0 \\
0 & -\frac{p}{d-1}I
\end{pmatrix}
\end{equation}
where $p\colon(0,L)\to\mathbb{R}$. This choice implies plastic incompressibility. The elastic distortion is then given by
\[
H_e=H-H_p=\begin{pmatrix}
u_1'-p & 0 \\
u_2'y & \left(u_2+\frac{p}{d-1}\right)I
\end{pmatrix}.
\]
Then the elastic strain is
\[
E_e=\begin{pmatrix}
u_1'-p & \frac{u_2'}{2}y^T \\
\frac{u_2'}{2}y & \left(u_2+\frac{p}{d-1}\right)I
\end{pmatrix}=
\begin{pmatrix}
e_1 & \frac{u_2'}{2}y^T \\
\frac{u_2'}{2}y & e_2I
\end{pmatrix}
\]
where we have introduced $e_1=u_1'-p$ and $e_2=u_2+(d-1)^{-1}p$.

We now look how the elastic free energy looks like when the displacement is of the form~\eqref{eqn:displacement} and the plastic distortion is of the form~\eqref{eqn:plastic}. Assuming an isotropic elastic response the elastic free energy density is
\[
W_e(E_e)=\frac{1}{2}\kappa(\tr E_e)^2+\mu|\dev E_e|^2
\]
where $\kappa\ge0$ and $\mu\ge0$ are the bulk and shear modulus respectively. But in our case
\[
\tr E_e=e_1+(d-1)e_2\quad\text{and}\quad\dev E_e=\begin{pmatrix}
\frac{d-1}{d}(e_1-e_2) & \frac{u_2'}{2}y^T \\
\frac{u_2'}{2}y & -\frac{1}{d}(e_1-e_2)I
\end{pmatrix},
\]
so that
\[
W_e(E_e)=\frac{1}{2}\kappa\left(e_1+(d-1)e_2\right)^2+\frac{d-1}{d}\mu(e_1-e_2)^2+\frac{1}{2}|y|^2\mu|u_2'|^2.
\]
We now compute the elastic free energy per unit axial length. This will suggest us how to appropriately choose the free energy in the one dimensional model. To this aim we integrate the elastic free energy density over the section. We find
\begin{equation}
\label{eqn:necking-small:constitutive-multidimensional}
\int_\Sigma W_e(E_e)dy=\frac{1}{2}A\kappa\left(e_1+(d-1)e_2\right)^2+\frac{d-1}{d}A\mu(e_1-e_2)^2+\frac{1}{2}J\mu|u_2'|^2,
\end{equation}
where
\[
A=\int_\Sigma dy\quad\text{and}\quad J=\int_\Sigma|y|^2dy
\]
are the polar and the polar second order moment of area.

\subsection{Kinematics of the one dimensional model}

We are now ready to build a purely one-dimensional model of the bar, drawing inspiration from the multidimensional kinematics discussed above. We stress that this formulation should be viewed as an independent model with its own intrinsic validity, regardless of the multidimensional framework, which was presented in the previous subsection solely as a motivation.
