Let $L>0$ be the length of the cylindrical bar. The macroscopic configuration of the bar is now described by the couples $z=(u,p)\in Z$ where $u_i\colon(0,L)\to\mathbb{R}$ for $i\in\{1,2\}$ and $p\colon(0,L)\to\mathbb{R}$. Here $u_1$ is the longitudinal displacement of the sections in the axis direction and $u_2$ is the quantity describing the transversal expansion of the sections. The configuration of the microscopic structure of the bar is described by the plastic distortion $p\colon(0,L)\to\mathbb{R}$. As suggested by the relations we found in previous subsection, we define $e_1=u_1'-p$ as the quantities quantifying the elastic distortion.

The space $Z$ of configurations $z=(u,p)$ will be clearly defined subsequently when the existence result will be proved. The generic virtual velocity will be denoted as $\tilde w=(\tilde v,\tilde q)\in Z$.

\subsection{Statics}

With the same reasoning that lead to choose the internal power]\eqref{eqn:powers-plasticity} in the context of the multidimensional plasticity theory, now we assume that the generalized internal force in our one-dimensional model is of the form
\[
\dual{\mathcal I,\tilde w}=\int_0^L\left(\zeta\tilde v_2'+\sigma_1(\tilde v_1'-\tilde q)+\sigma_2(\tilde v_2+(d-1)^{-1}\tilde q)\right)dx+\int_0^L\tau\tilde qdx.
\]
The first integral describes the elastic response that couples the macroscopic kinematic variables $u$ with the microscopic variable $p$. Here $\zeta\colon(0,L)\to\mathbb{R}$, $\sigma_1\colon(0,L)\to\mathbb{R}$ is the longitudinal elastic stress and $\sigma_2\colon(0,L)\to\mathbb{R}$ is the longitudinal elastic force. The last integral describes the microscopic response and $\tau\colon(0,L)\to\mathbb{R}$ is the microscopic force.

Similarly, the external generalized force is required to be of the form
\[
\dual{\mathcal X,\tilde w}=\int_0^Lf\cdot\tilde vdx+h(L)\cdot\tilde v(L)-h(0)\cdot\tilde v(0).
\]
Here $f\colon(0,L)\to\mathbb{R}^2$ is an external force distributed along the bar and $h\colon\{0,L\}\to\mathbb{R}^2$ is an external force acting at the ends of the bar. The first component of both $f$ and $h$ is referred to the axial direction, while the second component is referred to the longitudinal direction.

The application of he principle of virtual powers, $\dual{\mathcal{I},\tilde w}=\dual{\mathcal{X},\tilde w}$ for all $\tilde w\in Z$, leads to equilibrium equations whose strong form is given by the following proposition.
\begin{prop}
The local form of the equilibrium condition is
\begin{subequations}
\begin{gather}
\sigma_1'+f_1=0\quad\text{in $(0,L)$} \label{eqn:local-form-1}\\
h_1(x)=\sigma_1(x)\quad\text{for $x\in\{0,L\}$} \label{eqn:local-form-2}\\
-\sigma_2+\zeta'+f_2=0\quad\text{in $(0,L)$} \label{eqn:local-form-3}\\
h_2(x)=\zeta(x)\quad\text{for $x\in\{0,L\}$} \label{eqn:local-form-4}\\
\sigma_1-(d-1)^{-1}\sigma_2-\tau=0\quad\text{in $(0,L)$} \label{eqn:local-form-5}
\end{gather}
\end{subequations}
\end{prop}
\begin{proof}
First we choose $\tilde v_2=0$ and $\tilde q=0$ and $\tilde v_1$ arbitrary. Then we get
\[
-\int_0^L\sigma_1'\tilde v_1dx+\sigma_1(L)\tilde v_1(L)-\sigma_1(0)\tilde v_1(0)=\int_0^Lf_1\tilde v_1dx+h_1(L)\tilde v_1(L)-h_1(0)\tilde v_1(0).
\]
We get~\eqref{eqn:local-form-1}~and~\eqref{eqn:local-form-2}. Next we choose $\tilde v_1=0$ and $\tilde q=0$ and $\tilde v_2$ arbitrary. Then
\[
-\int_0^L\zeta'\tilde v_2dx+\zeta(L)\tilde v_2(L)-\zeta(0)\tilde v_2(0)+\int_0^L\sigma_2\tilde v_2dx=\int_0^Lf_2\tilde v_2dx+h_2(L)\tilde v_2(L)-h_2(0)\tilde v_2(0).
\]
We get~\eqref{eqn:local-form-3}~and~\eqref{eqn:local-form-4}. Then we choose $\tilde v=0$ and $\tilde q$ arbitrary obtaining
\[
\int_0^L(\sigma_1-(d-1)^{-1}\sigma_2-\tau)\tilde qdx=0.
\]
This implies~\eqref{eqn:local-form-5}.
\end{proof}

\subsection{Constitutive relations}

To find appropriate constitutive relations for our one-dimensional model, we first choose the free energy and dissipation potential functionals. The free energy functional $\Psi\colon Z\to\mathbb{R}$ is chosen as
\[
\Psi(z)=\int_0^L\left(W_e(e_1,e_2)+W_p(p)\right)dx
\]
where $W_e\colon\mathbb{R}\times\mathbb{R}\to\mathbb{R}$ and $W_p\colon\mathbb{R}\to\mathbb{R}$ are now the elastic and plastic free energies per unit axial lenght. We specialize to the case
\begin{equation}
\label{eqn:necking-small:free-energy-density}
W_e(e_1,e_2)=\frac{1}{2}\kappa(e_1+(d-1)e_2)^2+\frac{1}{2}\mu(e_1-e_2)^2\quad\text{and}\quad W_p(p)=\frac{1}{2}l^2\mu|u_2'|^2+\frac{1}{2}Bp^2.
\end{equation}
where $\kappa$ is a bulk modulus, $\mu$ is a shear modulus, $l$ is length parameter related to the geometry of the section and $B$ is the hardening parameter. The above formula for $W_e$ is motivated by what we found in~\eqref{eqn:necking-small:constitutive-multidimensional} when discussing the multidimensional model of the bar. The dissipation potential functional $\Phi\colon Z\to\mathbb{R}$ is supposed to be
\begin{equation}
\label{eqn:necking-small:dissipation-potential-density}
\Phi(\dot z)=\int_0^LR(\dot p)dx
\end{equation}
where $R\colon\mathbb{R}\to[0,\infty]$ is the plastic dissipation potential per unit axial length, which is required to be convex and satisfying $R(0)=0$. A psiibile particular $R$ could be the one dimensional analog of the Von-Mises yield criterion, i.e.\ $R(\dot p)=Y|\dot p|$ where $Y>0$ is the yield strength.

Consistently with that choices of $\Psi$ and $\Phi$, applying~\eqref{eqn:modeling:constitutive} we find the following constitutive relations
\begin{subequations}
\label{eqn:constitutive-small-neck}
\begin{gather}
\zeta=\mu l^2u_2' \\
\sigma_1=\kappa(e_1+(d-1)e_2)+\mu(e_1-e_2) \\
\sigma_2=(d-1)\kappa(e_1+(d-1)e_2)-\mu(e_1-e_2) \\
\tau\in Bp+\partial R(\dot p).
\end{gather}
\end{subequations}
Notice that the general discussion of Section~\ref{sec:ppv} of Chapter~\ref{chap:modeling} implies that these constitutive relations are compatible with the dissipation inequality.
\begin{rmk}(the Mandel stress)
Introducing the constitutive relations~\eqref{eqn:constitutive-small-neck} into~\eqref{eqn:local-form-5} we get
\[
\frac{d}{d-1}\mu(e_1-e_2)-Bp\in\partial R(\dot p).
\]
We will refer to
\[
m=\frac{d}{d-1}\mu(e_1-e_2)
\]
as the Mandel stress. It is interpreted as the elastic force felt by the microscopic configuration and we notice that it depends on the shear modulus only.
\end{rmk}

\section{Existence and uniqueness result}

\label{sec:small-neck:existence}

In this section we discuss the existence and uniqueness of solutions of evolutions . For simplicity we will assume homogeneous Dirichlet boundary conditions $u(x)=0$ for $x\in\{0,L\}$.

We define the spaces $U=H^1((0,L),\mathbb{R}\times\mathbb{R})$ and $P=L^2((0,L))$. Let also $Z=U\times P$ the space of configurations of the system. We will write the generic configuration as $z=(u,p)\in Z$ with $u\in U$ and $p\in P$ and the generic virtual rate as $w=(v,q)\in Z$ with $v\in U$ and $q\in P$.

The problem associated with the model described in this chapter is of the form of an evolutionary rate-independent problem with quadratic free energy of the form considered Section~\ref{sec:evi} of Chapter~\ref{chap:solution-concepts}. According to~\eqref{eqn:necking-small:free-energy-density}, the free energy $\Psi\colon Z\to\mathbb{R}$ is defined by
\[
\Psi(z)=
\begin{multlined}[t]
\int_0^L\left[\frac{1}{2}\kappa(u_1'+(d-1)u_2)^2+\frac{1}{2}\mu\left(u_1'-u_2-\frac{d}{d-1}p\right)^2+\frac{1}{2}l^2\mu |u_2'|^2+\frac{1}{2}Bp^2\right]dx
\end{multlined}
\]
Its FrĂ©chet differential is $\mathcal{A}=\partial\Psi\colon Z\to Z^*$ given by
\[
\dual{\mathcal{A}z,w}=
\begin{multlined}[t]
\int_0^Ll^2\mu u_2'v_2'dx+\int_0^L\left[\kappa(u_1'+(d-1)u_2)+\mu\left(u_1'-u_2-\frac{d}{d-1}p\right)\right](v_1'-q)dx+\\
+\int_0^L\left[(d-1)\kappa(u_1'+(d-1)u_2)-\mu\left(u_1'-u_2-\frac{d}{d-1}q\right)\right]\left(v_2+(d-1)^{-1}p\right)dx+\\
+\int_0^LBpqdx.
\end{multlined}
\]
According to~\eqref{eqn:necking-small:dissipation-potential-density} the dissipation potential $\Phi\colon Z\to\mathbb{R}\cup\{\infty\}$ is defined by
\[
\Phi(w)=\int_0^LR(q)dx.
\]
The external generalized force is $\mathcal{X}\colon[0,T]\to Z^*$,
\[
\dual{\mathcal{X}(t),w}=\int_0^Tf(t)\cdot vdx.
\]

Now to prove the existence of solution we need only to satisfy the assumptions of Theorem~\ref{thm:han-reddy}. This will be done under the following assumptions:
\begin{itemize}
\item[(G)] $L>0$ and $d=2$ or $d=3$,
\item[($\psi$)] $\kappa$, $\mu$, $l$ and $B$ are positive constants,
\item[(R)] $R\colon\mathbb{R}\to\mathbb{R}$ is given by $R(q)=Y|q|$ for some $Y>0$,
\item[($f$)] $f\in H^1((0,T),U^*)$ and $f(0)=0$.
\end{itemize}
\begin{thm}
Assume hypotheses (G), ($\psi$), (R) and ($f$). Then there exists a unique $z\in\text{AC}([0,T],Z)$ satisfying $z(0)=0$ and $\mathcal{A}z(t)+\partial\Phi(\dot z(t))\ni\mathcal{X}(t)$ for a.e.\ $t\in(0,T)$.
\end{thm}
\begin{proof}
We verify the hypothesis of Theorem~\ref{thm:han-reddy}.

\emph{Step 1.} It is trivial to check that $\mathcal{A}$ is linear and continuous. To prove coercivity we show that there exists $\alpha>0$ such that $\dual{\mathcal{A}z,z}\ge\alpha\norm{z}_{Z}^2$. If we call $e_1=u_1'-p$ and $e_2=u_2+(d-1)^{-1}p$ we have by applying Young inequality repeatedly
\[
\begin{split}
\dual{\mathcal{A}z,z}=2\int_0^L\psi dx&=\kappa\norm{e_1+(d-1)e_2}_2^2+\mu\norm{e_1-e_2}_2^2+l^2\mu\norm{u_2'}_2^2+B\norm{p}_2^2=\\
&=\kappa\norm{u_1'+(d-1)u_2}_2^2+\mu\left\lVert u_1'-u_2-\frac{d}{d-1}p\right\rVert_2^2+l^2\mu\norm{u_2'}_2^2+B\norm{p}_2^2\ge\\
&\ge\begin{multlined}[t]
\left(1-\theta\right)\kappa\norm{u_1'}_2^2+\left(1-\frac{1}{\theta}\right)(d-1)^2\kappa\norm{u_2}_2^2+(1-\tau)\mu\norm{u_1'-u_2}_2^2+\\
l^2\mu\norm{u_2'}_2^2+\left[\left(1-\frac{1}{\tau}\right)\left(\frac{d}{d-1}\right)^2\mu+B\right]\norm{p}_2^2
\end{multlined}\\
&\ge
\begin{multlined}[t]
\left[\left(1-\theta\right)\kappa+(1-\eta)(1-\tau)\mu\right]\norm{u_1'}_2^2+\\
+\left[\left(1-\frac{1}{\theta}\right)(d-1)^2\kappa+\left(1-\frac{1}{\eta}\right)(1-\tau)\mu\right]\norm{u_2}_2^2+l^2\mu\norm{u_2'}_2^2+\\
+\left[\left(1-\frac{1}{\tau}\right)\left(\frac{d}{d-1}\right)^2\mu+B\right]\norm{p}_2^2
\end{multlined}
\end{split}
\]
for all $\theta$, $\tau$ and $\eta$ in $(0,\infty)$. We restrict to $\theta$, $\tau$ and $\eta$ in $(0,1)$. Then by PoincarĂ¨ inequality there exist $C_1>0$ and $C_2>0$ such that
\[
\begin{split}
\dual{\mathcal{A}z,z}\ge
\begin{multlined}[t]
C_1\left[\left(1-\theta\right)\kappa+(1-\eta)(1-\tau)\mu\right]\norm{u_1}_{1,2}^2+\\
+\left[C_2l^2\mu+\left(1-\frac{1}{\theta}\right)(d-1)^2\kappa+\left(1-\frac{1}{\eta}\right)(1-\tau)\mu\right]\norm{u_2}_{1,2}^2+\\
+\left[\left(1-\frac{1}{\tau}\right)\left(\frac{d}{d-1}\right)^2\mu+B\right]\norm{p}_2^2
\end{multlined}
\end{split}
\]
Now we choose $\theta$, $\tau$ and $\eta$ sufficiently close to 1 so that the coefficients multiplying $\norm{u_1}_{1,2}^2$, $\norm{u_2}_{1,2}^2$ and $\norm{p}_2^2$ are positive and we get the desired coercivity.

\emph{Step 2.} Since $\Phi(w)=Y\norm{q}_1$ we see immediately that $\Phi$ is convex, proper, continuous and 1-homogeneous.

\emph{Step 3.} Since $f\in H^1((0,T),U^*)$ and $f(0)=0$ then $\mathcal{X}\in H^1((0,T),Z^*)$ and $\mathcal{X}(0)=0$.
\end{proof}

\section{Analytical solution to the tensile test problem}

In this section we obtain explicit solutions to the boundary value problem describing the tensile test. We will consider two different variants of the problem: in the first variant the ends are left free to slide in the transversal direction, while in the second variant the ends are clamped. We will see that the behavior of the solutions in this two situations is qualitatively similar, and in particular necking does not occur.

\subsection{Tensile test with free-sliding ends}
\label{subsec:homogeneous-solution}

Suppose that $f(x,t)=0$, $h_1(L,t)=h_1(0,t)=F(t)$, and $h_2(L,t)=h_2(0,t)=0$ for all $x\in(0,L)$ and $t\in[0,T]$. We assume that $F(0)=0$ and that $F$ is monotonically increasing. From equation~\eqref{eqn:local-form-1} and~\eqref{eqn:local-form-2} we have that $\sigma_1=F$ on all $(0,L)$. Then
\[
\kappa(e_1+(d-1)e_2)=F-\mu(e_1-e_2)\quad\text{and}\quad e_1=\frac{F-(d-1)\kappa e_2+\mu e_2}{\kappa+\mu}.
\]
Moreover
\[
e_1-e_2=\frac{F-d\kappa e_2}{\kappa+\mu}.
\]
Then
\[
\begin{split}
\sigma_2&=(d-1)\left(F-\mu(e_1-e_2)\right)-\mu(e_1-e_2)=(d-1)F-d\mu(e_1-e_2)=\\
&=(d-1)F-d\frac{\mu}{\kappa+\mu}F+d^2\frac{\kappa\mu}{\kappa+\mu}e_2=\frac{(d-1)\kappa-\mu}{\kappa+\mu}F+d^2\frac{\kappa\mu}{\kappa+\mu}e_2=\\
&=\frac{(d-1)\kappa-\mu}{\kappa+\mu}F+\frac{d^2}{d-1}\frac{\kappa\mu}{\kappa+\mu}p+d^2\frac{\kappa\mu}{\kappa+\mu}u_2.
\end{split}
\]
Now from equation~\eqref{eqn:local-form-3} we have
\[
l^2\mu u_2''=\frac{(d-1)\kappa-\mu}{\kappa+\mu}F+\frac{d^2}{d-1}\frac{\kappa\mu}{\kappa+\mu}p+d^2\frac{\kappa\mu}{\kappa+\mu}u_2.
\]
The solution is given by
\[
u_2=-\frac{p}{d-1}-\frac{(d-1)\kappa-\mu}{d^2\kappa\mu}F.
\]
since conditions $h_2(L,t)=h_2(0,t)$ are satisfied because $u_2'=0$. Notice that
\[
e_2=u_2+\frac{p}{d-1}=-\frac{(d-1)\kappa-\mu}{d^2\kappa\mu}F.
\]
Now the Mandel stress is given by
\[
m=\frac{d}{d-1}\frac{\mu}{\kappa+\mu}(F-d\kappa e_2)=F.
\]
When $F\le Y$ then the response is purely elastic and $p=0$. When $F>Y$ then $\dot p>0$ and
\[
F-Bp=Y.
\]
This implies $p=B^{-1}(F-Y)$. Assuming $(d-1)\kappa-\mu>0$ the sections constricts in traction.

\subsection{Tensile test with clamped ends}

\subsubsection*{Setup of the problem}

Suppose that $f(x,t)=0$, $h_1(L,t)=h_1(0,t)=F(t)$, and $u_2(L,t)=u_2(0,t)=0$ for all $x\in(0,L)$ and $t\in[0,T]$. We assume that $F(0)=0$ and that $F$ is monotonically increasing. By the same computations of Section~\ref{subsec:homogeneous-solution} we find the equation
\begin{equation}
\label{eqn:ode-u2}
l^2\mu u_2''=\frac{(d-1)\kappa-\mu}{\kappa+\mu}F+\frac{d^2}{d-1}\frac{\kappa\mu}{\kappa+\mu}p+d^2\frac{\kappa\mu}{\kappa+\mu}u_2.
\end{equation}

\subsubsection*{Solution in the elastic range}

In the elastic range $p=0$ everywhere in $(0,L)$. In this case a particular solution is given by
\[
x\mapsto-\frac{(d-1)\kappa-\mu}{d^2\kappa\mu}F
\]
and a solution of the homogeneous equation which is also symmetric with respect to $x=L/2$ is
\[
x\mapsto\cosh\left(d\sqrt{\frac{\kappa}{\kappa+\mu}}\frac{x-\frac{L}{2}}{l}\right).
\]
Then taking into account the boundary conditions we find the solution
\begin{equation}
\label{eqn:elastic-solution}
u_2=e_2=\frac{(d-1)\kappa-\mu}{d^2\kappa\mu}\left(\frac{\cosh\left(d\sqrt{\frac{\kappa}{\kappa+\mu}}\frac{x-\frac{L}{2}}{l}\right)}{\cosh\left(d\sqrt{\frac{\kappa}{\kappa+\mu}}\frac{L}{2l}\right)}-1\right)F.
\end{equation}
When $(d-1)\kappa-\mu>0$ the sections constricts in traction. The middle section is the most affected by the constriction. The Mandel stress is the elastic range is given by
\[
m=\frac{d}{d-1}\frac{\mu}{\kappa+\mu}(F-d\kappa e_2)=\frac{d}{d-1}\frac{\mu}{\kappa+\mu}\left(1-\frac{(d-1)\kappa-\mu}{d\mu}\left(\frac{\cosh\left(d\sqrt{\frac{\kappa}{\kappa+\mu}}\frac{x-\frac{L}{2}}{l}\right)}{\cosh\left(d\sqrt{\frac{\kappa}{\kappa+\mu}}\frac{L}{2l}\right)}-1\right)\right)F.
\]
Its maximum is reached in the middle section so we conclude that the Mandel stress reach yield strength here first.

\subsubsection*{Solution in the plastic range}

From the above considerations we are let to seek for a solution where $p>0$ in the interval $(L/2-\epsilon,L/2+\epsilon)$ where $\epsilon\in[0,L/2]$ is a function of time.
\begin{rmk}
Since the solution is symmetric with respect to $x=L/2$ from now on we focus on the $(0,L/2)$ half of the bar only.
\end{rmk}
On $(0,L/2-\epsilon)$ the solution is given by $p=0$ and $u_2$ of the form
\begin{equation}
\label{eqn:epsilon-small-u2}
u_2=\frac{(d-1)\kappa-\mu}{d^2\kappa\mu}\left(\frac{\cosh\left(d\sqrt{\frac{\kappa}{\kappa+\mu}}\frac{x-\frac{L}{2}}{l}\right)}{\cosh\left(d\sqrt{\frac{\kappa}{\kappa+\mu}}\frac{L}{2l}\right)}-1\right)F+C_1\sinh\left(d\sqrt{\frac{\kappa}{\kappa+\mu}}x\right)
\end{equation}
where $C_1\in\mathbb{R}$. On $(L/2-\epsilon,L/2)$ we have
\[
m-Bp=Y
\]
\[
\frac{d}{d-1}\frac{\mu}{\kappa+\mu}(F-d\kappa u_2-\frac{d\kappa}{d-1}p)-Bp=Y.
\]
Then we find
\begin{equation*}
\label{eqn:p-as-function-of-y}
p=\frac{\frac{d}{d-1}\frac{\mu}{\kappa+\mu}F-\frac{d^2}{d-1}\frac{\kappa\mu}{\kappa+\mu}u_2-Y}{\frac{d^2}{(d-1)^2}\frac{\mu\kappa}{\kappa+\mu}+B}.
\end{equation*}
Substituting that identity into equation~\eqref{eqn:ode-u2} we have obtain a differential equation in $y$ of the form
\[
y''=ay+b
\]
where $a$ and $b$ depends on time only. A solution which is symmetric with respect to $x=L/2$ is of the form
\begin{equation}
\label{eqn:epsilon-large-u2}
y=-\frac{b}{a}+C_2\cosh\left(\sqrt{a}\left(x-\frac{L}{2}\right)\right).
\end{equation}
We now look for a solution $u_2\in C^2([0,L])$. When $\epsilon=L/2$ the solution is simply given by
\[
u_2=\frac{b}{a}\left(\frac{\cosh\left(\sqrt{a}\left(x-\frac{L}{2}\right)\right)}{\cosh\left(\sqrt{a}\frac{L}{2}\right)}-1\right)
\]
When $\epsilon\in(0,L/2)$ we consider~\eqref{eqn:epsilon-small-u2} on $(0,L/2-\epsilon)$ and~\eqref{eqn:epsilon-large-u2} on $(L/2-\epsilon,L/2)$ and we impose the continuity of $u_2$ and $u_2'$ at $x=L/2-\epsilon$. Solving the resultant linear system we find the constants $C_1$ and $C_2$ that we do not report.

\subsubsection*{Determination of $\epsilon$}

We are left with the determination of $\epsilon\in[0,L/2]$ as a function of time. To this aim we consider the Mandel stress computed the elastic range at $x=L/2$. We call $F_Y$ the value of $F$ for which that Mandel stress reach $Y$. We find
\[
F_Y=\frac{d-1}{d}\frac{k+\mu}{\mu}\left(-\frac{(d-1)\kappa-\mu}{d\mu}\left(\frac{1}{\cosh\left(d\sqrt{\frac{\kappa}{\kappa+\mu}}\frac{L}{2l}\right)}-1\right)+1\right)^{-1}Y.
%=\frac{Y(\kappa + \mu)(d - 1)}{d\mu \left[ \frac{1}{d\mu} \left( \frac{1}{\cosh(Ld\sqrt{\kappa} / 2l\sqrt{\kappa + \mu})} - 1 \right)(\kappa + \mu - d\kappa) + 1 \right]}.
\]
For $F\le F_Y$ we have $\epsilon=0$. To determine $\epsilon$ when $F>F_Y$ we consider the equation $m(L/2-\epsilon)=Y$. That equation cannot be solved analytically in $\epsilon$. Instead we can find explicitly $F$ as a function $f=\colon[0,\epsilon_0)\to\mathbb{R}$ of $\epsilon$. Here $[0,\epsilon_0)$ is the maximal interval where $f$ is well defined. After a meticulous analysis of the analytical expression of $f$ we find that $\epsilon_0>L/2$. This means that when the load $F$ reach the value
\[
f(L/2)=\frac{d-1}{d}\frac{\kappa+\mu}{\mu}Y,
\]
the plastic distortion satisfies $p>0$ in the entire interval $(0,L)$. We conclude that
\[
\epsilon=\begin{cases}
0 & \text{if $F\le F_Y$} \\
f^{-1}(F) & \text{if $F_Y<F\le f(L/2)$} \\
L/2 & \text{if $F>f(L/2)$}.
\end{cases}
\]

\subsubsection*{Absence of necking}

From the analytical expression one can see that for $F>f(L/2)$ the solution $u_2$ depends linearly on $F$. In particular as $F\to\infty$ we have that $u_2\to-\infty$ uniformly on all compact sets contained in $(0,L)$. We conclude that the small deformation model is not able to predict necking. Indeed the shrinkage of the cross section appears to be not localized on a small region around the middle of the bar but to be distributed along its entire length.

\subsubsection*{The limit of thin bar}

To get further insight on the solution we look at the limit $l\to0^+$.
\begin{prop}
In the limit $l\to0^+$ the solution $u_2$ that we have found converges pointwise on $(0,L)\times[0,T]$ to the homogeneous solution of Section~\ref{subsec:homogeneous-solution}. This convergence is also uniform on compact subsets of $(0,L)\times[0,T]$.
\end{prop}
\begin{proof}
It is a direct computation.
\end{proof}
This results confirm the absence of necking in the small deformation model. Indeed it says that a thin bar deforms almost uniformly. 

%\subsubsection*{Some plots}

\chapter{Model at large deformations}
\label{chap:large-neck}

As we showed in the previous chapter, the one dimensional small deformations model of a bar is not able to predict necking. This leads us to refine the one dimensional model allowing large deformations. This is the content of this chapter. The discussion is structures as in the previous chapter. In the first section we develop the model, in the second section we obtain an existence theorem of solutions to the boundary value problem produced by that model. In the third section we face the problem of studying the boundary value problem describing the tensile test: in this case we will not be able to obtain any explicit solution, so we will propose some methods to gain qualitative insight on the behavior of the solution, in particular regarding the onset of necking.

\section{Formulation}

In a similar manner to the previous chapter, before developing the strictly one-dimensional model, we examine the kinematics of a fully multidimensional bar under large strain. We assume that bending and shear are neglected; consequently, cross-sections remain plane, undergoing only axial translation and uniform transversal stretching.

\subsection{Motivation form the multidimensional kinematics}

Let $\Omega=(0,L)\times\Sigma\subset\mathbb{R}^d$ with $L>0$ and $\Sigma\subset\mathbb{R}^{d-1}$ the reference configuration of the cylindrical bar. The generic material point will be denoted as $(X,Y)\in\Omega$ with $X\in(0,L)$ and $Y\in\Sigma$. We consider deformations of the form
\begin{equation}
\label{eqn:necking-large:deformation}
(X,Y)\mapsto\begin{pmatrix}
\chi_1(X) \\
\chi_2(X)Y
\end{pmatrix}
\end{equation}
where $\chi_1\colon(0,L)\to\mathbb{R}$ satisfy $\chi_1'>0$ and $\chi_2\colon(0,L)\to(0,\infty)$. The corresponding deformation gradient is
\[
F=\begin{pmatrix}
\chi_1' & 0 \\
\chi_2'Y & \chi_2I
\end{pmatrix}.
\]
The plastic distortion is assumed to be of the form
\begin{equation}
\label{eqn:necking-large:plastic}
F_p=\begin{pmatrix}
P & 0 \\
0 & P^{-\frac{1}{d-1}}I
\end{pmatrix}
\end{equation}
where $P\colon(0,L)\to(0,\infty)$. This choice implies plastic incompressibility. The elastic distortion is then given by the Kroner--Lee multiplicative decomposition
\[
F_e=FF_p^{-1}=\begin{pmatrix}
\chi_1'P^{-1} & 0 \\
\chi_2'P^{-1}Y & \chi_2P^\frac{1}{d-1}I
\end{pmatrix}=
\begin{pmatrix}
E_1 & 0 \\
GY & E_2I
\end{pmatrix},
\]
where we have defined $E_1=\chi_1'P^{-1}$, $E_2=\chi_2P^{\frac{1}{d-1}}$ and $G=\chi_2'P^{-1}$.
%We define
%\[
%e_1=\log E_1=\epsilon_1-p\quad\text{and}\quad e_2=\log E_2=\epsilon_2+(d-1)^{-1}p.
%\]
%Now the spatial velocity gradient is given by
%\[
%L=\dot FF^{-1}=\begin{pmatrix}
%\dot\epsilon_1 & 0 \\
%\left(\frac{\dot\chi_2'}{\chi_1'}-\frac{\chi_2'}{\chi_1'}\dot\epsilon_2\right) & \dot\epsilon_2I
%\end{pmatrix}
%\]
%and the plastic rate is
%\[
%L_p=\dot F_pF_p^{-1}=\begin{pmatrix}
%\dot p & 0 \\
%0 & -\frac{\dot p}{d-1}I
%\end{pmatrix}.
%\]
%Then the elastic rate is
%\[
%L_e=\dot F_eF_e^{-1}=L-F_eL_pF_e^{-1}=\begin{pmatrix}
%\dot e_1 & 0 \\
%\left(\frac{\dot G}{E_1}-\frac{G}{E_1}\dot e_2\right)Y & \dot e_2I
%\end{pmatrix}.
%\]
%%Consider the case $d=2$. In this case the first principal invariant of $C_e=F_e^TF_e$ is
%%\begin{gather*}
%%I_1=\tr(C_e)=E_1^2+G^2|Y|^2+(d-1)E_2^2 \\
%%I_2=\frac{1}{2}\left(\tr(C_e)^2-\tr(C_e^2)\right)=\frac{(d-1)(d-2)E_2^4+2(d-1)E_2^2(E_1^2+G^2|Y|^2)-2G^2E_2^2|Y|^2}{2} \\
%%I_3=\det(C_e)=E_1^2E_2^{2(d-1)}.
%%\end{gather*}
%%A possible elastic free energy density is the neo-Hookeian free energy density
%%\[
%%\psi=\begin{cases}
%%a(I_1-d)+b(I_2-d) & \text{if $d=2$} \\
%%a(I_1-d)+b(I_2-d)+w(I_3) & \text{if $d=3$}.
%%\end{cases}
%%\]
To motivate the development of the one-dimensional constitutive relations, we examine the neo-Hookean elastic free-energy density under the aforementioned kinematic assumptions. This will serve as a basis for the development of the constitutive model presented in the following section. Assuming the particular kinematic assumptions above, the first principal invariant of $C_e=F_e^TF_e$ is
\[
I_e=\tr(C_e)=E_1^2+G^2|Y|^2+E_2^2
\]
and the determinant of $F_e$ is
\[
J_e=\det F_e=E_1E_2,
\]
so that the neo-Hookean elastic free energy density is given by
\begin{equation}
\label{eqn:necking-large:neo-hookean}
\psi_e=C\left(\frac{I_e}{J_e}-2\right)+w(J_e)=C\left(\frac{E_1^2+G^2|Y|^2+E_2^2}{E_1E_2}-2\right)+w(E_1E_2).
\end{equation}


\subsection{Kinematics}

We now proceed to develop a purely one-dimensional model of the bar. This model is formulated independently of the fully multidimensional one; therefore, the discussion in the previous subsection should be viewed merely as a motivation for the specific choices made in the following derivation.

Let $L>0$ the length of the cylindrical bar. The macroscopic configuration of the bar is described by $\chi_1\colon(0,L)\to\mathbb{R}$ with $\chi_1'>0$ and $\chi_2\colon(0,L)\to(0,\infty)$. Here $\chi_1$ describes the positions of the sections and $\chi_2$ is the transversal deformation factor of the sections. The configuration of the microscopic structure of the bar, that is the plastic distortion, is described by $P\colon(0,L)\to(0,\infty)$. As suggested by the multidimensional kinematics, we define the elastic distortions as $E_1=\chi_1'P^{-1}$, $E_2=\chi_2P^{\frac{1}{d-1}}$ and $G=\chi_2'P^{-1}$.

The configuration space $Z$ of the system are thus the elements of the form $z=(\chi,P)\colon(0,L)\to\mathbb{R}\times(0,\infty)\times(0,\infty)$. The virtual rates are denoted as $\tilde w=(\tilde v,\tilde Q)\colon(0,L)\to\mathbb{R}\times(0,\infty)\times(0,\infty)$. The right functional setting will be discussed in Section~\ref{sec:existence-necking} where the existence theorem is proved.

\subsection{Statics}

The internal generalized force is chosen so that clearly appear the elastic forces and stresses, conjugate to the virtual rates of the elastic distortions, and plastic microforces and microstresses power, conjugate to the the virtual rate of the plastic distortion and its spatial derivative:
\[
\dual{\mathcal{I},\tilde w}=\int_0^L\left(\zeta\tilde{\dot G}+\sigma_1\tilde{\dot E}_1+\sigma_2\tilde{\dot E}_2\right)dX+\int_0^L\left(\tau\tilde{Q}+\kappa\tilde{Q}'\right)dX.
\]
Here $\zeta\colon(0,L)\to\mathbb{R}$ is the transversal elastic stress, $\sigma_1\colon(0,L)\to\mathbb{R}$ is the longitudinal elastic stress and $\sigma_2\colon(0,L)\to\mathbb{R}$ is the transversal elastic force, while $\tau\colon(0,L)\to\mathbb{R}$ is the microscopic force and $\kappa\colon(0,L)\to\mathbb{R}$ the microscopic stress. Moreover the virtual rates $\tilde{\dot E}_1$, $\tilde{\dot E}_2$ and $\tilde{\dot G}$ are defined by the relations
\begin{gather*}
G^{-1}\tilde{\dot G}=(\chi_2')^{-1}\tilde{v}_2'-P^{-1}\tilde{Q} \\
E_1^{-1}\tilde{\dot E}_1=(\chi_1')^{-1}\tilde{v}_1'-P^{-1}\tilde{Q} \\
E_2^{-1}\tilde{\dot E}_2=\chi_2^{-1}\tilde{v}_2+(d-1)^{-1}P^{-1}\tilde{Q},
\end{gather*}
Taking into account these definitions the internal generalized force can be rewritten more explicitly as
\begin{multline*}
\dual{\mathcal{I},\tilde w}=\int_0^LG\zeta\left((\chi_2')^{-1}\tilde v_2'-P^{-1}\tilde Q\right)dX+\\
+\int_0^L\left[E_1\sigma_1\left((\chi_1')^{-1}\tilde v_1'-P^{-1}\tilde Q\right)+E_2\sigma_2\left(\chi_2^{-1}\tilde v_2+(d-1)^{-1}P^{-1}\tilde Q\right)\right]dX+\\
+\int_0^L\left[\tau\tilde Q+\kappa\tilde Q'\right]dX.
\end{multline*}
\begin{rmk}
In the small strain model of Chapter~\ref{chap:small-neck} we did not included the microscopic stress $\kappa$. However, we are now including it because we expect that in the large deformation regime, it is necessary to obtain an existence result in Sobolev spaces. This is suggested by the existence theorem for three-dimensional elastoplasticity at large strains presented in~\cite{mielke}. It will ensure the necessary compactness needed to handle the complex nonlinear coupling implied by the present model. The insertion of the microstress $\kappa$ will prevent the plastic distortion field $P$ to exhibiting jumps, so it works as a regularization.
\end{rmk}

The external generalized force is chosen of the form
\[
\dual{\mathcal X,\tilde w}=\int_0^Lf\cdot\tilde vdx+h(L)\cdot\tilde v(L)-h(0)\cdot\tilde v(0).
\]
As in the small strain model, $f\colon(0,L)\to\mathbb{R}^2$ is an external force distributed along the bar and $h\colon\{0,L\}\to\mathbb{R}^2$ is an external force acting at the ends of the bar. The first component of both $f$ and $h$ is referred to the axial direction, while the second component is referred to the longitudinal direction.

Applying the principle of virtual powers, $\dual{\mathcal{I},\tilde w}=\dual{\mathcal{X},\tilde w}$ for all $\tilde w\in Z$, we get the equilibrium equations whose strong form is given below.
\begin{prop}
The local form of the equilibrium condition is
\begin{subequations}
\label{eqn:indeinite-equation-necking-large}
\begin{gather}
(P^{-1}\sigma_1)'+f_1=0\quad\text{in $(0,L)$} \\
h_1(X)=P(X)^{-1}\sigma_1(X)\quad\text{for $X\in\{0,L\}$} \\
-P^{\frac{1}{d-1}}\sigma_2+(P^{-1}\zeta)'+f_2=0\quad\text{in $(0,L)$} \\
h_2(X)=P(X)^{-1}\zeta(X)\quad\text{for $X\in\{0,L\}$} \\
\chi_1'P^{-2}\sigma_1-(d-1)^{-1}\chi_2P^{\frac{1}{d-1}-1}\sigma_2+\chi_2'P^{-2}\zeta-\tau+\kappa'=0\quad\text{for $X\in\{0,L\}$} \\
\kappa(X)=0\quad\text{for $X\in\{0,L\}$}
\end{gather}
\end{subequations}
\end{prop}
\begin{proof}
Rearranging the terms we have
\begin{multline*}
\dual{\mathcal{I},\tilde w}=\int_0^L\left[P^{-1}\sigma_1\tilde v_1'+P^{\frac{1}{d-1}}\sigma_2\tilde v_2+P^{-1}\zeta\tilde v_2'\right]dX+\\
+\int_0^L\left[\left(\tau-\chi_1'P^{-2}\sigma_1+(d-1)^{-1}\chi_2P^{\frac{1}{d-1}-1}\sigma_2-\chi_2'P^{-2}\zeta\right)\tilde Q+\kappa\tilde Q'\right]dX.
\end{multline*}
By integration by parts and application of the fundamental lemma of the calculus of variation we get the thesis.
\end{proof}
As we did also in the previous chapter, we define the Mandel stress as the elastic force felt by the microscopic configuration, that is
\begin{equation}
\label{eqn:neck-large-mandel}
m=\chi_1'P^{-2}\sigma_1-(d-1)^{-1}\chi_2P^{\frac{1}{d-1}-1}\sigma_2+\chi_2'P^{-2}\zeta.
\end{equation}

\subsection{Constitutive relations}

To appropriately build the constitutive relations we consider a free energy $\Psi\colon Z\to\mathbb{R}$ and a dissipation potential $\Phi\colon Z\to\mathbb{R}$ of the same structure that we sow in the previous plasticity models.
\begin{equation}
\Psi(z)=\int_0^L\left(W(E_1,E_2,G)+H(P,P')\right)dX\quad\text{and}\quad\Phi(z,\dot z)=\int_0^LR(P,\dot P)dX.
\end{equation}
Here $W\colon(0,\infty)\times(0,\infty)\times\mathbb{R}\to\mathbb{R}$ and $H\colon(0,\infty)\times\mathbb{R}\to\mathbb{R}$ are the smooth elastic and plastic free energy densities, and $R\colon(0,\infty)\times\mathbb{R}\to[0,\infty]$, convex in $\dot P$ and satisfying $R(0)=0$, is dissipation potential per unit axial length. Consistently with that choice of $\Psi$ and $\Phi$ we find the following constitutive relations
\begin{subequations}
\label{eqn:constitutive-neck-large}
\begin{gather}
\sigma_i=\dind{W}{E_i}\quad\text{and}\quad\zeta=\dind{W}{G} \\
\tau\in\dind{H}{P}+P^{-1}\partial_{\dot P}R\quad\text{and}\quad\kappa=\dind{H}{P'}.
\end{gather}
\end{subequations}
The general discussion made in Section~\ref{sec:ppv} of Chapter~\ref{chap:modeling} implies that the dissipation inequality is satisfied by these constitutive relations.

%\begin{prop}
%The constitutive relations~\eqref{eqn:constitutive-neck-large} satisfy the dissipation inequality.
%\end{prop}
%\begin{proof}
%Let $\tau=\dind{H}{P}+P^{-1}\xi$ with $\xi\in\partial R(\dot PP^{-1})$. Then the dissipation per unit axial length is given by
%\[
%\zeta\dot G+\sigma_2\dot E_2+\sigma_2\dot E_2+\tau\dot P+\kappa\dot P'-\dot\psi=\xi P^{-1}\dot P.
%\]
%By definition of subdifferential we have $0=R(0)\ge R(P^{-1}\dot P)-\xi P^{-1}\dot P$ so that $\xi P^{-1}\dot P\ge R(P^{-1}\dot P)\ge0$. This implies that the dissipation is nonnegative.
%\end{proof}
We will see particular examples of constitutive relations in the next section, after we have clarified which assumptions are required to produce boundary value problems which are well-posed. This is indeed the content of next section.

\section{Existence result}
\label{sec:existence-necking}

In this section we prove the existence of energetic solutions. A self-contained introduction to this solution concept, including an explanation of the notation employed in this section, can be found in Section~\ref{sec:energetic-solution} of Chapter~\ref{chap:solution-concepts}. The aim of this section is to verify the hypotheses required to apply the abstract existence result provided by Theorem~\ref{thm:existence-energetic-solutions}.

\subsection{Formulation of the initial boundary value problem}

We now describe how the model formulated in the previous section, can be treated within the framework of energetic solutions.

\subsubsection*{Dirichlet conditions}

Suppose that at time $t\in[0,T]$ the following Dirichlet conditions are imposed
\begin{equation}
\label{eqn:Dirichlet-necking}
\chi_i(X)=g_i(X,t)\quad\text{for $X\in\Gamma_D^i$ and $i\in\{1,2\}$},
\end{equation}
where $\Gamma_D^i\subset\{0,L\}$ and $g_i\colon(0,L)\times[0,T]\to\mathbb{R}$ satisfy $g_1'>0$ and $g_2>0$. To avoid free translations of the bar, it is required that $\Gamma_D^1\neq\emptyset$, but $\Gamma_D^2$ can be empty instead. Conditions~\eqref{eqn:Dirichlet-necking} can be converted into time-independent conditions by expressing $\chi_i$ as
\[
\chi_1(X)=g_1(y_1(X),t)\quad\text{and}\quad\chi_2(X)=g_2(y_1(X),t)y_2(X),
\]
with $y_i\colon(0,L)\to\mathbb{R}$, $y_1'>0$ and $y_2>0$, and requiring that
\[
y_1(X)=X\quad\text{for $X\in\Gamma_D^1$}\quad\text{and}\quad y_2(X)=1\quad\text{for $X\in\Gamma_D^2$}.
\]
Having made that change of variables, the unknown of the problem are $y$ and $P$. We notice that the elastic distortions $E_1,E_2$ and $G$ are given by
\[
E_1=g_1'(y_1,t)y_1'P^{-1},\quad E_2=g_2(y_1,t)y_2P\quad\text{and}\quad G=g_2'(y_1,t)y_1'y_2P^{-1}+g_2(y_1,t)y_2'P^{-1}.
\]

\subsubsection*{Function spaces}

Let $Y=W^{1,q_Y}((0,L),\mathbb{R}^2)$ with $q_Y\in(1,\infty)$ be the space of $y$ and $Q=L^{q_H}((0,L))\cap W^{1,r_H}((0,L))$ with $q_H\in(1,\infty)$, $r_H\in(1,q_H]$ be the space of $P$. Let $Z=Y\times Q$. The Dirichlet conditions are imposed requiring that $y,P$ belong respectively to the admissible spaces
\[
\mathscr{Y}=\left\{y\in Y\;\middle|\;\text{$y_1(X)=X$ for $X\in\Gamma_D^1$ and $y_2(X)=1$ for $X\in\Gamma_D^2$}\right\}\quad\text{and}\quad\mathscr{Q}=Q.
\]
Let $\mathscr{Z}=\mathscr{Y}\times\mathscr{Q}$. It is evident that $\mathscr{Z}$ is a weakly closed subset of $Z$. We also define the space of allowed variations in $\mathscr{Z}$ as
\[
Z_0=\left\{w=(v,q)\in Z\;\middle|\text{$w_1(X)=v_1(X)=0$ for $X\in\{0,L\}$}\right\}.
\]

\subsubsection*{Energy functional and dissipation distance}

To complete the formulation of the initial boundary value problem, we need to specify the energy functional $\mathcal{E}\colon\mathscr{Z}\times[0,T]\to(-\infty,\infty]$ and the dissipation distance $\mathcal{D}\colon\mathscr{Q}\times\mathscr{Q}\to[0,\infty]$. The energy functional is defined by
\begin{multline*}
\mathcal{E}(y,P,t)=\int_0^L W\left(g_1'(y_1,t)y_1'P^{-1},g_2(y_1,t)y_2P,g_2'(y_1,t)y_1'y_2P^{-1}+g_2(y_1,t)y_2'P^{-1}\right)dX+\\
+\int_0^LH(P,P')dX-\int_0^L\left(f_1(t)g_1(y_1,t)+f_2(t)g_2(y_1,t)y_2\right)dX.
\end{multline*}
The dissipation distance can be expressed in term of a dissipation distance density $D\colon\mathbb{R}^2\to[0,\infty]$ as
\[
\mathcal{D}(P_0,P_1)=\int_0^LD(P_0,P_1)dX.
\]
According to Remark~\ref{rmk:minimum-dissipation}, $D$ can be defined from the dissipation potential density $R$ by
\begin{equation}
\label{eqn:dissipation-distance-density}
D(P_0,P_1)=\inf\left\{\int_0^1R(P(t),\dot{P}(t))dt\;\middle|\;\text{$P\in C^1([0,1])$ and $P(i)=P_i$ for $i\in\{0,1\}$}\right\}.
\end{equation}

\subsection{Assumptions and preliminary results}

We now discuss the assumptions under which we will prove the validity of hypotheses ($\mathcal{E}$1)-($\mathcal{E}$4), ($\mathcal{D}$1)-($\mathcal{D}$2) and (C1)-(C2) of Section~\ref{sec:energetic-solution} in Appendix~\ref{chap:solution-concepts}. Subsequently, these assumptions will be implicitly supposed to be valid and will not be recalled.
\begin{rmk}[on notation]
Sometimes the notation $G=E_3$ and $E=(E_1,E_2,E_3)$ will be used. Given $E,N\in\mathbb{R}^3$, then $NE\in\mathbb{R}^3$ is the component-wise multiplication between $E$ and $N$, i.e., $(NE)_i=N_iE_i$ for $i\in\{1,2,3\}$.
\end{rmk}

The assumption on the Dirichlet data is
\begin{itemize}[itemsep=1em]
\item[(G)] $g_1\in C^2(\mathbb{R}\times[0,T])$, $g_2\in C^2(\mathbb{R}\times[0,T],(0,\infty))$ and there exists $0<\gamma<\Gamma<\infty$ for $i\in\{1,2\}$ such that $\gamma<g_1'<\Gamma$, $\gamma<g_2<\Gamma$ and $|g_2'|\le\Gamma$.
\end{itemize}
Only to prove Proposition~\ref{prop:power-control-necking} we will assume also the following additional requirement
\begin{itemize}
\item[($\hat{\text{G}}$)] $g_2'=0$.
\end{itemize}
We do not know whether the proof works without ($\hat{\text{G}}$). Anyway assuming ($\hat{\text{G}}$), it is not restrictive for our purposes.

The elastic free energy density is supposed to satisfy the following assumptions
\begin{itemize}[itemsep=1em]
\item[(W1)] there exists $\mathbb{W}\colon\mathbb{R}^4\to(-\infty,\infty]$ continuous such that
\[
W(E_1,E_2,G)=\mathbb{W}(E_1,E_2,G,E_1E_2)
\]
for all $E_1,E_2,G\in\mathbb{R}$ and $\mathbb{W}$ is convex in $(E_1,G,E_1E_2)$,
\item[(W2)] there exist $C_W>0$ and $q_W\in(1,\infty)$ such that $W(E_1,E_2,G)\ge C_W(-1+|E_1|^{q_W}+|E_2|^{q_W}+|G|^{q_W})$ for all $E_1,E_2,G\in\mathbb{R}$,
\item[(W3)] $W(E_1,E_2,G)=\infty$ if and only if $E_1\le0$ or $E_2\le0$,
\item[(W4)] $W$ is differentiable in $\dom W$ and there exist $C^W_0\in\mathbb{R}$, $C^W_1>0$ and a modulus of continuity $\omega^W$ such that
\begin{itemize}
\item[(i)] for all $E\in\dom W$ and $i\in\{1,2,3\}$ it holds $\left|E_i\dind{W}{E_i}(E)\right|\le C_1^W(C_0^W+W(E))$,
\item[(ii)] let $K(E)=E\cdot\dind{W}{E}(E)$, then $|K(NE)-K(E)|\le\omega^W(|N-1|)(W(E)+C_0^W)$ for all $N\in(0,\infty)^3$.
\end{itemize}
\end{itemize}
Assumption (W1) is a sort policonvexity requirement necessary to ensure lower semicontinuity, (W2) is a growth condition, (W3) is an incompenetrability condition for the elastic distortion and (W4) is a further regularity assumption which is needed to get suitable a priori estimates. Later on we will use the following consequence of (W4).
\begin{lemma}
\label{lemma:tipo-ball-kirchhoff-control}
Fix $0<\delta<1$ small enough. Then there exists $C>0$ such that
\[
W(NE)+\max_{i\in\{1,2,3\}}\left|E_i\dind{W}{E_i}(NE)\right|\le C(W(E)+C_0^W)
\]
for all $E\in(0,\infty)\times(0,\infty)\times\mathbb{R}$ and $N\in(1-\delta,1+\delta)^3$. Here $NE\in\mathbb{R}^3$ denotes the component-wise multiplication, i.e., $(NE)_i=N_iE_i$ for all $i\in\{1,2,3\}$.
\end{lemma}
\begin{proof}
\emph{Step 1.} For all $i\in\{1,2,3\}$, by (W4, i)
\[
\begin{split}
\left|E_i\dind{W}{E_i}(NE)\right|=|N_i|^{-1}\left|N_iE_i\dind{W}{E_i}(NE)\right|&\le|N_i|^{-1}C_1^W(C_0^W+W(NE))\le\\
&\le\frac{C_1^W}{1-\delta}(C_0^W+W(NE)).
\end{split}
\]

\emph{Step 2.} We have that
\[
|W(NE)-W(E)|=\left|\int_0^1\frac{d}{d\lambda}W(\tilde N(\lambda)E)d\lambda\right|,
\]
where $\tilde N(\lambda)=(1-\lambda)+\lambda N$, i.e., $\tilde N_i(\lambda)=(1-\lambda)+\lambda N_i\in(1-\delta,1+\delta)$ for all $i\in\{1,2,3\}$. Then
\[
\begin{split}
|W(NE)-W(E)|&=\left|\int_0^1\dind{W}{E}(\tilde N(\lambda)E)\cdot(N-1)d\lambda\right|\le\sum_{i=1}^3\int_0^1\left|\tilde N_i(\lambda)E_i\dind{W}{E_i}(\tilde N(\lambda)E)\right|\frac{|N_i-1|}{\tilde N_i(\lambda)}d\lambda\le\\
&\le\frac{\delta}{1-\delta}C_1^W\int_0^1(W(\tilde N(\lambda)E)+C_0^W)d\lambda.
\end{split}
\]

\emph{Step 3.} Let $\theta(E)=\sup_{N\in(1-\delta,1+\delta)^3}W(NE)$. From Step 2
\[
|W(NE)-W(E)|\le\theta(E)-W(E)\le\frac{\delta}{1-\delta}C_1^W(\theta(E)+C_0^W).
\]
This implies
\[
\left(1-\frac{\delta}{1-\delta}C_1^W\right)\theta(E)\le W(E)+\frac{\delta}{1-\delta}C_1^WC_0^W.
\]
If $\delta$ is small enough then
\[
\theta(E)\le\frac{W(E)+\frac{\delta}{1-\delta}C_1^WC_0^W}{1-\frac{\delta}{1-\delta}C_1^W}.
\]

\emph{Step 4.} From Step 3
\[
|W(NE)-W(E)|\le\frac{\delta}{1-\delta}C_1^W\left(\frac{W(E)+\frac{\delta}{1-\delta}C_1^WC_0^W}{1-\frac{\delta}{1-\delta}C_1^W}+C_0^W\right)\le\left(\frac{1}{\frac{\delta}{1-\delta}C_1^W}-1\right)^{-1}(W(E)+C_0^W).
\]

\emph{Step 5.} From Step 1
\[
\begin{split}
\max_{i\in\{1,2,3\}}\left|E_i\dind{W}{E_i}(NE)\right|\le\frac{C_1^W}{1-\delta}(C_0^W+\theta(E))&\le\frac{C_1^W}{1-\delta}\left(\frac{W(E)+\frac{\delta}{1-\delta}C_1^WC_0^W}{1-\frac{\delta}{1-\delta}C_1^W}+C_0^W\right)=\\
&=\frac{C_1^W}{1-\delta(1+C_1^W)}(W(E)+C_0^W).
\end{split}
\]
\emph{Step 6.} Summing together the results of Step 4 and Step 5 we get the thesis.
\end{proof}
The plastic free energy density satisfies
\begin{itemize}[itemsep=1em]
\item[(H1)] $H\colon\mathbb{R}^2\to(-\infty,\infty]$ is continuous and $H(P,\cdot)$ is convex for all $P\in\mathbb{R}$,
\item[(H2)] there exist $C_H>0$ and $q_H\in(1,\infty)$, $r_H\in(1,q_H]$ such that $H(P,P')\ge C_H(-1+|P|^{q_H}+|P'|^{r_H})$ for all $P,P'\in\mathbb{R}$,
\item[(H3)] there exists $\Pi>0$ such that $H(P,P')=\infty$ if and only if $P\le\Pi$.
\end{itemize}
The convexity assumption (H1) is required for lower semicontinuity, (H2) is a growth condition ensuring that the hardening and the plastic gradient regularization is sufficiently strong. Assumption (H3) is a strong incompenetrability condition for the plastic distortion.

To handle correctly the nonlinear coupling between elastic and plastic distortions, we require the following compatibility condition on the H\"older exponents
\begin{itemize}
\item[(\"O)] $\frac{1}{q_Y}=\frac{1}{q_W}+\frac{1}{q_H}$.
\end{itemize}

The requirements on the dissipation potential density are
\begin{itemize}[itemsep=1em]
\item[(d1)] $D\colon\mathbb{R}^2\to[0,\infty]$ is lower semicontinuous,
\item[(d2)] $D$ is a quasidistance, in the sense that $D(P_0,P_1)=0$ if and only if $P_0=P_1$ and $D(P_0,P_2)\le D(P_0,P_1)+D(P_1,P_2)$ for all $P_0,P_1,P_2\in\mathbb{R}$,
\item[(d3)] $D$ is finite and continuous on $[\Pi,\infty)\times[\Pi,\infty)$, where $\Pi$ is the constant appearing in (H3).
\end{itemize}

\subsection{Verification the properties of the energy functional}

We start with the following lemma which is necessary to handle the nonlinear coupling between elastic and plastic distortion.
\begin{lemma}
\label{lemma:inverse-young}
Let $a,b>0$. Then for all $\delta>0$ and $\rho>1$
\[
\frac{a}{b}\ge\rho\delta^\frac{\rho-1}{\rho}a^\frac{1}{\rho}-(\rho-1)\delta b^\frac{1}{\rho-1}
\]
\end{lemma}
\begin{proof}
By Young inequality, if $A,B>0$ then
\[
AB\le\frac{A^\rho}{\rho}+\frac{B^{\rho^*}}{\rho^*}
\]
and
\[
A^\frac{1}{\rho}B^\frac{1}{\rho^*}\le\frac{A}{\rho}+\frac{B}{\rho^*}.
\]
It follows that
\[
A\ge\rho A^\frac{1}{\rho}B^\frac{1}{\rho^*}-\frac{\rho}{\rho^*}B.
\]
If we put $A=a/b$ and $B$ such that
\[
B^\frac{1}{\rho^*}=\delta^\frac{1}{\rho^*}b^\frac{1}{\rho}
\]
the thesis follows.
\end{proof}
We now prove that ($\mathcal{E}$2) is satisfied. We start with proving the boundedness of sublevels.
\begin{prop}[Boundedness of energy sublevels]
The sublevels of $\mathcal{E}(\cdot,t)$ are bounded in $Z$ for all $t\in[0,T]$.
\end{prop}
\begin{proof}
From (W1) and (H1) it follows
\begin{equation}
\label{eqn:LS-neck-coercivity-1}
\mathcal{E}(y,P,t)\ge
\begin{multlined}[t]
C\Big(-1+\norm{g_1'(y_1,t)y_1'P^{-1}}_{q_W}^{q_W}+\norm{g_2(y_1,t)y_2P}_{q_W}^{q_W}+\\
+\norm{g_2'(y_1,t)y_1'y_2P^{-1}+g_2(y_1,t)y_2'P^{-1}}_{q_W}^{q_W}+\norm{P}_{q_H}^{q_H}+\norm{P'}_{r_H}^{r_H}\Big).
\end{multlined}
\end{equation}
By H\"older inequality
\[
\norm{y_1'}_{q_Y}=\norm{y_1'P^{-1}P}_{q_Y}\le\norm{y_1'P^{-1}}_{q_W}\norm{P}_{q_H}
\]
so that using Lemma~\ref{lemma:inverse-young} with $\rho=q_W/q_Y$ one gets
\[
\norm{y_1'P^{-1}}_{q_W}^{q_W}\ge\frac{\norm{y_1'}_q^{q_W}}{\norm{P}_{q_H}^{q_W}}\ge\rho\delta^\frac{\rho-1}{\rho}\norm{y_1'}_{q_Y}^{q_Y}-(\rho-1)\delta\norm{P}_{q_H}^{q_H}
\]
for all $\delta>0$. We have used that $q_W/(\rho-1)=q_H$. Moreover
\[
\norm{y_1'P^{-1}}_{q_W}=\norm{g_1'(y_1,t)^{-1}g_1'(y_1,t)y_1'P^{-1}}_{q_W}\le\gamma^{-1}\norm{g_1'(y_1,t)y_1'P^{-1}}_{q_W}.
\]
This implies that
\begin{equation}
\label{eqn:bound-y1'}
\norm{g_1'(y_1,t)y_1'P^{-1}}_{q_W}^{q_W}\ge\gamma^{q_W}\rho\delta^\frac{\rho-1}{\rho}\norm{y_1'}_{q_Y}^{q_Y}-\gamma^{q_W}(\rho-1)\delta\norm{P}_{q_H}^{q_H}.
\end{equation}
Similarly by H\"older and Young inequalities, for all $\delta>0$
\[
\norm{y_2}_{q_Y}^{q_Y}=\norm{y_2PP^{-1}}_{q_Y}^{q_Y}\le\norm{y_2P}_{q_W}^{q_Y}\norm{P^{-1}}_{q_H}^{q_Y}\le\frac{1}{\rho\delta^\rho}\norm{y_2P}_{q_W}^{q_W}+\frac{\delta^{\rho^*}}{\rho^*}\norm{P^{-1}}_{q_H}^{q_H}\le\frac{1}{\rho\delta^\rho}\norm{y_2P}_{q_W}^{q_W}+\frac{\delta^{\rho^*}}{\rho^*}L\Pi^{-q_H}
\]
where now $\rho=1+q_W/q_H$. Last inequality holds since $P>\Pi$ a.e.\ on the sublevels of $\mathcal{E}$. Then
\begin{equation}
\label{eqn:bound-y2}
\norm{g_2(y_1,t)y_2P}_{q_W}^{q_W}\ge\gamma^{q_W}\rho\delta^\rho\norm{y_2}_q^q-\gamma^{q_W}\frac{\rho}{\rho^*}\delta^{\rho+\rho^*}\Pi^{-q_H}.
\end{equation}
Substituting~\eqref{eqn:bound-y1'} and~\eqref{eqn:bound-y2} into \eqref{eqn:LS-neck-coercivity-1} with $\delta$ sufficiently small, one gets that on sublevels of $\mathcal{E}$ the norms $\norm{y_1'}_{q_Y}$, $\norm{y}_{q_Y}$, $\norm{P}_{q_H}$ and $\norm{P'}_{r_H}$ are bounded. By PoincarĂ© inequality it follows that also $\norm{y_1}_{1,{q_Y}}$ is bounded. It remains to prove that $\norm{y_2'}_{q_Y}$ is bounded. Let
\[
G=g_2'(y_1,t)y_1'y_2P^{-1}+g_2(y_1,t)y_2'P^{-1}.
\]
Then
\[
y_2'=\frac{GP-g_2'(y_1,t)y_1'y_2}{g_2(y_1,t)}.
\]
Il follows that
\[
\begin{split}
\norm{y_2'}_{q_Y}&\le\frac{1}{\gamma}\norm{GP}_{q_Y}+\frac{1}{\gamma}\norm{g_2'(y_1,t)y_1'y_2}_{q_Y}\le\frac{1}{\gamma}\norm{G}_{q_W}\norm{P}_{q_H}+\frac{\Gamma}{\gamma}\norm{y_1'y_2}_{q_Y}\le\\
&\le\frac{1}{\gamma}\norm{G}_{q_W}\norm{P}_{q_H}+\frac{\Gamma}{\gamma}\norm{y_1'}_{q_Y}\norm{y_2}_\infty.
\end{split}
\]
Since $L^\infty((0,T))$ is continuously embedded in $W^{1,q_Y}((0,L))$, it follows that for all $\epsilon>0$ there exists $C>0$ such that
\[
\norm{y_2}_\infty\le C(\norm{y_2}_{q_Y}+\epsilon\norm{y_2'}_{q_Y})\quad\text{for all $y_2\in W^{1,{q_Y}}((0,L))$}.
\]
On a sublevel of $\mathcal{E}$ the quantities $\norm{G}_{q_W}$, $\norm{P}_{q_H}$, $\norm{y_1'}_{q_Y}$ and $\norm{y_2}_{q_Y}$ are bounded, so that there exists $M>0$ such that
\[
\norm{y_2'}_{q_Y}\le M(1+\epsilon\norm{y_2'}_{q_Y}).
\]
Choosing $\epsilon$ small enough, it follows that $\norm{y_2'}_{q_Y}$ is bounded on the sublevels of $\mathcal{E}$. This concludes the proof.
\end{proof}
We now move to the proof of the weak lower semicontinuity of $\mathcal E$. We first need the following technical result.
\begin{prop}[convergence of elastic distortion]
\label{prop:convergence-minors}
Let $y^k,y\in W^{1,q_Y}((0,L),\mathbb{R}\times\mathbb{R})$ and $P_k,P\in C^0([0,L])$ such that
\begin{gather*}
y^k\rightharpoonup y\quad\text{in $W^{1,q_Y}((0,L))$} \\
P_k\to P\quad\text{in $C^0([0,L])$}.
\end{gather*}
Assume also that $P_k,P\ge \Pi>0$ a.e.\ in $(0,L)$. Let also $\gamma_1^k,\gamma_1,\gamma_2^k,\gamma_2,\rho_2^k,\rho_2\in C^0([0,L])$ such that
\begin{align*}
\gamma_1^k\to\gamma_1\quad\text{in $C^0([0,L])$} \\
\gamma_2^k\to\gamma_2\quad\text{in $C^0([0,L])$} \\
\rho_2^k\to\rho_2\quad\text{in $C^0([0,L])$}
\end{align*}
Then, up to extracting a subsequence,
\begin{align*}
\gamma_1^k(y_1^k)'P_k^{-1}&\rightharpoonup \gamma_1y_1'P^{-1}\quad&\text{in $L^1((0,L))$} \\
\gamma_2^ky_2^kP_k&\to \gamma_2y_2P\quad&\text{in $C^0([0,L])$} \\
\rho_2^k(y_1^k)'y_2^kP_k^{-1}+\gamma_2^k(y_2^k)'P_k^{-1}&\rightharpoonup \rho_2y_1'y_2P^{-1}+\gamma_2y_2'P^{-1}\quad&\text{in $L^1((0,L))$} \\
\gamma_1^k(y_1^k)'\gamma_2^ky_2^k&\rightharpoonup \gamma_1y_1'\gamma_2y_2\quad&\text{in $L^1((0,L))$}
\end{align*}
\end{prop}
\begin{proof}
First notice that $P_k^{-1}\to P^{-1}$ in $L^\infty((0,L))$. Indeed
\[
|P_k^{-1}-P^{-1}|=\frac{|P_k-P|}{|P_kP|}\le\frac{|P_k-P|}{\Pi^2}.
\]
Notice also that, since $W^{1,q}((0,L))$ is compactly embedded in $C^0([0,L])$, up to extracting a subsequence $y_2^k\to y_2$ in $C^0([0,L])$. Now the results follows from Lemma~\ref{lemma:lemmetto-cretino} below.
\end{proof}
\begin{lemma}
\label{lemma:lemmetto-cretino}
Let $f_k,f\in L^q((0,L))$  with $q\in(1,\infty)$, $g_k,g\in L^\infty((0,L))$ and $h_k,h\in C^0([0,L])$ such that
\begin{gather*}
f_k\rightharpoonup f\quad\text{in $L^q((0,L))$} \\
g_k\to g\quad\text{in $L^\infty((0,L))$} \\
h_k\to h\quad\text{in $C^0([0,L])$}.
\end{gather*}
Then $f_kg_kh_k\rightharpoonup fgh$ in $L^1((0,L))$.
\end{lemma}
\begin{proof}
Let $\phi\in L^\infty((0,L))$. Then
\begin{multline*}
\left|\int_0^L(f_kg_kh_k-fgh)\phi dX\right|\le\\
\le\left|\int_0^L(g_k-g)f_kh_k\phi dX\right|+\left|\int_0^Lgf_k(h_k-h)\phi dX\right|+\left|\int_0^Lg(f_k-f)h\phi dX\right|.
\end{multline*}
Since $f_k$ are bounded in $L^q((0,L))$, $h_k$ are bounded in $C^0([0,L])$, then $f_kh_k\phi$ and $gf_k\phi$ are bounded in $L^1((0,L))$. Moreover $gh\phi\in L^{q^*}((0,L))$. Then the thesis follows.
\end{proof}
We are now ready to get the weakly lower semicontinuity of $\mathcal{E}$.
\begin{prop}[lower semicontinuity of the energy]
For all $t\in[0,T]$ the map $\mathcal{E}(\cdot,t)$ is lower semicontinuous.
\end{prop}
\begin{proof}
Let $(y^k,P_k)\rightharpoonup(y,P)$ in $Y\times Q$. We have to prove that
\[
\mathcal{E}(y,P,t)\le\liminf_{k\to\infty}\mathcal{E}(y_k,P_k,t).
\]
We take a (not relabeled) subsequence such that $\mathcal{E}(y_k,P_k,t)$ has a limit. Then we need to show that
\begin{equation}
\label{eqn:lower-semicontinuity-E-limit}
\mathcal{E}(y,P,t)\le\lim_{k\to\infty}\mathcal{E}(y_k,P_k,t).
\end{equation}
If $\mathcal{E}(y_k,P_k,t)\to\infty$ we have nothing to prove. Then we assume that instead it converges to a finite limit. In this case we can assume that $\mathcal{E}(y_k,P_k,t)<\infty$ for all $k$. This implies that $P_k>\Pi$ a.e.\ in $(0,L)$, so that also $P\ge \Pi$ a.e.\ in $(0,L)$.

By Rellich--Kondrachov theorem, up to extracting a subsequence, $y_i^k\to y_i$ for $i\in\{1,2\}$ and $P_k\to P$ in $C^0([0,L])$.  Moreover $g_1'(y_1^k,t)\to g_1'(y_1,t)$, $g_2(y_1^k,t)\to g_2(y_1,t)$ and $g_2'(y_1^k,t)\to g_2'(y_1,t)$ in $C^0([0,L])$. Indeed if $\gamma\in C^1(\mathbb{R})$ then
\begin{equation}
\label{eqn:bound-gamma}
\begin{split}
|\gamma(y_1^k)-\gamma(y_1)|&=\left|\int_0^1\frac{d}{d\lambda}\gamma((1-\lambda)y_1^k+\lambda y_1)d\lambda\right|=\left|\int_0^1\gamma'((1-\lambda)y_1^k+\lambda y_1)(y_1-y_1^k)d\lambda\right|\le\\
&\le\norm{y_1-y_1^k}_\infty\int_0^1|\gamma'((1-\lambda)y_1^k+\lambda y_1)|d\lambda.
\end{split}
\end{equation}
Since $y_1^k$ converges to $y_1$ uniformly, then $y_1^k(t)$ takes values in a bounded set as $k\in\mathbb{N}$ and $t\in[0,T]$. Then the continuity of $\gamma'$ implies that the last integral in~\eqref{eqn:bound-gamma}. We have proved that $\gamma(y_1^k)\to\gamma(y_1)$ in $C^0([0,T])$, and this provides the desired results.

Applying Proposition~\ref{prop:convergence-minors}, it follows that, up to extracting a subsequence,
\begin{align*}
g_1'(y_1^k,t)(y_1^k)'P_k^{-1}&\rightharpoonup g_1(y_1,t)y_1'P^{-1}\quad&\text{in $L^1((0,L))$} \\
g_2(y_1^k,t)y_2^kP_k&\to g_2(y_1,t)y_2P\quad&\text{in $C^0([0,L])$} \\
g_2'(y_1^k,t)(y_1^k)'y_2^kP_k^{-1}+g_2(y_1^k,t)(y_2^k)'P_k^{-1}&\rightharpoonup g_2'(y_1,t)y_1'y_2P^{-1}+g_2(y_1,t)y_2'P^{-1}\quad&\text{in $L^1((0,L))$} \\
g_1'(y_1^k,t)(y_1^k)'g_2(y_1^k,t)y_2^k&\rightharpoonup g_1'(y_1,t)y_1'g_2(y_1,t)y_2\quad&\text{in $L^1((0,L))$}.
\end{align*}
By these convergence results, together with $P_k\to P$ in $C^0([0,L])$ and $P_k'\rightharpoonup P'$ in $L^{r_H}((0,T))$, and by the convexity assumptions (W0) and (H0), we can apply Theorem~\ref{thm:lower-semicontinuity-integral-functional} which leads to~\eqref{eqn:lower-semicontinuity-E-limit}. This concludes the proof.
\end{proof}
Until now we have established ($\mathcal{E}$1) and ($\mathcal{E}$2). We now prove ($\mathcal{E}$3) and ($\mathcal{E}$4).
\begin{prop}
\label{prop:power-control-necking}
Then there exists $\mathscr{D}\subset\mathscr{Z}$ such that $\dom\mathcal{E}(\cdot,t)=\mathscr{D}$ for all $t\in[0,T]$. Moreover
\begin{itemize}
\item[(i)] for all $z\in\mathscr{D}$ the function $\mathcal{E}(z,\cdot)$ is $C^1([0,T])$,
\item[(ii)] there exist $C_1^E$ and $C_0^E$ such that $|\partial_t\mathcal{E}(z,t)|\le C_1^E(C_0^E+\mathcal{E}(z,t))$ for all $z\in\mathscr{Z}$ and $t\in[0,T]$,
\item[(iii)] there exists a modulus of continuity $\omega^E$ such that for all $z\in\mathscr{D}$ and $t,s\in[0,T]$,
\[
|\partial_t\mathcal{E}(z,t)-\partial_t\mathcal{E}(z,s)|\le\omega^E(|t-s|)\min\{\mathcal{E}(z,t)+C_0^W,\mathcal{E}(z,s)+C_0^W\}.
\]
\end{itemize}
\end{prop}
\begin{proof}
Since $g_1'>0$ and $g_2>0$, it is clear that
\[
\mathscr{D}=\left\{(y,P)\in Y\times Q\;\middle|\;\text{$y_1',y_2,P>0$ a.e.\ on $(0,L)$}\right\}.
\]

\emph{Proof of (i)} Let $E_1=g_1'(y_1,t)y_1'P^{-1}$, $E_2=g_2(y_1,t)y_2P$ and $G=g_2(y_1,t)y_2'P^{-1}$ as functions of $(y,y',P,t)\in\mathbb{R}^2\times\mathbb{R}^2\times\mathbb{R}\times t\in[0,T]$. Let $q=(y,P)\in\mathscr{D}$. Then $(E_1,E_2,G)\in\dom W$ a.e.\ on $(0,L)$ and everywhere on $[0,T]$. Then $t\mapsto W(E_1,E_2,G)$ is differentiable in $[0,T]$ and a.e.\ in $(0,L)$ and
\[
\frac{d}{dt}W(E_1,E_2,G)=E_1\dind{W}{E_1}(E_1,E_2,G)\frac{\dot{g}'_1(y_1,t)}{g'_1(y_1,t)}+E_2\dind{W}{E_2}(E_1,E_2,G)\frac{\dot{g}_2(y_1,t)}{g_2(y_1,t)}+G\dind{W}{G}(E_1,E_2,G)\frac{\dot{g}_2(y_1,t)}{g_2(y_1,t)}
\]
there. We have to prove that for all $t\in[0,T]$, there exists
\begin{equation}
\label{eqn:partial-time-derivative-energy}
\partial_t\mathcal{E}(z,t)=\lim_{\epsilon\to0}\frac{\mathcal{E}(z,t+\epsilon)-\mathcal{E}(z,t)}{\epsilon}=\int_0^L\frac{d}{dt}W(E_1,E_2,G)dX.
\end{equation}
By Lagrange theorem there exists $\eta\in(0,1)$ depending on $X$, $t$ and $\epsilon$, such that
\[
\frac{\mathcal{E}(z,t+\epsilon)-\mathcal{E}(z,t)}{\epsilon}=\int_0^LW(g_1'(y_1,t+\eta\epsilon)y_1'P^{-1},g_2(y_1,t+\eta\epsilon)y_2P,g_2(y_1,t+\eta\epsilon)y_2'P^{-1})dX.
\]
For $\epsilon$ small, $g_1'(y_1,t+\eta\epsilon)/g_1'(y_1,t)$ and $g_2(y_1,t+\eta\epsilon)/g_2(y_1,t)$ are close to 1 uniformly on $(0,L)\times[0,T]$. Thus, for $\epsilon$ small enough, we can apply Lemma~\ref{lemma:tipo-ball-kirchhoff-control} and we get
\begin{multline*}
-C_0^W\le W(g_1'(y_1,t+\eta\epsilon)y_1'P^{-1},g_2(y_1,t+\eta\epsilon)y_2P,g_2(y_1,t+\eta\epsilon)y_2'P^{-1})\le\\
\le C(W(g_1'(y_1,t)y_1'P^{-1},g_2(y_1,t)y_2P,g_2(y_1,t)y_2'P^{-1})+C_0^W)\in L^1((0,L)).
\end{multline*}
Then, by dominated convergence theorem, we can pass to the limit $\epsilon\to 0$ and we get~\eqref{eqn:partial-time-derivative-energy}.

\emph{Proof of (ii).} Taking into account the fact that $\frac{\dot{g}'_1}{g'_1}$ and $\frac{\dot{g}_2}{g_2}$ are bounded in $\mathbb{R}\times[0,T]$, assumption (W4, i) yields
\[
|\partial_t\mathcal{E}(y,P,t)|\le 3C_1^W\max\left\{\bigg\lVert\frac{\dot{g}'_1}{g'_1}\bigg\rVert_\infty,\bigg\lVert\frac{\dot{g}_2}{g_2}\bigg\rVert_\infty\right\}\int_\Omega(C_0^W+W(E_1,E_2,G))dX.
\]
This implies the existence of $C_0^E\in\mathbb{R}$ and $C_1^E>0$ such that $|\partial_t\mathcal{E}(q,t)|\le C_1^E(C_0^E+\mathcal{E}(q,t))$ for all $(q,t)\in\mathscr{D}\times[0,T]$.

\emph{Proof of (iii).} Let
\[
K_i=E_i\dind{W}{E_i}\quad\text{for $i\in\{1,2,3\}$}\quad\text{and}\quad V=\left(\frac{\dot{g}'_1(y_1,t)}{g'_1(y_1,t)},\frac{\dot{g}_2(y_1,t)}{g_2(y_1,t)},\frac{\dot{g}_2(y_1,t)}{g_2(y_1,t)}\right)
\]
as functions of $(X,t)\in(0,L)\times[0,T]$. Then
\[
\frac{d}{dt}W(E)=K\cdot V.
\]
Let $E\in\mathbb{R}$ and $z\in\mathscr{D}$ such that $\sup_{t\in[0,T]}\mathcal{E}(z,t)$ and let $t,s\in[0,T]$. By the regularity properties (G) of $g$, there exists modulus of continuity $\omega^V$ and $\omega^G$ independent of $q$ such that
\[
|V(t)-V(s)|\le\omega^V(|t-s|)\quad\text{and}\quad\max\left\{\frac{g_1'(y_1,s)}{g_1'(y_1,t)},\frac{g_2(y_1,s)}{g_2(y_1,t)}\right\}\le\omega^V(|t-s|)
\]
on $(0,L)$. Moreover $|K|\le 3C_1^W(W(E)+C_0^W)$ and there exists $M>0$ independent of $z$, such that $\norm{V}_\infty\le M$. Then by (W2, i) and (W2, ii),
\[
\begin{split}
|\partial_t\mathcal{E}(z,t)-\partial_t\mathcal{E}(z,s)|&\le\int_0^L|K(t)\cdot V(t)-K(s)\cdot V(s)|dX\le\\
&\le\int_0^L|K(t)-K(s)||V(s)|dX+\int_0^L|K(t)||V(t)-V(s)|dX\le\\
&\le \left(L\omega^W(\omega^G(|t-s|))M+3C_1^W\omega^V(|t-s|)\right)\int_0^L(W(E(t))+C_0^W)dX\\
&\le\left(L\omega^W(\omega^G(|t-s|))M+3C_1^W\omega^V(|t-s|)\right)(\mathcal{E}(z,t)+C_0^W).
\end{split}
\]
This concludes the proof.
\end{proof}

\subsection{Verification the properties of the dissipation distance}

Notice that (d1) implies that $\mathcal{D}$ is well defined. Property ($\mathcal{D}$1) follows immediately form (d2). We now prove ($\mathcal{D}$2).
\begin{prop}
Let $P_k\rightharpoonup P$ and $\hat{P}_k\rightharpoonup\hat{P}$ in $Q$. Then $\mathcal{D}(P,\hat{P})\le\liminf_{k\to\infty}\mathcal{D}(P_k,\hat{P}_k)$.
\end{prop}
\begin{proof}
Let $P_k,P,\hat{P}_k,\hat{P}\in Q$ such that
\[
P_k\rightharpoonup P\quad\text{and}\quad\hat{P}_k\rightharpoonup\hat{P}\quad\text{in $Q$.}
\]
By Proposition~\ref{prop:sobolev-weak-lp-strong} it follows that
\[
P_k\to P\quad\text{and}\quad\hat{P}_k\to\hat{P}\quad\text{in $C^0([0,L])$.}
\]
Now
\[
\liminf_{k\to\infty}\mathcal{D}(P_k,\hat{P}_k)=\liminf_{k\to\infty}\int_0^LD(P_k,\hat{P}_k)dX=\lim_{k\to\infty}\inf_{j\ge k}\int_0^LD(P_j,\hat{P}_j)dX\ge\lim_{k\to\infty}\int_0^L\inf_{j\ge k}D(P_j,\hat{P}_j)dX.
\]
By monotone convergence the last limit can be took inside the integral yielding
\[
\liminf_{k\to\infty}\mathcal{D}(P_k,\hat{P}_k)\ge\int_0^L\liminf_{k\to\infty}D(P_k,\hat{P}_k)dX\ge\int_0^L D(P,\hat{P})dX.
\]
The last inequality holds since $D$ is lower semicontinuous. This concludes the proof.
\end{proof}

\subsection{Verification of the compatibility conditions}

We now verify (C1) and (C2). In view of Proposition~\ref{prop:tool-verify-compatibility} we need to verify (PC1) and (PC2). Notice that (PC2) has been established in Proposition~\ref{prop:power-control-necking} (iii). Thus we need to verify (PC1) only. A stronger version of (PC1) is the following proposition.
\begin{prop}
Let $(z_k,t_k),(z,t)\in Z\times[0,T]$ such that $(z_k,t_k)\rightharpoonup(q,t)$ in $Z\times[0,T]$ and $z_k\in\mathscr{D}$. Then for all $\hat{z}\in Z$,
\[
\limsup_{k\to\infty}\left(\mathcal{E}(\hat{z},t_k)+\mathcal{D}(z_k,\hat{z})\right)\le\mathcal{E}(\hat{z},t)+\mathcal{D}(z,\hat{z}).
\]
\end{prop}
\begin{proof}
Let $(z_k,t_k)\rightharpoonup (z,t)$ in $Z\times[0,T]$. If $\hat{z}\notin\mathscr{D}$ there is nothing to prove since $\mathcal{E}(\hat{z},t)=\infty$. Then assume that $\hat{z}\in\mathscr{D}$. By Proposition`\eqref{prop:power-control-necking} the map $\mathcal{E}(\hat{z},\cdot)$ is continuous. Then $\mathcal{E}(\hat{z},t_k)\to\mathcal{E}(\hat{z},t)$ and it remains to prove only that
\[
\limsup_{k\to\infty}\mathcal{D}(z_k,\hat{z})\le\mathcal{D}(z,\hat{z}).
\]
Recall that this is the same as
\[
\limsup_{k\to\infty}\mathcal{D}(P_k,\hat{P})\le\mathcal{D}(P,\hat{P}).
\]
Since $\hat{z}\in\mathscr{D}$, then (H3) implies that $\hat{P}>\Pi$ a.e.\ in $(0,L)$, so that $\hat{P}\ge \Pi$ in $[0,L]$. Since also $z_k\in\mathscr{D}$ then, for the same reason, also $P_k\ge \Pi$ in $[0,L]$. Notice also that by~\ref{prop:sobolev-weak-lp-strong}, $P_k\to P$ in $C^0([0,T])$. Then actually
\[
\lim_{k\to\infty}\mathcal{D}(P_k,\hat{P})=\lim_{k\to\infty}\int_0^LD(P_p,\hat{P})dX=\mathcal{D}(P,\hat{P}),
\]
where last inequality holds because $D$ is finite and continuous on $[\Pi,\infty)\times[\Pi,\infty)$ by (d3). This concludes the proof.
\end{proof}

\subsection{Conclusion of the existence proof}

All the hypotheses of Theorem~\ref{thm:existence-energetic-solutions} have been established. Thus applying that theorem we get desired existence result.
\begin{thm}
\label{thm:existence-necking-large}
Let $z_0=(y_0,P_0)\in\mathscr{Z}$ such that $\mathcal{E}(z_0,0)<\infty$. Then there exists an energetic solution $z=(y,P)\colon[0,T]\to\mathscr{Z}$ such that $z(0)=z_0$. 
\end{thm}

\subsection{Particular constitutive relations that satisfy the hypotheses of the existence theorem}

\label{subsec:constitutive-large-neck}

We now discuss particular constitutive relations that satisfies the hypotheses required to apply Theorem~\ref{thm:existence-necking-large}.

Taking inspiration from the neo-Hooken elastic free energy density~\eqref{eqn:necking-large:neo-hookean}, we consider
\[
W(E_1,E_2,G)=
\begin{cases}
\mathbb{W}(E_1,E_2,G,E_1E_2) & \text{if $E_1>0$ and $E_2>0$} \\
\infty & \text{otherwise},
\end{cases}
\]
with
\[
\quad \mathbb{W}(E_1,E_2,G,J)=A\frac{|E_1-1|^p+|E_2-1|^p+|lG|^p}{J^\alpha}+B|J-1|^r
\]
where $A,B,l>0$, $r>1$, $0<\alpha\le p-1$ and $p>1+\frac{\alpha}{r}$. The parameter $l>0$ is connected with the diameter of the cross section $\Sigma$. We now check that this particular $W$ satisfies (W1), (W2), (W3) and (W4) of previous section. We start with the check of the assumptions (W1) and (W2). Hypotheses (W1) is a consequence of the following lemma.
\begin{lemma}
Let $w\colon(0,\infty)\to\mathbb{R}$ strictly convex and let $g\colon(0,\infty)\times(0,\infty)\to\mathbb{R}$ given by $g(r,y)=\frac{r^p}{y^\alpha}+w(y)$ for some $p>1$ and $0<\alpha\le p-1$. Let also $n\in\mathbb{N}$ and $f\colon\mathbb{R}^n\times(0,\infty)\to\mathbb{R}$ given by $f(x,y)=g(|x|,y)$. Then $f$ is strictly convex in $\mathbb{R}^n\times(0,\infty)$.
\end{lemma}
\begin{proof}
We first prove that $g$ is strictly convex. The hessian matrix of $g$ is given by
\[
\nabla^2g(r,y)=\begin{pmatrix}
p(p-1)\frac{r^{p-2}}{y^\alpha} & -p\alpha\frac{r^{p-1}}{y^{\alpha+1}} \\
-p\alpha\frac{r^{p-1}}{y^{\alpha+1}} & \alpha(\alpha+1)\frac{r^p}{y^{\alpha+2}}+w''(y)
\end{pmatrix}.
\]
Then
\begin{equation}
\begin{split}
\det\nabla^2g(r,p)&=p(p-1)\frac{r^{p-2}}{y^\alpha}\left(\alpha(\alpha+1)\frac{r^p}{y^{\alpha+2}}+w''(y)\right)-p^2\alpha^2\frac{r^{2p-2}}{y^{\alpha+2}}=\\
&=p\alpha(p-\alpha-1)\frac{r^{2p-2}}{y^{\alpha+2}}+p(p-1)\frac{r^{p-2}}{y^\alpha}w''(y)>0.
\end{split}
\end{equation}
and
\[
\tr\nabla^2g(r,y)=p(p-1)\frac{r^{p-2}}{y^\alpha}+\alpha(\alpha+1)\frac{r^p}{y^{\alpha+2}}+w''(y)>0.
\]
This proves that $\nabla^2g$ is everywhere positive-definite. This implies that $g$ is strictly convex. Then for any $(x_0,y_0),(x_1,y_1)\in\mathbb{R}^n\times(0,\infty)$ and $0<\lambda<1$ it holds
\[
\begin{split}
f((1-\lambda)(x_0,y_0)+\lambda(x_1,y_1))&=\frac{|(1-\lambda)x_0+\lambda x_1|^p}{y^\alpha}+w(y)\le\\
&\le\frac{\left[(1-\lambda)|x_0|+\lambda |x_1|\right]^p}{\left[(1-\lambda)y_0+\lambda y_1)\right]^\alpha}+w((1-\lambda)y_0+\lambda y_1))=\\
&=g((1-\lambda)(|x_0|,y_0)+\lambda(|x_1|,y_1))<(1-\lambda)g(|x_0|,y_0)+\lambda g(|x_1|,y_1)=\\
&=(1-\lambda)f(x_0,y_0)+\lambda f(x_1,y_1).
\end{split}
\]
This is exactly the thesis.
\end{proof}
We now prove the growth condition (W2) for $q_W=p/\left(1+\frac{\alpha}{r}\right)>1$. Lemma~\ref{lemma:inverse-young} yields for all $\delta>0$ and $\rho>1$ that
\begin{multline}
\mathbb{W}(E_1,E_2,G,J)\ge A\rho\delta^\frac{\rho-1}{\rho}|E_1-1|^\frac{p}{\rho}+A\rho\delta^\frac{\rho-1}{\rho}|E_2-1|^\frac{p}{\rho}+A\rho\delta^\frac{\rho-1}{\rho}|lG|^\frac{p}{\rho}-\\
-(2A+1)(\rho-1)\delta J^\frac{\alpha}{\rho-1}+B|J-1|^r.
\end{multline}
Choosing $\rho$ such that $\frac{\alpha}{\rho-1}=r$ we get $\frac{p}{\rho}=q_H$. Moreover choosing $\delta>0$ sufficiency small we have
\[
\inf_{J>0}\left[-(2A+1)(\rho-1)\delta J^r+B|J-1|^r\right]>-\infty.
\]
This is implied immediately from the following lemma.
\begin{lemma}
Let $r\ge1$. Then there exists $C>0$ such that $|x-1|^r\ge C|x|^r-1$ for all $x\in\mathbb{R}$.
\end{lemma}
\begin{proof}
By the equivalence of the norms there exists $K>0$ such that
\[
(|x-1|^r+1)^{1/r}\ge K(|x-1|+1)\ge K|x|.
\]
Now the thesis follows immediately.
\end{proof}
A possible choice of the hardening free energy density is
\[
H(P,P')=
\begin{cases}
C(P-1)^q+\frac{D}{P-\Pi}+E|lP'|^q & \text{if $P>\Pi$} \\
\infty & \text{otherwise}.
\end{cases}
\]
where $q>1$, $0<\Pi<1$ and $C,D,E>0$. It trivially satisfies hypotheses (H1), (H2) and (H3).
\begin{rmk}
\label{rmk:am-fed}
In what follow we will consider only the following refined version of the hardening free energy
\begin{equation}
\label{eqn:hardening-amstrong-federik-modified}
H(P,P')=
\begin{cases}
C\left(P^q+\frac{q}{\delta}e^{-\delta(P-1)}\right)+\frac{D}{P-\Pi}+E|lP'|^q & \text{if $P>p$} \\
\infty & \text{otherwise},
\end{cases}
\end{equation}
where $C,D,E,\delta>0$ and $q>1$. This constitutive relation still satisfies the assumptions (H1), (H2) and (H3). The expression~\eqref{eqn:hardening-amstrong-federik-modified} is obtained as a modification of the Armstrong--Frederick kinematical hardening model which can reproduce better the experimentally observed relation between force and elongation. According to the Armstrong--Frederick model, the back-stress $\beta$ (that in our model correspond to the conservative microforce $\beta=\dind{H}{P}$) is required to satisfy the condition
\begin{equation}
\label{eqn:armstrong-frederick}
\dot\beta=\delta(C\dot P-|\dot P|\beta)
\end{equation}
for some $\delta,C>0$, see~\cite{steffranc}. In tension $\dot P\ge0$ and~\eqref{eqn:armstrong-frederick} becomes
\[
\delta\dot P=\frac{\dot\beta}{C-\beta}=-\frac{d}{dt}\log(C-\beta).
\]
This suggest the relation
\begin{equation}
\label{eqn:armstrong-frederick-constitutive}
\log\frac{C-\beta}{C}=-\delta(P-1)\quad\text{so that}\quad\beta=C(1-e^{-\delta(P-1)}).
\end{equation}
The main feature of this relation is that $\beta$ is bounded from above by $C$ and $P\to C^-$ as $P\to\infty$. Now~\eqref{eqn:armstrong-frederick-constitutive} corresponds to the energy density
\[
H=C\left(P+\frac{1}{\delta}e^{-\delta(P-1)}\right).
\]
Formula~\eqref{eqn:hardening-amstrong-federik-modified} is a modification of this as the linear growth in $P$ has been substituted with a superlinear growth in order to satisfy (H2). The superlinear growth makes $\beta$ unbounded as $\beta\to\infty$, but the main features of the original Armstrong--Frederick model are preserved.
\end{rmk}
The disipation potential density can be chosen as
\begin{equation}
R(P,\dot P)=
\begin{cases}
Y\frac{|\dot P|}{P} & \text{if $P\ge\Pi$} \\
\infty & \text{otherwise},
\end{cases}
\end{equation}
where $Y>0$ is the yield strength. The corresponding dissipation distance density $D\colon\mathbb{R}\to[0,\infty]$ can be calculated by mean of formula~\eqref{eqn:dissipation-distance-density}. We find that
\begin{equation}
\label{eqn:dissipation-distance-density-particular}
D(P_0,P_1)=
\begin{cases}
Y|\log P_1-\log P_0| & \text{if $P_0,P_1\ge p$} \\
\infty & \text{otherwise}.
\end{cases}
\end{equation}
Indeed notice that for each $P\in C^1([0,1],(0,\infty))$ connecting $P_0$ with $P_0$, it holds
\[
\int_0^1R(P(t),\dot{P}(t))dt\ge Y\int_0^1\left|\frac{\dot{P}(t)}{P(t)}\right|dt\ge Y\left|\int_0^1\frac{\dot{P}(t)}{P(t)}dt\right|=Y|\log P_1-\log P_0|.
\]
This implies $D(P_0,P_1)\ge Y|\log P_1-\log P_0|$. If $P_i<\Pi$ for some $i\in\{0,1\}$, then every $C^1$ curve $P$ connecting $P_0$ with $P_1$, has $\int_0^1R(P(t),\dot{P}(t))dt=\infty$. If instead $P_0,P_1\ge\Pi$ then we can take $P(t)=P_0^tP_1^{1-t}\ge\Pi$ and in this case
\[
\int_0^1R(P(t),\dot{P}(t))dt=Y\int_0^1\left|\frac{\dot{P}(t)}{P(t)}\right|dt=Y|\log P_1-\log P_1|.
\]
This implies $D(P_0,P_1)=Y|\log P_1-\log P_0|$. Now it is easy to see that $D$ in~\eqref{eqn:dissipation-distance-density-particular} satisfies the assumptions (d1), (d2) and (d3).

\section{Qualitative analysis of the tensile test problem}

We consider the following boundary value problem describing the tensile test: the bar in not subject to any distributed load, that is $f=0$. Moreover the end sections are left free to slide in the transversal direction, this means that $h_2(X,t)=0$ for $X\in\{0,L\}$ and $t\in[0,T]$ and $\Gamma_D^2=\emptyset$. The longitudinal position of the end sections is assigned, this means that $\Gamma_D^1=\{0,L\}$. In particular we consider the boundary conditions $\chi_1(X,t)=\eta(t)X$ for $X\in\{0,L\}$ and $t\in[0,T]$, where $\eta\in C^\infty([0,T],(0,\infty))$ is the imposed elongation. We suppose that $\eta(0)=1$ and $\eta$ is nondecreasing. To handle the boundary conditions we define $g\colon\mathbb{R}^2\times[0,T]\to\mathbb{R}^2$ by $g(y,t)=(\eta(t)y_1,y_2)$ so that $\chi(X,t)=\eta(t)y_1(X,t)$ and $\chi_2(X,t)=y_2(X,t)$. Then the unknowns of the problem are $y\colon(0,L)\times[0,T]\to\mathbb{R}^2$ and $P\colon(0,L)\times[0,T]\to\mathbb{R}$ with the constrain $y_1(X,t)=X$ for $X\in\{0,L\}$.

We will see that a trivial strong solution to this problem is the spatially homogeneous one that is $\bar{y}_1(X,t)=X$ for all $X\in(0,L)$ and $t\in[0,T]$, $\bar{y}_2(\cdot,t)$ and $\bar{P}(\cdot,t)$ constant for all $t\in[0,T]$. However we expect that this trivial solution is unstable when $\eta$ is sufficiently large, in the sense that it does not satisfy the global stability condition
\[
\mathcal{E}(\bar{y}(t),\bar{P}(t),t)\le\mathcal{E}(\hat y,\hat P,t)+\mathcal{D}(\bar P(t),\hat P)\quad\text{for all $(\hat y,\hat P)\in\mathscr{Z}$}.
\]
The instability of the trivial solution would correspond with the onset of necking, when a non uniform localized solution becomes energetically favorable.

We will discuss first the stability of the trivial solution when the elastic response only is considered. In this case we will be able to obtain some explicit results. Then we will show our efforts to extend the analysis also in the elastoplastic case. In this second case we cannot obtain explicit results, however we can gain some insight about the phenomenology of necking.
\begin{rmk}
Most of the stability analysis below is only formal. Some additional effort should be made in order to get fully rigorous results. The mathematical theory that should be employed to reach a full justification of the procedure below is that of bifurcation theory in Banach spaces, probably with some nontrivial adjustments needed to treat the nonsmooth character of plasticity theory. Some results regarding this theory in the smooth context and applications to instability phenomena in nonlinear elasticity can be found in our review~\cite{progetto-ponno}.
\end{rmk}

\subsection{Purely elastic model}

In the purely elastic model, equations~\eqref{eqn:indeinite-equation-necking-large} reduces to
\begin{subequations}
\label{eqn:indefinite-equations-necking-large-elastic}
\begin{gather}
\sigma_1'=0\quad\text{in $(0,L)$} \\
-\sigma_2+\zeta'=0\quad\text{in $(0,L)$} \\
\zeta(X)=0\quad\text{for $X\in\{0,L\}$}.
\end{gather}
\end{subequations}
From the first equation it follows that $\sigma_1=F$, where $F$ is a constant, representing the axial reaction force needed to impose the boundary conditions on $\chi_1$. The constitutive relations yields $\sigma_1=W_1(E_1,E_2,G)$, $\sigma_2=W_2(E_1,E_2,G)$ and $\zeta=W_3(E_1,E_2,G)$, where $E_1=\chi_1'$, $E_2=\chi_2$ and $G=\chi_2'$. We have denoted the partial derivatives of $W$ with respect to its arguments $E_1$, $E_2$ and $G$ with the subscripts $1,2$ and $3$ respectively. Inserting these constitutive relations into~\eqref{eqn:indefinite-equations-necking-large-elastic}, we get
\begin{subequations}
\label{eqn:definite-equations-necking-large-elastic}
\begin{gather}
\frac{d}{dX}W_1(\chi_1',\chi_2,\chi_2')=0\quad\text{in $(0,L)$} \\
-W_2(\chi_1',\chi_2,\chi_2')+\frac{d}{dX}W_3(\chi_1',\chi_2,\chi_2')=0\quad\text{in $(0,L)$} \\
W_3(\chi_1'(X),\chi_2(X),\chi_2'(X))=0\quad\text{for $X\in\{0,L\}$}.
\end{gather}
\end{subequations}
We first look for homogeneous solutions of the form $\bar{\chi}_1(X,t)=\eta(t)X$, $\bar\chi_2(X,t)=\bar E_2(t)$ where $\bar E_2\colon(0,T)\to(0,\infty)$ is the only unknown. We will refer to a solution of this type as a trivial solution. Notice that $\bar E_1(t)=\bar\chi_1(X,t)=\eta(t)$ and $\bar G(t)=\bar\chi_2'(X,t)=0$ for all $X\in(0,L)$ and $t\in[0,T]$. To find $\bar E_2$ we need only to solve the nonlinear equation
\[
W_2(\eta(t),\bar E_2(t),0)=0.
\]
This yields $\bar E_2$ as a function of $\eta$.

We now turn to the study of the stability of the homogeneous solution $(\bar{\chi},\bar{P})$. To this aim we should check whether the second variation of the energy becomes indefinite for $\eta$ large enough. This can be done as follows: we insert a perturbation of the homogeneous solution $\chi_1=\bar\chi_1+v_1$ and $\chi_2=\bar\chi_2+v_2$ into problem~\eqref{eqn:definite-equations-necking-large-elastic}, then we linearize the resulting problem around the homogeneous solution and we look for nontrivial solutions. The linearized problem reads as follows
\begin{subequations}
\label{eqn:perturbed-eqn}
\begin{gather}
\bar W_{11}v_1''+\bar W_{12}v_2'+\bar W_{13}v_2''=0\quad\text{in $(0,L)$} \\
\begin{multlined}[c]
-\bar W_{12}v_1'-\bar W_{22}v_2-\bar W_{23}v_2'+\\
+\bar W_{13}v_1''+\bar W_{23}v_2'+\bar W_{33}v_2''=0
\end{multlined}
\quad\text{in $(0,L)$}
\end{gather}
\end{subequations}
together with the boundary conditions
\begin{gather}
\label{eqn:perturbed-bound}
v_1(X)=0\quad\text{for $X\in\{0,L\}$} \\
v_2'(X)=0\quad\text{for $X\in\{0,L\}$}.
\end{gather}
We have denoted with a superimposed bar, the evaluation at $(\eta,\bar E_2,0)$. From mechanical ground, it is reasonable to assume that $\sigma_i=W_i$ for $i\in\{1,2\}$ is even in $G$ and $\zeta=W_3$ is odd in $G$. Then $\bar W_{i3}=0$ for $i\in\{1,2\}$. Moreover we assume that $\bar W_{33}>0$, $\bar W_{11}>0$, $\bar W_{22}>0$. Then~\eqref{eqn:perturbed-eqn} simplifies to
\begin{subequations}
\begin{gather}
\bar W_{11}v_1''+\bar W_{12}v_2'=0\quad\text{in $(0,L)$} \\
\bar W_{33}v_2''-\bar W_{12}v_1'-\bar W_{22}v_2=0 \quad\text{in $(0,L)$}.
\end{gather}
\end{subequations}
The first equation yields
\begin{equation}
\label{eqn:v1prime}
v_1'=-\frac{\bar{W}_{12}}{\bar{W}_{11}}v_2+K_1
\end{equation}
for some constant $K_1\in\mathbb{R}$. Substituting into the second equation we obtain
\[
\bar W_{33}v_2''+\left(\frac{\bar{W}_{12}^2}{\bar{W}_{11}}-\bar W_{22}\right)v_2=\bar W_{12}K_1 \quad\text{in $(0,L)$}.
\]
Let
\[
\lambda=\frac{\frac{(\bar W_{12})^2}{\bar W_{11}}-\bar W_{22}}{\bar W_{33}}.
\]
Taking into account the boundary condition $v_2'(X)=0$  for $X\in\{0,L\}$, we find that a nontrivial solution exists if and only if $\lambda=\frac{k^2\pi^2}{L^2}$ for $k\in\{1,2,\dots\}$ and is of the form
\[
v_2(X)=\frac{\bar W_{12}K_1}{\frac{\bar{W}_{12}^2}{\bar{W}_{11}}-\bar W_{22}}+K_2\cos\left(\sqrt{\lambda}X\right).
\]
Here $K_2\in\mathbb{R}$ is a constant. Inserting in~\eqref{eqn:v1prime} we get that
\[
v_1(X)=\left(-\frac{\bar W_{12}}{\bar W_{11}}\frac{\bar W_{12}K_1}{\frac{\bar{W}_{12}^2}{\bar{W}_{11}}-\bar W_{22}}+1\right)K_1X-\frac{\bar{W}_{12}}{\bar{W}_{11}}\frac{K_2}{\sqrt{\lambda}}\sin\left(\sqrt{\lambda}X\right)+K_3
\]
with $K_3\in\mathbb{R}$. Boundary condition imposes that $K_1=0$ and $K_3=0$. From these considerations we conclude that the homogeneous solution becomes unstable when $\lambda=\pi^2/L^2$ for the first time. The shape of the instability is showed schematically in Figure~\ref{fig:instability-mode-elastic}
\begin{figure}[t]
\centering
\subfloat[][Reference configuration]
{
\begin{minipage}{0.45\textwidth}
\centering
\begin{tikzpicture}[scale=0.6]
\begin{axis}[hide axis,clip=false,x=1cm,y=1cm]
\addplot[name path=A, no markers, draw=none] table[x index=0, y index=1] {figures/instabilityelref.dat};
\addplot[name path=B, no markers, draw=none] table[x index=0, y expr=-\thisrowno{1}] {figures/instabilityelref.dat};
\addplot[black] fill between[of=A and B];
\end{axis}
\end{tikzpicture}
\end{minipage}
}
\\
\subfloat[][Bifurcation mode]
{
\begin{minipage}{0.45\textwidth}
\centering
\begin{tikzpicture}[scale=0.6]
\begin{axis}[hide axis,clip=false,x=1cm,y=1cm]
\addplot[name path=A, no markers, draw=none] table[x index=0, y index=1] {figures/instabilityel.dat};
\addplot[name path=B, no markers, draw=none] table[x index=0, y expr=-\thisrowno{1}] {figures/instabilityel.dat};
\addplot[black] fill between[of=A and B];
\end{axis}
\end{tikzpicture}
\end{minipage}
}
\caption{Bifurcation mode of the elastic bar under traction.}
\label{fig:instability-mode-elastic}
\end{figure}

%Let $f=f(u,p)$ and $g=g(u,p)$ form $\mathbb{R}^m\times\mathbb{R}^m$ to $\mathbb{R}^m$ be smooth. Let also $A,B\in\mathbb{R}^{m\times m}$ and $c\in\mathbb{R}^m$. Let $V$ some Banach space and consider the problem to find $u\colon(0,L)\to\mathbb{R}^m$ such that
%\[
%\begin{cases}
%f(u(x),u'(x))+\frac{d}{dx}g(u(x),u'(x))=0 & \text{for $x\in(0,L)$} \\
%Au(x)+Bu'(x)=c & \text{for $x\in\{0,L\}$}.
%\end{cases}
%\]
%We formally write this problem as $\mathcal{L}(u)=0$. Let $u$ be a solution and suppose that 
%\[
%\dind{f}{u}(u(x),u'(x))=F_u,\quad\dind{f}{p}(u(x),u'(x))=F_p,\quad\dind{g}{u}(u(x),u'(x))=G_u\quad\text{and}\quad\dind{g}{p}(u(x),u'(x))=G_p
%\]
%for all $x\in(0,L)$, where $F_u,F_p,G_u,G_p\in\mathbb{R}^{m\times m}$ are constant. Formally, the linearized perturbed problem $\mathcal{L}'(u)v$, with $v\colon(0,L)\to\mathbb{R}$, reads
%\[
%\begin{cases}
%F_uv(x)+(F_p+G_u)v'(x)+G_pv''(x)=0 & \text{for $x\in(0,L)$} \\
%Av(x)+Bv'(x)=0 & \text{for $x\in\{0,L\}$}.
%\end{cases}
%\]
%We look for particular solution of the differential equation of the form $x\mapsto e^{rx}w$ with $w\in\mathbb{C}$ and $w\in\mathbb{R}^m$. This is a solution if and only $r$ and $w$ solve the following polynomial eigenvalue problem
%\[
%(r^2G_p+r(F_p+G_u)+F_u)w=0.
%\]
%Assume that there exist.

Now let $\bar{F}(t)=W_1(\eta(t),\bar{E}_2(t),0)$ be the axial force along the homogeneous solution. We now show that the instability can occur only after that $F$ reaches its maximum. Indeed let us view $\bar{F}$ as a function of $\eta$, so that
\[
\frac{d\bar{F}}{d\eta}=\bar W_{11}+\bar W_{12}\frac{d\bar E_2}{d\eta}.
\]
On the other hand we know that $\bar W_2=W_2(\eta,\bar{E}_2,0)=0$ so that, by differentiating with respect to $\eta$,
\[
\bar W_{12}+\bar W_{22}\frac{d\bar E_2}{d\eta}=0\quad\text{that implies}\quad\frac{d\bar E_2}{d\eta}=-\frac{\bar W_{12}}{\bar W_{22}}.
\]
Then
\[
\frac{d\bar F}{d\eta}=\bar W_{11}-\frac{\bar W_{12}^2}{\bar W_{22}}=-\bar W_{33}\frac{\bar W_{11}}{\bar W_{22}}\lambda.
\]
Now, at the first stages of deformation the force increases with elongation so $d\bar F/d\eta>0$ that implies $\lambda<0$ and no instability can occur. It $\bar{F}$ has a (local) maximum then $\lambda$ become positive and instability occur if it reach the value $\pi^2/L^2$. This clearly happen if the bar is sufficiently long.

Consider now the particular constitutive model described in Section~\ref{subsec:constitutive-large-neck}. If we choose
\begin{equation}
\label{eqn:choice-parameters}
\text{$A=1$, $B=10^{-4}$, $p=2$, $\alpha=1$ and $r=2$},
\end{equation}
we get the graphs of $\bar F$ and $\lambda$ as functions of $\eta$ reported in Figure~\ref{fig:instability}. Notice that $F$ has a maximum and $\lambda$ became positive so that instability occur if $L$ is sufficiently large.
\begin{rmk}
We notice that the particular choice of the parameters in~\eqref{eqn:choice-parameters} is not mechanically reasonable for metals, and was motivated only by the need to show that the mechanical model that we have developed in this chapter can exhibit instability phenomena. The inaccuracy of~\eqref{eqn:choice-parameters} stems to the fact that, since $B$ is small compared to $A$, the nearly-compressibility behavior of metals is not correctly reproduced. Moreover~\eqref{eqn:choice-parameters} implies that cross-sections growth in traction, this is clearly wrong for metals.
\end{rmk}

\begin{figure}
\begin{tikzpicture}
\begin{axis}[xlabel={$\eta$},ylabel={$\bar F$},xmin=1,xmax=6,ymin=0,width=0.45\textwidth,ylabel style={rotate=-90}]
\addplot[-] table [x=E1,y=F] {figures/instability.dat};
\end{axis}
\end{tikzpicture}
\hfill
\begin{tikzpicture}
\begin{axis}[xlabel={$\eta$},ylabel={$\lambda$},xmin=1,xmax=6,width=0.45\textwidth,ylabel style={rotate=-90}]
\addplot[-] table [x=E1,y=lambda] {figures/instability.dat};
\addplot[dashed,domain=1:6] {0};
\end{axis}
\end{tikzpicture}
