\caption{Graphs of $\bar F$ and $\lambda$ as functions of the imposed elongation $\eta$ for the homogeneous deformation in the purely elastic model with the choice of the value of the parameters in~\ref{eqn:choice-parameters}.}
\label{fig:instability}
\end{figure}

\subsection{Elastoplastic model}
\label{subsec:plastic-model}

We now study the tensile test in the complete elastoplastic model. We start with explaining how a trivial solution of the form $\bar{y}_1(X,t)=\eta(t)X$, $\bar{y}_2(X,t)=\bar{y}_2(t)$ and $\bar P(X,t)=\bar{P}(t)$ for all $X\in(0,L)$ and $t\in[0,T]$ can be computed. In the elastic range, $\bar y_2(t)$ can be found as we showed previously while $\bar P(t)=1$. When the Madel stress~\eqref{eqn:neck-large-mandel} overcomes the yield strength $Y$, then $\dot{\bar P}>0$ and the plastic force is everywhere given by $\partial_{\dot P}R(\bar P,\dot{\bar P})=Y/\bar P$. Then the balance of microforces coupled with the balance in the transversal direction gives a nonlinear algebraic equation that allows to compute $\bar y_2$ and $\bar P$.

Next we study the stability of the trivial solution so we have to check whether the global stability condition
\begin{equation}
\label{eqn:stability-necking}
\mathcal{E}(\bar{y}(t),\bar{P}(t),t)\le\mathcal{E}(\hat y,\hat P,t)+\mathcal{D}(\bar{P}(t),\hat P)\quad\text{for all $(\hat y,\hat P)\in\mathscr{Z}$}.
\end{equation}
is satisfied. Since $\mathcal{D}$ is a quasidistance, and in particular $\mathcal{D}(P_0,P_1)\ge0$ and equality holds if and only if $P_0=P_1$, the global stability condition is equivalent to say that $(\bar y(t),\bar P(t))$ is a global minimizer of $\mathcal{J}_t\colon\mathscr{Z}\to\mathbb{R}\cup\{\infty\}$ defined by
\[
\mathcal{J}_t(\hat y,\hat Q)=\mathcal{E}(\hat y,\hat P,t)+\mathcal{D}(\bar P(t),\hat P).
\]
The aim of what follows would be to identify conditions that ensure that, for sufficiently large elongations,~\eqref{eqn:stability-necking} is violated, and to understand qualitatively the feature of the stable localized solution. We were actually not able to get conclusive results in this sense, so we now propose some heuristic considerations that suggest that instability in the form of necking is likely to occur if suitable constitutive relation for the hardening free energy density are chosen.
\begin{rmk}
Our study of stability will be local, meaning that we will study the behavior of $\mathcal{J}_t$ near $(\bar{y}(t),\bar{P}(t))$ only, taking into account first and second order variations. In particular our analysis will not be able to identify the cases in which $(\bar{y}(t),\bar{P}(t))$ is a local but not a global minimum of $\mathcal{J}_t$: if this happen, according to the definition of energetic solution, the configuration must jump from the trivial to the energetically favorable one. On the other hand the experimental observations and the theoretical and numerical studies mentioned in the introduction of Chapter~\ref{chap:intro-neck} suggest that necking happen without jumps. Thus we expect that the estimates we will find of the threshold of instability detected by our local analysis corresponds with the true condition for the beginning of necking. See also the discussion in Chapter~\ref{chap:solution-concepts} regarding the potential limitations of energetic solutions in identifying the exact onset of instability.
\end{rmk}
%The aim of what follows is to study the conditions under which there exists $t\in(0,T]$ such that~\eqref{eqn:stability-necking} is violated and understand qualitatively the behavior of the true stable solution. We will show that choosing suitably the constitutive relations, there exists $t_0\in(0,T]$ such that, if $t>t_0$ then~\eqref{eqn:stability-necking} is violated. To see this we will look at the behavior of $\mathcal{J}_t$ locally near $(\bar y(t),\bar P(t))$ studying its first and second variations on the directions in which it is smooth (notice indeed that $\mathcal{J}_t$ is non-smooth due to the non-smooth dissipation distance $\mathcal{D}$). It will result that the direction in which $\mathcal{J}_t$ decreases, preventing $(\bar y(t),\bar P(t))$ to be a minimizer, corresponds to an instability mode qualitatively similar to that we found in the purely elastic model and shown in Figure~\ref{fig:instability-mode-elastic}. This is interpreted as the onset of a neck in one of the extremities of the bar.
We start our analysis assuming that the stability condition is not violated in the elastic range. This is certainly true in the case of metals since yielding starts when the geometric nonlinearities have still a negligible effect. So fix $t\in[0,T]$ and suppose that the yield point has been already reached at that time. Given the difficulties in fully characterizing the true stability condition~\eqref{eqn:stability-necking}, we define instead two simplified stability conditions that can however give us some information about the occurrence of necking. We call them~\emph{stability under force control} and~\emph{regularized stability} and we discuss whether they are violated below. We will describe also our not conclusive attempts to get information regarding the occurrence of the \emph{true instability}.

\subsubsection*{Stability under force control}

To introduce this first simplified stability condition, let us define
\[
\bar F(t)=\bar{P}(t)^{-1}W_1(\eta(t)\bar{P}(t)^{-1},\bar{y}_2(t)\bar P(t),\bar{y}_2'(t)\bar{P}(t)^{-1}),
\]
the reaction tensile force acting on the ends of the bar along the trivial solution. Consider the function $J_t\colon(0,\infty)^3\to\mathbb{R}\cup\{\infty\}$ given by
\[
J_t(\rho,y_2,P)=W(\rho P^{-1},y_2P,0)+H(P,0)+D(\bar{P}(t),P)-\bar{F}(t)\rho.
\]
The quantity $J_t(\rho,y_2,P)-J_t(\eta(t),\bar{y}_2(t),\bar{P}(t))$ is the energy per unit length that must be supplied to the system to bring it from the configuration $\bar y_1(t)$, $\bar y_2(t)$ and $\bar P(t)$ to the configuration $y_1(X)=\rho X$, $y_2(X)=y_2$ and $P(X)=P$ if the ends are now left free to move and the tensile force is kept equal to $\bar F(t)$. It includes both the energy stored by the system in the form of free energy and the energy dissipated as the plastic distortion is changed. Now our first simplified stability condition is the following.
\begin{defi}[stability under force control]
We say that the trivial uniform solution at time $t\in(0,T]$, is stable under force control if $(\eta(t),\bar{y}_2(t),\bar{P}(t))$ is a local minimizer of $J_t$.
\end{defi}
The mechanical meaning of this definition is the following: the trivial uniform solution $(\eta,\bar{y}_2(t),\bar{P}(t))$ is stable under force control when it is the most energetically favored configuration among all other uniform configurations when the condition of fixed tensile force is applied. We now study whether the stability of the trivial uniform solution under force control is lost for sufficiently large elongations when the particular constitutive relations proposed in Section~\ref{subsec:constitutive-large-neck} are considered. In this particular case $J_t$ is not differentiable at $P=\bar{P}(t)$ but $J_t\in C^1(\mathscr H^+_t)$ and $J_t\in C^1(\mathscr H^-_t)$, where
\[
\mathscr H^+_t=\{(\rho,y_2,P)\in(0,\infty)^3\;|\;P\ge\bar{P}(t)\}\quad\text{and}\quad \mathscr H^-_t=\{(\rho,y_2,P)\in(0,\infty)^3\;|\;\Pi<P\le\bar{P}(t)\}.
\]
We easily find that
\[
\nabla J_t(\eta,\bar y_2(t),\bar P(t)^-)=\left(0,0,-\frac{2Y}{\bar P(t)}\right)\quad\text{and}\quad\nabla J_t(\eta,\bar y_2(t),\bar P(t)^+)=(0,0,0).
\]
Then, wether $(\eta(t),\bar y_2(t),\bar P(t))$ is a local minimum of $J_t$ depends on the eigenvalues of the hessian matrix
\[
h(t)=\nabla^2 J_t(\eta(t),\bar y_2(t),\bar P(t)^+).
\]
It is clear that at $t=0$ the trivial solution is stable and that $h(0)$ is positive definite. Then instability under force control occurs when $h(t)$ become singular. We now show that this happen exactly when $\bar{F}$ reaches its maximum, suggesting an agreement between our model with the ConsidĂ¨re criterion. Indeed the following lemma holds.
\begin{lemma}
\label{lemma:stability-force}
Let
\[
a(t)=h_{11}(t),\quad
b(t)=\begin{pmatrix}
h_{22}(t) & h_{23}(t) \\
h_{32}(t) & h_{33}(t)
\end{pmatrix}\quad\text{and}\quad
c(t)=\begin{pmatrix}
h_{21}(t) \\
h_{31}(t)
\end{pmatrix}
\]
so that
\[
h(t)=\begin{pmatrix}
a(t) & c(t)^T \\
c(t) & b(t)
\end{pmatrix}.
\]
Then it holds that
\[
\frac{d\bar F}{d\eta}(t):=\frac{d\bar F}{dt}(t)\left(\frac{d\eta}{dt}(t)\right)^{-1}=\frac{\det h(t)}{\det b(t)}.
\]
\end{lemma}
\begin{proof}
Notice that
\[
\bar F(s)=\dind{J_t}{\rho}(\eta(s),\bar y_2(s),\bar P(s))+\bar{F}(t).
\]
Then, differentiating with respect to $s$, computing for $s=t$ and dividing by $\frac{d\eta}{dt}(t)$, we get
\begin{equation}
\label{eqn:dF-deta-1}
\frac{d\bar F}{d\eta}=\dind{J_t}{\rho\partial\rho}(\eta,\bar y_2,\bar P)+\dind{J_t}{\rho\partial y_2}(\eta,\bar y_2,\bar P)\frac{d\bar y_2}{d\eta}+\dind{J_t}{\rho\partial P}(\eta,\bar y_2,\bar P)\frac{d\bar P}{d\eta}.
\end{equation}
On the other hand, the balance in the transversal direction and the balance of microforces can be written respectively as
\[
\dind{J_t}{y_2}(\eta,\bar y_2,\bar P)=0\quad\text{and}\quad\dind{J_t}{P}(\eta,\bar y_2,\bar P)=0.
\]
Differentiating with respect to $\eta$ we get
\[
\begin{dcases}
\dind{J_t}{\rho\partial y_2}(\eta,\bar y_2,\bar P)+\dind{J_t}{y_2\partial y_2}(\eta,\bar y_2,\bar P)\frac{d\bar y_2}{d\eta}+\dind{J_t}{y_2\partial P}(\eta,\bar y_2,\bar P)\frac{d\bar P}{d\eta}=0 \\
\dind{J_t}{\rho\partial P}(\eta,\bar y_2,\bar P)+\dind{J_t}{y_2\partial P}(\eta,\bar y_2,\bar P)\frac{d\bar y_2}{d\eta}+\dind{J_t}{P\partial P}(\eta,\bar y_2,\bar P)\frac{d\bar P}{d\eta}=0.
\end{dcases}
\]
Then
\[
\begin{pmatrix}
\dfrac{d\bar P}{d\eta} \\
\dfrac{d\bar P}{d\eta}
\end{pmatrix}=
-b^{-1} c.
\]
Substituting into~\eqref{eqn:dF-deta-1} we get
\[
\frac{d\bar F}{d\eta}=a-c\cdot b^{-1}c.
\]
But since
\[
\det h=\det b\left(a-c\cdot b^{-1}c\right)
\]
the thesis follows.
\end{proof}
It is easy to see that the constitutive model described in Section~\ref{subsec:constitutive-large-neck} implies that $\det b(t)>0$. Then previous lemma shows indeed that instability under force control occur when $\bar F$ reaches its maximum. In Figure~\ref{fig:instabilitypl} the result is demonstrated numerically: we adopted the Armstrong--Frederick hardening function of Remark~\ref{rmk:am-fed} and we tuned the amount of hardening (in particular the parameters $C$ and $\delta$) so that $\bar{F}$ exhibited a maximum. By a numerical computation one can find also that at the instant $t_0\in(0,T]$ when $\det h(t_0)=0$, the kernel of $h(t_0)$ is the span of a vector of the form $(\theta,v_2,q)$ with $\theta>0$, $v_2<0$ and $q>0$. This means that, if at time $t_0$ the bar is constrained to remains uniform, allowed to change its length and with the tensile $\bar{F}(t_0)$ kept fixed, it tends to shrink and increase its plastic distortion. This behavior constitutes the expected mechanism for the initiation of necking. Returning to the actual boundary value problem where the elongation, rather than the tensile force, is controlled, our findings suggest that when the total elongation is sufficiently large, the bar tends to shrink and elongate more than what is predicted by the trivial uniform solution. However, since the global boundary conditions prevent this additional deformation from occurring everywhere, the bar can only satisfy this tendency at a specific point along its length, thus initiating the necking process.
\begin{figure}[htp]
\begin{tikzpicture}
\begin{axis}[xlabel={$\eta$},ylabel={$\bar F$},xmin=1,xmax=1.35,width=0.45\textwidth]
\addplot[-,thick] table {figures/force.dat};
\end{axis}
\end{tikzpicture}
\hfill
\begin{tikzpicture}
\begin{axis}[xlabel={$\eta$},ylabel={$\det h$},xmin=1,xmax=1.35,width=0.45\textwidth]
\addplot[-,thick] table {figures/hessian.dat};
\addplot[domain=1:1.35,samples=2,color=black,dashed] {0};
\end{axis}
\end{tikzpicture}
\caption{Graphs of $\bar F$ and $\det h$ as functions of the elongation $\eta$ along the trivial solution in the plastic model. Notice that the maximum of $\bar{F}$ is reached when $h$ vanishes at $\eta_\text{m}=1.1474$.}
\label{fig:instabilitypl}
\end{figure}

\subsubsection*{Regularized stability}

We now turn to our second simplified notion of stability whose introduction is motivated by the follwing considerations. The feature that makes inequality~\eqref{eqn:stability-necking}, or equivalently the minimality of $\mathcal{J}_t$ at $(\bar{y}(t),\bar{P}(t))$, difficult to analyze, is the lack of smoothness of $\mathcal{D}$. This indeed prevents us from considering the first and second variation of $\mathcal{J}_t$ to check whether $(\bar{y}(t),\bar{P}(t))$ is a (local) minima. The idea to circumvent these difficulties would be to consider only variations in the directions where the one-sided directional derivatives exists. Specifically we restrict to the particular constitutive relation for the dissipation potential discussed in Section~\ref{subsec:constitutive-large-neck} and we define the two smooth functionals $\mathcal{J}_t^\varsigma\colon\mathscr{Z}\to\mathbb{R}\cup\{\infty\}$ for $\varsigma\in\{\pm1\}$ as
\[
\mathcal{J}_t^\varsigma(\hat y,\hat P,t)=\int_0^LW(\eta(t)\hat y_1'\hat P^{-1},\hat y_2\hat P,\hat y_2'\hat P^{-1})dX+\int_0^LH(\hat P,\hat P')dX+\int_0^LD^\varsigma(\bar P(t),\hat P)dt,
\]
with $D^\varsigma(P_0,P_1)=\varsigma Y\log(P_1/P_0)$. This definitions allows to write $\mathcal{J}_t(\hat z)=\mathcal{J}_t^\varsigma(\hat z)$ if $\hat z\in\mathscr{Z}^\varsigma_t$, where
\[
\mathscr{Z}^\varsigma_t=\{\hat z=(\hat y,\hat P)\in\mathscr{Z}\;|\;\text{$\varsigma(\hat P-\bar P(t))\ge0$ in $(0,L)$}\}.
\]
The following lemma express how the minimality of $\mathcal{J}_t$is related to that of $\mathcal{J}_t^\varsigma$.
\begin{lemma}
The global stability condition
\begin{equation}
\label{eqn:min-neck}
\mathcal{J}_t(\bar{z}(t))\le\mathcal{J}_t(\hat z)\quad\text{for all $\hat z\in\mathscr{Z}$}
\end{equation}
implies the following couple of one-sided  global stability conditions
\begin{equation}
\label{eqn:min-neck-sigma}
\mathcal{J}_t^\varsigma(\bar{z}(t))\le\mathcal{J}_t^\varsigma(\hat z)\quad\text{for all $\hat z\in\mathscr{Z}^\varsigma_t$ and $\varsigma\in\{\pm1\}$}.
\end{equation}
%Moreover if $\overline q(t)$ satisfies
%\begin{subequations}
%\label{eqn:condiz}
%\begin{gather}
%\dual{\partial\mathcal{J}_t^-(\overline q(t)),w}>0\quad\text{for all $w\in Q$ with $Q\le0$ in $(0,L)$ not identically vanishing} \\
%\text{and}\\
%\partial\mathcal{J}_t^+(\overline q(t))=0,\quad\text{$\partial^2\mathcal{J}^+_t(\overline{q}(t))$ is positive definite}
%\end{gather}
%\end{subequations}
%then
%\begin{equation}
%\label{eqn:local-minimum}
%\dual{\partial^+\mathcal{J}_t(\overline{q}),w}>0\quad\text{or}\quad\begin{cases}
%\dual{\partial^+\mathcal{J}_t(\overline{q}),w}=0 \\
%\dual{(\partial^+)^2\mathcal{J}_t(\overline{q})w,w}>0
%\end{cases}\qquad\text{for all $w\in Q$},
%\end{equation}
%where $\partial^+$ denotes the one-sided G\^ateaux derivative.
%\end{lemma}
%\begin{proof}
%That~\eqref{eqn:min-neck} implies~\eqref{eqn:min-neck-sigma} is trivial. Next consider any $w\in Q$ and decompose it as $w=w^++w^-$ with $Q^+\ge0$ and $Q^-\le0$. Then it is immediate to check that
%\end{proof}
%\begin{rmk}
%We suspect that~\eqref{eqn:local-minimum} does not implies that $\overline{q}(t)$ is a local minimum of $\mathcal{J}_t$ but it is a weaker stability property that takes into account only the directional derivatives up to second order instead of the whole local behavior of $\mathcal{J}_t$ ner $\overline q(t)$.
%\end{rmk}
%\begin{lemma}
%The stability condition
%\begin{equation}
%\label{eqn:min-neck}
%\mathcal{J}_t(\bar{q}(t))\le\mathcal{J}_t(\hat q)\quad\text{for all $\hat q\in\mathscr{Q}$}
%\end{equation}
%is equivalent to
%\begin{equation}
%\label{eqn:min-neck-sigma}
%\mathcal{J}_t^\varsigma(\bar{q}(t))\le\mathcal{J}_t^\varsigma(\hat q)\quad\text{for all $\hat q\in\mathscr{Q}^\varsigma_t$ and $\varsigma\in\{\pm1\}$}.
%\end{equation}
\end{lemma}
\begin{proof}
Since $\mathcal{J}_t(\hat z)=\mathcal{J}_t^\varsigma(\hat z)$ if $\hat z\in\mathscr{Z}^\varsigma_t$, the results follows immediately.
\end{proof}
%\begin{proof}
%That~\eqref{eqn:min-neck} implies~\eqref{eqn:min-neck-sigma} is trivial. Conversely suppose that~\eqref{eqn:min-neck-sigma} holds and, by contradiction, that $\mathcal{J}_t(\bar{q}(t))>\mathcal{J}_t(\hat q)$ for some $\hat q\in\mathscr{Q}$. If $\hat q\in\mathscr{Q}^\varsigma$ for some $\varsigma\in\{\pm1\}$ we immediately get the desired contradiction. Otherwise let $\hat q^+\in\mathscr{Q}^+$ be defined by $\hat y^+=\hat y$ and $\hat P^+:=\max\{\hat P,\bar P(t)\}$. Then
%\[
%\mathcal{J}_t^+(\bar{q}(t))=\mathcal{J}_t(\bar{q}(t))>\mathcal{J}_t(\hat q)\ge\mathcal{J}_t^+(\hat q^+).
%\]
%This is a contradiction.
%\end{proof}
Thus a sufficient condition for instability is that at least one among~\eqref{eqn:min-neck-sigma} is violated, so we now study whether this happen. Considering the way in which the trivial solution is computed (see the beginning of Section`\ref{subsec:plastic-model}) it is immediate to check that $\partial\mathcal{J}^+_t(\bar z(t))=0$ for all $t\in[0,T]$. This implies that for all $w=(v,q)\in Z_0$ with $q\le0$ in $(0,L)$, it holds that
\[
\dual{\partial\mathcal{J}^-_t(\bar z(t)),w}=\dual{\partial\mathcal{J}^+_t(\bar z(t)),w}-2Y\int_0^L\frac{q}{\bar P(t)}dX=-2Y\int_0^L\frac{q}{\bar P(t)}dX\ge0.
\]
This means that~\eqref{eqn:min-neck-sigma} with $\varsigma=-1$ is satisfied, at least locally for $\hat z$ near $\bar z(t)$, for all $t\in[0,T]$. This means that instability modes in which the plastic distortion $P$ diminish everywhere with respect to $\bar P(t)$ are excluded. This result is expected: as the bar remains under tension, there is no driving force that could justify reversal of plastic flow. So we turn to study whether~\eqref{eqn:min-neck-sigma}, for $\varsigma=1$, is violated. To this aim we could study the second variation of $\mathcal{J}_t^+$ at $(\bar{y}(t),\bar P(t))$: if we are able to find $w=(v,q)\in Z_0$ with $q\ge0$ in $(0,L)$ such that $\dual{\partial^2\mathcal{J}_t^+(\bar z(t))w,w}<0$, we have indeed that ~\eqref{eqn:min-neck-sigma}, for $\varsigma=1$, is violated. Since our efforts to find such $w$ were unsuccessful (see also the subsection below which gives a presentation of our attempts in that direction), in what follows we presents a simplified analysis in which the constraint $q\ge0$ is disregarded. This leads us to the following second simplified notion of stability.
\begin{defi}[regularized stability]
We say that the trivial uniform solution satisfies the regularized stability condition at $t\in(0,T]$ if $\partial^2\mathcal{J}_t^+(\bar y(t),\bar P(t))$ is positive definite.
\end{defi}
\begin{rmk}
\label{rmk:stability-regularized}
We have already established that instability modes where $P$ decreases everywhere in $(0,L)$ are excluded. Furthermore, if the trivial uniform solution satisfies the regularized stability condition, instability modes where $P$ increases everywhere in $(0,L)$ are likewise excluded. Consequently, if we assume a priori that instability modes cannot exhibit a plastic increment that is negative in some regions and positive in others (as is expected for a bar under tension, where $P$ should not decrease at any point) then the regularized stability condition would imply true local stability. However, if the trivial uniform solution becomes unstable in the regularized sense, true local stability is not necessarily compromised. Indeed, even if the second variation $\partial^2\mathcal{I}_t^+(\bar{y}(t),\bar{P}(t))$ is not positive definite, no actual instability occurs as long as there is no direction $w=(v,q) \in Z_0$ with $q\ge 0$ such that $\langle\partial^2\mathcal{I}_t^+(\bar{y}(t),\bar{P}(t))w,w\rangle<0$. These considerations imply that the instant at which regularized stability is lost represents a lower bound for the onset of necking.
\end{rmk}
We now seek the instant at which the regularized stability condition is lost. This can be achieved by determining whether there exists a non-trivial direction $w\in Z_0$ such that $\partial^2\mathcal{J}_t^+(\bar{z}(t))w = 0$. It is easy to prove that
\[
\dual{\partial^2\mathcal{J}_t^+(\bar z(t))w,\tilde w}=\int_0^L\left(M(t)w'\cdot \tilde w'+N(t)w\cdot\tilde w'+N^Tw'\cdot\tilde w+K(t)w\cdot\tilde w\right)dX
\]
where
\[
M=\begin{pmatrix}
    W_{y_1'y_1'} & \bar{W}_{y'_1 y_2'} & 0 \\
    \bar{W}_{y'_1 y_3} & \bar{W}_{y'_2 y'_2} & 0 \\
    0             & 0             & \bar{H}_{P'P'}
\end{pmatrix},\quad
N=\begin{pmatrix}
    0 & \bar{W}_{y'_1 y_2} & \bar{W}_{y_1'P} \\
    0 & \bar{W}_{y'_2 y_2} & \bar{W}_{y'_2P} \\
    0 & 0  & \bar{H}_{PP'}
\end{pmatrix}\quad\text{and}\quad
K=\begin{pmatrix}
    \bar{W}_{y_1y_1} & \bar{W}_{y_1y_2} & \bar{W}_{y_1y_3} \\
    \bar{W}_{y_2y_1} & \bar{W}_{y_2y_2} & \bar{W}_{y_2y_3} \\
    \bar{W}_{y_3y_1} & \bar{W}_{y_3y_2} & \bar{W}_{y_3y_3}+\bar{H}_{PP}+\bar{D}_{22}^+ \\
\end{pmatrix}.
\]
Here the bar denotes quantities computed along the trivial solution. It is understood that $W$ is computed in $E_1=\eta y_1'P^{-1}$, $E_2=y_2P$ and $G=y_2'P^{-1}$ so it can be considered as a function of $\eta,y_1',y_2,y_2',y_3$. Moreover $\bar{D}_{22}^+$ denotes the partial derivatives of $D^+$ with respect to its second argument. Now, the strong form of $\partial^2\mathcal{I}_t^+(\bar{z}(t))w=0$ is
\begin{equation}
\label{eqn:2order}
Aw''+Bw'+Cw=0\quad\text{in $(0,L)$},
\end{equation}
together with the boundary conditions $w_1(X)=0$, $w_2'(X)=0$ and $w_3'(X)=0$ for $X\in\{0,L\}$. We have defined $A=M$, $B=(N-N^T)/2$ and $C=-K$. After some tedious computations one finds
\begingroup
\tiny
\begin{subequations}
\label{eqn:matrici-piccoline}
\begin{align}
A&=\begin{pmatrix}
a_1 & 0 & 0 \\
0 & a_2 & 0 \\
0 & 0 & a_3
\end{pmatrix}=\begin{pmatrix}
    \bar{W}_{y'_1 y'_1} & \bar{W}_{y'_1 y'_2} & 0 \\
    \bar{W}_{y'_1 y'_2} & \bar{W}_{y'_2 y'_2} & 0 \\
    0             & 0             & \bar{H}_{22}
\end{pmatrix}=\begin{pmatrix}
\eta^2 \frac{\bar W_{11}}{\bar P^2} & 0 & 0 \\
0 & \frac{\bar W_{33}}{\bar P^2} & 0 \\
0 & 0 & \bar H_{P'P'}
\end{pmatrix}
\\
B &=\begin{pmatrix}
0 & b_1 & b_2 \\
-b_1 & 0 & 0 \\
-b_2 & 0 & 0
\end{pmatrix}= \begin{pmatrix}
    0 & \bar{W}_{y'_1 y_2} & \bar{W}_{y'_1 {P}} \\
    -\bar{W}_{y'_1 y_2} & 0 & \bar{W}_{y'_2 {P}} \\
    -\bar{W}_{{P} y'_1} & -\bar{W}_{{P} y'_2} & 0
\end{pmatrix}=\begin{pmatrix}
0 & \eta \bar{W}_{12} & \eta \frac{\bar{y}_2}{\bar{P}^2} \bar{W}_{12} - \eta^2 \frac{\bar{W}_{11}}{\bar{P}^3} - \eta \frac{\bar{W}_1}{\bar{P}^2} \\
-\eta \bar{W}_{12} & 0 & 0 \\
-\left( \eta \frac{\bar{y}_2}{\bar{P}^2} \bar{W}_{12} - \eta^2 \frac{\bar{W}_{11}}{\bar{P}^3} - \eta \frac{\bar{W}_1}{\bar{P}^2} \right) & 0 & 0 
\end{pmatrix} \\
C &=\begin{pmatrix}
0 & 0 & 0 \\
0 & c_1 & c_2 \\
0 & c_2 & c_3
\end{pmatrix}=\begin{pmatrix}
    0 & 0 & 0 \\
    0 & -\bar{W}_{y_2 y_2} & -\bar{W}_{y_2 \bar{P}} \\
    0 & -\bar{W}_{{P} y_2} & -\bar{W}_{{P} {P}} - \bar{H}_{PP} - \bar{D}_{22}
\end{pmatrix}=
\begin{pmatrix}
0 & 0 & 0 \\
0 & -\bar{P}^2 \bar{W}_{22} & -\bar{W}_2 + \frac{\eta}{\bar{P}} \bar{W}_{12} - \bar{y}_2 \bar{P} \bar{W}_{22} \\
0 & -\bar{W}_2 + \frac{\eta}{\bar{P}} \bar{W}_{12} - \bar{y}_2 \bar{P} \bar{W}_{22} & -\eta^2 \frac{\bar{W}_{11}}{\bar{P}^4} - \bar{y}_2^2 \bar{W}_{22} - 2\eta \frac{\bar{W}_1}{\bar{P}^3} + 2\eta \frac{\bar{y}_2}{\bar{P}^2} \bar{W}_{12} - \bar{H}_{PP} - \bar{D}_{22}
\end{pmatrix}
\end{align}
\end{subequations}
\endgroup
where the bar denotes quantities computed along the trivial solution. The system of second order partial differential equations~\eqref{eqn:2order} can be written more explicitly as
\begin{subequations}
\label{eqn:equations2order}
\begin{gather}
a_1 w_1'' + b_1 w_2' + b_2 w_3' = 0, \\
a_2 w_2'' - b_1 w_1' + c_1 w_2 + c_2 w_3 = 0, \\
a_3 w_3'' - b_2 w_1' + c_2 w_2 + c_3 w_3 = 0
\end{gather}
\end{subequations}
in $(0,L)$. Taking into account the boundary conditions we write $w_1$, $w_2$ and $w_3$ as the following formal series
\begin{equation}
\label{eqn:series-formal}
w_1=\sum_{k=1}^\infty w_1^k\sin\left(\gamma_kx\right),\quad w_2=w_2^0+\sum_{k=1}^\infty w_2^k\cos\left(\gamma_kx\right)\quad\text{and}\quad w_3=w_3^0+\sum_{k=1}^\infty w_3^k\cos\left(\gamma_kx\right),
\end{equation}
where $\gamma_k=\frac{\pi k}{L}$. Substituting into~\eqref{eqn:equations2order} we get
\begin{subequations}
\label{eqn:series}
\begin{gather}
\sum_{k=1}^\infty\left(-a_1\gamma_k^2w_1^k-b_1\gamma_kw_2^k-b_2\gamma_kw_3^k\right)\sin\left(\gamma_kx\right)=0,\\
c_1w_2^0+c_2w_3^0+\sum_{k=1}^\infty\left((c_1-a_2\gamma_k^2)w_2^k-b_1\gamma_kw_1^k+c_2w_3^k\right)\cos\left(\gamma_kx\right)=0, \\
c_2w_2^0+c_3w_3^0+\sum_{k=1}^\infty\left(c_2w_2^k-b_2\gamma_kw_1^k+(c_3-a_3\gamma_k^2)w_3^k\right)\cos\left(\gamma_kx\right)=0
\end{gather}
\end{subequations}
in $(0,L)$, where. Due to the orthogonality relations
\[
\int_0^L\sin(\gamma_kx)\sin(\gamma_jx)dx=\begin{cases}
\frac{L}{2} & \text{if $k=j$} \\
0 & \text{if $k\neq j$}
\end{cases}\quad\text{and}\quad\int_0^L\cos(\gamma_kx)\cos(\gamma_jx)dx=\begin{cases}
L & \text{if $k=j=0$} \\
\frac{L}{2} & \text{if $k=j\neq0$} \\
0 & \text{if $k\neq j$},
\end{cases}
\]
we find that~\eqref{eqn:series} is equivalent to $M_kw^k=0$ for all $k\in\mathbb{N}_0$ where $w^0=(w_2^0,w_3^0)\in\mathbb{R}^2$, $w^k=(w_1^k,w_2^k,w_3^k)\in\mathbb{R}^3$ for $k\in\mathbb{N}$ and
\[
M_0=\begin{pmatrix}
c_1 & c_2 \\
c_2 & c_3
\end{pmatrix}\quad\text{and}\quad M_k=\begin{pmatrix}
-a_1\gamma_k^2 & -b_1 & -b_2 \\
-b_1 & c_1-a_2\gamma_k^2 & c_2 \\
-b_2 & c_2 & c_3-a_3\gamma_k^2
\end{pmatrix}\quad\text{for $k\in\mathbb{N}$}.
\]
Thus the existence of a nontrivial solution is equivalent to the existence of some $k\in\mathbb{N}_0$ such that $\det M_k=0$. In Figure~\ref{fig:dets}, on the left, the numerically computed values of $\det M_k$ as functions of $\eta$ are shown. We notice that $\det M_0$ never vanish and the first determinant to vanish is $\det M_1$ slightly after the tensile force reaches its maximum.
\begin{figure}[htp]
\begin{tikzpicture}
\begin{axis}[xlabel={$\eta$},ylabel={$\det M_k$},xmin=1,xmax=1.35,width=0.45\textwidth,every axis plot/.append style={black, thick},no markers,legend pos=south east]
\addplot [solid] table [x index=0,y index=1] {figures/dets.dat};
\addlegendentry{$k=0$};
\addplot [dashed] table [x index=0,y index=2] {figures/dets.dat};
\addlegendentry{$k=1$};
\addplot [dotted] table [x index=0,y index=3] {figures/dets.dat};
\addlegendentry{$k=2$};
\addplot [dashdotted] table [x index=0,y index=4] {figures/dets.dat};
\addlegendentry{$k=3$};
\addplot [dashdotdotted] table [x index=0,y index=5] {figures/dets.dat};
\addlegendentry{$k=4$};
\addplot[domain=1:1.35,samples=2,color=black,dashed,ultra thin] {0};
\end{axis}
\end{tikzpicture}
\hfill
\begin{tikzpicture}
\begin{axis}[xlabel={$L$},ylabel={$\eta_1$},xmin=200,xmax=900,width=0.45\textwidth,every axis plot/.append style={black, thick},no markers,legend pos=south east]
\addplot [solid,smooth] table {figures/etan.dat};
\addplot[domain=200:1000,samples=2,color=black,dashed,ultra thin] {1.1474};
\end{axis}
\end{tikzpicture}
\caption{On the left, value of $\det M_k$ for $k\in\{0,1,2,3,4\}$. Notice the $\det M_0$ never vanishes while $\det M_1$ is the first determinant to vanish at elongation $\eta_1=1.1597$, slightly greater than the elongation $\eta_\text{m}=1.1474$ corresponding to the maximum of $\bar F$. On the right is $\eta_1$ as $L$ changes. Notice that $\eta_1$ tends to $\eta_\text{m}$ as $L\to\infty$.}
\label{fig:dets}
\end{figure}
In the same figure, on the right, is shown the value of the elongation $\eta_1$ when $\det M_1$ vanish as a function of the length of $L$ of the bar. In the limit $L\to\infty$ it results that $\eta_1$ converges to $\eta_\text{m}$, the elongation corresponding to the maximum tensile force: this again suggest that our model agrees with ConsidĂ¨re criterion. This is indeed justified by the following lemma.
\begin{lemma}
Assume that as $L\to\infty$ the value $\eta_k$ corresponding to vanishing $\det M_k$ has a finite limit. Then that limit coincides to $\eta_\text{m}$ for all $k\in\mathbb{N}$.
\end{lemma}
\begin{proof}
Notice that $\gamma_k\to0^+$ when $L\to\infty$. After some computations it results that for each $\eta$
\[
\det M_k=\gamma_k^2(a_1(c_1c_3 - c_2^2) + b_1^2c_3 + b_2^2c_1 - 2b_1b_2c_2)+\mathscr{o}(\gamma_k^2)
\]
where $\mathscr{o}(\gamma_k^2)$ is uniform for $\eta$ constrained in a compact set. Now let $\tilde\eta_k\colon(0,\infty)\to[1,\infty)$ be the curve that assigns to each $L$ the corresponding value of $\eta_k$ at which $\det M_k$ vanish. Computing $\det M_k$ for $\eta=\tilde\eta_k(L)$, dividing it for $\gamma_k^2$, and letting $L\to\infty$, we get that
\[
a_1(c_1c_3 - c_2^2) + b_1^2c_3 + b_2^2c_1 - 2b_1b_2c_2=0\quad\text{for $\eta=\lim_{L\to\infty}\hat{\eta}_k(L)$.}
\]
This last condition is equivalent to $\det M=0$, where
\[
M=\begin{pmatrix}
    a_1  & -b_1 & -b_2 \\
    -b_1 &  c_1 &  c_2 \\
    -b_2 &  c_2 &  c_3
\end{pmatrix}.
\]
On the other and the equilibrium of the trivial solution and the definition of $\bar F$ give
\begin{gather*}
\bar{F} = \bar{W}_{y'_1} \\
-\bar{W}_{y_2} + \frac{d}{dx} \bar{W}_{y'_2} = 0 \\
-\bar{W}_{y_3} + \frac{d}{dx} \bar{W}_{y'_3} = 0.
\end{gather*}
Differentiating these equations, solving for $\left(\frac{d\bar y_2}{d\eta};\frac{d\bar P}{d\eta}\right)$ from the second and the third and substituting in the first, we get
\[
\begin{split}
\frac{d\bar{F}}{d\eta} &= \bar{W}_{y'_1 y_2} \frac{d\bar{y}_2}{d\eta} + \bar{W}_{y'_1 \bar{P}} \frac{d\bar{P}}{d\eta} + \bar{W}_{y'_1 \eta} = 
\begin{pmatrix} \bar{W}_{y'_1 y_2} \\ \bar{W}_{y'_1 \bar{P}} \end{pmatrix} \cdot 
\begin{pmatrix} \frac{d\bar{y}_2}{d\eta} \\ \frac{d\bar{P}}{d\eta} \end{pmatrix} + \bar{W}_{y'_1 \eta}=\\
&=- \begin{pmatrix} \bar{W}_{y'_1 y_2} \\ \bar{W}_{y'_1 \bar{P}} \end{pmatrix} \cdot 
\begin{pmatrix} 
    \bar{W}_{y_2 y_2} & \bar{W}_{y_2 \bar{P}} \\ 
    \bar{W}_{\bar{P} y_2} & \bar{W}_{\bar{P} \bar{P}} + \bar{H}_{11} + \bar{D}_{22} 
\end{pmatrix}^{-1} 
\begin{pmatrix} \bar{W}_{y_2 \eta} \\ \bar{W}_{\bar{P} \eta} \end{pmatrix} + \bar{W}_{y'_1 \eta}.
\end{split}=
\]
Now reasoning as in the proof of Lemma~\ref{lemma:stability-force} and using~\eqref{eqn:matrici-piccoline}, it follows that $\frac{d\bar{F}}{d\eta}$ vanish if and only if $\det M$ vanish. This concludes the proof.
\end{proof}

We have thus proved that when $\eta\ge\eta_1$ the trivial solution does not satisfy the regularized stability condition. When $\det M_1$ vanishes, the kernel of $\partial^2\mathcal{J}^+_t(\bar z(t))$ becomes nontrivial and is spanned by $w$
\[
w_1(x)=w_1^1\sin(\gamma_1x),\quad w_2(x)=w_2^1\cos(\gamma_1x)\quad\text{and}\quad w_3(x)=w_3^1\cos(\gamma_1x)
\]
with $w^1=(w^1_1,w^1_2,w^1_3)$ satisfying $M_1w^1=0$. If we compute numerically $w^1$ we find that $w^1_1>0$, $w^1_2<0$ and $w^1_3>0$, so interpreted as a variation of the configuration of the bar it appears qualitatively as the instability mode we found in the purely elastic case and reported in Figure~\ref{fig:instability-mode-elastic}. As previously observed in Remark~\ref{rmk:stability-regularized}, although $w$ belongs to the kernel of $\partial^2\mathcal{J}_t^+(\bar{z}(t))$, it does not represent a true instability mode since it fails to satisfy the condition $q=w_3\ge0$. Consequently, we have not proven that necking necessarily occurs at this stage; rather, we have established that if necking were to occur, it must do so for $\eta>\eta_1$.

%The results obtained so far fail to prove either the instability of $\overline q(t)$ for $\eta > \eta_1$ or its stability for all $\eta < \eta_1$. Indeed when $\eta>\eta_1$ we know that there exists a direction $w=(v,Q)$ where $\mathcal{J}^+_t$ decreases, but since that $Q$ is not everywhere positive we cannot conclude that also $\mathcal{J}_t$ decreases in that direction. Conversely, when $\eta<\eta_1$ we know for sure that $\mathcal{J}_t$ increases in all that the directions $w=(v,Q)$ with $Q\ge0$ or with $Q\le0$, but not in directions whose $Q$ has mixed sign. However we expect that $\eta_1$ is at least an estimate of the critical elongation at which the trivial solution becomes unstable.

\subsubsection*{True instability} In the previous subsection we have proved that the kernel of $\partial^2\mathcal{J}^+_t(\bar{z}(t))$ never contains $w\in Z_0$ with $q\ge 0$. However to prove that $\bar{z}(t)$ is not a minimizer of $\mathcal{J}^+_t(\bar{z}(t))$ for some $\eta>\eta_1$ it would be sufficient to show that such $w$ exists with the property that $\dual{\mathcal{J}^+_t(\bar{z}(t))w,w}<0$. Here we describe our attempts in that direction, that however were unsuccessful. Consider a finite dimensional subspace of $Z_0$ spanned by $\{w^1,\dots,w^m\}$ that we collect in the matrix field $O\colon(0,L)\to\mathbb{R}^{3\times m}$ given by $O=(w^1,\dots,w^m)$. Then the generic element of that subspace is $w=Oc$ where $c\in\mathbb{R}^m$. Thus
\[
\dual{\mathcal{J}^+_t(\bar{z}(t))w,w}=c^TG(t)c,
\]
where the symmetric matrix $G\in\mathbb{R}^{d\times d}$ is given by
\[
G(t)=\int_0^L\left((O')^TM(t)O'+(O')^TN(t)O+O^TN(t)^TO'+O^TK(t)O\right)dX.
\]
Thus out aim is to find $O$ and $\eta>\eta_1$ such that there exists $c\in\mathbb{R}^{m}$ with $c^TGc<0$ and satisfying the constrain $(Oc)_3\ge0$ in $(0,L)$.

We first consider the choice
\[
O(x)=\begin{pmatrix}
\sin(\gamma_1x) & 0 & 0 & 0 & 0 \\
0 & 1 & \cos(\gamma_1x) & 0 & 0 \\
0 & 0 & 0 & 1 & \cos(\gamma_1x)
\end{pmatrix}
\]
that correspond to a truncation of the formal series~\eqref{eqn:series-formal} at the first orders. The condition $(Oc)_3\ge0$ in this case reads as $|c_5|<c_4$. Let
\[
\mathscr{A}=\{(c_4,c_5)\in\mathbb{R}^2\;|\;\text{there exists $(c_1,c_2,c_3)\in\mathbb{R}^3$ such that $c^TGc<0$}\}.
\]
To characterize $\mathscr{A}$ the following lemma is useful.
\begin{lemma}
Let $m,n\in\mathbb{N}$. Let $A\in\mathbb{R}^{m\times m}$ and $C\in\mathbb{R}^{n\times n}$ be symmetric matrices. Assume that $A$ is positive-definite. Let also $B\in\mathbb{R}^{m\times n}$ and define the symmetric matrix
\[
M=\begin{pmatrix}
A & B^T \\
B & C
\end{pmatrix}.
\]
Then for all $b\in\mathbb{R}^n$ it holds that
\[
\min_{a\in\mathbb{R}^m}\begin{pmatrix}
a^T & b^T
\end{pmatrix}
M
\begin{pmatrix}
a \\
b
\end{pmatrix}=b^T(C-BA^{-1}B^T)b
\]
\end{lemma}
\begin{proof}
For each $b\in\mathbb{R}^n$ consider the map
\begin{equation}
\label{eqn:objective}
\mathbb{R}^m\ni a\mapsto
\begin{pmatrix}
a^T & b^T
\end{pmatrix}
M
\begin{pmatrix}
a \\
b
\end{pmatrix}.
\end{equation}
Its gradient and hessian are respectively
\[
a\mapsto Aa+B^Tb\quad\text{and}\quad a\mapsto A.
\]
Since $A$ is positive-definite, a minimum point of~\eqref{eqn:objective} is
\[
a=-A^{-1}B^Tb.
\]
Thus substituting that $a$ in~\eqref{eqn:objective} we conclude.
\end{proof}
In our case the previous lemma implies that for all $(c_4,c_5)\in\mathbb{R}^2$ it holds
\[
\min_{(c_1,c_2,c_3)\in\mathbb{R}^3}c^TGc=\begin{pmatrix}
c_1 & c_2
\end{pmatrix}
S
\begin{pmatrix}
c_1 \\ c_2
\end{pmatrix},
\]
where
\[
S=
\begin{pmatrix}
G_{44} & G_{45} \\
G_{54} & G_{55}
\end{pmatrix}-
\begin{pmatrix}
G_{41} & G_{42} & G_{43} \\
G_{51} & G_{52} & G_{53} \\
\end{pmatrix}
\begin{pmatrix}
G_{11} & G_{12} & G_{13} \\
G_{21} & G_{22} & G_{23} \\
G_{31} & G_{32} & G_{33}
\end{pmatrix}^{-1}
\begin{pmatrix}
G_{41} & G_{51} \\
G_{42} & G_{52} \\
G_{43} & G_{53} \\
\end{pmatrix}.
\]
The positive definiteness of the 3-by-3 block can be checked explicitly and is always satisfied in the range of constitutive parameters explored by our numerical computations. Now we conclude that
\[
\mathscr{A}=\left\{(c_4,c_5)\in\mathbb{R}^2\;\middle|\;\begin{pmatrix}
c_1 & c_2
\end{pmatrix}
S
\begin{pmatrix}
c_1 \\ c_2
\end{pmatrix}<0\right\}.
\]
From the numerical computations it result that for $\eta>\eta_1$ matrix $S$ is of the form
\[
S=\begin{pmatrix}
S_{11} & 0 \\
0 & S_{22}
\end{pmatrix}
\]
with $S_{11}>0$ and $S_{22}<0$. Then the set $\mathscr{A}$ is the cone defined by
\[
|c_5|\ge-\frac{S_{11}}{S_{22}}|c_4|.
\] 
We conclude that the condition for the existence of a $c\in\mathbb{R}^5$ with $c^TGc<0$ and $|c_5|<c_4$ is $-S_{11}/S_{22}<1$. Unfortunately this never happens, at least in the range of constitutive parameters we have explored.

Another choice of $O$ that we have considered is
\begin{equation}
\label{eqn:basis}
O=\begin{pmatrix}
\delta\left(1-e^{-\frac{x}{\delta}}\right)-\frac{x}{L}\delta\left(1-e^{-\frac{L}{\delta}}\right) & 0 & 0 & 0 \\
0 & 1 & -e^{-\frac{x}{\delta}} & 0 \\
0 & 0 & 0 & e^{-\frac{x}{\delta}}
\end{pmatrix}
\end{equation}
where $\delta>0$ is a parameter to be chosen. The first basis function represents a dilation of the bar localized at the left end; the linear combinations of the second and third basis functions are chosen to represents a localized shrinkage of the cross-sections at the left end and a possible expansion of cross-sections on the right of the bar; eventually the third basis function represents a localized increase of the plastic distortion at the left end. The parameter $\delta$ tune how much the considered basis functions are localized. The idea behind this choice was that we expect that necking should initiate as a localized shrinkage of the cross-sections near one end: this because the eigenvector found in previous subsection (that we recall is not a true instability mode) has this feature, so this type of behavior seems to be the instability mode preferred by the bar under tension. To understand whether an instability mode can be obtained as a linear combination of the basis functions in~\eqref{eqn:basis}, we explored different values of $\delta$ in the range from $\frac{1}{50}l$ to $10 L$ (recall that $l$ is the internal length scale associated with the diameter of the bar while $L$ is the length of the bar). However what we found is that $G$ remains positive definite also for $\eta>\eta_1$ for all the ranges of constitutive parameters that we have considered.

We have obtained similar results to those we just explicitly described even enriching the basis $O$, considering more terms of the formal series~\eqref{eqn:series-formal}, adding other possible shapes of localized necks (for example considering a localization at the center of the bar) and considering their combination.

%[delta*(1-exp(-x/delta))-x/L*delta*(1-exp(-L/delta)),0,0;sin(pi*x/L),0,0;0,cos(pi/L*x),0;0,1,0;0,-x/delta*exp(-x/delta),0;0,-exp(-x/delta),0;0,0,exp(-x/delta)]

\part{A model describing pressure sensitive and dilatant plastic materials}
\label{part:my-model}

\chapter{Introduction and review of preexisting mechanical models}
\label{chap:intro-pressure-sensitive}

Until the 1950s, the application of plasticity theory remained confined to metals. In 1952, Drucker and Prager~\cite{DruckerPrager1952} used the same framework of metal plasticity to describe the mechanical behavior of soils and rocks, interpreting the classic Mohr-Coulomb~\cite{Coulomb1776,Mohr1900} failure criterion as a characterization of the yield limit. They also proposed a new yield criterion, now known as the Drucker-Prager criterion.

The characteristic of the Mohr-Coulomb and the Drucker-Prager yield criteria, absent in the classical plasticity of metals, is that the yield strength increases with increasing pressure. This behavior is appropriate to describe the plasticity of materials such as soils and rocks due to their granular nature. In fact in these cases the plastic behavior is due to the sliding of the grains of which the material is composed. The sliding is opposed by the internal friction between the grains and increases as the grains are pressed against each other. As we saw in Section~\ref{sec:plasticity-review} of Chapter~\ref{chap:modeling}, in the classical formulation of plasticity theory, once the admissibility set for the stress is fixed, the flow rule of the plastic strain is automatically determined by the normality condition of the plastic flow law (see Remark~\ref{rmk:flow-rule} and the discussion reported below). In this case the flow rule is said to be \emph{associated}. When the admissibility criterion also takes pressure into account, the normality condition dictates that the plastic stain cannot remain deviatoric, so the hypothesis of plastic incompressibility must be abandoned. This actually corresponds to a real mechanical phenomenon called \emph{dilatancy} and has a precise micromechanical interpretation: when the plastic flow occurs, the grains slide over each other and, in doing so, they pass from a more optimal packing configuration to a less optimal one, causing an increase in volume. However, the associated flow rule has the drawback of overestimating the dilatancy. To overcome this, non-associated flow rules have been developed, i.e., the plastic flow law is chosen independently of the set of admissible stresses.

The use of the non-associated flow law involves additional mathematical complications because it breaks the standard variational structure of classical plasticity theory, in the sense that it cannot immediately rewritten as a balance of generalized forces, including both a macroscopic and a microscopic equilibrium. For that reason the usual formulation of non-associated plasticity is not compatible with the view of mechanical systems presented in Section~\ref{sec:ppv}. However, recently~\cite{morafrancfort,StefanelliVikelis2024} it has been demonstrated that non-associated plasticity actually admits a variational formulation and existence results have been obtained.

This second part of the thesis is dedicated to the development of an alternative approach to describe plastic materials exhibiting pressure sensitivity and dilatancy. The aim is to build a mechanical theory, directly coherent with the general view of a mechanical systems described in Section~\ref{sec:ppv}, where the intuition of Coulomb~\cite{Coulomb1776} that the friction between the grains increase with their contact pressure is made explicit. We will see that our model, if particular constitutive revelations are assumed, is formally able to recover the equations provided by the original approach. But, since our model possesses a clear variational structure by construction, no ad hoc reformulations are required to restore it, allowing us to establish an existence theory in a more direct and elegant manner.

The discussion is organized as follows. This introductory chapter reviews the standard extension of the plasticity theory presented in Section~\ref{sec:plasticity-review} to account for plastic compressibility and non-associated flow rules. Furthermore, we describe the Drucker--Prager yield criterion and provide an overview of the known existence results. For the sake of simplicity, this review is confined to the case of small deformations. The following chapters are instead dedicated to the formulation of our alternative approach.

\section{Extension of plasticity theory incorporating plastic compressibility and non-associated flow rules}
\label{sec:plasticity-review-soil}

We now describe how the theory presented in Section~\ref{sec:plasticity-review} is extended to include plastic compressibility and non-associated flow rules. The formulation presented here is the standard way used to perform that extension. In the following chapters we will propose an alternative approach.

Let $\Omega\subset\mathbb{R}^d$, open, bounded and Lipschitz, be the reference configuration of a body. Its configurations are assumed to be the couples $z=(u,E_p)\in Z$ where $u\colon\Omega\to\mathbb{R}^d$ is the displacement and $E_p\colon\Omega\to\text{Sym}$ is the plastic strain. Notice that plastic incompressibility is not assumed since $E_p$ is not required to be deviatoric. The elastic strain is $E_e=Eu-E_p$. Analogously of what we did in Section~\ref{sec:plasticity-review} of Chapter~\ref{chap:modeling}, we require that the internal and external virtual power functionals are of the form
\[
\dual{\mathcal{I},\tilde w}=\int_\Omega\left(T_e:\tilde V_e+T_p:\tilde V_p\right)dx
\quad\text{and}\quad
\dual{\mathcal{X},\tilde w}=\int_\Omega f\cdot\tilde vdx+\int_{\partial\Omega}h\cdot\tilde vdx
\]
for all $\tilde w=(\tilde v,\tilde V_p)\in Z$. For simplicity we have assumed that $K_p=0$, i.e., we are not considering plastic strain gradient effects since they are not relevant in the present discussion. Then the strong form of the equilibrium condition $\mathcal{I}=\mathcal{X}$ is
\begin{gather}
\diver T_e+f=0\quad\text{in $\Omega$} \\
T_en=h\quad\text{in $\partial\Omega$} \\
T_e-T_p=0\quad\text{in $\Omega$}
\end{gather}
where the first two equations describe the balance of macroforces while the last equation represents the balance of plastic microforces. The main difference with respect to equations~\eqref{eqn:equilibrium-plasticity} is that in the microforce balance the whole elastic stress tensor $T_e$, and not only its deviator, appears.

We now discuss the choice of constitutive relations. We assume that the free energy $\Psi\colon Z\to\mathbb{R}$ is of the form
\[
\Psi(z)=\int_\Omega\left(W_e(E_e)+W_p(E_p)\right)dx,
\]
where $W_e$ and $W_p$ form $\text{Sym}$ to $\mathbb{R}$ are the elastic and plastic free energy densities which are supposed to be differentiable. This gives the constitutive relation for the elastic stress $T_e=W_e'(E_e)$. If we split the plastic force $T_p$ as the sum of a conservative back-stress $B_p$ and a dissipative plastic force $A_p$, then $B_p=W_p'(E_p)$. Notice that, having written $T_p=A_p+B_p$, the equilibrium of microforces can be written as
\begin{equation}
\label{eqn:Ap}
A_p=T_e-B_p=-\dind{W}{E_p}(Eu,E_p)\quad\text{in $\Omega$},
\end{equation}
where $W(Eu,E_p)=W_e(Eu-E_p)+W_p(E_p)$. To characterize the plastic response
\begin{itemize}
\item[(i)] we require the admissibility condition $A_p\in C$ where $C=\{f\le0\}\subset\text{Sym}$, with $f\colon\text{Sym}\to\mathbb{R}$ a convex \emph{yield function} satisfying $f(0)<0$, and
\item[(ii)] we impose the flow rule $\dot E_p=0$ if $A_p\in\inter C$ and $\dot E_p$ belongs to the normal outer cone to $L(A_p)=\{A\in\text{Sym}\;|\;g(A)\le g(A_p)\}$ at $A_p$ if $A_p\in\partial C$, where $g\colon\text{Sym}\to\mathbb{R}$ is a convex \emph{plastic potential}.
\end{itemize}
When the yield function $f$ coincides with the plastic potential $g$, then the flow rule reduces to $\dot E_p\in N_C(A_p)$ which is the normality rule. When $f=g$ we say that the flow rule is associated, while when $f\neq g$ we say that the flow rule is non-associated. This is the way in which the plastic response is characterized in the classical presentations of non-associated plasticity models. However this formulation does not fit directly into the framework of Section~\ref{sec:ppv} of Chapter~\ref{chap:modeling} and is not suitable for proving existence theorems. A better reformulation is described in~\cite{Laborde1987,morafrancfort,StefanelliVikelis2024} and is reproduced in the following lemma.
\begin{lemma}
The admissibility condition and the flow rule are equivalent to
\begin{equation}
\label{eqn:reformulation}
A_p\in\hat{C}(A_p)\quad\text{and}\quad\dot E_p\in N_{\hat{C}(A_p)}(A_p),
\end{equation}
where
\[
\hat{C}(A_p)=\left\{A\in\text{Sym}\;|\;g(A)\le g(A_p)-f(A_p)\right\}.
\]
In turn, the inclusions in~\eqref{eqn:reformulation} are also equivalent to
\begin{equation}
\label{eqn:reformulation-2}
A_p\in\partial\hat{R}(A_p,\dot E_p),
\end{equation}
where $\hat{R}\colon\text{Sym}\times\text{Sym}\to[0,\infty]$ is defined as
\begin{equation}
\label{eqn:R-hat}
\hat{R}(A_p,\dot E_p)=\sup_{A\in\hat{C}(A_p)}A:\dot E_p=\sigma_{\hat{C}(A_p)}(\dot E_p).
\end{equation}
\end{lemma}
\begin{proof}
It is trivial to see that $A_p\in\hat{C}(A_p)$ if and only if $f(A_p)\le 0$, or equivalently $A_p\in C$. Moreover $A_p\in\inter\hat{C}(A_p)$ if and only if $A_p\in\inter C$ and $A_p\in\partial\hat{C}(A_p)$ if and only if $A_p\in\partial C$. This implies also that if $A_p\in\inter C$, then $N_{\hat{C}(A_p)}(A_p)=\{0\}$. Instead, if $A_p\in\partial C$ then $f(A_p)=0$ so that $\hat{C}(A_p)=L(A_p)$ and $N_{\hat{C}(A_p)}(A_p)=N_{L(A_p)}$. This proves the first equivalence.

The second equivalence follows from a well known results of complex analysis~\cite[Corollary 5.2]{ekeland-temam}.
\end{proof}
Now in order to write the inclusion~\eqref{eqn:reformulation-2} as a true constitutive relation, i.e., expressing the dependence of $A_p$ on kinematical quantities alone, one can use~\eqref{eqn:Ap}. Thus we define $R\colon\text{Sym}\times\text{Sym}\times\text{Sym}\to[0,\infty]$ by
\begin{equation}
\label{eqn:Rhat-R}
R(Eu,E_p,\dot E_p)=\hat{R}\left(-\dind{W}{E_p}(Eu,E_p),\dot E_p\right)
\end{equation}
and we require that $A_p\in\partial_{\dot E_p}R(Eu,E_p,\dot E_p)$. This correspond to assuming that the dissipation potential $\Psi\colon Z\times Z\to[0,\infty]$ is defined as
\[
\Psi(z,\dot z)=\int_\Omega R(Eu,E_p,\dot E_p)dx.
\]
\begin{rmk}
If the dependence on plastic strain gradients is included, the balance of microforces~\eqref{eqn:Ap} becomes
\[
A_p=T_e-B_p+\diver K_p.
\]
The quantity $\diver K_p$ is not defined as a function in general. This prevents the possibility of defining $R$ from $\hat R$ by a simple substitution. In this case, see~\cite{StefanelliVikelis2024}, the definition of $R$ kept as in~\eqref{eqn:Rhat-R}.
\end{rmk}

\section{The Mohr--Coulomb and Drucker--Prager yield criteria and non associated flow rules}
\label{sec:yield-criteria}

In this section we show how the Mohr--Coulomb and Drucker--Prager yield criterion can be formulated within the notation of previous section. Moreover we will describe the non-associated flow rule commonly employed to describe dilatancy.

\subsection{The Mohr--Coulomb criterion}

The Mohr--Coulomb criterion was the first criterion failure of cohesive materials such as rocks and soil. The original formulation states that failure happen at a material point where the Cauchy stress tensor $T\in\text{Sym}$ there exists a direction $n\in\partial B(0,1)$ such that
\begin{equation}
\label{eqn:mhor-coulomb}
\tau=-\sigma\tan\phi+c,
\end{equation}
where $\sigma=Tn\cdot n$ and $\tau=|Tn-\sigma n|$ are the normal and shear stress respectively acting on the plane orthogonal to $n$. Here $\phi\in[0,\pi/2)$ is the angle of internal friction and $c\ge0$ is the cohesion. The idea behind~\eqref{eqn:mhor-coulomb} is that the resistance of the material to shear stresses increases with compression. One can show (see~\cite{Lubliner1990}) that failure occur on the two hyperplanes whose normal form an angle of $\pi/2+\phi/2$ with respect to the eigenspace corresponding to the greatest eigenvalue; this is the reason why $\phi$ is called the angle of internal friction.
%In the Mohr plane, that is the half plane of the couples $(\sigma,\tau)\in\mathbb{R}\times[0,\infty)$, the failure locus determined by~\eqref{eqn:mhor-coulomb} is a cone as showed in Figure~\ref{fig:mhor-coulomb}.
%Now in can be shown (see~\cite{Lubliner1990}) that as $n$ varies in $\partial B(0,1)$ the couples $(\sigma,\tau)$ are confined within a circle 
%\[
%\text{centered at $\left(\dfrac{T_\text{min}+T_\text{max}}{2},0\right)$ and with radius $\dfrac{T_\text{max}-T_\text{min}}{2}$},
%\]
%where $T_\text{min}$ and $T_\text{max}$ are the largest and smallest eigenvalues to $T$. The boundary of this circle is entirely covered. Then failure occur when the circle became tangent to the cone.
After some computations one finds that the failure criterion can be written directly in term of $T$ as
\begin{equation}
\label{eqn:f-mohr-coulomb}
f(T)=F(\dev T)+\frac{2}{d}(\tr T)\sin\phi-2c\cos\phi=0.
\end{equation}
with $f\colon\text{Sym}\to\mathbb{R}$ and $F\colon\text{DevSym}\to\mathbb{R}$ given by
\begin{equation}
\label{eqn:F-mohr-coulomb}
F(S)=S_\text{max}-S_\text{min}+(S_\text{max}+S_\text{min})\sin\phi,
\end{equation}
where $S_\text{min}$ and $S_\text{max}$ are the maximum and minimum eigenvalues of $S$.

Now the Mohr--Coulomb criterion is included in the theory described in Section~\ref{sec:plasticity-review-soil} by using $f$ in~\eqref{eqn:f-mohr-coulomb} as the yield function.

\subsection{The Drucker--Prager yield criterion}

When $d=2$ it is easy to check that $F$ in~\eqref{eqn:F-mohr-coulomb} can be also written as $F(S)=\sqrt{2}|S|$. In this case the yield function becomes
\begin{equation}
\label{eqn:f-mohr-coulomb-2d}
f(A)=\sqrt{2}|\dev A|+(\tr A)\sin\phi-2c\cos\phi.
\end{equation}
In the general case, this formula suggest to consider the yield function $f\colon\text{Sym}\to\mathbb{R}$ of the form
\begin{equation}
\label{eqn:f}
f(A)=|\dev A|-\left(f_0-a\frac{\tr A}{d}\right)
\end{equation}
where $f_0>0$ and $a>0$. The corresponding criterion is known as Drucker--Prager yield criterion and can be seen as an approximation of the Mohr--Coulomb criterion. Since the hydrostatic pressure is $p=-\tr T/d$, the Drucker--Prager criterion can be formulated as $|\dev T|\le f_0+ap$. Thus it is a generalization of the Von Mises criterion where the yield strength is made dependent on the hydrostratic pressure.

\subsection{Non associated flow rules and the dilatancy angle}

Restrict now to the case of the Drucker--Prager criterion, the discussion being similar in the case of the Mohr--Coulomb crierion. If the normality rule corresponding to $g=f$ is assumed, then $E_p$ evolves as
\[
\dot E_p=\gamma\dind{f}{A}(A_p)=\gamma\left[\frac{\dev A_p}{|\dev A_p|}+\frac{a}{d}I\right]
\]
where $\gamma$ is the plastic multiplier that is required to satisfy the Kuhn--Tucker compatibility conditions
\[
\gamma\ge0,\quad f(A_p)\le0\quad\text{and}\quad\gamma f(A_p)=0.
\]
Then it holds that
\[
|\dev\dot E_p|=\gamma\quad\text{and}\quad\tr\dot E_p=\gamma a
\]
so
\[
\tr\dot E_p=a|\dev\dot E_p|.
\]
This implies that the accumulation of deviatoric plastic distortion is always accompanied with an increase in the volumetric plastic distortion. This behavior is called dilatancy and is common in granular materials. However the normality rule overestimates the dilatancy, so that one would like to consider a relation of the type
\[
\tr\dot E_p=b|\dev\dot E_p|
\]
with $b<a$. This motivate the need of introducing a non-associated flow rule by choosing $g$ as
\begin{equation}
\label{eqn:g}
g(A)=|\dev A|-\left(g_0-b\frac{\tr A}{d}\right)
\end{equation}
with $g_0,b>0$. In the case $d=2$ we can define $g$ with the same structure as $f$ in~\eqref{eqn:f-mohr-coulomb-2d}, that is
\[
g(A)=\sqrt{2}|\dev A|+(\tr A)\sin\psi-2c\cos\psi
\]
where $c\ge0$ is still the cohesion and $\psi\in[0,\pi/2)$ is called the dilatancy angle (that is usually chosen satisfying $\psi\le\phi$).

\section{An existence result for non-associative plasticity}

An existence theorem for non-associative compressible plasticity at small deformations has been derived in~\cite{morafrancfort} and is reported below. It is obtained assuming no hardening and adding a viscous plastic microforce.
\begin{thm}
\label{thm:existence-non-associative}
Let $\Omega\subset\mathbb{R}^d$ be open, bounded and Lipschitz. Let $\mathfrak{C}\in\mathscr{L}(\text{Sym},\text{Sym})$ be a symmetric and positive definite elasticity tensor. Let $\eta>0$ a plastic viscosity. Let $K\subset\text{DevSym}$ be bounded, closed and convex and such that $0\in\inter K$, and define the admissibility set
\[
C=\left\{A\in\text{Sym}\;|\;\text{$\dev A\in\left(f_0-a\frac{\tr A}{d}\right)K$ and $\tr A\ge-M$}\right\}
\]
for some $a>0$, $f_0>0$ and $M>0$. Let also
\[
G=\left\{A\in\text{Sym}\;|\;\text{$\dev A\in\left(g_0-b\frac{\tr A}{d}\right)K$ and $\tr A\ge-M$}\right\},
\]
where $b>0$ and $g_0>0$. Define  $f$ and $g$ as the signed distance from $C$ and $G$ respectively, and let $\hat{R}$ defined as in~\eqref{eqn:R-hat}. Let $(u_0,E_p^0)\in H^1_0(\Omega,\mathbb{R}^d)\times L^2(\Omega,\text{Sym})$. Then there exists a unique couple $(u,E_p)$ with
\[
u\in H^1((0,T),H^1_0(\Omega,\mathbb{R}^d))\quad\text{and}\quad E_p\in W^{1,\infty}((0,T),L^2(\Omega,\text{Sym}))
\]
such that, setting $T=\mathfrak{C}(Eu-E_p)$, the following conditions hold:
\begin{itemize}
\item[(i)] the initial condition $u(0)=u_0$,
\item[(ii)] the macroscopic equilibrium
\[
\diver T(t)=0\quad\text{in the sense of distributions for all $t\in[0,T]$,}
\]
\item[(iii)] the flow rule
\[
\eta\dot E_p(t)-T(t)+\partial_{\dot E_p}\hat{R}(T(t),\dot E_p(t))\ni0\quad\text{for all $t\in[0,T]$.}
\]
\end{itemize}
\end{thm}
\begin{rmk}[The limit of vanishing viscosity]
Using a time rescaling approach it is possible to prove also the existence of a limit solution $(u,E_p)$ in the limit $\eta\to0^+$ of vanishing viscosity, with $u\in C^0([0,T],\text{BD}(\Omega))$ and $E_p\in W^{1,\infty}((0,T),\mathscr{M}(\bar\Omega,\text{Sym}))$, where $\text{BD}(\Omega)$ is the space of functions of bounded variations and $\mathscr{M}(\bar\Omega,\text{Sym})$ the space of bounded Radon measures.
\end{rmk}
\begin{rmk}[The cap models]
Notice that, in the notation of the statement of Theorem~\ref{thm:existence-non-associative}, the Drucker--Prager model is recovered if one set $K=\bar B(0,1)\subset\text{DevSym}$, except for the addiction of the condition $\tr A\ge-M$ in the definition of the sets $C$ and $G$. This additional condition is required to make $C$ and $G$ bounded subsets of $\text{DevSym}$. This is a technical requirement needed to prove the existence theorem. However, adding condition to bound the set of admissible deformations under compressive loads is a common practice also from the modeling viewpoint and this kind of models are known as cap models. Indeed this adjustment allows to describe the possibility to compact the material with compressive loads.
\end{rmk}

\chapter{Proposal of an alternative mechanical model: the regime of small deformations}
\label{chap:pressure-sensitive-small}

In this chapter we develop an alternative approach to the modeling of dilatant and pressure sensitive plastic materials. We will confine to the regime of small deformations, the extension to large deformations will be performed in the following chapter.

The approach we are going to present is based on the believe that some kinematical constrain on the volumetric part of the plastic distortion must be considered and that the constraint must be treated with the method of Lagrange multipliers. The Lagrange multiplier associated with that constrain is interpreted as a micropressure, representing how much the grains constituting the materials are pressed against each other. The friction between the grains is then modeled as a frictional internal microforce opposing deviatoric plastic rates and depending on the micropressure, according to the original intuition of Coulomb~\cite{Coulomb1776}.

The kinematical constrain that we will consider will be a one-to-one relation between the volumetric plastic distortion and an internal variable, representing the damage state of the body. This picture was suggested by the qualitative discussion in~\cite{micro-geo}, where the idea that the increase in the volumetric part of the plastic distortion in geomaterials is due to the formations of microcracks, is proposed. This led to authors to couple plasticity with damage, even though they do not explicitly consider a link between the volumetric plastic distortion and the damage variable. This is instead our assumption.

The chapter is organized as follows. In the first section the model is formally developed in its generality. In the second section a micro-mechanical interpretation is proposed, thus leading to the development of more particularized constitutive relations in the third section. In the fourth section we also establish the formal equivalence of our model to the classical one described in previous section when particular constitutive relations are chosen. In the fifth section we prove an existence result exploiting the abstract existence theory that we have developed in Section~\ref{sec:existence-theorem-abstract-constrained}, thus clarifying the mathematical formulation of the problem and the assumptions on constitutive relations.

\section{Formulation of the model}

Let $\Omega\subset\mathbb{R}^d$, open, bounded and Lipschitz, be the reference configuration of the body. Assume that the configuration of the body is fully described by the displacement field $u\colon\Omega\to\mathbb{R}^d$, the plastic distortion\footnote{For simplicity we assume that $E_p$ is symmetric, thus excluding plastic spin.} $E_p\colon\Omega\to\text{Sym}$ and an internal damage variable $\alpha\colon\Omega\to\mathbb{R}$. Only nonnegative values of $\alpha$ will be considered: $\alpha=0$ corresponds to a sound material while $\alpha\to\infty$ to a completely damaged material. We split the plastic distortion as $E_p=\pi+\frac{\lambda}{d}I$ where $\pi=\dev E_p\colon\Omega\to\text{DevSym}$ is the deviatoric plastic distortion and $\lambda=\tr E_p\colon\Omega\to\mathbb{R}$ the volumetric plastic distortion. The generic configuration of the body is $z=(u,\pi,\alpha,\lambda)=(y,\lambda)\in Z$ with $y=(u,\pi,\alpha)\in Y$ and $\lambda\in\Lambda$: the spaces $Z$, $Y$ and $\Lambda$ will be precisely defined in the following section. The virtual rates will be denoted as $\tilde w=(\tilde h,\tilde\mu)\in Z$ with $\tilde h=(\tilde v,\tilde\rho,\tilde\beta)\in Y$ and $\tilde\mu\in\Lambda$.

 We consider a kinematical constrain of the form $\lambda=L(\alpha)$, where $L\colon\mathbb{R}\to\mathbb{R}$ is assumed to be smooth and satisfying $L(0)=0$. This constrain models the fact that the volume changes of the microscopic structure of the body are directly linked with its damage state that represents qualitatively the amount of microcracks opened in the body. The constrain can be formulated in weak form as
\[
\int_\Omega\lambda qdx=\int_\Omega L(\alpha)qdx=:\dual{q,\mathcal{L}(y)}\quad\text{for all $q\in\Lambda^*$},
\]
where we have introduced the map $\mathcal{L}\colon Y\to\Lambda$. The set of kinematically admissible configurations is $K=\{z=(y,\lambda)\in Z\;|\;\lambda=\mathcal{L}(y)\}$. The constrain is imposed with the method of Lagrange multipliers, so we add the Lagrange multiplier (or constraint reaction) $\mathcal{P}\in-N_K(z)$ as we discussed in Subsection~\ref{subsec:lagrange-multipliers}. We can parametrize it in terms of a field $p\colon\Omega\to\mathbb{R}$, $p\in\Lambda^*$ as
\[
\dual{\mathcal{P},\tilde w}=\dual{p,\tilde\mu-\mathcal{L}'(y)\tilde h}=\int_\Omega p(\tilde\mu-L'(\alpha)\tilde\beta)dx
\]
so that the normality condition between the reaction and the set of admissible configurations is automatically satisfied.
\begin{rmk}(Mechanical interpretation of $p$)
\label{rmk:interpretation-of-p}
Notice that the constraint reaction $\mathcal{P}$ ``pushes'' $\lambda$ towards positive or negative values according to the sign of $p$, so that $p$ can be mechanically interpreted as a microscopic internal pressure. If $L'(\alpha)>0$ then the constraint reaction also ``push'' the damage variable $\alpha$ towards the opposite direction. In this case, when $p<0$, the damage is ``pushed'' to increase. This behavior can be exploited to model the tendency of geomaterials to damage when subject to traction. The tendency of geomaterials to damage in presence shear loads will emerge by the coupling with the plastic behavior.
\end{rmk}

Following the usual ideas of gradient plasticity theory that we have seen repeatedly in previous chapters, we assume that the internal generalized force is given of the form
\begin{equation}
\label{eqn:internal-power}
\begin{multlined}
\dual{\mathcal{I},\tilde w}=\int_\Omega\left(T:\grad\tilde v+K\threedots\grad^2\tilde v\right)dx+\int_\Omega T_e:\tilde V_edx+\\
+\int_\Omega\left(T_p:\tilde\rho+K_p\threedots\grad\tilde\rho\right)dx+\int_\Omega\left(\tau\tilde\beta+\kappa\cdot\grad\tilde\beta\right)dx,
\end{multlined}
\end{equation}
where $\tilde V_e:=E\tilde v-\tilde\rho-\tilde\mu I$. The first integral is related to the macroscopic response. Here $T\colon\Omega\to\text{Sym}$ is the macrostress and $K\colon\Omega\to\text{Lin}\otimes\mathbb{R}$ is the macrohyperstress. The presence of the second order gradient of $\tilde v$ is needed to ensure compactness for the proof of the existence theorem. The second integral describe the coupling between the macroscopic and the microscopic response and $T_e\colon\Omega\to\text{Sym}$ is the elastic stress. The third integral is associated with the plastic response. Here $T_p\colon\Omega\to\text{DevSym}$ is the plastic deviatoric microforce and $K_p\colon\Omega\to\text{DevSym}\otimes\mathbb{R}$ is the plastic deviatoric microstress. In Figure~\ref{fig:rheological-new} we
report a picture that represents schematically the meaning of the first three integrals in the expression of $\mathcal{I}$.
\begin{figure}
\centering
\begin{tikzpicture}[thick, scale=1]
% Ingressi laterali
\draw (-0.5,0) -- (0,0);
\draw (4,0) -- (3.5,0);

% Connessioni verticali
\draw (0,0) -- (0,1) -- (1.25,1);
\draw (0,0) -- (0,-1) -- (0.5,-1);
\draw (3.5,0) -- (3.5,-1) -- (3,-1);
\draw (3.5,0) -- (3.5,1) -- (2.25,1);

% Ramo Superiore: Blocco T
\draw (1.25,0.75) rectangle (2.25,1.25);
\node[anchor=south] at (1.75,1.25) {$T$};

% Ramo Inferiore: Molla Te (disegnata segmento per segmento)
\draw (0.5,-1) -- (0.571,-0.75) -- (0.714,-1.25) -- (0.857,-0.75) -- (1,-1.25) -- (1.143,-0.75) -- (1.286,-1.25) -- (1.429,-0.75) -- (1.5,-1);
\node[anchor=north] at (1,-1.25) {$T_e$};
\draw (1.5,-1) -- (2.5,-1);

% Ramo Inferiore: Smorzatore Tp
\draw (2.5,-1.125) -- (2.5,-0.875); % Pistone
\draw (2,-0.75) -- (3,-0.75) -- (3,-1.25) -- (2,-1.25); % Cilindro
\node[anchor=north] at (2.5,-1.25) {$T_p$};
\end{tikzpicture}
\caption{Rheological model describing the choice of the internal generalized force. The
box in upper branch represents the (generic) macroscopic response. In the lower branch,
the spring represents the elastic response and the piston represents the plastic response. A more detailed discussion on the micro-mechanical interpretation is in Section~\ref{sec:micro-mechanical}.}
\label{fig:rheological-new}
\end{figure}
The fourth integral describe the response of the damage variable, in particular $\tau\colon\Omega\to\mathbb{R}$ is the damage microforce and $\kappa\colon\Omega\to\mathbb{R}^d$ the damage microstress. The external generalized force is assumed to be given by
\[
\dual{\mathcal{X},\tilde w}=\int_\Omega f\cdot\tilde vdx+\int_{\partial\Omega}h\cdot\tilde vdx,
\]
where $f\colon\Omega\to\mathbb{R}^d$ is the volumetric force and $h\colon\partial\Omega\to\mathbb{R}^d$ is the surface force acting on the external boundary. For simplicity we assume that no external torque is applied to the body and that there is no external loads acring on the microscopic configuration. These additional external loads could be added to describe more accurately the external actions acting on the body.

Now the equilibrium condition is $-\mathcal{I}+\mathcal{X}+\mathcal{P}=0$. The following lemma gives its strong form.
\begin{prop}
\label{prop:strong-form-pressure-sensitive-small}
Assuming that $\Omega$ is piecewise smooth and the fields appearing in $\mathcal{I}$, $\mathcal{X}$ and $\mathcal{P}$ are smooth, the strong form of the equilibrium condition is
\begin{align*}
&\diver(T+T_e-\diver K)+f=0\quad\text{in $\Omega$} \\
&(T+T_e-\diver K)n-\diver_P((Kn)P)=h\quad\text{in $\Gamma_N\cap\partial^{d-1}\Omega$} \\
&\{\!\!\{(Kn)\xi\}\!\!\}=0\quad\text{in $\partial^{d-2}\Omega$} \\
&\dev T_e-T_p+\diver K_p=0\quad\text{in $\Omega$} \\
&p=-\frac{1}{d}\tr T_e\quad\text{in $\Omega$} \\
&K_p n=0\quad\text{in $\partial^{d-1}\Omega$} \\
&-L'(\alpha)p-\tau+\diver\kappa=0\quad\text{in $\Omega$} \\
&\kappa\cdot n=0\quad\text{in $\partial^{d-1}\Omega$},
\end{align*}
where $\partial^m\Omega$ denotes the set of $m$-dimensional faces of $\partial\Omega$ (for example in the case $d=2$ then $\partial^1\Omega$ are the edges and $\partial^0\Omega$ are the vertices of $\partial\Omega$). Moreover $P$ is the projector onto the tangent space to the boundary of $\Omega$ and $\xi$ is the outer normal vector to the boundary $d-1$ dimensional face and tangent to it and $\{\!\!\{\cdot\}\!\!\}$ denotes the sum of a quantity defined along the two sides of a $d-2$ dimensional face.
\end{prop}
\begin{proof}
By integration by parts we can write
\begin{multline*}
\int_\Omega\left(T:\grad\tilde v+K\threedots\grad^2\tilde v\right)dx=\\
=-\int_\Omega\diver(T-\diver K)\cdot\tilde vdx+\int_{\partial^{d-1}\Omega}\left[(T-\diver K)n\cdot\tilde v+Kn:\grad\tilde v\right]dx.
\end{multline*}
Now denoting with $P=I-n\otimes n$ the projector on $\partial^{d-1}\Omega$ we have
\[
Kn:\grad\tilde v=Kn:\left(\dind{\tilde v}{n}\otimes n+\grad_P\tilde v\right)=(Kn)n\cdot\dind{\tilde v}{n}+Kn:\grad_P\tilde v
\]
and
\[
Kn:\grad_P\tilde v=(Kn)P:\grad_P\tilde v=\diver_P(P(Kn)^T\tilde v)-\diver_P((Kn)P)\cdot\tilde v.
\]
But by integration by parts
\[
\int_{\partial^{d-1}\Omega}\diver_P(P(Kn)^T\tilde v)dx=\int_{\partial^{d-2}\Omega}\{\!\!\{P(Kn)^T\tilde v\cdot\tau\}\!\!\}dx=\int_{\partial^{d-2}\Omega}\{\!\!\{(Kn)\tau\}\!\!\}\cdot\tilde vdx.
\]
Here $\{\!\!\{\cdot\}\!\!\}$ denotes sum of its argument evaluated on both sides of $\partial^{d-2}\Omega$ and $\tau$ denotes the unit normal vector to $\partial^{d-2}\Omega$ and outer tangent to $\partial^{d-1}\Omega$. We have obtained
\begin{multline*}
\int_\Omega\left(T:\grad\tilde v+K\threedots\grad^2\tilde v\right)dx=\\
=-\int_\Omega\diver(T-\diver K)\cdot\tilde vdx+\int_{\partial^{d-1}\Omega}\left[(T-\diver K)n-\diver_P((Kn)P)\right]\cdot\tilde vdx+\\
+\int_{\partial^{d-1}\Omega}(Kn)n\cdot\dind{\tilde v}{n}dx+\int_{\partial^{d-2}\Omega}\{\!\!\{(Kn)\tau\}\!\!\}\cdot\tilde vdx.
\end{multline*}
Moreover the remaining terms of the internal generalized force can be written as
\begin{multline*}
-\int_\Omega\diver T_e:\tilde vdx+\int_{\partial^{d-1}\Omega}T_en\cdot\tilde vdx+\int_\Omega(T_p-\dev T_e-\diver K_p):\tilde\rho dx+\\
+\int_{\partial^{d-1}\Omega}K_pn:\tilde\rho dx-\int_\Omega\frac{1}{d}\tr T_e\tilde\mu dx+\int_\Omega(\tau\tilde\rho-\diver\kappa)\tilde\beta dx+\int_{\partial^{d-1}\Omega}\kappa\cdot n\tilde\beta dx.
\end{multline*}
Now the results follows from the fundamental lemma of the calculus of variations.
\end{proof}
\begin{rmk}(Again on the mechanical interpretation of $p$)
The strong form of the equilibrium conditions tell us that $p=-\frac{1}{d}\tr T_e$. Thus $p$ is identified with the pressure of the elastic stress but is does not include the pressure $-\frac{1}{d}\tr T$ of the purely macroscopic stress.
\end{rmk}

We now turn to the choice of the constitutive relations. We assume that the free energy functional $\Psi\colon Z\to\mathbb{R}$ is given by $\Psi(z)=\Psi_0(z)+\frac{1}{2}\dual{\mathcal{A}y,y}$ with $\Psi_0\colon Z\to\mathbb{R}$ of the form
\[
\Psi_0(z)=\int_\Omega\left(W_e\left(Eu-\pi-\frac{1}{d}\lambda I\right)+W_p(\alpha,\pi)+W_d(\alpha)\right)dx
\]
and
\[
\frac{1}{2}\dual{\mathcal{A}y,y}=\int_\Omega\left(\frac{\mathfrak{a}}{2}|\grad^2u|^2+\frac{\mathfrak{b}}{2}|\grad\pi|^2+\frac{\mathfrak{c}}{2}|\grad\alpha|^2\right)dx.
\]
Here $\mathfrak{a},\mathfrak{b},\mathfrak{c}>0$, $W_e\colon\text{Sym}\to[0,\infty)$ is the elastic energy density, $W_p\colon\mathbb{R}\times\text{DevSym}\to[0,\infty)$ is the plastic hardening energy density and $W_d\colon[0,\infty)\to\mathbb{R}$ is the density of the energy captured by damage. We assume that $W_e,W_p,W_d$ are smooth. Next we choose that the dissipation potential functional is of the form $\Phi\colon\Lambda^*\times Y\to[0,\infty]$ with $\Phi(p,h)=\Phi_0(p,h)+\frac{1}{2}\dual{\mathcal{D}h,h}$ where
\[
\Phi_0(p,h)=\int_\Omega\left(R(p,\rho)+D(\rho,\beta)\right)dx
\]
and
\[
\frac{1}{2}\dual{\mathcal{D}h,h}=\int_\Omega\left(\frac{1}{2}\mathfrak{D}Ev:Ev+\frac{\mathfrak{d}}{2}|\rho|^2+\frac{\mathfrak{e}}{2}|\beta|^2\right)dx,
\]
where the macroscopic viscosity tensor $\mathfrak{D}\in\text{Sym}\otimes\text{Sym}$ is symmetric and positive definite, $R\colon\mathbb{R}\times\text{DevSym}\to[0,\infty)$ is the plastic dissipation potential density made dependent on the micropressure, $\mathfrak{d}>0$ is a plastic viscosity, $D\colon\text{DevSym}\times\mathbb{R}\to[0,\infty]$ a damage dissipation potential density made dependent of the rate of deviatoric plastic distortion and $\mathfrak{e}>0$ is a damage viscosity. We assume that $R(p,\cdot)$ and $D$ are convex and vanish at the origin. Form these expressions for $\Psi$ and $\Phi$ we get the following constitutive relations
\begin{align}
T=\mathfrak{D}E\dot u\qquad & K=\mathfrak{a}\grad^2u \\
T_e=\dind{W_e}{E_e}\left(Eu-\pi-\frac{\lambda}{d}I\right)\qquad & \\
T_p\in\dind{W_p}{\pi}(\alpha,\pi)+\partial_\rho R(p,\dot\pi)+\partial_\rho D(\dot\pi,\dot\alpha)+\mathfrak{d}\dot\pi\qquad & K_p=\mathfrak{b}\grad\pi \\
\tau\in \dind{W_p}{\alpha}(\alpha,\pi)+\dind{W_d}{\alpha}(\alpha)+\partial_\beta D(\dot\pi,\dot\alpha)+\mathfrak{e}\dot\alpha\qquad & \kappa=\mathfrak{c}\grad\alpha.
\end{align}
A more particularized choice of constitutive relations is proposed and discussed in Section~\ref{sec:particular-constitutive}.

\section{A possible micro-mechanical interpretation of the model}
\label{sec:micro-mechanical}

We assume that the material consists of an assembly of interlocking elastic crystalline grains, possibly held together by a binding matrix. The macroscopic deformation of the body described by $u$ is due to the combination of the following two phenomena:
\begin{itemize}
\item[(i)] the mutual (irreversible) displacement of the grains described by the plastic strain $E_p$, including their deviatoric sliding described by $\pi=\dev E_p$ and the opening of microcracks within binding matrix the between the grains described by $\lambda=\tr E_p$,
\item[(ii)] the (reversible) elastic deformation of the crystalline structure of the grains and the binding matrix, described by $E_e=Eu-E_p$.
\end{itemize}
The internal variable $\alpha$ is a qualitative description of the damage state of the material associated with the fact that the cohesion between the grains deteriorates. The kinematical constrain $\lambda=L(\alpha)$ reflect the fact that the damage is correlated with the opening of microcracks, that in turn are associated with an increase of volume. The internal pressure $p$ describes how much the grains are pushed against each other (if $p\ge0$) or bonded by the binding matrix (if $p\le0$), see also Remark~\ref{rmk:interpretation-of-p} above.

Starting from this micro-mechanical picture we are now in position to give an interpretation to the terms appearing in the expression~\eqref{eqn:internal-power} of the generalized internal power and in the definition of the free energy and dissipation potential.

\subsubsection*{Elastic response} The elastic stress $T_e$ is the elastic internal action of the grains and the binding matrix that opposes to their deformation and $W_e$ represents the stored elastic energy.

\subsubsection*{Deviatoric plastic response} The forces exchanged by the grains in the ``direction'' of deviatoric sliding are described by $T_p$ and $K_p$ which opposes to the push of the elastic stress. In particular the presence of $K_p$ (qualifying the model as a model of gradient elastoplasticity) allows to make this internal interaction nonlocal, reflecting the finite size of the grains: the internal length scale is determined by how the constitutive parameter $\mathfrak{b}$ relates to other local stiffness parameters. The plastic dissipation potential density $R$ aims to describe the internal friction between the grains and is assumed to depends on the pressure in accordance with Coulomb intuition~\cite{Coulomb1776}. Instead the hardening free energy density $W_p$ can describe an additional resistance of the grains to slide, due to the geometric interlocking between them. The dependence of $W_p$ on $\alpha$ reflects the deterioration of the capacity of the grains to interlock due to increasing damage. The addiction of the plastic viscosity regularizes the evolution of $\pi$ introducing rate dependence.

\subsubsection*{Volumetric plastic response and damage response}

The spherical part of the plastic strain is subject to the push of the elastic stress which is opposed by the internal pressure $p$ that impose the constrain $\lambda=L(\alpha)$. For simplicity we neglect the possibility that the material can be compacted under compressive loads by closing the microcracks, so that $\lambda$ is not allowed to evolve independently on damage. The damage variable receives the reaction $L'(\alpha)p$ that is opposed by the damage microforce $\tau$ and microstress $\kappa$. As for the deviatoric response, the presence of $\kappa$ makes the damage response nonlocal, reflecting the finite size of the grains determined by the parameter $\mathfrak{c}$. The dissipation potential density $D$ allows to relate the evolution of $\alpha$ to that of $\pi$: this is crucial to describe the fact that new microcracks opens during a deviatoric plastic straining. As we will see in the next section an appropriate choice of $D$ allows also to impose the unidirectional evolution of damage (the fact that damage can only increase with time). The damage free energy density $W_d$ describe the energy expended to create damage (even thought it is stored as a free energy, it cannot be recovered due to the unidirectional evolution of damage). The term $\dind{W_p}{\alpha}(\alpha,\pi)$ is the damaging mechanism associated with decreasing plastic hardening. The damage viscosity is included to regularize the evolution of $\alpha$ and include rate dependence.

\subsubsection*{Macroscopic response}

In the formulation of the model we have also included a purely macroscopic response in terms of a macroscopic stress $T$ and a macroscopic hyperstress $K$. This is done primary for a mathematical need in view of the proof of the existing theorem. The viscous stress add a viscous regularization, the conservative hyperstress ensure compactness. However we can give to these terms a mechanical interpretation. The viscous stress can describe an overall viscosity of the material, for example due the presence of fluid filling the voids between the grains. The addiction of the hyperstress allows to include contact torques and to consider concentrated loads and was already been employed in the modeling of soils and rocks (see~\cite[Chapter 10]{bifurcation-geomechanics}).

\section{More particularized constitutive assumptions}
\label{sec:particular-constitutive}

Taking into consideration the micro-mechanical interpretation provided in previous section, we now propose specialized constitutive relations. Further technical assumptions will be given in the following section devoted to the development of the existence theory.

We consider a linear elastic response by choosing an elastic free energy density of the form
\[
W_e(\alpha,E_e)=\frac{1}{2}\mathfrak{C}E_e:E_e,
\]
where $\mathfrak{C}\in\text{Sym}\otimes\text{Sym}$ is a symmetric and positive definite elasticity tensor and $d_e\colon\mathbb{R}\to[0,1]$. Next we assume that the plastic dissipation potential density is of the form
\[
R(p,\rho)=Y(p)|\rho|
\]
so that the plastic microforce is given by
\[
\partial_\rho R(p,\dot\pi)=\begin{cases}
\bar B(0,Y(p)) & \text{if $\dot\pi=0$} \\
\left\{Y(p)\frac{\dot\pi}{|\dot\pi|}\right\} & \text{if $\dot\pi\neq0$}.
\end{cases}
\]
Here $Y\colon\mathbb{R}\to[0,\infty)$ is supposed to be continuous. The simplest choice, motivated by the yield criteria discussed in Section~\ref{sec:yield-criteria}, is $Y(p)=Y_0+\mathfrak{f}p$ with $Y_0>0$ and $\mathfrak{f}>0$. We then assume that the hardening free energy is of the form
\[
W_p(\alpha,\pi)=d(\alpha)\tilde W_p(\pi)
\]
where $\tilde W_p\colon\text{DevSym}\to[0,\infty)$ is smooth and $d\colon\mathbb{R}\to[0,1]$ is a smooth function that is bounded, non-increasing and $d(0)=0$. This describe a plastic hardening that possibly deteriorates as damage growths.
