To describe the unidirectional character of damage and to link its evolutions with the accumulation deviatoric plastic distortion, we assume that
\[
D(\rho,\beta)=\iota_Q(\rho,\beta)\quad\text{where $Q=\{(\rho,\beta)\in\text{DevSym}\times\mathbb{R}\;|\;\beta\ge|\rho|\}$}.
\]
This impose that $\dot\alpha\ge|\dot\pi|$ so that a deviatoric plastic strain is always accompanied with an increase of damage and thus a change in the spherical plastic strain. In this way we can model the dilatant behavior of granular materials.

\section{Formally recovering the classical model}

In this section we show that the constitutive framework outlined in previous section is able to provide, at least formally at the level of the strong form of the equations, the same equations predicted by the usual framework of non-associated plastic materials described in Chapter~\ref{chap:intro-pressure-sensitive}.
\begin{prop}[Formal equivalence with the classical model]
Assume the constitutive relations of the particularized form considered in Section~\ref{sec:particular-constitutive}. Assume that $T$, $K$, $K_p$, $\kappa$ vanish. Assume also that $L(\alpha)=b\alpha$ $Y(p)=(a-b)p$, $W_p=0$ and $W_d(\alpha)=f_0\alpha$, where $b,f_0>0$ and $a\ge b$. Assume now that a smooth evolution satisfying the balance equations exists. Then, recalling the notation of Chapter~\ref{chap:intro-pressure-sensitive}, the evolution satisfies the admissibility condition $f(T_e)\le0$ and the flow rule
\[
\dot E_p\in
\begin{cases}
\{0\} & \text{if $f(T_p)<0$} \\
N_{L(T_e)}(T_e) & \text{if $f(T_p)=0$},
\end{cases}
\]
provided that
\begin{itemize}
\item[(i)] $f$, $g$ are the functions associated to (non-associative) Drucker Prager model, i.e.\ the ones given by~\eqref{eqn:f} and~\eqref{eqn:g}, and
\item[(ii)] it holds that $p=-\frac{\tr T_e}{d}>-\frac{f_0}{a}$ along the whole evolution.
\end{itemize}
\end{prop}
\begin{rmk}
Assumption (ii) is certainly true if sufficiently small external traction loads are imposed on the body. If (ii) is violated, then the original Drucker--Prager model became ill-posed, since no elastic stress exists satisfying the admissibility condition $f(T_e)\le0$. 
\end{rmk}
\begin{proof}
We distinguish two cases, according to fact that $\dot\pi$ is zero or different form zero.

\textit{Case $\dot\pi\neq0$.} We first prove that $\dot\alpha=|\dot\pi|$. If this was false, in view of the constrain $\dot\alpha\ge|\dot\pi|$, it must holds $\dot\alpha>|\dot\pi|$ and in this case $\partial D(\dot\pi,\dot\alpha)=\{0\}$. Then the balance of the damage microforce becomes $-L'(\alpha)p-W_d'(\alpha)=0$ or $-bp-f_0=0$. But from (ii) it follows that $-bp-f_0<\left(\frac{b}{a}-1\right)f_0\le0$, this is a contradiction. We conclude that $\dot\alpha=|\dot\pi|$ so the generic element of $\partial D(\dot\pi,\dot\alpha)$ is of the form
\[
\left(-\tilde\tau\frac{\dot\pi}{|\dot\pi|},\tilde\tau\right)\quad\text{for $\tilde\tau\le0$}.
\]
Then the balance of plastic and damage microforces can be written as
\begin{gather}
\dev T_e-Y(p)\frac{\dot\pi}{|\dot\pi|}+\tilde\tau\frac{\dot\pi}{|\dot\pi|}=0 \\
-L'(\alpha)p-\dind{W_d}{\alpha}(\alpha)-\tilde\tau=0.
\end{gather}
Thus we have
\[
\dev T_e=\left(Y(p)+L'(\alpha)p+\dind{W_d}{\alpha}(\alpha)\right)\frac{\dot\pi}{|\dot\pi|}.
\]
It follows that
\[
|\dev T_e|=Y(p)+L'(\alpha)p+\dind{W_d}{\alpha}(\alpha)=(a-b)p+bp+f_0=f_0-\frac{a}{d}\tr T_e
\]
which is equivalent to $f(T_e)=0$ so that the yield condition is satisfied. Moreover there exists $\gamma\ge0$ such that
\[
\dot\pi=\gamma\frac{\dev T_e}{|\dev T_e|}.
\]
From $\dot\lambda=L'(\alpha)\dot\alpha=b|\dot\pi|=b\gamma$ we conclude that
\[
\dot E_p=\dot\pi+\frac{\dot\lambda}{d}I=\gamma\dind{g}{A}(T_e)
\]
so that the flow rule is satisfied.

\textit{Case $\dot\pi=0$.} When $\dot\pi=0$ then $\dot\alpha=0$ for the same reasoning of before. Then the generic element of $\partial D(0,0)$ is of the form $(\tilde T_p,\tilde\tau)\in\text{DevSym}\times\mathbb{R}$ with $\tilde\tau\le-|\tilde T_p|$. Moreover the generic element of $\partial_\rho R(p)$ is a $\bar T_p\in\text{DevSym}$ with $|\bar T_p|\le Y(p)$. Then the balance of plastic and damage microforces
\begin{gather}
\dev T_e-\bar T_p-\tilde T_p=0 \\
-L'(\alpha)p-\dind{W_d}{\alpha}(\alpha)-\tilde\tau=0
\end{gather}
gives
\[
|\dev T_e|\le|\bar T_p|+|\tilde T_p|\le Y(p)+L'(\alpha)p+\dind{W_d}{\alpha}(\alpha)=f_0-\frac{a}{d}\tr T_e
\]
which is equivalent to $f(T_e)\le0$. Thus we have proved that $T_e$ satisfies the admissibility condition.
\end{proof}
\begin{rmk}
For simplicity, with the previous proposition, we have proved the equivalence between our model for pressure sensitive plastic materials and the classical model, in the particular case in which Drucker--Prager yield criterion is adopted. However the result still holds with more general yield criteria.
\end{rmk}

\section{Existence theory}

In this section we obtain an existence result for the model we have developed in previous sections. We will make use of the abstract existence theory developed in Section~\ref{sec:existence-theorem-abstract-constrained} whose development was indeed motivated by the present application.

\subsection{Assumptions, definitions and formulation of the problem}

We start giving the assumptions on the constitutive relations that we found sufficient to ensure the existence of solutions. We suppose that the function $L$, defining the kinematical relation between $\alpha$ and $\lambda$, satisfies $L\in C^{1,1}(\mathbb{R})$, $\norm{L}_\infty,\norm{L'}_\infty,\text{Lip}(L),\text{Lip}(L')\le C_L$ for some constant $C_L>0$ and $L(0)=0$. We further assume that
\[
W_e(\alpha,E_e)=\frac{1}{2}\mathfrak{C}E_e:E_e,\quad W_p(\alpha,\pi)=d(\alpha)\tilde W_p(\pi)\quad\text{and}\quad R(p,\rho)=Y(p)\tilde R(\rho)
\]
Here $\mathfrak{C}\in\text{Sym}\otimes\text{Sym}$ is the symmetric and positive definite elasticity tensor. We suppose that $\tilde W_p\in C^{1,1}(\text{DevSym},[0,\infty))$ with $\tilde W_p(0)=0$, $\tilde W_p'(0)=0$ and that there exists a constant $C_p>0$ such that $0\le\tilde W_p(\pi)\le C_p$, $|\tilde W_p'(\pi)|\le C_p,\text{Lip}(W_p),\text{Lip}(W_p')\le C_p$ and that $d\in C^{1,1}(\mathbb{R},[0,\infty))$ is bounded and with bounded derivative (we call $C_d>0$ a constant such that $\norm{d}_\infty,\norm{d'}_\infty,\text{Lip}(d),\text{Lip}(d')\le C_d$). We require that $W_d\in C^{1,1}(\mathbb{R})$ with $W_d'(0)=0$ and that there exist $C_d^2,C_d^1,C_d^0>0$ such that $-C_d^0\le W_d(\alpha)\le C_d^2|\alpha|^2+C_d^1|\alpha|+C_d^0$ and $|W_d'(\alpha)\alpha|\le C_d^2|\alpha|^2+C_d^1|\alpha|+C_d^0$. Next we assume that the plastic dissipation potential density is of the form $R(p,\rho)=Y(p)\tilde R(\rho)$ with $Y\in C^{0,1}(\mathbb{R},[0,\infty))$ and $\tilde R\in C^{0,1}(\text{DevSym},[0,\infty))$ convex and satisfying $\tilde R(0)=0$. We eventually assume that $D\colon\text{DevSym}\times\mathbb{R}\to[0,\infty]$ is convex, lower semicontinuous, Lipschitz on its domain and $D(0,0)=0$.
\begin{rmk}(Exclusion of the original model of Drucker and Prager)
Notice that the assumptions that we have just stated prevent us from considering the constitutive relations that allowed us to show the equivalence between our formulation and the original one of Drucker and Prager in previous section. This is not limiting since it is known~\cite{morafrancfort} that the original model of Drucker and Prager is mathematically ill posed.
\end{rmk}

The function spaces where we formulate the problem are
\begin{gather}
Y=\{y=(u,\pi,\alpha)\;|\;\text{$u\in H^2_0(\Omega,\mathbb{R}^d)$, $\pi\in H^1(\Omega,\text{DevSym})$ and $\alpha\in H^1(\Omega)$}\} \\
H=\{y=(u,\pi,\alpha)\;|\;\text{$u\in H^1_0(\Omega,\mathbb{R}^d)$, $\pi\in L^2(\Omega,\text{DevSym})$ and $\alpha\in L^2(\Omega)$}\} \\
\Lambda=\Lambda^*=L^2(\Omega)
\end{gather}
and $Z=Y\times\Lambda$. Here we have assumed for simplicity that homogeneous boundary conditions on $u$ are imposed; the general case is a trivial extension, but we do not treat it to avoid an excessively involved notation.
\begin{rmk}(On notation)
Notice that the function spaces and the variables defined here directly correspond with the abstract counterparts considered in Section~\ref{sec:existence-theorem-abstract-constrained} with the only difference that $U$ is replaced by $Y$, $u$ is replaced by $y$ and $v$ by $h$.
\end{rmk}
Taking into account the definitions made in previous sections, we consider $\mathcal{L}\colon H\to\Lambda$, $\Psi\colon Z\to\mathbb{R}$, $\Psi_0\colon H\to\mathbb{R}$, $\mathcal{A}\in\mathscr{L}(Y,Y^*)$, $\Phi,\Phi_0\colon\Lambda^*\times\Lambda\to[0,\infty]$ and $\mathcal{D}\in\mathscr{L}(H,H^*)$. We also consider $f\in H^1((0,T),H^{-1}(\Omega,\mathbb{R}^3))$ with $f(0)=0$ and define $\mathcal{X}\in H^1((0,T),H^*)$ with
\[
\dual{\mathcal{X}(t),h}=\dual{f(t),v}\quad\text{for all $h\in H$ and $t\in[0,T]$}.
\]
Next consider the initial state $z_0=(y_0,\lambda_0)=0$.

Following the discussion of Section~\ref{sec:existence-theorem-abstract-constrained} we consider the following problem.
\begin{prb}
\label{prb:pressure-sensitive-small}
Find $(z,p)=(y,\lambda,p)$ with $y\in\text{AC}([0,T],Y)$, $\lambda\in C^0([0,T],\Lambda)$ and $p\in C^0([0,T],\Lambda^*)$ such that $z(0)=z_0$ and
\begin{align}
D_y\Psi_0(z(t))+\mathcal{A}y(t)+\partial_h\Phi_0(p(t),\dot y(t))+\mathcal{D}\dot y(t)+\mathcal{L}'(y(t))^*p(t)&\ni\mathcal{X}(t) \\
D_\lambda\Psi_0(z(t))-p(t)&=0 \\
\lambda(t)&=\mathcal{L}(y(t))
\end{align}
for a.e.\ $t\in(0,T)$.
\end{prb}

%\begin{rmk}(Particular instances of constitutive relations)
%A particular instance of the function $L$ is
%\[
%L(\alpha)=
%\]
%of $\tilde W_p$ and $d$ could be
%\[
%\tilde W_p(\pi)=\sqrt{\mathfrak{p}_1|\pi|^2+\mathfrak{p}_0}\quad\text{and}\quad d(\alpha)=\begin{cases}
%(1-\alpha)^2 & \text{if $\alpha\in[0,1]$} \\
%0 & \text{if $\alpha>1$},
%\end{cases}
%\]
%where $\mathfrak{p}_0,\mathfrak{p}_1>0$. In this way when $\alpha=0$ the material is not damaged and when $\alpha\ge1$ the material is completely damaged and hardening disappears.
%\end{rmk}
%The function $Y\colon\mathbb{R}\to[0,\infty)$ is the pressure dependent yield strength and the standard choice is
%\[
%Y(p)=\begin{cases}
%0 & \text{if $p<-\frac{Y_0}{\mathfrak{f}}$} \\
%Y_0+\mathfrak{f}p & \text{if $p\ge-\frac{Y_0}{\mathfrak{f}}$},
%\end{cases}
%\]
%where $\mathfrak{f}>0$. The function $\tilde R\colon\text{DevSym}\to[0,\infty)$ is supposed to be convex and satisfying $\tilde R(0)=0$. To describe the fact that the accumulation of deviatoric plastic strain is always accompanied by damaging, and to prescribe that damage is an unidirectional process (the fact that damage can only increase), we set
%\[
%D(\rho,\beta)=\iota_A(\rho,\beta)+\tilde D(\beta)
%\]
%where
%\[
%A=\{(\rho,\beta)\in\text{DevSym}\times\mathbb{R}\;|\;\beta\ge\mathfrak{g}|\rho|\}\quad\text{with $\mathfrak{g}>0$}
%\]
%and $\tilde{D}\colon[0,\infty)\to[0,\infty)$ is convex and satisfies $\tilde D(0)=0$.

\subsection{Proof of the existence theorem}


The existence theorem we are going to prove is the following.
\begin{thm}
\label{thm:existence-pressure-small}
Consider the assumptions of previous subsection. Then there exists $(z,p)=(y,\lambda,p)$ solution to Problem~\ref{prb:pressure-sensitive-small} with $y\in W^{1,\infty}((0,T),Y)$, $\dot y\in C^0([0,T],H)$, $\lambda\in C^0([0,T],\Lambda)$ and $p\in C^0([0,T],\Lambda^*)$.
\end{thm}
As anticipated, the proof rely on the abstract existence theorem of Section~\ref{sec:existence-theorem-abstract-constrained}, so our aim is now to verify the required hypotheses. It is evident that $Y$ is compactly embedded in $H$. The properties of $\mathcal{L}$ are verified in the following lemma.
\begin{lemma}
It holds that $\mathcal{L}\in C^{0,1}(H,\Lambda)\cap C^{1,1}(H,\Lambda)$ and $\norm{\mathcal{L}(y)}_\Lambda$, $\norm{\mathcal{L}'(y)}_{\mathscr{L}(H,\Lambda)}$ are bounded uniformly in $y\in H$.
\end{lemma}
\begin{proof}
It holds that $\norm{\mathcal{L}(y)}_\Lambda=\norm{L(\alpha)}_2\le C_L|\Omega|$ and $\norm{\mathcal{L}(y_1)-\mathcal{L}(y_2)}_\Lambda=\norm{L(\alpha_1)-L(\alpha_2)}_2\le C_L\norm{\alpha_1-\alpha_2}_2\le C_L\norm{y_1-y_2}_H$. This proves that $\mathcal{L}\in C^{0,1}(H,\Lambda)$ and that $\norm{\mathcal{L}(y)}_\Lambda$ is uniformly bounded.

Next notice that $\mathcal{L}$ is G\^ateaux differentiable with G\^ateaux derivative given by $\mathcal{L}'(y)h=L'(\alpha)\beta\in\Lambda$, since by a simple application of Lagrange theorem and dominated convergence theorem it holds that
\[
\left\|\frac{{L}(\alpha+\epsilon\beta)-{L}(\alpha)}{\epsilon}-L'(\alpha)\beta\right\|_\Lambda^2=\int_\Omega|L'(\alpha+\epsilon\eta\beta)-L'(\alpha)|^2|\beta|^2dx\to0
\]
as $\epsilon\to0$, where $\eta\in(0,1)$ depends on $\epsilon$ and $x$. The dominating function is $4C_L^2|\beta|^2\in L^1(\Omega)$ and since $L'$ is continuous it holds that $|L'(\alpha+\epsilon\eta\beta)-L'(\alpha)|\to0$ pointwise. It clearly holds that $\norm{\mathcal{L}'(y)h}_\Lambda=\norm{L'(\alpha)\beta}_\Lambda\le C_L\norm{\beta}_2\le C_L\norm{y}_H$ thus $\norm{\mathcal{L}'(y)}_{\mathscr{L}(H,\Lambda)}\le C_L$ is uniformly bounded. Moreover $\norm{(\mathcal{L}'(y_1)-\mathcal{L}'(y_2))h}_\Lambda=\norm{({L}'(\alpha_1)-{L}'(\alpha_2))\beta}_\Lambda\le C_L\norm{\alpha_1-\alpha_2}_2\norm{\beta}_2\le C_L\norm{y_1-y_2}_H\norm{h}_H$ so that $\norm{\mathcal{L}'(y_1)-\mathcal{L}'(y_2)}_{\mathscr{L}(H,\Lambda)}\le C_L\norm{y_1-y_2}_H$. This shows that $\mathcal{L}'\in C^{0,1}(H,\mathscr{L}(H,\Lambda))$. This also implies that $\mathcal{L}$ is FrĂ¨chet differentiable.
\end{proof}
Next we verify the properties of $\Psi_0$.
\begin{lemma}
The map $\Psi_0$ satisfies the assumptions ($\Psi$1) and ($\Psi$2) of Section~\ref{sec:existence-theorem-abstract-constrained}.
\end{lemma}
\begin{proof}
It is trivial to see that the elastic part of $\Psi_0$ satisfies the required hypotheses, and in particular
\[
\dual{D_\lambda\Psi_0(z),\mu}=-\int_\Omega\frac{1}{d}\tr\left[\mathfrak{C}\left(Eu-\pi-\frac{\lambda}{d}I\right)\right]\mu dx
\]
so that
\[
\norm{D_\lambda\Psi_0(z)}_{\Lambda^*}\le\frac{|\mathfrak{C}|}{d}\left\|Eu-\pi-\frac{\lambda}{d}I\right\|_2\le C_\Psi^3\norm{z}_{H\times\Lambda}
\]
for some $C_\Psi^3>0$. So we need to chech only that
\[
\Psi_1(z)=\int_\Omega\left(W_p(\alpha,\pi)+W_d(\alpha)\right)dx
\]
satisfies the required properties. Using the Lagrange theorem and the dominated convergence theorem we see that $\Psi_1$ admits the G\^ateaux differential
\[
\dual{D\Psi_1(z),w}=\int_\Omega\left(d'(\alpha)\tilde W_p(\pi)\beta+d(\alpha)\tilde W_p'(\pi):\rho+W_d'(\alpha)\beta\right)dx.
\]
In particular it is crucial the fact that $|\tilde W_p(\pi)|$ has at most a linear growth in $\pi$, ensuring that $d'(\alpha)\tilde W_p(\pi)\beta\in L^1(\Omega)$. It is immediate to see that $|\dual{D\Psi_1(z),z}|$ as at most a quadratic growth in $\norm{z}_{H\times\Lambda}$. It remains to show that $D\Psi_1$ is Lipschitz, this will also imply that $\Psi_1$ is FrĂ¨chet differentiable. To this aim we notice that
\[
\begin{split}
\left|\left\langle D_z\Psi_1(z_1)-D_z\Psi_1(z_2),w\right\rangle\right|&\le
\begin{multlined}[t]
\int_{\Omega}\left|d'(\alpha_1)\tilde W_p(\pi_1)-d'(\alpha_2)\tilde W_p(\pi_2)\right||\beta|\,dx+\\
+\int_{\Omega}\left|d(\alpha_1)\tilde W_p'(\pi_1)-d(\alpha_2)\tilde W_p'(\pi_2)\right||\rho|\,dx+\\
+\int_{\Omega}\left|W_d'(\alpha_1)-W_d'(\alpha_2)\right||\beta|\,dx\le
\end{multlined}\\
&\le
\begin{multlined}[t]
\left\|d'(\alpha_1)\tilde W_p(\pi_1)-d'(\alpha_2)\tilde W_p(\pi_2)\right\|_2\|\beta\|_2+\\
+\left\|d(\alpha_1)\tilde W_p'(\pi_1)-d(\alpha_2)\tilde W_p'(\pi_2)\right\|_2\|\rho\|_2+\\
+C_d\|\alpha_1-\alpha_2\|_2\|\beta\|_2\le
\end{multlined}\\
&\le C_d{C}_p\left(\|\pi_1-\pi_2\|_2+\|\alpha_1-\alpha_2\|_2\right)\left(\|\beta\|_2+\|\rho\|_2\right)+C_d\|\alpha_1-\alpha_2\|_2\|\beta\|_2,
\end{split}
\]
where we used the crucial assumption that $\tilde W_p$ and $\tilde W_p'$ are bounded to make the estimate
\[
\begin{split}
\left\|d'(\alpha_1)\tilde W_p(\pi_1)-d'(\alpha_2)\tilde W_p(\pi_2)\right\|_2&\le\left\|d'(\alpha_1)(\tilde W_p(\pi_1)-\tilde W_p(\pi_2))\right\|_2+\left\|(d'(\alpha_1)-d'(\alpha_2)\tilde W_p(\pi_2)\right\|_2\le\\
&\le C_dC_p\norm{\pi_1-\pi_2}_2+C_dC_p\norm{\alpha_1-\alpha_2}_2
\end{split}
\]
and the similar one with $\left\|d(\alpha_1)\tilde W_p'(\pi_1)-d(\alpha_2)\tilde W_p'(\pi_2)\right\|_2$. This shows that $D_z\Psi_1$ is FrĂ¨chet differentiable.
\end{proof}
The linear operator $\mathcal{A}$ is trivially continuous and is coercive with respect to the seminorm $|\cdot|_Y$ in $Y$ defined by
\[
|y|_Z^2=\int_\Omega\frac{1}{2}\left(|\grad^2u|^2+|\grad\pi|^2+|\grad\alpha|^2\right)dx.
\]
It is trivial to see that $\norm{\cdot}_Y,\norm{\cdot}_H$ and $|\cdot|_Y$ satisfy the property~\eqref{eqn:abstract-poincare}. The properties of $\Phi_0$ are verified in the following lemma.
\begin{lemma}
The map $\Phi_0$ satisfies ($\Phi$1), ($\Phi$2) and ($\Phi$3) of Section~\ref{sec:existence-theorem-abstract-constrained}.
\end{lemma}
\begin{proof}
It is trivial to see that $\Phi_0(p,0)=0$ and that $\Phi_0(p,\cdot)$ is convex. To see that $\Phi_0$ is lower semicontinuous, take a sequence $h_k\to h$ in $H$: we have to prove that
\[
\liminf_{k\to\infty}\Phi_0(p,h_k)\ge\Phi_0(p,h).
\]
To this aim, up to extracting a subsequence $\{h_{k_j}\}_{j\in\mathbb{N}}$, we can assume that the inferior limit is actually a limit, and extracting a further subsequence we can assume that $h_{k_j}\to h$ a.e.\ in $\Omega$. Thus by Fatou lemma
\[
\begin{split}
\liminf_{k\to\infty}\Phi_0(p,h_k)&=\lim_{j\to\infty}\Phi_0(p,h_{k_j})=\lim_{j\to\infty}\int_\Omega\left(Y(p)\tilde R(\rho_{k_j})+D(\rho_{k_h},\beta_{k_j})\right)dx\ge\\
&\ge\int_\Omega\liminf_{j\to\infty}\left(Y(p)\tilde R(\rho_{k_j})+D(\rho_{k_j},\beta_{k_j})\right)dx\ge\int_\Omega\left(Y(p)\tilde R(\rho)+D(\rho,\beta)\right)dx=\Phi_0(p,h).
\end{split}
\]
In the last inequality we have used the fact that $\tilde R$ is continuous and $D$ is lower semicontinuous. Moreover it is trivial to see that $\dom\Phi_0=\Lambda^*\times D_\Phi$. The fact that $D_\Phi\subset H$ is closed and convex follows from the convexity and lower semicontinuity of $\Phi(p,\cdot)$. The fact that $\Phi_0(p,\cdot)$ is lower semicontinuous implies that $D_\Phi$ is closed. Thus we have proved ($\Phi$1) and ($\Phi$2).

Now we check ($\Phi$3). Let $p,p_1,p_2\in\Lambda^*$ and $h,h_1,h_2\in D_\Phi$. Then we immediately check the first inequality
\[
\begin{split}
\left|\Phi_0(p,h_1)-\Phi(p,h_2)\right|&\le\int_{\Omega}Y(p)\left|\tilde{R}(\rho_1)-\tilde{R}(\rho_2)\right|dx+\int_{\Omega}\left|D(\rho_1,\beta_1)-D(\rho_2,\beta_2)\right|dx\le\\
&\le\int_{\Omega}\left(C_{Y}^0+C_{Y}^1|p|\right)\mathrm{Lip}(\tilde{R})|\rho_1-\rho_2|dx+\int_{\Omega}\mathrm{Lip}(D)\left(|\rho_1-\rho_2|+|\beta_1-\beta_2|\right)dx\le\\
&\le\mathrm{Lip}(\tilde{R})\left(C_{Y}^0|\Omega|^{1/2}+C_{Y}^1\|p\|_{\Lambda^*}\right)\|\rho_1-\rho_2\|_2+\mathrm{Lip}(D)|\Omega|^{1/2}\left(\|\rho_1-\rho_2\|_2+\|\beta_1-\beta_2\|_2\right),
\end{split}
\]
the second inequality
\[
\begin{split}
\left|\Phi_0(p_1,h)-\Phi_0(p_2,h)\right|&\le\int_{\Omega}\left|Y(p_1)-Y(p_2)\right|\tilde{R}(\rho)dx\le\mathrm{Lip}(Y)\mathrm{Lip}(\tilde{R})\|p_1-p_2\|_{\Lambda}\|\rho\|_2\le\\
&\le\mathrm{Lip}(Y)\mathrm{Lip}(\tilde{R})\|p_1-p_2\|_{\Lambda}\|h\|_{H},
\end{split}
\]
and the third
\[
\begin{multlined}
\left|\Phi(p_1,h_1)-\Phi(p_2,h_1)+\Phi(p_2,h_2)-\Phi(p_1,h_2)\right|\le\\
\begin{split}
&\le\int_{\Omega}\left|Y(p_1)\tilde{R}(\rho_1)-Y(p_2)\tilde{R}(\rho_1)+Y(p_2)\tilde{R}(\rho_2)-Y(p_1)\tilde{R}(\rho_2)\right|dx=\\
&=\int_{\Omega}\left|Y(p_1)-Y(p_2)\right|\left|\tilde{R}(\rho_1)-\tilde{R}(\rho_2)\right|dx\le\\
&\le\mathrm{Lip}(Y)\mathrm{Lip}(\tilde{R})\|p_1-p_2\|_{\Lambda}\|\rho_1-\rho_2\|_2\le\mathrm{Lip}(Y)\mathrm{Lip}(\tilde{R})\|p_1-p_2\|_{\Lambda}\|h_1-h_2\|_{H}.
\end{split}
\end{multlined}
\]
This concludes the proof.
\end{proof}
That $\mathcal{D}$ belongs to $\mathscr{L}(H,H^*)$ is trivial and the coercivity follows immediately from the Korn inequality. To complete the check of the hypotheses needed to apply the theorem of Section~\ref{sec:existence-theorem-abstract-constrained} we are left with the check of condition~\eqref{eqn:initial-stability}. It is enough to notice that $0=\mathcal{L}(0)$, $p_0=D_\lambda\Psi_0(0)=0$ and $D_u\Psi_0(0)=0$.

Now that we have completed the check of all the hypotheses, Theorem~\ref{thm:existence-pressure-small} follows.

\chapter{Proposal of an alternative mechanical model: the regime of large deformations}

In this chapter we extend the model treated in previous chapter to the regime of large deformations. Our discussion will be limited to the modeling aspect, leaving the development of an existence theory as an open problem.

The present chapter is merely a generalization of the previous one, so we will not enter again in the details of the micro-mechanical interpretation and of the choice of constitutive relations, since the discussion is completely analogous.

\section{Formulation of the model}

Let $\Omega\subset\mathbb{R}^d$, open, bounded and Lipschitz be the reference configuration of the body. Its configuration is assumed to be completely described by the deformation field $\chi\colon\Omega\to\mathbb{R}^d$, the plastic distortion $F_p\colon\Omega\to\text{Lin}$ and the damage variable $\alpha\colon\Omega\to\mathbb{R}$. We denote with $z=(\chi,F_p,\alpha)\in Z$ the generic configuration of the body; the particular definition of configuration space $Z$ can only be understood once a complete theory of existence has been developed. We denote with $\tilde w=(\tilde v,\tilde V_p,\tilde\beta)\in Z$ the generic virtual velocity.

We consider a kinematical constrain of the form $\det F_p=J(\alpha)$ where $J\colon\mathbb{R}\to(0,\infty)$ defines the relation between the volumetric part of the plastic distortion and the state of damage of the material. Thus the set of kinematically admissible configurations is $K=\{z=(\chi,F_p,\alpha)\in Z\;|\;\det F_p=J(\alpha)\}$. The constrain is imposed by including the constrain reaction $\mathcal{P}\in-N_K(z)$. We parametrize it in terms of a field $p\colon\Omega\to\mathbb{R}$ as
\[
\dual{\mathcal{P},\tilde w}=\int_\Omega p\left(\cof F_p:\tilde V_p-J'(\alpha)\tilde\beta\right)dx=\int_\Omega p\left(\tr\tilde L_p-J'(\alpha)\tilde\beta\right)dx
\]
so that the normality condition is automatically satisfied. We have considered the plastic rate $\tilde L_p:=\tilde V_pF_p^{-1}$ (see also Remark~\ref{rmk:plasticity-lie-group}). The field $p$ belong to some function space $P$ that could be clarified by the development of the existence theory.
\begin{rmk}
The field $p$ still have the mechanical interpretation of a internal plastic micropressure as observed in previous chapter.
\end{rmk}

The structure of the internal and external generalized forces is chosen in complete analogy with the model at small deformations. In particular the internal generalized force is assumed to be given by
\[
\begin{multlined}
\dual{\mathcal{J},\tilde w}=\int_\Omega(P:\grad\tilde v+K\threedots\grad^2\tilde v)dx+\int_\Omega P_e:\tilde V_edx+\\
+\int_\Omega\left(T_p:\tilde V_p+K_p\threedots\grad\tilde V_p\right)dx+\int_\Omega(\tau\tilde\beta+\kappa\cdot\grad\tilde\beta)dx,
\end{multlined}
\]
where $P\colon\Omega\to\text{Lin}$ is the macroscopic Piola stress, $K\colon\Omega\to\text{Lin}\otimes\mathbb{R}$ can be called the macroscopic Piola hyperstress, $P_e\colon\Omega\to\text{Lin}$ is the elastic hyperstress. We recall from Section~\ref{sec:plasticity-review} that $\tilde V_e:=(\grad\tilde v)F_p^{-1}-F_e\tilde V_pF_p^{-1}$ is the elastic distortion rate. The plastic response is characterized as usual by the plastic microforce $T_p\colon\Omega\to\text{Lin}$ and by the plastic microstress $K_p\colon\Omega\to\text{Lin}\otimes\mathbb{R}$. The damage variable is subject to the damage microforce $\tau\colon\Omega\to\mathbb{R}$ and to the damage microstress $\kappa\colon\Omega\to\mathbb{R}^d$. The external generalized force is supposed to be given by
\[
\dual{\mathcal{X},\tilde w}=\int_\Omega f\cdot\tilde vdx+\int_{\partial\Omega}h\cdot\tilde vdx,
\]
where $f\colon\Omega\to\mathbb{R}^d$ is the volumetric external force and $h\colon\partial\Omega\to\mathbb{R}^d$ is the surface force acting on the boundary of the body. Additional external loads, such as external torque and microscopic loads can be considered as well.

In complete analogy to the results of previous chapter, the strong form of the equilibrium condition $-\mathcal{I}+\mathcal{X}+\mathcal{P}=0$ is the one reported in the following proposition.
\begin{prop}
\label{prop:strong-form-pressure-large}
Assuming that $\Omega$ is piecewise smooth and the fields appearing in $\mathcal{I}$, $\mathcal{X}$ and $\mathcal{P}$ are smooth, the strong form of the equilibrium condition is
\begin{align*}
&\diver(P+P_eF_p^{-T}-\diver K)+f=0\quad\text{in $\Omega$} \\
&(P+P_eF_p^{-1}-\diver K)n-\diver_\Pi((Kn)\Pi)=h\quad\text{in $\Gamma_N\cap\partial^{d-1}\Omega$} \\
&\{\!\!\{(Kn)\xi\}\!\!\}=0\quad\text{in $\partial^{d-2}\Omega$} \\
&F_e^TP_eF_p^{-T}-T_p+\diver K_p+p\cof F_p=0\quad\text{in $\Omega$} \\
&K_p n=0\quad\text{in $\partial^{d-1}\Omega$} \\
&-J'(\alpha)p-\tau+\diver\kappa=0\quad\text{in $\Omega$} \\
&\kappa\cdot n=0\quad\text{in $\partial^{d-1}\Omega$},
\end{align*}
where $\partial^m\Omega$ denotes the set of $m$-dimensional faces of $\partial\Omega$ (for example in the case $d=2$ then $\partial^1\Omega$ are the edges and $\partial^0\Omega$ are the vertices of $\partial\Omega$). Moreover $\Pi$ is the projector onto the tangent space to the boundary of $\Omega$, $\xi$ is the outer normal vector to the boundary of a $d-1$ dimensional face and tangent to it and $\{\!\!\{\cdot\}\!\!\}$ denotes the sum of a quantity defined along the two sides of a $d-2$ dimensional face.
\end{prop}
\begin{proof}
The proof is similar to that of the analogous result of previous chapter.
\end{proof}

The constitutive relations are obtained by fixing the free energy functional $\Psi\colon Z\to\mathbb{R}$ and $\Phi\colon Z\to[0,\infty]$. In analogy to what made in previous chapter we consider
\[
\begin{multlined}
\Psi(z)=\int_\Omega\bigg(\frac{\mathfrak{a}}{q_{\chi}}|\grad^2\chi|^{q_\chi}+W_e\left(\alpha,(\grad\chi)F_p^{-1}\right)+W_p(\alpha,G_p)+\frac{\mathfrak{b}}{q_p}|\grad G_p|^{q_p}+\\
+W_d(\alpha)+\frac{\mathfrak{c}}{q_d}|\grad\alpha|^{q_d}\bigg)dx
\end{multlined}
\]
and
\[
\Phi(p,w)=\int_\Omega\left(\frac{1}{2}\mathfrak{D}L:L+\frac{\mathfrak{d}}{p_p}|S_p|^{p_p}+R(p,S_p)+\frac{\mathfrak{e}}{p_d}|\beta|^{p_d}+D(S_p,\beta)\right)dx.
\]
Notice that the plastic hardening free energy density and the plastic gradient regularization is made dependent on $G_p:=(\det F_p)^{-1}F_p$ only, thus excluding a back stress ``acting on'' the volumetric plastic distortion. Similarly the plastic dissipation potential density $R$ and the plastic viscosity is made dependent on the deviatoric part $S_p$ of $L_p:=V_pF_p^{-1}$. The macroscopic viscosity dissipation potential density is chosen quadratic in the deformation gradient $D:=\sym\left(\grad v(\grad\chi)^{-1}\right)$. Thus we get the following constitutive relations
\begin{align}
P=(\mathfrak{D}D)\,(\grad\chi)^T\quad & K=\mathfrak{a}|\grad^2\chi|^{q_\chi-2}\grad^2\chi \\
P_e=\dind{W_e}{F_e}(\alpha,(\grad\chi)F_p^{-1})\quad & \\
\begin{multlined}[c]
T_p\in\dind{}{F_p}\left[W_p(\alpha,G_p)+\frac{\mathfrak{b}}{q_p}|\grad G_p|^{q_p}\right]
+\\
+\left(\mathfrak{d}|S_p|^{p_p-2}S_p+\dind{R}{S_p}(\alpha,S_p)+\dind{D}{S_p}(S_p,\alpha)\right)F_p^{-T}
\end{multlined}\quad & K_p=\mathfrak{b}|\grad F_p|^{q_p-2}\grad F_p+\dind{}{\grad F_p}\frac{\mathfrak{b}}{q_p}|\grad G_p|^{q_p} \\
\tau\in W_d'(\alpha)+\mathfrak{e}|\dot\alpha|^{p_d-2}\dot\alpha+\dind{D}{\beta}(S_p,\dot\alpha)\quad & \kappa=\mathfrak{c}|\grad\alpha|^{q_d-2}\grad\alpha
\end{align}

\begin{rmk}
By multiplying the fourth equation appearing in Proposition~\ref{prop:strong-form-pressure-large} we get
\[
F_e^TP_e-T_pF_p^T+(\diver K_p)F_p^T+p(\det F_p)I=0\quad\text{in $\Omega$}.
\]
Taking the trace and noticing that $\tr(-T_pF_p+(\diver K_p)F_p^T)=0$ with the particular constitutive relations chosen, we find that
\[
p=-\frac{1}{d\det F_p}\tr(F_e^TP_e).
\]
Thus $p$ is a sort of pressure of the push forward of the elastic Piola stress into the local elastically relaxed space.
\end{rmk}

\section{Tensors}

\toprule
$\text{Lin}=\mathbb{R}^{d\times d}$ & $d\times d$ matrices \\
$\text{Lin}^+$ & $d\times d$ matrices with positive determinant \\
$\text{Sym}$ & $d\times d$ symmetric matrices \\
$\text{Dev}$ & $d\times d$ deviatoric matrices \\
$\text{DevSym}$ & $d\times d$ symmetric and deviatoric matrices \\
$\text{DevPla}$ & defined as $\text{DevSym}\times(\text{DevSym}\otimes\mathbb{R})$ \\
$\text{Lin}^1$ & $d\times d$ matrices with unit determinant \\

\end{tabular}
\end{center}

\chapter{Tools from mathematical analysis}

In this chapter we collect some tools of linear and nonlinear analysis used in the main body of this thesis.

\section{Some classical results}

\subsection{A general form of the Gronwall inequality}

\begin{prop}(Gronwall inequality)
\label{prop:gronwall}
Let $z\in L^\infty((0,T))$. Let $C\in\mathbb{R}$ and $\alpha$, $\beta$ in $L^1((0,T),[0,\infty))$ such that
\begin{equation*}
z(t)\le C+\int_0^t\left(\alpha(s)y(s)+\beta(s)\right)ds\quad\text{for a.e. $t\in(0,T)$}.
\end{equation*}
Then
\begin{equation*}
z(t)\le e^{\int_0^t\alpha(s)ds}\left(C+\int_0^t\beta(s)e^{-\int_0^s\alpha(r)dr}ds\right)\quad\text{for a.e. $t\in(0,T)$}.
\end{equation*}
\end{prop}
\begin{proof}
Let
\begin{equation*}
Z(t)=C+\int_0^t\left(\alpha(s)z(s)+\beta(s)\right)ds
\end{equation*}
so that $z(t)\le Z(t)$. Clearly $Z\in\text{AC}([0,T])$. It holds that
\begin{equation*}
\dot Z(t)=\alpha(t)z(t)+\beta(t)\le\alpha(t)Z(t)+\beta(t)\quad\text{for a.e. $t\in(0,T)$}.
\end{equation*}
Multiplying by $e^{-\int_0^t\alpha(s)ds}$ one gets
\begin{equation*}
\frac{d}{dt}\left(Z(t)e^{-\int_0^t\alpha(s)ds}\right)\le\beta(t)e^{-\int_0^t\alpha(s)ds}
\end{equation*}
and integrating on $(0,t)$
\begin{equation*}
Z(t)e^{-\int_0^t\alpha(s)ds}\le C+\int_0^t\beta(s)e^{-\int_0^s\alpha(r)dr}ds
\end{equation*}
Then
\begin{equation*}
z(t)\le Z(t)\le e^{\int_0^t\alpha(s)ds}\left(C+\int_0^t\beta(s)e^{-\int_0^s\alpha(r)dr}\right)\quad\text{for a.e. $t\in(0,T)$}.
\end{equation*}
This is exactly the thesis.
\end{proof}
The same inequality has also a discrete version that can be proved similarly.
\begin{prop}[discrete Gronwall inequality]
\label{prop:discrete-gronwall}
Let $z_k\in\mathbb{R}$, $a_k\ge0$, $b_k\ge 0$ for $k\in\mathbb{N}$ and $C\in\mathbb{R}$, $\tau>0$ such that
\[
z_k\le C+\tau\sum_{j=1}^k(a_jz_j+b_j)\quad\text{for $k\in\mathbb{N}$}.
\]
Then
\[
z_k\le\left(C+\tau\sum_{j=1}^kb_j\right)e^{\tau\sum_{j=1}^ka_j}\quad\text{for $k\in\mathbb{N}$}.
\]
\end{prop}

\section{Linear functional analysis}

\subsection{Other}

\begin{prop}
\label{prop:sobolev-weak-lp-strong}
Let $\Omega\subset\mathbb{R}^d$ be open, bounded and Lipschitz. Let $p\in[1,\infty]$ and let $u_n\rightharpoonup u$ in $W^{1,p}(\Omega)$. Then $u_n\to u$ in $L^p(\Omega)$. Moreover if $p>d$ then $u_k\to u$ in $C^{0,\alpha}(\bar{\Omega})$ where $\alpha=1-\frac{d}{p}$.
\end{prop}
\begin{proof}
Since $u_n\rightharpoonup u$ in $W^{1,p}(\Omega)$ then $u_n$ are bounded in $W^{1,p}(\Omega)$. By Rellich--Kondrakov theorem each subsequence of $u_n$ admits a further subsequence that converges strongly in $L^p(\Omega)$. Since we already know the weak convergence in $W^{1,p}(\Omega)$ then the limit is always $u$. Then necessarily the whole sequence $u_n$ converges strongly to $u$ in $L^2(\Omega)$.

The result when $p>d$ follows from Morrey's theorem and a similar argument.
\end{proof}

\subsection{Bochner spaces}

\begin{defi}
\label{defi:ac-bv}
Let $V$ be a Banach space. We say that $u\colon[0,T]\to V$ is absolutely continuous and we write $u\in AC([0,T],V)$ if there exists $m\in L^1((0,T),(0,\infty))$ such that
\begin{equation*}
\norm{u(t)-u(s)}\le\int_0^Tm(r)dr\quad\text{for all $0\le s<t\le T$.}
\end{equation*}
Given $u\colon[0,T]\to V$ and $0\le a<b\le T$ we define the total variation on $[a,b]$
\begin{equation*}
\text{Var}(u,[a,b])=\sup\left\{\sum_{k=1}^N\norm{u(t_k)-u(t_{k-1})}\;\middle|\;a=t_0<\dots<t_N=b\right\}.
\end{equation*}
We say that $u$ has bounded variation and we write $u\in BV([0,T],V)$ if $\text{Var}(u,[0,T])<\infty$.
\end{defi}

\begin{prop}
\label{prop:ac-w}
Let $V$ be a Banach space. Then $W^{1,1}((0,T),V)\subset AC([0,T],V)$. If $V$ is separable and reflexive the equality holds.
\end{prop}
\begin{proof}
Let $u\in W^{1,1}((0,T),V)$. Then
\begin{equation*}
\norm{u(t)-u(s)}=\left\lVert\int_s^t\dot u(r)dr\right\rVert\le\int_s^t\norm{\dot u(r)}dr.
\end{equation*}
This proves that $u\in AC([0,T],V)$ with $m=\norm{\dot u}$.

Now suppose that $V$ is reflexive. We have that
\begin{equation*}
\left\lVert\frac{u(t+h)-u(t)}{h}\right\rVert\le\frac{1}{h}\int_t^{t+h}m(r)dr.
\end{equation*}
By the Lebesgue differentiation theorem the left hand side is bounded as $h\to 0$ for a.e.\ $t\in(0,T)$. Then for each $h_n\to 0$, up to extracting a subsequence,
\begin{equation*}
\frac{u(t+h_n)-u(t)}{h_n}\rightharpoonup v\quad\text{for some $v\in V$}.
\end{equation*}
We prove that $v$ is common to each sequence. Since $V^*$ is separable and reflexive then there exists $\xi_i$ dense in $V^*$. Now for each $i$ the map $t\mapsto\dual{\xi_i,u(t)}$ is $AC([0,T])$. Then for a.e. $t\in(0,T)$
\begin{equation}
\label{eqn:convergence-test-derivivative-ac}
\lim_{h\to0}\left\langle\xi_i,\frac{u(t+h)-u(t)}{h}\right\rangle\quad\text{exists for all $i$.}
\end{equation}
Then if $h_n\to0$ and $k_n\to 0$ are such that
\begin{equation*}
\frac{u(t+h_n)-u(t)}{h_n}\rightharpoonup v\quad\text{and}\quad\frac{u(t+k_n)-u(t)}{k_n}\rightharpoonup w
\end{equation*}
from~\eqref{eqn:convergence-test-derivivative-ac} it follows that $\dual{\xi_i,v-w}=0$ for all $i$. This implies $v=w$. Up to now we have proved that there exists $v\colon(0,T)\to V$ such that
\begin{equation*}
\frac{u(t+h)-u(t)}{h}\rightharpoonup v(t)\quad\text{for a.e. $t\in(0,T)$}.
\end{equation*}
\textbf{Now one should prove that this convergence is actually strong. This will show automatically that $u\in W^{1,1}((0,T),V)$.}
\end{proof}

\begin{thm}[Aubin--Lions--Simon lemma]
\label{thm:aubin-lions}
Let $T>0$ and $V\subset H\subset E$ be Banach spaces. Assume that the embedding of $V$ in $H$ is compact and the embedding of $H$ in $E$ is continuous. Let $p$ and $q$ in $[1,\infty]$. Then the space
\[
\left\{u\in L^p((0,T),V)\;\middle|\;\dot u\in L^q((0,T),E)\right\}
\]
is compactly embedded in
\begin{itemize}
\item[(i)] $L^p((0,T),H)$ if $p<\infty$
\item[(ii)] $C^0([0,T],H)$ if $p=\infty$ and $q>1$.
\end{itemize}
\end{thm}
\begin{proof}
See~\cite[Theorem~II.5.16]{math-tool-ns}.
\end{proof}


\subsection{Generalized mixed elliptic problems}

We start with the following theorem.
\begin{thm}[Babu\^ska]
\label{thm:babuska}
Let $V$ and $Q$ be reflexive Banach spaces. Let $b\colon Q\times V\to\mathbb{R}$ be bilinear form. Let $Z=\left\{z\in V\middle|\text{$b(q,z)=0$ for all $q\in Q$}\right\}$ and define $W=V/Z$. Assume that $b$ is continuous so that there exists $M>0$ such that
\[
b(q,v)\le M\norm{q}_Q\norm{v}_V\quad\text{for all $(q,v)\in Q\times V$},
\]
Assume also that there exists $\beta>0$ such that
\[
\sup_{q\in Q\setminus\{0\}}\frac{b(q,w)}{\norm{q}_Q}\ge\beta\norm{w}_W
\]
for all $w\in W$ and that if $q\in Q$ satisfies
\[
b(q,v)=0\quad\text{for all $v\in V$}
\]
then $q=0$. Then for each $f\in Q'$ there exists a unique $w\in W$ such that
\[
b(q,w)=\dual{f,q}\quad\text{for all $q\in Q$}
\]
and
\[
\norm{w}_W\le\frac{1}{\beta}\norm{f}_{Q'}.
\]
\end{thm}
\begin{proof}
\emph{Step 1}. Consider the linear operator $B\colon W\to Q'$ defined by
\[
\dual{Bw,q}=b(w,q).
\]
We notice that
\[
|\dual{Bw,q}|=\inf_{z\in Z}|b(w+z,q)|\le M\norm{q}_Q\inf_{z\in Z}\norm{w+z}_V=M\norm{q}_Q\norm{w}_W
\]
so that $B\in\mathscr{L}(W,Q')$.

\emph{Step 2}. We prove that $B$ is injective. Indeed if $w\in W$ satisfies $Bw=0$ we have
\[
\beta\norm{w}_W\le\sup_{q\in Q\setminus\{0\}}\frac{\dual{Bw,q}}{\norm{q}_Q}=0
\]
so that $w=0$.

\emph{Step 3}. We prove that $\text{range}(B)$ is closed. Indeed let $f_n=Bw_n$ be such that $f_n\to f$ in $Q$. Then $f_n$ is a Cauchy sequence. Now
\[
\beta\norm{w_n-w_m}\le\sup_{q\in Q\setminus\{0\}}\frac{\dual{B(w_n-w_m),q}}{\norm{q}_Q}\le\norm{f_n-f_m}_{Q'}.
\]
Then also $w_n$ is a Cauchy sequence in $W$ and $w_n\to w$ in $W$ for some $w\in W$. Now by continuity of $B$ we have $Bw=f$ so that $f\in\text{range}(B)$.

\emph{Step 4}. We prove that $\text{range}(B)=Q'$. Indeed let $\null(B^T)=\left\{q\in Q\middle|\text{$b(q,v)=0$ for all $v\in V$}\right\}=\{0\}$ by hypotheses. On the other hand $\null(B^T)=\text{range}(B)^\perp$. Since $Q$ is reflexive $\text{range}(B)=(\text{range}(B)^\perp)^\perp=Q'$.

\emph{Step 5}. We have proved that $B$ is a bijection from $W$ to $Q'$. Then for each $f\in Q'$ there exists a unique $w\in W$ such that $Bw=f$. Moreover
\[
\beta\norm{w}_W\le\sup_{q\in Q\setminus\{0\}}\frac{\dual{Bw,q}}{\norm{q}_Q}=\sup_{q\in Q\setminus\{0\}}\frac{\dual{f,q}}{\norm{q}_Q}\le\norm{f}_{Q'}.
\]
This completes the proof.
\end{proof}
\begin{lemma}
\label{lemma:babuska}
Assume that the hypotheses of Theorem~\ref{thm:babuska} holds. Then
\[
\sup_{w\in W\setminus\{0\}}\frac{b(q,w)}{\norm{w}_W}\ge\beta\norm{q}_Q
\]
for all $q\in Q$.
\end{lemma}
\begin{proof}
For each $f\in Q'$ let $w_f\in W$ the solution to
\[
b(q,w)=\dual{f,q}\quad\text{for all $q\in Q$}.
\]
Notice that $\norm{f}_{Q'}\ge\beta\norm{w}_W$. Now
\[
\norm{q}_Q=\sup_{f\in Q'\setminus\{0\}}\frac{\dual{f,q}}{\norm{f}_{Q'}}=\sup_{f\in Q'\setminus\{0\}}\frac{b(q,w_f)}{\norm{f}_{Q'}}\le\sup_{f\in Q'\setminus\{0\}}\frac{b(q,w_f)}{\beta\norm{w}_{W}}\le\sup_{w\in W\setminus\{0\}}\frac{b(q,w)}{\beta\norm{w}_W}.
\]
This completes the proof.
\end{proof}
Then we get immediately the following corollary.
\begin{cor}
\label{cor:babuska}
Assume the hypotheses of Theorem~\ref{thm:babuska}. Then for each $g\in W'$ there exists a unique $p\in Q$ such that
\[
b(p,w)=\dual{g,w}\quad\text{for all $w\in W$}
\]
and
\[
\norm{p}_Q\le\frac{1}{\beta}\norm{g}_{W'}.
\]
\end{cor}
\begin{proof}
Apply first Lemma~\ref{lemma:babuska} and then Theorem~\ref{thm:babuska} with the role of $W$ and $Q$ swapped.
\end{proof}
\begin{rmk}
\label{rmk:babuska}
Assume that $g\in V'$ is such that $\dual{g,z}=0$ for all $z\in Z$. Then we can define $g$ as an element of $W'$. Indeed for each $w\in W$ we take a representative $v\in V$ still denoted with $w$ and we define $\dual{g,w}=\dual{g,v}$. The definition does not depend on the choice of the representative. Moreover
\[
\norm{g}_{W}=\sup_{w\in W\setminus\{0\}}\frac{\dual{g,w}}{\norm{w}_W}=\sup_{v\in V\setminus\{0\}}\frac{\dual{g,v}}{\norm{v}_V}=\norm{g}_{V'}.
\]
where we use that since $V$ is reflexive for each $w\in W$ there exists a representative $v\in V$ such that $\norm{w}_W=\norm{v}_V$ and that for any other representative $u\in V$ we have $\norm{w}_W\le\norm{u}_V$. In this case the Corollary~\ref{cor:babuska} can be formulated as follows. There exists a unique $p\in Q$ such that
\[
b(p,v)=\dual{g,v}\quad\text{for all $v\in V$}
\]
and
\[
\norm{p}_Q\le\frac{1}{\beta}\norm{g}_{V'}.
\]
\end{rmk}
The following theorem is the main result of this section. The result is proved in~\cite{nicolaides} in the Hilbert spaces setting and we have generalized it for Banach spaces.
\begin{thm}
\label{thm:mixed-elliptic}
Let $V$, $\tilde V$, $Q$ and $\tilde Q$ be reflexive Banach spaces. Let $b\colon Q\times\tilde V\to\mathbb{R}$ be a bilinear form. Let $\tilde{Z}=\left\{\tilde{z}\in\tilde V\middle|\text{$b(q,\tilde z)=0$ for all $q\in Q$}\right\}$ and define $\tilde W=\tilde V/\tilde Z$. Assume that there exists $M>0$ such that
\[
b(q,\tilde v)\le M\norm{q}_Q\norm{\tilde v}_{\tilde V}\quad\text{for all $q\in Q$ and $\tilde v\in\tilde V$}.
\]
Assume also that there exists $\beta>0$ such that
\[
\sup_{q\in Q\setminus\{0\}}\frac{b(q,\tilde w)}{\norm{q}_Q}\ge\beta\norm{\tilde w}_{\tilde W}\quad\text{for all $\tilde w\in\tilde W$}
\]
and that if $q\in Q$ satisfies
\[
b(q,\tilde v)=0\quad\text{for all $\tilde v\in\tilde V$}
\]
then $q=0$. Let $c\colon\tilde Q\times V\to\mathbb{R}$ be a bilinear form. Let $Z=\left\{z\in V\middle|\text{$c(\tilde q,z)=0$ for all $\tilde q\in\tilde Q$}\right\}$ and define $W=V/Z$. Assume that there exists $N>0$ such that
\[
c(\tilde q,v)\le N\norm{\tilde q}_{\tilde Q}\norm{v}_{V}\quad\text{for all $\tilde q\in\tilde Q$ and $v\in V$}.
\]
Assume also that there exists $\gamma>0$ such that
\[
\sup_{\tilde q\in \tilde Q\setminus\{0\}}\frac{c(\tilde q,w)}{\norm{\tilde q}_{\tilde Q}}\ge\gamma\norm{w}_{W}\quad\text{for all $w\in W$}
\]
and that if $\tilde q\in\tilde Q$ satisfies
\[
c(\tilde q,v)=0\quad\text{for all $v\in V$}
\]
then $\tilde q=0$. Let $a\colon V\times\tilde V\to\mathbb{R}$ be a bilinear form. Assume that there exists a constant $L$ such that
\[
a(v,\tilde v)\le L\norm{v}_V\norm{\tilde v}_{\tilde V}\quad\text{for all $v\in V$ and $\tilde v\in\tilde V$}.
\]
Assume also that there exists $\alpha>0$ such that
\[
\sup_{\tilde v\in\tilde V\setminus\{0\}}\frac{a(z,\tilde v)}{\norm{\tilde v}_{\tilde V}}\ge\alpha\norm{z}_V\quad\text{for all $z\in Z$}
\]
and that if $\tilde z\in\tilde Z$ satisfies
\[
a(v,\tilde z)=0\quad\text{for all $v\in V$}
\]
then $\tilde z=0$. Then for all $f\in\tilde V'$ and $g\in\tilde Q'$ there exists a unique $(u,p)\in V\times Q$ such that
\begin{gather*}
a(u,\tilde v)+b(p,\tilde v)=\dual{f,\tilde v}\quad\text{for all $\tilde v\in\tilde V$} \\
c(\tilde q,u)=\dual{g,\tilde q}\quad\text{for all $\tilde q\in\tilde Q$}.
\end{gather*}
Moreover
\begin{gather*}
\norm{u}_V\le\frac{1}{\gamma}\left(1+\frac{L}{\alpha}\right)\norm{g}_{\tilde Q'}+\frac{1}{\alpha}\norm{f}_{\tilde V'} \\
\norm{p}_Q\le\frac{1}{\beta}\left(1+\frac{L}{\alpha}\right)\norm{f}_{\tilde V'}+\frac{L\beta}{\gamma}\left(1+\frac{L}{\alpha}\right)\norm{g}_{\tilde Q'}.
\end{gather*}
\end{thm}
\begin{proof}
By Theorem~\ref{thm:babuska} there exists a unique $w\in W$ such that
\[
c(\tilde q,w)=\dual{g,\tilde q}\quad\text{for all $\tilde q\in\tilde Q$}
\]
and
\[
\norm{w}_W\le\frac{1}{\gamma}\norm{g}_{\tilde Q'}.
\]
We identify $w$ with one of its representative in $V$ and we can choose it such that $\norm{w}_V=\norm{w}_W$. We notice that
\[
a(w,\tilde v)\le L\norm{w}_V\norm{\tilde v}_{\tilde V}.
\]
Then $a(w,\cdot)\in\tilde V'\subset\tilde Z'$ so that $\dual{f,\cdot}-a(w,\cdot)\in\tilde Z'$. Then by Theorem~\ref{thm:babuska} the equation
\[
a(z,\tilde z)=\dual{f,\tilde z}-a(w,\tilde z)\quad\text{for all $\tilde z\in\tilde Z$}
\]
has a unique solution $z\in Z$ that satisfies
\[
\norm{z}_V\le\frac{1}{\alpha}\left(\norm{f}_{\tilde V'}+\frac{L}{\gamma}\norm{g}_{\tilde Q'}\right).
\]
Now define $u=w+z\in V$ and we notice that
\begin{equation}
\label{eqn:u-eqn}
a(u,\tilde z)=\dual{f,\tilde z}\quad\text{for all $\tilde z\in\tilde Z$}
\end{equation}
and
\[
\norm{u}_V\le\frac{1}{\gamma}\left(1+\frac{L}{\alpha}\right)\norm{g}_{\tilde Q'}+\frac{1}{\alpha}\norm{f}_{\tilde V'}.
\]
Now we notice that by~\eqref{eqn:u-eqn} we have
\[
\dual{f,\tilde z}-a(u,\tilde z)=\dual{f,\tilde z}-a(w,\tilde z)-a(z,\tilde z)=0\quad\text{for all $\tilde z\in\tilde Z$}.
\]
Then by Remark~\ref{rmk:babuska} we have $\dual{f,\cdot}-a(u,\cdot)\in\tilde{W}'$ and the equation
\[
b(p,\tilde v)=\dual{f,\tilde v}-a(u,\tilde v)\quad\text{for all $v\in\tilde V$}
\]
has a unique solution $p\in Q$ that satisfies
\[
\norm{p}_Q\le\frac{1}{\beta}\left(\norm{f}_{\tilde V'}+L\norm{u}_V\right)\le\frac{1}{\beta}\left(1+\frac{L}{\alpha}\right)\norm{f}_{\tilde V'}+\frac{L\beta}{\gamma}\left(1+\frac{L}{\alpha}\right)\norm{g}_{\tilde Q'}.
\]
This concludes the proof.
\end{proof}

\section{Nonlinear functional analysis}

\subsection{Other}

\begin{lemma}
\label{lemma:check-gateaux}
Let $X$ and $Y$ be Banach spaces. Let $U$ an open subset of $X$ and $F\colon U\to Y'$. Let $x\in U$. Assume that there exists $A\in\mathscr{L}(X,Y')$ and for all $v\in X$ there exists $\omega\colon\mathbb{R}\to[0,\infty)$ with $\omega(\epsilon)\to0$ as $\epsilon\to0$ such that
\[
\left|\left\langle\frac{F(x+\epsilon v)-F(x)}{\epsilon}-Av,y\right\rangle\right|\le\omega(\epsilon)\norm{y}_Y
\]
for all $y\in Y$ and $\epsilon\in\mathbb{R}$ sufficiently small. Then $F$ is Gateaux differentiable in $x$ with Gateaux differential $A$.
\end{lemma}
\begin{proof}
Fix $v\in X$. Then by hypotheses there exists $\omega\colon\mathbb{R}\to[0,\infty)$ with $\omega(\epsilon)\to0$ as $\epsilon\to0$ such that
\[
\left\lVert\frac{F(x+\epsilon v)-F(x)}{\epsilon}-Av\right\lVert_{Y'}=\sup_{y\in Y\setminus\{0\}}\frac{\left\langle\frac{F(x+\epsilon v)-F(x)}{\epsilon}-Av,y\right\rangle}{\norm{y}_Y}\le\omega(\epsilon).
\]
Then
\[
\left\lVert\frac{F(x+\epsilon v)-F(x)}{\epsilon}-Av\right\lVert_{Y'}\to0
\]
as $\epsilon\to 0$. This is exactly the thesis.
\end{proof}

\begin{lemma}
\label{lemma:check-continuity-gateaux}
Let $X$ and $Y$ be two Banach spaces. Let $A_n$ and $A$ in $\mathscr{L}(X,Y')$ such that for all $x\in X$ and $y\in Y$ we have
\[
|\dual{A_nx-Ax,y}|\le\omega_n\norm{x}_X\norm{y}_Y
\]
where $\omega_n\to0$. Then $A_n\to A$ in $\mathscr{L}(X,Y')$.
\end{lemma}
\begin{proof}
We have
\[
\norm{A_n-A}_{\mathscr{L}(X,Y')}=\sup_{x\in X\setminus\{0\}}\frac{\norm{A_nx-Ax}_{Y'}}{\norm{x}_X}=\sup_{x\in X\setminus\{0\}}\sup_{y\in Y\setminus\{0\}}\frac{\dual{A_nx-Ax,y}}{\norm{x}_X\norm{y}_Y}\le\omega_n\to 0.
\]
This concludes the proof.
\end{proof}

\begin{prop}
\label{prop:Lipschitz-c1-compact}
Let $V$ a Banach space and let $f\in C^1(V)$ and $K\subset V$ compact. Then $f$ is Lipschitz continuous on $K$.
\end{prop}
\begin{proof}
By contradiction let $u_n$ and $v_n$ two sequences in $K$ such that
\begin{equation*}
\frac{|F(u_n)-F(v_n)|}{\norm{u_n-v_n}}\ge n.
\end{equation*}
By compactness of $K$ up to extracting subsequences we can assume that $u_n\to u$ and $v_n\to v$. If $u\neq v$ then
\begin{equation*}
\frac{|F(u_n)-F(v_n)|}{\norm{u_n-v_n}}\to\frac{|F(u)-F(v)|}{\norm{u-v}}<\infty
\end{equation*}
which is a contradiction. If $u=v$ then
\begin{equation*}
\frac{|F(u_n)-F(v_n)|}{\norm{u_n-v_n}}=\frac{|\dual{DF(u),u_n-v_n}+o(\norm{u_n-v_n})|}{\norm{u_n-v_n}}\le\norm{DF(u)}+o(1)\to\norm{DF(u)}<\infty
\end{equation*}
which is a contradiction.
\end{proof}

\subsection{Implicit function theorem}

\begin{thm}
\label{thm:IFT}
Let $X$, $Y$ and $E$ be Banach spaces. Let $U$ and open set of $X\times Y$ and $(x_0,y_0)\in U$. Let $F\in C^1(U,E)$ such that $F(x_0,y_0)=0$ and $D_x F(x_0,y_0)\in\text{Iso}(X,E)$. Then we can find $r>0$ and $s>0$ with $B(x_0,r)\times B(y_0,s)\subset U$ such that there exist a unique $\chi\in C^1(B(y_0,s),B(x_0,r))$ satisfying
\[
F(\chi(y),y)=0\quad\text{for all $y\in B(y_0,s)$}.
\]
Moreover if $F$ is of class $C^r$ with $r\ge2$ also $\chi$ is of class $C^r$.
\end{thm}
\begin{proof}
See~\cite{niremberg}.
\end{proof}

\subsection{Fixed point theorems}

A classical version of the Schauder fixed point in Banach spaces is the following.
\begin{thm}[Schauder fixed point theorem]
\label{thm:schauder}
Let $X$ be a Banach space and $C\subset X$ closed and convex. Let $F\colon C\to C$ continuous such that $F(C)\subset K$, where $K\subset C$ is compact. Then $F$ admits a fixed point.
\end{thm}
\begin{proof}
See~\cite{niremberg}.
\end{proof}
The following corollary holds.
\begin{cor}
\label{cor:schauder}
Let $X$ be a Banach space and $C\subset X$ closed and convex. Let $F\colon C\to C$ continuous such that $F(C)$ is precompact. Then $F$ admits a fixed point.
\end{cor}
\begin{proof}
Let $F(C)\subset K\subset C$ be the closure of $F(C)$. It is clear that $K$ is compact. Then by Theorem~\ref{thm:schauder} it follows that $F$ has a fixed point.
\end{proof}
We also report the following improved version.
\begin{cor}
\label{cor:schauder-improved}
Let $X$ be a Banach space and let $F\colon X\to X$ continuous such that $F(B(0,r))$ is precompact in $X$ for all $r>0$. Assume also that there exists $a\in[0,\infty)$ and $b\in[0,1)$ such that $\norm{F(x)}_X\le a+b\norm{x}_X$ for all $x\in X$. Then $F$ admits a fixed point.
\end{cor}
\begin{proof}
Let $C=\overline{B}(0,r)$ so that $F(C)\subset\overline{B}(0,a+br)$ and $K=\overline{F(C)}$ is compact. Notice that $F(C)\subset K\subset\overline{B}(0,a+br)$. We want to find $r>0$ such that $F$ maps $C$ into itself. This is certainly true if $a+br<r$ or equivalently $r>\frac{a}{1-b}$. With such $r$ then $F,C,K$ satisfy the hypotheses of Theorem~\ref{thm:schauder} so that the thesis follows.
\end{proof}

\subsection{Local existence theorem for Cauchy problems in Banach spaces}

\begin{thm}
\label{thm:cauchy-lipschitz}
Let $X$ be a Banach space and let $U$ be an open subset of $X\times\mathbb{R}$ with $(x_0,0)\in U$. Let $G\in C^1(U,X)$. Then there exists $T>0$ such that the Cauchy problem
\[
\begin{cases}
\dot x(t)=G(x(t),t) & \text{for $t\in[0,T]$} \\
x(0)=x_0
\end{cases}
\]
admits a unique solution $x\in C^2([0,T],X)$.
\end{thm}
\begin{proof}
Since $G$ is of class $C^1$ there there exists a neighborhood $V\subset U$ of $(x_0,0)$ such that $G$ and $D_xG$ are bounded in $V$. Let $M=\sup_V\norm{G}_X<\infty$ and $L=\sup_V\norm{D_x G}_{\mathscr{L}(X,X)}$. We notice that $G$ is Lipschitz continuous with respect to $x$ and uniformly in $t$ on $V$ with Lipschitz constant $L$. Let $B=B(x_0,r)$ for some $r>0$ and $T>0$ such that $B(x_0,r)\times(-T,T)\subset V$. We introduce the normed space $\mathscr{X}=C^0([0,T],B)$ endowed with the norm $\norm{x}_\mathscr{X}=\sup_{t\in[0,T]}\norm{x(t)}_X$. Since $\mathscr{X}$ is a closed subset of $C^0([0,T],B)$ it is a complete metric space. Now introduce the map
\[
T\colon B\to C^0([0,T],B)
\]
defined by
\[
T(x)(t)=x_0+\int_0^tG(x(s),s)ds.
\]
We notice that $\norm{T(x)-x_0}_X\le TM$. Up to choose a smaller $T$ we can assume that $TM<r$ so that actually $T\colon B\to B$. Moreover for any $x_1$ and $x_2$ in $X$ we have
\[
\norm{T(x_2)-T(x_1)}_{\mathscr{X}}\le\int_0^T\norm{G(x_2(s),s)-G(x_1(s),s)}_Xds\le TL\norm{x_2-x_1}_\mathscr{X}.
\]
Up to choose a smaller $T$ we can assume that $TL<1$. In this case $T$ is a strict contraction. By the Banach--Caccioppoli fixed point theorem there exists a unique $x\in B$ such that $Tx=x$. Since $Tx$ is of class $C^2$ then also $x$ is of class $C^2$.
\end{proof}

\subsection{Browder--Minty theorem}

The results of this section are taken from~\cite{intro-pde} and~\cite{roubicek-pde}.

Let $X$ be a Banach space and $T\colon X\to X^*$ be a nonlinear operator and $f\in X^*$. The Browder--Minty theorem allows to study the existence of solutions to the equation $T(x)=f$ when there is not any variational formulation. The key assumption is that $T$ is coercive and monotone, a property that makes $T$ similar in some sense to an elliptic operator.
\begin{defi}
Let $X$ be a Banach space and let $T\colon X\to X^*$. We say that
\begin{itemize}
\item[(i)] $T$ is bounded if maps bounded sets of $X$ into bounded sets of $X^*$,
\item[(ii)] $T$ is coercive if
\[
\frac{\dual{T(x),x}}{\norm{x}}\to\infty\quad\text{as $\norm{x}\to\infty$}
\]
and that
\item[(iii)] $T$ is monotone if
\[
\dual{T(x)-T(y),x-y}\ge 0\quad\text{for all $x$ and $y$ in $X$}.
\]
\end{itemize}
\end{defi}
We now prove a lemma that establish the equivalence between the equation $T(x)=f$ and a variational inequality under the assumption that $T$ is monotone.
\begin{lemma}
\label{lemma:equality-inequality}
Let $T\colon X\to X^*$ be continuous and $f\in X^*$. Then if $x\in X$ is a solution to the variational inequality
\[
\dual{T(y)-f,y-x}\ge0\quad\text{for all $y\in X$}
\]
it is also a solution to te equation $T(x)=f$. Moreover if $T$ is monotone then any solution to the equation $T(x)=f$ is also a solution to the variational inequality.
\end{lemma}
\begin{proof}
Let $y=x+tv$ with $t>0$ and $v\in X$. Then if $x\in X$ is a solution to the variational inequality we have
\[
\dual{T(x+tv)-f,v}\ge 0.
\]
Letting $t\to0$ we have $\dual{T(x)-f,v}\ge0$. By arbitrariness of $v$ we have $T(x)=f$. Conversely let $x\in X$ be such that $T(x)=f$. By monotonicity
\[
\dual{T(y)-f,y-x}=\dual{T(y)-T(x),y-x}\ge 0.
\]
Then $x$ is a solution to the variational inequality.
\end{proof}
Now we study the equation $T(x)=f$ when the Banach space is finite dimensional. This results will be used to get the general result using the Galerkin method.
\begin{prop}
\label{prop:elliptic-finite-dimensional}
Let $T\colon\mathbb{R}^n\to\mathbb{R}^n$ be continuous and coercive. Then for every $f\in\mathbb{R}^n$ the equation $T(x)=f$ has at least one solution.
\end{prop}
\begin{proof}
Without loss of generality we cn assume that $f=0$. Indeed otherwise define $S(x)=T(x)-f$ and notice that $S$ is still continuous and coercive. Indeed
\[
\frac{S(x)\cdot x}{|x|}=\frac{T(x)\cdot x}{|x|}-\frac{f\cdot x}{|x|}\ge\frac{T(x)\cdot x}{|x|}-|f|.
\]
Now define $G\colon\mathbb{R}^n\to\mathbb{R}^n$ by $G(x)=x-T(x)$. We notice that
\[
G(x)\cdot x=|x|^2-T(x)\cdot x.
\]
Since $T$ is coercive there exists an $r>0$ such that $|x|^2-G(x)\cdot x=T(x)\cdot x>0$ for all $x\in\mathbb{R}\setminus B(0,r)$. We define $\Pi\colon\mathbb{R}^n\to C$ with $C=\bar{B}(0,r)$ by
\[
\Pi(x)=
\begin{cases}
x & \text{if $|x|\le r$} \\
r\frac{x}{|x|} & \text{if $|x|>r$}.
\end{cases}
\]
We set $H(x)=\Pi(G(x))$ and we notice that $H\colon C\to C$ and it is continuous. By Brouwer fixed point theorem there exists $x\in C$ such that $H(x)=x$. If $x\in\mathring{C}$ then $x=H(x)=\Pi(G(x))=G(x)$ so that $T(x)=0$. If we prove that $x\notin\partial C$ we have concluded. By contradiction assume that $|x|=r$. Then $|G(x)|\ge r$ and
\[
|x|^2=x\cdot x=H(x)\cdot x=\frac{r}{|G(x)|}G(x)\cdot x<\frac{r}{|G(x)|}|x|^2\le|x|^2
\]
where we used that $|x|^2>G(x)\cdot x$ for $|x|\ge r$. This is a contradiction.
\end{proof}
We are now in position to prove the Browder--Minty theorem.
\begin{thm}
\label{thm:browder-minty}
Let $X$ be a reflexive and separable Banach space. Let $T\colon X\to X^*$ be bounded, continuous, coercive and monotone. Then for every $f\in X^*$ there exists a solution $x\in X$ to the equation $T(x)=f$.
\end{thm}
\begin{proof}
As anticipated the proof relies on the Galerkin method.

\emph{Step 1}. Let $\{y_n\}\subset X$ such that the linear combinations of $y_n$ are dense in $X$. Let
\[
X_n=\text{span}\left\{y_m\right\}_{m=1}^n.
\]
We define $f_n\in X_n^*$ by $\dual{f_n,x}=\dual{f,x}$ for all $x\in X_n$. We also define $T_n\colon X_n\to X_n^*$ by $\dual{T_n(x),y}=\dual{T(x),y}$ for all $x$ and $y$ in $X_n$.

\emph{Step 2}. We prove that $T_n$ are still bounded, continuous and coercive. For all $x\in X_n$ we have
\[
\norm{T_n(x)}_{X_n^*}=\sup_{y\in X_n\setminus\{0\}}\frac{\dual{T_n(x),y}}{\norm{y}_{X_n}}\le\sup_{y\in X\setminus\{0\}}\frac{\dual{T(x),y}}{\norm{y}_X}=\norm{T(x)}_X
\]
so the boundedness of $T_n$ follows form the boundedness of $T$. Moreover let $x_k\to x$ in $X_n$. Then
\[
\begin{split}
\norm{T_n(x_k)-T_n(x)}_{X_n^*}=\sup_{y\in X_n\setminus\{0\}}\frac{\dual{T_n(x_k)-T_n(x),y}}{\norm{y}_{X_n}}&\le\sup_{y\in X\setminus\{0\}}\frac{\dual{T(x_k)-T(x),y}}{\norm{y}_X}=\\
&=\norm{T(x_k)-T(x)}_{X^*}\to0
\end{split}
\]
and this proves that $T_n$ is continuous. Moreover for $x\in X_n$
\[
\frac{\dual{T_n(x),x}}{\norm{x}_{X_n}}=\frac{\dual{T(x),x}}{\norm{x}_{X}}
\]
so the coercivity of $T_n$ follows immediately from the coercivity of $T$. By Proposition~\ref{prop:elliptic-finite-dimensional} we have that for each $n$ there exists $x_n\in X_n$ that solves $T_n(x_n)=f_n$.

\emph{Step 3}. We have the estimate
\[
\frac{\dual{T(x_n),x_n}}{\norm{x_n}_X}\le\frac{\dual{T_n(x_n),x_n}}{\norm{x_n}_X}=\frac{\dual{f_n,x_n}}{\norm{x_n}_X}=\frac{\dual{f,x_n}}{\norm{x_n}_X}\le\norm{f}.
\]
Since $T$ is coercive then $x_n$ are bounded in $X$ and since $T$ is bounded then $T(x_n)$ are bounded in $X^*$. Then up to a subsequence
\begin{gather*}
x_n\rightharpoonup x\quad\text{in $X$} \\
T(x_n)\overset{*}{\rightharpoonup}g\quad\text{in $X^*$}
\end{gather*}
for some $x\in X$ and $g\in X^*$.

\emph{Step 4}. We first prove that $g=f$. To this aim we fix any $y_m$ and we notice that
\[
\dual{g-f,y_m}=\lim_{n\to\infty}\dual{T(x_n)-f,y_m}=0.
\]
Then by density we have indeed $g=f$. Next we have
\[
\dual{T(x_n),x_n}=\dual{T_n(x_n),x_n}=\dual{f_n,x_n}=\dual{f,x_n}\to\dual{g,x}.
\]
\emph{Step 5}. Eventually using the monotonicity of $T$ we have
\[
0\ge\dual{T(y)-T(x_n),y-x_n}\to\dual{T(y)-g,y-x}=\dual{T(y)-f,y-x}.
\]
Applying Lemma~\ref{lemma:equality-inequality} we get the thesis.
\end{proof}
Actually the same existence result can be obtained with a weaken condition then monotonicity.
\begin{defi}
Let $X$ be a Banach space and let $T\colon X\to X^*$. We say that $T$ is pseudomonotone if it is bounded and every time
\[
\text{$x_k\rightharpoonup x$ in $X$}\quad\text{and}\quad\limsup_{k\to\infty}\dual{T(x_k),x_k-x}\le0
\]
then
\[
\liminf_{k\to\infty}\dual{T(x_k),x_k-y}\ge\dual{T(x),x-y}
\]
for all $y\in X$.
\end{defi}
If $T$ is pseudomonotone it posses also a nice continuity property.
\begin{prop}
\label{prop:pseudomonotone-demicontinuous}
Let $T\colon X\to X^*$ be pseudomonotone. Then $T$ is demicontinuous, i.e., for all $x_k\to x$ in $X$ it holds that $T(x_k)\overset{*}{\rightharpoonup}T(x)$ in $X^*$.
\end{prop}
\begin{proof}
Let $x_k\to x$ in $X$. Then $\{x_k\}$ is bounded in $X$ and since $T$ is bounded also $\{T(x_k)\}$ is bounded in $X^*$. Then, for all subsequence of $\{x_k\}$ there exists a further subsequence $\{x_{k_j}\}$ such that $T(x_{k_j})\overset{*}{\rightharpoonup}g$ for some $g\in X^*$. Then
\[
\liminf_{j\to\infty}\dual{T(x_{k_j}),x_{k_j}-x}=\dual{g,x-x}=0.
\]
Then, since $T$ is pseudomonotone
\[
\dual{g,x-y}=\limsup_{j\to\infty}\dual{T(x_{k_j}),x_{k_j}-y}\le\dual{T(x),x-y}\quad\text{for all $y\in X$}.
\]
By arbitrariness of $y$, it follows that $T(x)=g$. We conclude that the whole sequence $\{T(x_k)\}$ converges weakly-star in $X^*$ to $T(x)$.
\end{proof}
The following generalization of Minty--Brower theorem holds.
\begin{thm}
\label{thm:browder-minty-generalized}
Let $X$ be a separable and reflexive Banach space. Let $T\colon X\to X^*$ coercive and pseudomonotone. Then for every $f\in X^*$ there exists $x\in X$ such that $T(x)=f$.
\end{thm}
\begin{proof}
The proof is similar to that of Theorem~\ref{thm:browder-minty} up to Step 4. The monotonicity is never used here and the demicontinuity (implied by pseudomonotonicity by Proposition~\ref{prop:pseudomonotone-demicontinuous}) is enough to prove that $T_k$ are continuous and to handle the other limit passages. Then we need only to adapt Step 5.

We know that $x_k\rightharpoonup x$ in $X$, that $T(x_k)\overset{*}{\rightharpoonup}f$ in $X^*$ and $\dual{T(x_k),x_k}\to\dual{f,x}$. Then in particular
\[
\dual{T(x_k),x_k-x}=\dual{T(x_k),x_k}-\dual{T(x_k),x}\to\dual{f,x}-\dual{f,x}=0.
\]
Since $T$ is pseudomonotone then
\[
\liminf_{k\to\infty}\dual{T(x_k),x_k-y}\ge\dual{T(x),x-y}
\]
for all $y\in X$. But
\[
\liminf_{k\to\infty}\dual{T(x_k),x_k-y}=\dual{f,x-y}.
\]
Then
\[
\dual{f,x-y}\ge\dual{T(x),x-y}
\]
for all $y\in X$. By arbitrariness of $y$ we have $T(x)=f$.
\end{proof}
The following proposition establish the relationship between monotone and pseudomonotone operators.
\begin{prop}
\label{prop:monotono-pseudomonotone}
Let $X$ be a Banach space. Let $T\colon X\to X^*$ be continuous, bounded and monotone. Then $T$ is pseudomonotone.
\end{prop}
\begin{proof}
Let $x_k\rightharpoonup x$ with
\[
\limsup_{k\to\infty}\dual{T(x_k),x_k-x}\le0.
\]
Since $T$ is monotone then
\[
\dual{T(x_k),x_k-x}\ge\dual{T(x),x_k-x}\to0.
\]
This implies also
\[
\liminf_{k\to\infty}\dual{T(x_k),x_k-x}\ge0.
\]
Then
\[
\lim_{k\to\infty}\dual{T(x_k),x_k-x}=0.
\]
Now let $y\in X$ and set $y_\epsilon=(1-\epsilon)x+\epsilon y$ for $\epsilon>0$. By monotonicity
\[
0\le\dual{T(x_k)-T(y_\epsilon),x_k-y_\epsilon}=\dual{T(x_k)-T(y_\epsilon),\epsilon(x-y)+x_k-x}.
\]
Rearranging the terms we have
\[
\epsilon\dual{T(x_k),x-y}\ge\dual{T(y_\epsilon),x_k-x}-\dual{T(x_k),x_k-x}+\epsilon\dual{T(y_\epsilon),x-y}.
\]
Passing to le limit $k\to\infty$ with $\epsilon>0$ fixed
\[
\epsilon\liminf_{k\to\infty}\dual{T(x_k),x-y}\ge\epsilon\dual{T(y_\epsilon),x-y}.
\]
Dividing by $\epsilon$ and passing to the limit $\epsilon\to0^+$ we obtain using the continuity of $T$ that
\[
\liminf_{k\to\infty}\dual{T(x_k),x-y}\ge\dual{T(x),x-y}.
\]
Eventually
\[
\liminf_{k\to\infty}\dual{T(x_k),x_k-y}=\lim_{k\to\infty}\dual{T(x_k),x_k-x}+\liminf_{k\to\infty}\dual{T(x_k),x-y}\ge0.
\]
This proves that $T$ is pseudomonotone.
\end{proof}
The following result is useful.
\begin{prop}
\label{prop:sum-pseudomonotone}
Let $X$ be a Banach space. Let $T_1$ and $T_2$ from $X$ into $X^*$ be pseudomonotone operators. Then also $T_1+T_2$ is pseudomonotone.
\end{prop}
\begin{proof}
Let $x_k\rightharpoonup x$ with
\[
\limsup_{k\to\infty}\dual{T_1(x_k)+T_2(x_k),x_k-x}\le0.
\]
We claim that
\begin{equation}
\label{eqn:desired-pseudo-sum}
\limsup_{k\to\infty}\dual{T_i(x_k),x_k-x}\le0
\end{equation}
for both $i=1$ and $i=2$. By contradiction
\[
\limsup_{k\to\infty}\dual{T_2(x_k),x_k-x}=\epsilon>0.
\]
Up to taking a subsequence we can assume that
\[
\lim_{k\to\infty}\dual{T_2(x_k),x_k-x}=\epsilon>0.
\]
Then
\begin{equation}
\label{eqn:absurb-pseudo-sum}
\limsup_{k\to\infty}\dual{T_1(x_k),x_k-x}\le-\epsilon<0.
\end{equation}
Then since $T_1$ is pseudomonotone
\[
\liminf_{k\to\infty}\dual{T_1(x_k),x_k-x}\ge0
\]
but this contradicts~\eqref{eqn:absurb-pseudo-sum}. Then~\eqref{eqn:desired-pseudo-sum} holds. Since $T_i$ is pseudomonotone then for all $y\in X$ we have
\[
\liminf_{k\to\infty}\dual{T_i(x_k),x_k-y}\ge\dual{T_i(x),x-y}.
\]
It follows that
\[
\liminf_{k\to\infty}\dual{T_1(x_k)+T_2(x_k),x_k-y}\ge\dual{T_1(x)+T_2(x),x-y}.
\]
This means that $T_1+T_2$ is pseudomonotone.
\end{proof}

The Minty--Browder theorem can be generalized also to the case in which $T$ is a set valued map, that is $T\colon X\rightsquigarrow X^*$. In particular we focus on the case in which $T=\partial\Phi+A$ where $\Phi\colon X\to\mathbb{R}\cup\{\infty\}$ is convex and $A\colon X\to X^*$ is pseudomonotone. In this case the problem that we want to study is that of finding $x\in X$ such that
\begin{equation}
\label{eqn:inclusion}
\partial\Phi(x)+A(x)\ni f,
\end{equation}
where $f\in X^*$. Here $\partial\Psi$ denotes the subdifferential of $\Psi$. The following generalization of Minty--Browder theorem holds.
\begin{thm}
\label{thm:inclusion}
Let $\Phi\colon X\to\mathbb{R}\cup\mathbb\{\infty\}$ be convex, proper and lower semicontinuous. Assume that for all $\epsilon>0$ there exist $\Phi_\epsilon\colon X\to\mathbb{R}$ convex and G\^ateaux differentiable with $\Phi_\epsilon'\colon X\to X^*$ demicontinuous and bounded, such that the following properties holds:
\begin{itemize}
\item[(i)] for all $x\in X$ and $\{x_\epsilon\}_{\epsilon>0}\subset X$ such that $x_\epsilon\rightharpoonup x$ in $X$ it holds that
\[
\Phi(x)\le\liminf_{\epsilon\to0^+}\Phi_\epsilon(x_\epsilon),
\]
\item[(ii)] for all $x\in X$
\[
\Phi(x)\ge\limsup_{\epsilon\to 0^+}\Phi_\epsilon(x).
\]
\end{itemize}
Let also $A\colon X\to X^*$ be pseudomonotone. Moreover assume that the following coercivity property holds: there exists $z\in\dom\Phi$ such that for all $1>\epsilon>0$ and $x\in X$ the inequality
\begin{equation}
\label{eqn:coercivity-property}
\dfrac{\Phi_\epsilon(x)+\dual{A(x),x-z}}{\norm{x}_X}\ge\zeta(\norm{x}_X)
\end{equation}
holds. Here $\zeta\colon[0,\infty)\to[0,\infty)$ satisfies the condition
\[
\lim_{r\to\infty}\zeta(r)=\infty.
\]
Then for all $f\in X^*$ there exists $x\in X$ such that~\eqref{eqn:inclusion} holds.
\end{thm}
\begin{proof}
The map $\Phi_\epsilon'$ is monotone since $\Phi_\epsilon$ is convex. Then by Proposition~\ref{prop:sum-pseudomonotone} the operator $\Phi_\epsilon'+A$ is pseudomonotone. Moreover it is bounded and demicontinuous. The coercivity property~\eqref{eqn:coercivity-property} allows to apply Theorem~\ref{thm:browder-minty-generalized} to conclude thet for all $\epsilon>0$ there exists $x_\epsilon\in X$ such that $\Phi_\epsilon'(x_\epsilon)+A(x_\epsilon)\ni f$. This is equivalent to
\begin{equation}
\label{eqn:variational-inequality}
\Phi_\epsilon(y)\ge\Phi_\epsilon(x_\epsilon)+\dual{f-A(x_\epsilon),y-x_\epsilon}\quad\text{for all $y\in X$}.
\end{equation}
The idea is now to prove that $\{x_\epsilon\}_{\epsilon>0}$ is bounded in $X$ in order to find a limit value by compactness. To this aim we notice that by property (ii) above, for each $\epsilon>0$ small enough the inequality $\Phi_\epsilon(z_\epsilon)\le\Phi(z)+1$ holds. Then by~\eqref{eqn:coercivity-property} and by~\eqref{eqn:variational-inequality} with $y=z$ we have
\[
\zeta(\norm{x_\epsilon})\norm{x_\epsilon}_X\le \Phi_\epsilon(x_\epsilon)+\dual{A(x_\epsilon),x_\epsilon-z}\le\Phi_\epsilon(z)+\dual{f,z-x_\epsilon}.
\]
This implies that $x_\epsilon\to0$. Then extracting a (not relabeled) subsequence we have $x_\epsilon\rightharpoonup x$. Then we take the supremum limit of~\eqref{eqn:variational-inequality}, and using properties (i, ii) above we get
\[
\begin{split}
\Phi(y)&\ge\limsup_{\epsilon\to 0^+}\left[\Phi_\epsilon(x_\epsilon)+\dual{f-A(x_\epsilon),y-x_\epsilon}\right]\ge\liminf_{\epsilon\to 0^+}\left[\Phi_\epsilon(x_\epsilon)+\dual{f-A(x_\epsilon),y-x_\epsilon}\right]\ge\\
&\ge\Phi(x)+\dual{f,y-x}+\liminf_{\epsilon\to 0^+}\dual{A(x_\epsilon),x_\epsilon-y}.
\end{split}
\]
The proof of the theorem is completed if we prove that
\[
\liminf_{\epsilon\to 0^+}\dual{A(x_\epsilon),x_\epsilon-y}\ge\dual{A(x),x-y}.
\]
In view of the fact that $A$ is pseudomonotone, it is enough to prove that
\[
\limsup_{\epsilon\to 0^+}\dual{A(x_\epsilon),x_\epsilon-x}\le0.
\]
But by~\eqref{eqn:variational-inequality} it follows that
\[
\begin{split}
\limsup_{\epsilon\to 0^+}\dual{A(x_\epsilon),x_\epsilon-x}&\le\limsup_{\epsilon\to 0^+}\left[\Phi_\epsilon(x)-\Phi_\epsilon(x_\epsilon)+\dual{f,x_\epsilon-x}\right]\le\\
&\le\limsup_{\epsilon\to0^+}\Phi_\epsilon(y)-\liminf_{\epsilon\to0^+}\Phi_\epsilon(x_\epsilon)\le\Phi(x)-\Phi(x)=0.
\end{split}
\]
as desired.
\end{proof}
%\begin{rmk}
%The Theorem holds true if (ii) is replaced by the requirement that for all $x\in X$ there exists $\{x_\epsilon\}_{\epsilon>0}\subset X$ with $x_\epsilon\to X$ in $X$ and
%\[
%\Phi(x)\ge\limsup_{\epsilon\to 0^+}\Phi_\epsilon(x_\epsilon).
%\]
%In this case we say that $\Phi_\epsilon$ Mosco converge to $\Phi$ as $\epsilon\to 0^+$.
%\end{rmk}
Given $\Phi\colon X\to\mathbb{R}\cup\{\infty\}$ convex, proper and lower semicontinuous, a possible way to obtain its regularization $\Phi_\epsilon$ for all $\epsilon>0$ is through the Yosida approximation
\[
\Phi_\epsilon(x):=\inf_{y\in X}\left(\frac{\norm{x-y}^2}{2\epsilon}+\Phi(y)\right).
\]
Notice that it is well defined as a function $\Phi_\epsilon\colon X\to\mathbb{R}$. The following result shows that the Yosida regularization satisfies the desired approximation properties.
\begin{lemma}
\label{lemma:yosida}
Let $X$ be a separable and reflexive Banach space and endow it with a strictly convex norm. Let $\Phi\colon X\to\mathbb{R}\cup\{\infty\}$ be convex, proper and lower semicontinuous. Then also $\Phi_\epsilon$ is convex, proper and lower semicontinuous. Moreover
\begin{itemize}
\item[(i)] for all $x\in X$ and $\{x_\epsilon\}_{\epsilon>0}\subset X$ such that $x_\epsilon\rightharpoonup x$ in $X$ it holds that
\[
\Phi(x)\le\liminf_{\epsilon\to0^+}\Phi_\epsilon(x_\epsilon),
\]
\item[(ii)] for all $x\in X$
\[
\Phi(x)\ge\limsup_{\epsilon\to 0^+}\Phi_\epsilon(x).
\]
\end{itemize}
and $\Phi_\epsilon$ is G\^ateaux differentiable with $\Phi_\epsilon'\colon V\to V^*$ bounded and demicontinuous.
\end{lemma}
\begin{proof}
See~\cite[Lemma 5.17]{roubicek-pde}.
\end{proof}
\begin{cor}
\label{cor:corollary-5-19}
Let $X$ be a reflexive and separable Banach space. Let $\Phi\colon X\to\mathbb{R}\cup\{\infty\}$ be convex, proper and lower semicontinuous. Let $A\colon V\to V^*$ pseudomonotone. Suppose then following coercivity assumption holds: there exists a $\zeta\colon[0,\infty)\to[0,\infty)$ with the property that
\[
\lim_{r\to\infty}\zeta(r)=\infty
\]
such that for all $1>\epsilon>0$
\[
\frac{\Phi_\epsilon(x)+\dual{A(x),x}}{\norm{x}}\ge\zeta{\norm{x}}\quad\text{for all $x\in X$},
\]
where $\Phi_\epsilon$ is the Yosida approximation of $\Phi$. Then for each $f\in X^*$ there exists at least one $x\in X$ such that~\eqref{eqn:inclusion} holds.
\end{cor}
\begin{proof}
Follows immediately form Lemma~\ref{lemma:yosida} and Theorem~\ref{thm:inclusion}.
\end{proof}

\subsection{Lower semicontinuity of integral functionals}

\begin{thm}[Lower semicontinuity of integral functionals]
\label{thm:lower-semicontinuity-integral-functional}
Let $\Omega\subset\mathbb{R}^d$ open bounded. Let $f\colon\Omega\times\mathbb{R}^n\times\mathbb{R}^m\to(-\infty,\infty]$ such that
\begin{itemize}
\item[(i)] $f(\cdot,z,p)$ is measurable for all $(z,p)\in\mathbb{R}^n\times\mathbb{R}^m$
\item[(ii)] $f(x,\cdot,\cdot)$ is continuous for a.e.\ $x\in\Omega$
\item[(iii)] $f(x,z,\cdot)$ is convex for all $x\in\Omega$ and $z\in\mathbb{R}^n$
\item[(iv)] there exists $\phi\in L^1(\Omega)$ such that $f(x,z,p)\ge\phi(x)$ for a.e. $x\in\Omega$ and all $(z,p)\in\mathbb{R}^n\times\mathbb{R}^m$.
\end{itemize}
Then for all measurable $y\colon\Omega\to\mathbb{R}^n$ and $u\colon\Omega\to\mathbb{R}^m$ is well defined
\[
I(y,u)=\int_\Omega f(x,y,u)dx.
\]
Moreover if $y_k,y\colon\Omega\to\mathbb{R}^n$ measurable satisfy $y_k\to y$ in measure and $u_k,u\in L^1(\Omega,\mathbb{R}^m)$ satisfy $u_k\to u$ in $L^1(\Omega,\mathbb{R}^n)$, then
\[
I(y,u)\le\liminf_{k\to\infty}I(y_k,u_k).
\]
\end{thm}

\section{Convex analysis}

\begin{ex}
Let $f(v)=\frac{1}{2}\norm{v}^2$. Then $f^*(\xi)=\frac{1}{2}\norm{\xi}^2$. Indeed
\begin{equation*}
\dual{\xi,v}-\frac{1}{2}\norm{v}^2\le\frac{2\norm{\xi}\norm{v}-\norm{v}^2}{2}\le\frac{\norm{\xi}^2}{2}.
\end{equation*}
This implies $f^*(\xi)\le\frac{\norm{\xi}^2}{2}$. To get the opposite inequality, fix $\epsilon>0$ and let $v\in V$ such that $\norm{v}=\norm{\xi}$ and $\dual{\xi,v}\ge\norm{\xi}^2-\epsilon$. Then
\begin{equation*}
f^*(\xi)\ge\norm{\xi}^2-\epsilon-\frac{1}{2}\norm{\xi}^2\ge\frac{1}{2}\norm{\xi}^2-\epsilon.
\end{equation*}
By arbitrariness of $\epsilon$ we conclude.
\end{ex}

\begin{prop}[properties of positively 1-homogeneous convex functions]
\label{prop:prop-pos-1-hom-convx}
Let $f\colon V\to(-\infty,\infty]$ be a proper, l.s.c., positively 1-homogeneous and convex function. Then
\begin{itemize}
\item[(i)] $f(0)=0$ and $f$ is subadditive
\item[(ii)] for each $x\in\dom f$ it holds $\partial f(x)=\{\xi\in\partial f(0)\;|\;f(x)=\dual{\xi,x}\}$
\item[(iii)] $f^*=\iota_K$ with $K=\partial f(0)$ and $f=\sigma_K$.
\end{itemize}
\end{prop}
\begin{proof}
\emph{Step 1.} Let $v\in\dom f$. Since $f$ is l.s.c. and is positive 1-homogeneous
\begin{equation*}
f(0)\le\liminf_{n\to\infty}f\left(\frac{1}{n}v\right)=\liminf_{n\to\infty}\frac{f(v)}{n}=0.
\end{equation*}
By positively 1-homogeneity and convexity we have
\begin{equation*}
\frac{1}{2}f(v_0+v_1)=f\left(\frac{v_0+v_1}{2}\right)\le\frac{f(v_0)+f(v_1)}{2}.
\end{equation*}
This proves (i).

\emph{Step 2.} We prove that for all $\xi\in V^*$ it holds that $f^*(\xi)\in\left\{0,\infty\right\}$. Indeed let $\xi\in V^*$ such that $f^*(\xi)<\infty$. Then since $f$ is positively 1-homogeneous, it holds $f^*(\xi)=2^{-1}f^*(\xi)$ and this implies~$f^*(\xi)=0$.

\emph{Step 3.} Let $v\in\dom f$ and $\xi\in\partial f(v)$. This is equivalent to the equality $f(v)+f^*(\xi)=\dual{\xi,v}$ which in turn implies that $f^*(\xi)<\infty$ and by previous step $f^*(\xi)=0$. Then $f(v)=\dual{\xi,v}$ and for all $w\in V$
\begin{equation*}
f(w)\ge f(v)+\dual{\xi,w-v}=\dual{\xi,w}.
\end{equation*}
This is equivalent to $\xi\in\partial f(0)$. We have proved that if $\xi\in\partial f(v)$ then $f(v)=\dual{\xi,v}$ and $v\in\partial f(0)$. It is immediate to check that also the converse is true. This proves (ii).

\emph{Step 4}. It holds that $\xi\in K$ if and only if $f^*(\xi)=f(0)+f^*(\xi)=\dual{\xi,0}=0$. This proves that $f^*=\iota_K$. Since $f$ is l.s.c. then $f=f^**=\iota_K^*=\sigma_K$. This establishes (iii).
\end{proof}
